Two Cities

Modern English Edition

Introduction to the Reader

Welcome to this modernized edition of A Tale of Two Cities by Charles Dickens.

Originally published in 1859, this classic novel is famous for its vivid depiction of the French Revolution, the tragic sacrifice of Sydney Carton, and the timeless themes of love, redemption, and rebirth.

This edition is adapted into clear, modern English. With all due respect to the original work, our goal is to preserve the rich storytelling, character depth, and emotional power of Charles Dickens' masterpiece while offering a more accessible reading flow. It is designed as a helpful tool for language learners, students, and anyone discovering this classic story for the first time.

We hope you enjoy reading this timeless story.

Book the First: Recalled to Life

CHAPTER I: The Period

It was the best of times, and it was the worst of times. It was an age of wisdom, and it was an age of foolishness. It was an epoch of belief, and it was an epoch of skepticism. It was a season of Light, and it was a season of Darkness. It was a spring of hope, and it was a winter of despair. We had everything ahead of us, and we had nothing ahead of us. We were all going straight to Heaven, and we were all going straight the other way. In short, the time was so much like our own that some of its loudest voices insisted that it could only be described in extremes, whether for better or for worse.

There was a king with a large jaw and a queen with a plain face on the throne of England; there was a king with a large jaw and a queen with a beautiful face on the throne of France. In both countries, it was crystal clear to the wealthy lords who controlled the government's wealth and power that things in general were settled forever.

It was the year of Our Lord 1775. Spiritual revelations were granted to England during that favored time, just as they are today. Mrs. Southcott had recently celebrated her twenty-fifth birthday, and a prophetic soldier in the Life Guards had announced her grand arrival by claiming that plans were in place for London and Westminster to be swallowed up. Even the famous Cock Lane ghost had been put to rest only twelve years earlier, after tapping out its messages, just like the spirits of this past year (which were surprisingly unoriginal) tapped out theirs. Meanwhile, much more down-to-earth messages had recently arrived for the English King and his people from a congress of British subjects in America. Strange as it seems, these letters turned out to be far more important to the human race than any messages ever received from the spirits of the Cock Lane family.

France, which was generally less interested in spiritual matters than England, was rolling smoothly downhill, printing paper money and spending it quickly. Guided by her priests, France also amused herself with such "merciful" deeds as sentencing a young man to have his hands cut off, his tongue torn out with pliers, and his body burned alive, simply because he did not kneel down in the rain to show respect to a dirty group of monks passing by fifty or sixty yards away. It is highly likely that in the forests of France and Norway, trees were already growing, marked by the Woodman, Fate, to be cut down and sawed into planks to build a certain movable wooden frame with a sack and a heavy knife, which would become terrifying in history. It is also highly likely that in the simple barns of farmers near Paris, there were rough carts sitting that very day—spattered with country mud, sniffed at by pigs, and used by chickens to roost—which the Farmer, Death, had already chosen to carry prisoners to their executions during the coming Revolution. But that Woodman and that Farmer, though they worked without stopping, worked in absolute silence. No one heard them walking around with quiet steps, especially because even suggesting they were active was considered unpatriotic and against God.

In England, there was hardly enough law and order to brag about. Bold burglaries by armed men and highway robberies happened in the capital city itself every single night. Families were publicly warned not to leave town without storing their furniture in secure warehouses. The robber in the dark was often a respectable shopkeeper by day; if recognized and challenged by a fellow merchant whom he stopped while acting as the bandit "Captain," he would calmly shoot him in the head and ride off. The mail coach was ambushed by seven thieves; the guard shot three of them dead before being shot dead himself by the other four because he ran out of ammunition, after which the mail was robbed without any trouble. Even the grand and powerful Lord Mayor of London was forced to halt and hand over his valuables on Turnham Green by a single robber, who stripped the famous man of his wealth in front of all his servants. Prisoners in London jails fought battles with their guards, and the authorities fired heavy guns loaded with bullets right into the crowd. Thieves cut diamond crosses from the necks of noble lords at royal parties. Soldiers marched into St. Giles's to search for illegal goods, the angry crowd shot at the soldiers, the soldiers shot back at the crowd, and nobody thought any of this was out of the ordinary. In the middle of all this, the executioner, always busy and completely useless, was constantly called upon: sometimes hanging long rows of various criminals, sometimes hanging a burglar on Saturday who had just been caught on Tuesday, sometimes branding dozens of people on the hand at Newgate Prison, and sometimes burning rebellious pamphlets at the door of Westminster Hall. Today he would take the life of a terrible murderer, and tomorrow he would hang a poor thief who had stolen only sixpence from a farm boy.

All these things, and a thousand more like them, happened during and around the year 1775. Surrounded by these events, while the Woodman and the Farmer worked unnoticed, the two rulers with the large jaws and the other two with the plain and beautiful faces made a great fuss and ruled with absolute authority. In this way, the year 1775 led these powerful rulers, along with millions of ordinary people—including the characters in this story—along the paths that lay ahead of them.

CHAPTER II: The Mail

The Dover road stretched out on a late November Friday night, before the first person this story is about. For him, the Dover road lay beyond the mail coach as it slowly struggled up Shooter's Hill. He walked up the hill through the mud next to the coach, just like the other passengers. They weren't doing it because they enjoyed walking in those conditions, but because the hill, the harness, the mud, and the coach itself were all so heavy that the horses had already stopped three times. On one occasion, they even pulled the coach sideways across the road, clearly intending to take it back to Blackheath. However, the reins, whip, coachman, and guard, working together, had made it clear that such a rebellion was forbidden—even though the horses' actions strongly suggested that some animals actually have reasoning. So, the team had given in and returned to their duty.

With drooping heads and shaking tails, they struggled through the thick mud, floundering and stumbling occasionally, as if their main joints were about to fall apart. Every time the driver stopped them to rest, with a cautious "Woah there! Easy now!", the left lead horse violently shook its head and harness—like an especially stubborn horse, protesting that the coach couldn't possibly get up the hill. Whenever the leader made that rattling noise, the passenger flinched, as a nervous traveler might, and felt uneasy.

A steaming mist filled all the low-lying areas, and it had sadly drifted up the hill like an evil spirit, seeking rest but finding none. It was a damp and intensely cold mist, slowly moving through the air in visible ripples that followed and spread over one another like waves on an unhealthy sea. It was dense enough to block out everything from the coach-lamps' light except for its own swirling movements and a few yards of road; and the steam from the hard-working horses' breath drifted into it, as if they had created the whole thing.

Two other passengers were trudging up the hill next to the mail coach. All three were bundled up to their cheekbones and over their ears, and wore sturdy riding boots. None of the three could have said, based on what they saw, what the other two looked like; and each was just as hidden by their many layers from their companions' minds as they were from their eyes. In those days, travelers were very hesitant to trust strangers quickly, because anyone on the road could be a robber or working with robbers. As for the latter, since every inn and pub could have someone on the bandit "Captain’s" payroll—from the owner down to the lowliest stable hand—it was the most likely thing to happen. So the guard of the Dover mail coach thought to himself that Friday night in November 1775, slowly making its way up Shooter’s Hill, as he stood on his special platform behind the coach, stamping his feet, and keeping an eye and a hand on the weapons chest in front of him, where a loaded shotgun lay on top of six or eight loaded horse pistols, with a cutlass at the bottom.

The Dover mail was in its usual state of affairs where the guard suspected the passengers, the passengers suspected each other and the guard, they all suspected everyone else, and the coachman was only sure about the horses; as to those animals, he could honestly swear on the Holy Bible that they weren't fit for the journey.

"Woah there!" said the coachman. "Alright, then! Just one more pull and you’ll be at the top, and damn you, because I’ve had enough trouble getting you this far!—Joe!"

"Hello!" the guard replied.

"What time do you make it, Joe?"

"A good ten past eleven."

"Damn it!" the annoyed coachman burst out, "and we're not even at the top of Shooter's yet! Tsk! Come on! Get moving!"

The lead horse, which had been trying to slow down, was sharply told by the whip to keep going, and it made a determined scramble forward. The three other horses followed suit. Once again, the Dover mail coach struggled onward, with its passengers' boots squelching along beside it. They had stopped when the coach did, and they stayed right next to it. If any of the three had been bold enough to suggest to another that they walk a little ahead into the mist and darkness, he would have been practically asking to be shot instantly as a highwayman.

That last burst of effort carried the mail coach to the top of the hill. The horses stopped to catch their breath, and the guard got down to put the skid-shoe on the wheel for the descent, and open the coach door to let the passengers in.

"Psst! Joe!" the coachman called out in a warning voice, looking down from his seat.

"What do you say, Tom?"

They both listened.

"I hear a horse cantering up, Joe."

"I hear a horse at a gallop, Tom," the guard replied, letting go of the door and nimbly climbing back to his spot. "Gentlemen! In the King's name, everyone!"

After this urgent warning, he cocked his shotgun and got ready to fight.

The passenger this story is about was on the coach step, getting inside; the two other passengers were right behind him, about to follow. He stayed on the step, half in and half out of the coach; they stayed on the road below him. They all looked from the coachman to the guard, then from the guard to the coachman, and listened. The coachman looked back, the guard looked back, and even the lead horse pricked up its ears and listened, without arguing.

The quiet that came after the coach stopped rumbling and working hard, combined with the silent night, made it incredibly still. The horses' panting made the coach shake a little, as if it were nervous. The passengers' hearts probably beat loud enough to be heard; but either way, the quiet moment clearly showed that people were out of breath, holding their breath, and their hearts were racing with anticipation.

The sound of a horse at a gallop came charging up the hill.

"Hey!" the guard shouted as loud as he could. "Hey there! Stop! I'll shoot!"

The horse suddenly stopped, and, with a lot of splashing and struggling, a man’s voice called from the mist, "Is that the Dover mail?"

"Don't you worry about what it is!" the guard shot back. "Who are you?"

"Is that the Dover mail?"

"Why do you want to know?"

"I want a passenger, if it is."

"What passenger?"

"Mr. Jarvis Lorry."

The passenger immediately showed that it was his name. The guard, the coachman, and the two other passengers looked at him suspiciously.

"Stay right there," the guard called to the voice in the mist, "because if I make a mistake, you'll never live to fix it. Mr. Lorry, answer clearly."

"What's going on?" the passenger asked then, with a slightly shaky voice. "Who wants me? Is it Jerry?"

("I don’t like Jerry’s voice, if it is Jerry," the guard grumbled to himself. "His voice is too hoarse for my liking, that Jerry.")

"Yes, Mr. Lorry."

"What is the matter?"

"A message sent after you from over there. T. and Co."

"I know this messenger, guard," said Mr. Lorry, stepping down onto the road—helped from behind more quickly than politely by the other two passengers, who immediately scrambled into the coach, shut the door, and rolled up the window. "He can come closer; there’s nothing wrong."

"I hope not, but I can’t be so sure of that," said the guard, muttering to himself. "Hey you!"

"Well! And hello to you too!" said Jerry, more hoarsely than before.

"Come on at a walking pace! Do you hear me? And if you’ve got holsters on that saddle of yours, don’t let me see your hand go anywhere near them. Because I’m very quick to make a mistake, and when I do, it’s with a bullet. So now let’s get a good look at you."

A horse and rider slowly emerged through the swirling mist and came up to the side of the mail coach, where the passenger was standing. The rider bent down, and, looking up at the guard, handed the passenger a small folded piece of paper. The rider’s horse was winded, and both horse and rider were covered with mud, from the horse’s hooves to the man’s hat.

"Guard!" said the passenger, in a quiet, confident, businesslike tone.

The watchful guard, with his right hand on the stock of his raised shotgun, his left on the barrel, and his eye on the rider, answered curtly, "Sir."

"There’s nothing to worry about. I work for Tellson’s Bank. You must know Tellson’s Bank in London, right? I’m going to Paris on business. Here’s a crown for a drink. Can I read this?"

"As long as you’re quick about it, sir."

He opened it by the light of the coach-lamp on that side, and read it—first to himself and then aloud: "'Wait at Dover for Mademoiselle.' It’s not long, you see, guard. Jerry, tell them my answer was, RECALLED TO LIFE."

Jerry jumped in his saddle. "That's a really strange answer, too," he said, his voice raspy.

"Take that message back, and they'll know I got this, just as if I'd written it myself. Get going. Good night."

With those words, the passenger opened the coach door and got in. His fellow travelers didn't help him at all; they had quickly hidden their watches and purses in their boots and were now all pretending to be asleep. Their only goal was to avoid having to do anything else.

The coach rumbled on again, with thicker swirls of mist wrapping around it as it started going downhill. The guard soon put his shotgun back in his weapon chest. After checking the rest of its contents, and after checking the extra pistols he wore in his belt, he then checked a smaller chest under his seat, which held a few blacksmith's tools, a couple of torches, and a tinderbox. He was so well-equipped that if the coach lamps got blown out by the wind and rain (which happened sometimes), he just had to shut himself inside, make sure the flint and steel sparks didn't land on the straw, and he could get a light pretty safely and easily (if he was lucky) in five minutes.

"Tom!" softly from the coach roof.

"Hello, Joe."

"Did you hear the message?"

"I did, Joe."

"What did you make of it, Tom?"

"Nothing at all, Joe."

"That's a coincidence, too," the guard thought, "because I didn't get anything from it either."

Jerry, left alone in the mist and darkness, got off his horse in the meantime, not just to give his tired horse a break, but also to wipe the mud from his face, and shake the water out of his hat brim, which looked like it could hold about half a gallon. After standing with the bridle draped over his heavily-splashed arm until he couldn't hear the mail coach's wheels anymore and the night was completely quiet again, he turned to walk down the hill.

"After that gallop from Temple Bar, old lady, I won't trust your front legs until we're on flat ground," said the hoarse messenger, glancing at his mare. "'Recalled to life.' That's a really strange message. A lot of that wouldn't work out for you, Jerry! Hey, Jerry! You'd be in a really bad way if bringing people back to life became popular, Jerry!"

CHAPTER III: The Night Shadows

It is a wonderful thing to think about, that every human being is designed to be a deep secret and mystery to everyone else. It is a sobering thought, when entering a large city at night, that every single one of those dark, crowded houses holds its own secret; that every room in every one of them holds its own secret; that every beating heart among the hundreds of thousands of people there, in some of its thoughts, is a secret even to the heart closest to it! Part of the terror, even of Death itself, can be traced back to this. I can no longer turn the pages of this dear book I loved, and hope to read it all eventually. I can no longer look into the depths of this bottomless water, where, as brief flashes of light hit it, I've caught glimpses of buried treasure and other things hidden underwater. It was fated that the book would spring shut, forever and ever, when I had only read one page. It was fated that the water would be frozen solid forever, just as the light was dancing on its surface, and I stood clueless on the shore. My friend is dead, my neighbor is dead, my love, the darling of my soul, is dead; it is the unavoidable sealing and keeping alive of the secret that was always part of that person, and which I'll carry in my own heart until I die. In any of the cemeteries in this city I walk through, is there anyone sleeping there who is more mysterious than its busy residents are, deep down, to me, or than I am to them?

When it came to this—his natural inheritance that couldn't be taken away—the messenger on horseback had exactly the same as the King, the Prime Minister, or the richest merchant in London. It was the same for the three passengers cooped up in the cramped space of one clunky old mail coach; they were complete mysteries to each other, as if each had been in their own private carriage with six horses, or even sixty, with the width of a county between him and the next.

The messenger rode back at an easy trot, stopping pretty often at pubs along the way to drink, but showing a tendency to keep to himself and keep his hat pulled low over his eyes. He had eyes that went well with that look: they were flat black, with no depth in color or shape, and set much too close together—as if they were afraid of being caught doing something separately if they stood too far apart. They had a sinister look, hidden under an old cocked hat that looked like a three-cornered spittoon, and above a large scarf for his chin and throat that hung down almost to his knees. When he stopped for a drink, he pulled this scarf away with his left hand only while pouring the alcohol in with his right; as soon as that was done, he wrapped himself back up.

"No, Jerry, no!" said the messenger, repeating the same idea as he rode. "It wouldn't work out for you, Jerry. Jerry, you honest tradesman, it wouldn't suit *your* line of work! Recalled—! Blow me if I don't think he's been drinking!"

His message confused his mind to such a degree that he felt forced, several times, to take off his hat to scratch his head. Except for the top of his head, which was patchily bald, he had stiff, black hair that stood up jaggedly all over, growing forward almost to his broad, flat nose. It looked so much like metalwork, far more like the top of a spiked wall than a head of hair, that even the best players of leapfrog would have avoided him as the most dangerous man in the world to jump over.

As he trotted back with the message he was to deliver to the night watchman in his booth at the door of Tellson's Bank, near Temple Bar (who would deliver it to higher officials inside), the night shadows took on shapes in his mind that came from the message, and took on shapes in the mare's mind that came from *her* private anxieties. They seemed to be many, because she shied away from every shadow on the road.

Meanwhile, the mail coach rumbled, jolted, rattled, and bumped along its exhausting path, with its three mysterious passengers inside. To them, too, the night shadows showed themselves in whatever shapes their sleepy eyes and wandering thoughts suggested.

Tellson's Bank was very active in the mind of the passenger. As the banker—with his arm slipped through the leather strap, which did its best to keep him from slamming into the next passenger and pushing him into the corner whenever the coach got a particularly bad jolt—dozed in his seat with half-closed eyes, the small coach windows, the coach lamp dimly shining through them, and the bulky silhouette of the passenger opposite him all transformed into the bank, where he conducted a great deal of business. The rattling of the harness became the clinking of coins, and more checks were processed in five minutes than Tellson's, with all its local and international connections, had ever paid out in three times that duration. Then the underground vaults at Tellson's, along with the valuable treasures and secrets known to the passenger (and he knew quite a lot about them), opened up before him. He walked among them with his large keys and a dimly burning candle, finding them safe, secure, intact, and quiet, just as he had last seen them.

But even though the bank was almost constantly in his thoughts, and the coach (in a blurry way, like the feeling of pain under medicine) was always present, there was another stream of thought that never stopped running all through the night. He was on his way to dig someone out of a grave.

Now, the night shadows did not show which of the many faces appearing before him was the true face of the buried person. But they were all the faces of a forty-five-year-old man, differing mainly in the emotions they showed and in how pale, worn, and ruined they looked. Pride, contempt, defiance, stubbornness, surrender, and sorrow followed one another, as did different kinds of sunken cheeks, deathly pale skin, and thin, skeletal hands and bodies. Yet, the face was generally the same face, and every head of hair was prematurely white. A hundred times, the dozing passenger asked this ghost:

"Buried how long?"

The answer was always the same: "Almost eighteen years."

"You had given up all hope of being dug out?"

"Long ago."

"You know that you are brought back to life?"

"They tell me so."

"I hope you care to live?"

"I can't say."

"Shall I show her to you? Will you come and see her?"

The answers to this question were different and contradictory. Sometimes the shaky reply was, "Wait! It would kill me if I saw her too soon." Sometimes it was spoken through a gentle flow of tears: "Take me to her." Sometimes it was wide-eyed and confused: "I don't know her. I don't understand."

After these imagined conversations, the passenger in his mind would dig, and dig, and dig—sometimes with a shovel, sometimes with a large key, and sometimes with his bare hands—to get this miserable creature out. Once finally pulled out, with dirt clinging to his face and hair, the man would suddenly crumble away to dust. The passenger would then startle awake, and roll down the window to feel the cold reality of the mist and rain on his cheek.

Yet even when his eyes were open to the mist and rain, the moving patch of light from the coach lamps, and the roadside hedges passing by in jerks, the night shadows outside the coach would merge with the night shadows in his mind. The actual bank building near Temple Bar, the real business of the day before, the real vaults, the real messenger sent after him, and the real answer sent back would all be there. Out of the middle of them, the ghostly face would rise, and he would speak to it again.

"Buried how long?"

"Almost eighteen years."

"I hope you care to live?"

"I can't say."

Dig—dig—dig—until an impatient shift from one of the other two passengers warned him to roll up the window, slip his arm back securely through the leather strap, and think about the two sleeping figures, until his mind lost its grip on them and they drifted away again into the bank and the grave.

"Buried how long?"

"Almost eighteen years."

"You had given up all hope of being dug out?"

"Long ago."

The words were still ringing in his ears as if they had just been spoken—as clearly as any spoken words he had ever heard in his life—when the tired passenger startled awake to the morning light, finding that the shadows of the night were gone.

He rolled down the window and looked out at the rising sun. There was a ridge of plowed land, with a plow sitting where it had been left the night before when the horses were unharnessed. Beyond that was a quiet woods, where many leaves of burning red and golden yellow still hung on the trees. Although the ground was cold and wet, the sky was clear, and the sun rose bright, calm, and beautiful. "Eighteen years!" said the passenger, looking up at the sun. "Gracious Creator of the day! To be buried alive for eighteen years!"

CHAPTER IV: The Preparation

When the mail coach finally arrived in Dover later that morning, the chief waiter at the Royal George Hotel opened the coach door as usual. He did so with quite a bit of ceremony, for traveling by mail coach from London in the winter was a major accomplishment that called for congratulating any adventurous traveler.

By then, there was only one adventurous traveler left to congratulate: the other two had already been dropped off at their destinations along the way. The musty interior of the coach—with its damp, dirty straw, unpleasant smell, and dark atmosphere—looked a lot like a large dog kennel. Mr. Lorry, shaking himself free of the straw, wrapped in a messy coat, with a floppy hat and muddy legs, looked rather like a large dog stepping out of his house.

"Will there be a ferry to Calais tomorrow, waiter?"

"Yes, sir, if the weather stays good and the wind is fair. The tide will be perfect around two in the afternoon, sir. Would you like a room, sir?"

"I won't go to bed until tonight; but I need a bedroom and a barber."

"And then breakfast, sir? Yes, sir. Right this way, please. Show the gentleman to the Concord room! Carry his bag and hot water to Concord. Take off his boots in Concord. (You'll find a nice coal fire there, sir.) Bring the barber to Concord. Hurry up now, get moving on Concord!"

Because the Concord bedroom was always given to mail coach passengers, and those passengers were always bundled up from head to toe, the room held a funny fascination for the staff at the Royal George: even though only one type of bundled-up traveler was ever seen entering it, all sorts of different men walked out. As a result, another waiter, two porters, several maids, and the landlady just happened to be hanging around at different spots along the hallway between Concord and the dining room when a sixty-year-old gentleman, formally dressed in a brown suit that was well-worn but very neat, with large square cuffs and big pocket flaps, walked by on his way to breakfast.

The dining room had no other guests that morning except for the gentleman in brown. His breakfast table was set in front of the fireplace, and as he sat there with the firelight shining on him, waiting for his food, he sat so still that he could have been posing for a portrait.

He looked very neat and systematic, sitting with a hand on each knee, while a loud watch ticked solemnly under his vest, as if its slow, steady pace was competing with the playful, quick-burning fire. He had good-looking legs and was a bit proud of them, as his brown stockings fit smoothly and tightly and were made of fine material; his shoes and buckles, too, though simple, were neat. He wore an odd, small, tight, light-colored wig that sat very close to his head; one would assume the wig was made of real hair, but it looked much more like it was spun from threads of silk or glass. His shirt, though not as fine as his stockings, was as white as the foam on the breaking waves of the nearby beach, or the distant sails glinting in the sunlight far out at sea. His face, which was usually kept calm and expressionless, was lit up under the old-fashioned wig by a pair of bright, lively eyes. It must have taken him a lot of effort in his younger years to train his face to have the cold, serious look required at Tellson’s Bank. He had a healthy color in his cheeks, and his face, though wrinkled, showed very few signs of worry. But perhaps the unmarried clerks at Tellson's Bank were mostly busy dealing with other people's worries; and perhaps secondhand worries, like secondhand clothes, are easy to put on and take off.

Looking more than ever like someone posing for a painting, Mr. Lorry drifted off to sleep. The arrival of his breakfast woke him up, and he told the waiter as he pulled up his chair:

"I want a room prepared for a young lady who might arrive at any time today. She may ask for Mr. Jarvis Lorry, or she might just ask for a gentleman from Tellson's Bank. Please let me know when she arrives."

"Yes, sir. Tellson's Bank in London, sir?"

"Yes."

"Yes, sir. We often have the honor of hosting your gentlemen as they travel back and forth between London and Paris, sir. There's a huge amount of travel done by the staff of Tellson and Company."

"Yes. We are very much a French firm, as well as an English one."

"Yes, sir. You don't travel back and forth like that much yourself, I take it, sir?"

"Not in recent years. It has been fifteen years since we—since I—last returned from France."

"Really, sir? That was before my time here, sir. Before any of our staff worked here. The George Hotel was under different management back then, sir."

"I believe so."

"But I would bet a good amount, sir, that a company like Tellson and Company was doing very well fifty years ago, let alone fifteen?"

"You could triple that and say a hundred and fifty, and still be close to the truth."

"Is that so, sir!"

Opening his mouth and eyes wide in surprise as he stepped back from the table, the waiter moved his napkin from his right arm to his left, stood in a relaxed posture, and watched the guest eat and drink as if looking down from a watchtower. This was, of course, following the ancient custom of waiters throughout history.

When Mr. Lorry finished breakfast, he went out for a walk on the beach. The tiny, narrow, crooked town of Dover seemed to hide from the shore, burying its head into the white chalk cliffs like a sea ostrich. The beach was a barren waste of stones and rocks piled wildly, and the sea did whatever it wanted—which was mostly destruction. It crashed against the town and thundered against the cliffs, constantly wearing away the coast. The air in the streets smelled so strongly of fish that you might have thought sick fish came up to the town to breathe it, just as sick humans went down to bathe in the sea. There was a little fishing in the harbor, and a lot of walking around at night to look out at the water, especially when the tide was coming in. Small shopkeepers who never seemed to do any actual business somehow made huge fortunes, and it was notable that nobody in the area could stand a lamplighter.

As the afternoon faded, the air—which had been clear enough at times to spot the coast of France—grew thick with mist and fog again, and Mr. Lorry’s thoughts seemed to cloud over as well. When it got dark and he sat in front of the fireplace waiting for dinner, just as he had waited for breakfast, his mind was busy digging, digging, digging in the glowing red coals.

A bottle of good red wine after dinner does not hurt someone daydreaming in the coals, except that it tends to make them stop working. Mr. Lorry had been relaxing for a long time, and had just poured his last glass with a look of complete satisfaction—the kind only found in a healthy-looking older gentleman who has finished a bottle—when the clatter of wheels came up the narrow street and rumbled into the inn yard.

He set his glass down without drinking. "This is the young lady!" he said.

A few minutes later, the waiter came in to announce that Miss Manette had arrived from London and would be happy to see the gentleman from Tellson’s.

"So soon?"

Miss Manette had eaten on the road and didn't need anything now, and she was very eager to see the gentleman from Tellson’s right away, if it was convenient for him.

The gentleman from Tellson's had no choice but to finish his glass with quiet desperation, adjust his odd little light wig over his ears, and follow the waiter to Miss Manette's room. It was a large, dark room, decorated in a gloomy, funeral-like style with black horsehair furniture and heavy dark tables. These tables had been polished so many times that the two tall candles sitting in the middle of the room were reflected darkly on every wooden surface; it looked as if *they* were buried in deep graves of black wood, and no real light could be expected from them until they were dug out.

The room was so dark that Mr. Lorry, walking carefully over the worn carpet, assumed at first that Miss Manette was in another room. But as he passed the two tall candles, he saw a young lady of about seventeen standing to greet him by the table between the candles and the fire. She wore a riding cloak and still held her straw sunhat by its ribbon in her hand. As he looked at her short, slender, pretty figure, her golden hair, her blue eyes looking at him searchingly, and her smooth young forehead that had a unique way of wrinkling and frowning—showing a look that wasn't quite confusion, wonder, alarm, or intense focus, but a mixture of all four—he suddenly remembered a child he had held in his arms while crossing this very Channel years ago, on a cold day when hail fell heavily and the sea was rough. The memory faded quickly, like a breath on the surface of the tall mirror behind her. The mirror's frame was decorated with a strange group of carved figures, some broken and headless, holding baskets to show off to carvings of goddesses. He made his formal bow to Miss Manette.

"Please take a seat, sir," she said in a clear, pleasant young voice. It had a slight foreign accent, but only a very little.

"I kiss your hand, miss," said Mr. Lorry, using the old-fashioned manners of his younger days, making another formal bow before sitting down.

"I received a letter from the bank yesterday, sir, informing me that some information—or discovery—"

"The exact word doesn't matter, miss; either one works."

"—regarding the small property of my poor father, whom I never saw because he died so long ago—"

Mr. Lorry shifted in his chair and looked uncomfortably at the mirror's strange carvings. As if *they* had any help to offer anyone in their ridiculous baskets!

"—makes it necessary for me to go to Paris, to speak with a representative of the bank who was kind enough to be sent there for this purpose."

"That would be me."

"So I was told, sir."

She curtsied to him (which is what young ladies did in those days), politely showing that she respected him as someone older and wiser. He bowed back to her again.

"I replied to the bank, sir, that since those who advise me think it necessary for me to go to France, and since I am an orphan and have no friends to go with me, I would be very grateful to travel under that gentleman's protection. The gentleman had already left London, but I believe a messenger was sent after him to ask him to wait for me here."

"I was happy," said Mr. Lorry, "to be given this responsibility. I will be even happier to carry it out."

"Sir, thank you. I am very grateful. The bank told me that the gentleman would explain the details of this business to me, and that I should prepare myself for some surprising news. I have done my best to prepare, and naturally, I am very anxious to know what it is."

"Naturally," said Mr. Lorry. "Yes—I—"

After a pause, he adjusted his tight light wig over his ears again and said, "It is very difficult to begin."

He didn't start right away, but hesitated and looked at her. Her young forehead wrinkled in that unique way—which was pretty and characteristic of her—and she raised her hand, as if automatically trying to catch or stop a passing shadow.

"Are you a complete stranger to me, sir?"

"Aren't I?" Mr. Lorry opened his hands and spread them with a polite smile.

The frown between her eyebrows and just above her delicate nose deepened as she sat down thoughtfully in the chair she had been standing next to. He watched her as she sat thinking, and the moment she looked up at him again, he continued:

"Since England is your adopted home, I assume I should address you as an English lady, Miss Manette?"

"If you please, sir."

"Miss Manette, I am a businessman. I have a business task to complete. As you listen to it, don't think of me as anything more than a talking machine—really, I'm not much else. With your permission, I will tell you the story of one of our clients."

"A story?"

He pretended to misunderstand why she repeated the word, adding quickly, "Yes, a client. In banking, we usually call our contacts our clients. He was a French gentleman, a man of science, highly educated—a Doctor."

"Not from Beauvais?"

"Why, yes, from Beauvais. Just like Monsieur Manette, your father, the gentleman was from Beauvais. Like your father, he was well-known in Paris. I had the honor of knowing him there. Our relationship was professional, but very confidential. At that time, I was working in our French branch, and had been there for—oh, twenty years."

"At that time—may I ask, what time was that, sir?"

"I am speaking of twenty years ago, miss. He married an English lady, and I was one of the trustees of his estate. His financial affairs, like those of many other French gentlemen, were kept entirely in Tellson's hands. In the same way, I have acted as a trustee in some form for dozens of our clients. These are purely business relationships, miss; there is no personal friendship in them, no special interest, nothing emotional. I have moved from one client to another throughout my career just as I move from one account to another during my workday. In short, I have no feelings; I am just a machine. To continue—"

"But this is my father's story, sir. And I am starting to think"—her wrinkled forehead focused intensely on him—"that when I was left an orphan because my mother only outlived my father by two years, it was you who brought me to England. I am almost sure it was you."

Mr. Lorry took her small, hesitating hand as she reached out to him trustingly, and kissed it with polite ceremony. He then led the young lady back to her chair, and, holding the back of the chair with his left hand while using his right to rub his chin, adjust his wig, or gesture as he spoke, stood looking down at her face while she sat looking up at him.

"Miss Manette, it *was* me. And you will see how true it is that I have no feelings—and that all my relationships are strictly professional—when you realize that I haven't seen you since that day. No; you have been a ward of Tellson's Bank ever since, and I have been busy with other bank business. Feelings! I have no time for them, no opportunity for them. I spend my whole life, miss, turning a giant money-grinding machine."

After this strange description of his daily work, Mr. Lorry flattened his light wig onto his head with both hands (which was completely unnecessary, since nothing could be flatter than it already was), and went back to his previous posture.

"So far, miss, as you noted, this is the story of your late father. Now comes the difference. If your father had not died when he did—don't be frightened! Why do you jump like that?"

She did jump, startled. And she grabbed his wrist with both hands.

"Please," said Mr. Lorry in a calming voice, placing his left hand over her trembling fingers that held him so tightly. "Please calm yourself—remember, this is just a matter of business. As I was saying—"

The look on her face shook him so much that he stopped, hesitated, and started over:

"As I was saying: if Monsieur Manette had not died; if he had suddenly and silently disappeared; if he had been carried off; if it was easy to guess what terrible place he was taken to, even though no one could find him; if he had an enemy with the power to fill out blank arrest warrants to lock anyone away in a silent prison forever—a power that even the bravest people in France were afraid to whisper about; if his wife had begged the king, the queen, the court, and the church for any news of him, all in vain; then the story of your father would be the story of this unfortunate gentleman, the Doctor of Beauvais."

"I beg you to tell me more, sir."

"I will. I am about to. Can you handle it?"

"I can bear anything except the uncertainty you are keeping me in right now."

"You speak calmly, and you *are* calm. That’s good!" (Though he looked less confident than his words suggested.) "A matter of business. Look at it as a matter of business—work that must be done. Now, if this doctor's wife, though she was a woman of great courage and strength, had suffered so intensely from this before her baby was born—"

"The baby was a daughter, sir."

"A daughter. A—a matter of business—don't be upset. Miss, if the poor lady had suffered so intensely before her baby was born that she decided to save her child from inheriting any of the agony she had felt, by raising her to believe that her father was dead—No, don't kneel! In Heaven's name, why are you kneeling to me?"

"For the truth. Oh, dear, good, kind sir, for the truth!"

"A—a matter of business. You are making me confused, and how can I do business if I am confused? Let's keep our heads clear. If you could kindly tell me, for instance, what nine times nine is, or how many shillings are in twenty pounds, it would be very encouraging. I would feel much more comfortable about your state of mind."

Without directly answering his odd request, she sat very still after he gently helped her up, and the hands holding his wrists were much steadier than before. This helped calm Mr. Jarvis Lorry down.

"That’s right, that’s right. Courage! Business! You have important, useful work ahead of you. Miss Manette, your mother decided to raise you this way. And when she died—brokenhearted, I believe, having never stopped her useless search for your father—she left you, at two years old, to grow up healthy, beautiful, and happy, without the dark cloud of wondering whether your father died quickly in prison or wasted away there over many long years."

As he spoke, he looked down with pity and admiration at her flowing golden hair, as if imagining that it could have already been turned gray by worry.

"You know that your parents had very little money, and that what they did have was secured for your mother and you. There has been no new discovery of money or property; but—"

He felt her grip tighten on his wrist, and he stopped. The expression on her forehead, which had caught his attention before and was now frozen, had deepened into a look of pain and horror.

"But he has been—been found. He is alive. Highly changed, most likely; perhaps almost a complete wreck, though we will hope for the best. Still, he is alive. Your father has been taken to the home of an old servant in Paris, and we are going there: I, to identify him if I can; you, to restore him to life, love, work, rest, and comfort."

A shiver ran through her body, and from her into him. She said in a low, clear, awestruck voice, as if speaking in a dream:

"I am going to see his ghost! It will be his ghost—not him!"

Mr. Lorry gently rubbed the hands holding his arm. "There, there, there! Look, now! You know the best and the worst of it now. You are on your way to see the poor, wronged gentleman, and after a smooth sea voyage and a land journey, you will soon be by his side."

She repeated in the same tone, sinking to a whisper, "I have been free, I have been happy, yet his ghost has never haunted me!"

"Only one more thing," said Mr. Lorry, emphasizing it to keep her attention. "He was found under another name; his own was forgotten or hidden long ago. It would be worse than useless to ask which; worse than useless to try to find out if he was forgotten for years or kept prisoner on purpose. It would be dangerous to ask questions now. It is better not to talk about this anywhere, in any way, and to get him out of France—at least for a while. Even I, safe as an Englishman, and even Tellson's Bank, as important as we are to French finance, avoid talking about it. I don't carry a single scrap of paper that mentions it. This is a completely secret mission. My credentials, notes, and records are all summed up in the single phrase, 'Recalled to Life,' which could mean anything. But what's wrong? She isn't hearing a word! Miss Manette!"

Completely still and silent, not even leaning back in her chair, she sat under his touch, totally unconscious. Her eyes were open and fixed on him, and that worried expression looked as if it were carved or branded into her forehead. She held his arm so tightly that he was afraid to pull away in case he hurt her, so he called out loudly for help without moving.

A wild-looking woman—who, even in his panic, Mr. Lorry noticed had a very red complexion, red hair, and wore a strangely tight dress and an enormous hat that looked like a wooden bucket or a giant block of Stilton cheese—came running into the room ahead of the hotel servants. She quickly solved the problem of freeing his arm from the poor young lady by placing a strong, muscular hand on his chest and pushing him flying back against the wall.

("I really think this must be a man!" was Mr. Lorry's breathless thought as he hit the wall.)

"Look at all of you!" this woman yelled at the hotel servants. "Why don't you go and fetch help, instead of standing there staring at me? I'm not much to look at, am I? Why don't you go get things? I'll show you what's what if you don't bring smelling salts, cold water, and vinegar right now!"

The servants quickly scattered to get these items, and she gently laid the unconscious girl on a sofa, tending to her with great skill and care. She called her "my precious!" and "my sweet bird!" and spread her golden hair over her shoulders with pride and tenderness.

"And you in brown!" she said, turning angrily to Mr. Lorry. "Couldn't you tell her what you had to say without scaring her to death? Look at her, with her pretty pale face and cold hands. Do you call *that* being a banker?"

Mr. Lorry was so embarrassed by this hard-to-answer question that he could only watch from a distance, feeling very quiet and humble. Meanwhile, the strong woman—having chased away the hotel servants by threatening to "let them know" what would happen if they stayed staring—gradually helped the young lady recover, gently encouraging her to lay her head on her shoulder.

"I hope she will be okay now," said Mr. Lorry.

"No thanks to you in brown, if she is. My darling girl!"

"I hope," said Mr. Lorry, after another quiet pause, "that you will be going with Miss Manette to France?"

"As if!" replied the strong woman. "If I was ever meant to cross the ocean, do you think God would have had me born on an island?"

This being another question that was hard to answer, Mr. Jarvis Lorry stepped away to think about it.

CHAPTER V: The Wine-shop

A large barrel of wine had been dropped and broken in the street. The accident happened while unloading it from a cart; the barrel had rolled out quickly, the metal bands burst, and it lay on the cobblestones right outside the door of the wine shop, shattered like a walnut shell.

Everyone nearby stopped whatever they were doing—or not doing—to run to the spot and drink the wine. The rough, uneven stones of the street, pointing in every direction and seeming designed specifically to cripple anyone walking on them, had dammed the wine into little pools. Each pool was surrounded by a crowding group, depending on its size. Some men knelt down, scooped the wine with their hands, and drank, or helped women leaning over their shoulders to drink before the wine could leak through their fingers. Others dipped into the puddles with chipped clay mugs, or even with scarves taken from women's heads, which they squeezed dry into babies' mouths. Some made small mud walls to block the wine as it flowed, while others, directed by neighbors looking out from high windows, ran around to block small streams of wine branching off in new directions. A few focused on the wine-soaked wood from the broken barrel, licking and even chewing the wet, wine-stained fragments with enjoyment. Since there was no drainage system, not only was all the wine scooped up, but so much dirt was taken up with it that the street actually looked clean—though nobody who knew the neighborhood would ever expect to see a street sweeper there.

Loud laughter and cheerful voices of men, women, and children filled the street while this wine hunt lasted. There was very little roughness in the play, and plenty of fun. There was a strong sense of community, as everyone wanted to share in the excitement together. This led, especially among the luckier ones, to happy hugs, toasts, handshakes, and even groups of a dozen people joining hands to dance. When the wine was gone, and the places where it had pooled were scraped clean down to the dirt by eager fingers, the celebration stopped just as suddenly as it had started. The man who had left his saw in the firewood went back to work. The woman who had left a small pot of hot ashes on a doorstep—trying to warm her own frozen fingers and toes or those of her child—returned to it. Men with bare arms, messy hair, and pale, ghost-like faces, who had come up into the winter light from dark cellars, walked back down to their rooms. A dark gloom settled over the street once more, looking far more natural to the street than sunshine ever did.

The wine was red, and it stained the ground of the narrow street in the Paris suburb of Saint Antoine where it had spilled. It also stained many hands, faces, bare feet, and wooden shoes. The hands of the woodcutter left red marks on the logs he cut, and the forehead of the woman who nursed her baby was stained by the dirty red rag she wrapped back around her head. Those who had licked the barrel wood had a wild, tiger-like red smear around their mouths. One tall, messy man with a long, dirty nightcap on his head dipped his finger in the muddy wine dregs and wrote on a wall—BLOOD.

The time was coming when a different kind of red wine would be spilled on these street stones, and its stain would be red on many of the people there.

Now that the dark cloud settled back over Saint Antoine, the darkness felt heavy. Cold, dirt, sickness, ignorance, and poverty ruled the neighborhood—all of them powerful forces, but poverty most of all. People who had been ground down by life shivered at every corner, walked in and out of doorways, looked from windows, and wore tattered clothes that fluttered in the wind. The system that ground them down was one that made young people look old. The children had aged faces and serious voices. On every face, carved into every wrinkle of age, was the sign of Hunger. It was everywhere. Hunger hung out of the tall houses in the tattered clothes drying on poles; Hunger was patched into the windows with straw, rags, wood, and paper; Hunger was seen in the tiny scraps of firewood the man sawed; Hunger stared down from the smokeless chimneys and rose from the filthy streets, where there wasn't even a scrap of discarded food to eat. Hunger was written on the baker's shelves, in every small, bad loaf of bread left in stock; and at the sausage shop, in the cheap, mystery-meat sausages offered for sale. Hunger rattled like dry bones among the roasting chestnuts in the metal cylinders, and Hunger was chopped into tiny pieces in the cheap bowls of potato scraps fried with a few drops of oil.

Its home was suitable in every way. The narrow, winding street was full of trash and bad smells, with other narrow streets branching off, all filled with people wearing rags and nightcaps, smelling of poverty, and looking grim. Yet in the desperate look of the people, there was still a wild, animal-like thought of fighting back. Though they looked beaten down, there were still fiery eyes among them, tight lips pressed white from anger, and foreheads wrinkled like the hangman's rope they thought about either escaping or using on their enemies. The shop signs were all grim pictures of need. The butcher painted only the leanest, poorest cuts of meat on his sign, and the baker showed only the coarsest, smallest loaves. The crude paintings of people drinking in the wine shops showed them huddling over tiny glasses of sour wine and cheap beer, whispering darkly to one another. Nothing was in good condition except for tools and weapons: the knives and axes in the shop windows were sharp and bright, the blacksmith's hammers were heavy, and the gunmaker's weapons looked deadly. The uneven cobblestone streets, full of mud and puddles, had no sidewalks, ending abruptly at the doors of the houses. The open sewer gutter ran down the middle of the street—when it ran at all, which was only after heavy rains, and then it flooded into the houses. Across the streets, far apart, single streetlamps were hung by a rope and pulley. At night, after the lamplighter lowered, lit, and raised them again, a weak line of dim wicks swung overhead, looking as if they were on a ship at sea. Indeed, they were at sea, and the ship and crew were in danger of a massive storm.

For the time was coming when the starving people of this area, having watched the lamplighter for so long in their hunger, would decide to improve on his method and hang men from those very ropes and pulleys to shine a light on their miserable lives. But that time had not come yet; and the winds blowing over France shook the rags of the poor in vain, for the wealthy, beautiful aristocrats took no warning.

The wine shop was on a corner, looking better than most other shops in the area. The owner stood outside in a yellow vest and green pants, watching the struggle for the spilled wine. "It's not my problem," he said with a shrug. "The market workers dropped it. Let them bring another one."

Just then, his eyes caught the tall joker writing his graffiti, and he called out to him across the street:

"Hey, Gaspard, what are you doing there?"

The man pointed to his writing with great seriousness, as jokers often do. But his joke fell flat, which is also typical.

"What is this? Are you crazy?" said the wine shop owner, crossing the street and wiping out the writing with a handful of mud scooped up from the ground. "Why are you writing on the public street? Isn't there—tell me—any other place to write such words?"

As he argued, he placed his clean hand (perhaps by accident, perhaps on purpose) over the joker’s heart. The joker tapped it with his own hand, jumped high in the air, and landed in a dramatic dance pose, holding one of his dirty shoes which he had pulled off his foot. He looked like an extremely wild, almost wolfish character.

"Put it back on, put it back on," said the owner. "Call wine, wine, and leave it at that." With that advice, he wiped his muddy hand on the joker’s tattered clothes—quite deliberately, as if it was the joker's fault his hand got dirty—then crossed the street again and entered his shop.

The wine shop owner was a thick-necked, tough-looking man of thirty. He must have had a hot temper, because even though it was a freezing cold day, he wore no coat, carrying it slung over his shoulder instead. His sleeves were rolled up, leaving his brown arms bare to the elbows. He wore nothing on his head, showing only his short, curly dark hair. He was a dark-complexioned man, with good, wide-set eyes. He looked friendly enough on the whole, but also stubborn and unyielding; clearly a man of strong determination and purpose. He was not someone you would want to meet in a tight spot, because nothing could make him back down.

His wife, Madame Defarge, sat behind the counter as he walked in. She was a stout woman about his age, with watchful eyes that rarely seemed to look directly at anything, large hands covered in heavy rings, a calm face, strong features, and great composure. There was a strength about her that suggested she rarely made mistakes in any of her business calculations. Being sensitive to the cold, she was wrapped in fur and had a bright shawl wrapped around her head, though her large earrings were still visible. Her knitting sat in front of her, but she had laid it down to pick her teeth with a toothpick. In this pose, holding her elbow in her hand, she said nothing when her husband came in, but gave a tiny cough. This, along with a slight raise of her dark eyebrows, signaled to her husband that he should look around at the clients to see if anyone new had arrived while he was outside.

The owner looked around the room until his eyes rested on an older gentleman and a young lady sitting in a corner. Other customers were there: two playing cards, two playing dominoes, and three standing by the counter nursing their small drinks. As he walked behind the counter, he noticed the older gentleman look at the young lady as if to say, "This is our man."

"What on earth are *you* doing here?" Monsieur Defarge thought to himself. "I don't know you."

But he pretended not to notice the two strangers, and started talking to the three customers drinking at the counter.

"How's it going, Jacques?" one of the three asked Monsieur Defarge. "Is all the spilled wine gone?" "Every drop, Jacques," Defarge replied.

When this exchange of the name "Jacques" was made, Madame Defarge, still picking her teeth, gave another tiny cough and raised her eyebrows slightly higher.

"It is not often," the second customer said to Defarge, "that many of these poor beasts taste wine, or anything other than hard black bread and death. Isn't that right, Jacques?" "That's right, Jacques," Defarge answered.

At this second exchange of the name, Madame Defarge, still picking her teeth calmly, coughed again and raised her eyebrows a bit more.

The last of the three spoke up, putting down his empty glass and smacking his lips. "Ah! So much the worse! It's a bitter taste these poor people always have in their mouths, and hard lives they live, Jacques. Am I right, Jacques?" "You are right, Jacques," Defarge responded.

This third exchange of the name was completed just as Madame Defarge put away her toothpick, kept her eyebrows raised, and shifted slightly in her seat.

"Ah, yes! True!" muttered her husband. "Gentlemen—my wife!"

The three customers tipped their hats to Madame Defarge with polite bows. She acknowledged their greeting with a nod and a quick look. Then she glanced casually around the shop, picked up her knitting with calm focus, and became absorbed in it.

"Gentlemen," said her husband, who had been watching her closely, "good day. The simple room you wanted to see, and were asking about when I went out, is on the fifth floor. The entrance to the stairs is in the small courtyard to the left," he pointed. "Right near the window of my shop. But now that I think of it, one of you has already been up there and can show the way. Goodbye, gentlemen!"

They paid for their wine and left. Defarge was watching his wife knit when the older gentleman stepped forward from his corner and asked for a quick word.

"Of course, sir," Defarge said, walking with him quietly to the door.

Their conversation was very short but intense. At the very first word, Defarge startled and listened closely. It didn't take a minute before he nodded and walked out. The gentleman then signaled to the young lady, and they went out too. Madame Defarge knitted with fast fingers and calm eyebrows, pretending to see nothing.

Leaving the wine shop, Mr. Jarvis Lorry and Miss Manette met Defarge in the doorway he had pointed out to the other men earlier. It opened into a dirty, smelly little courtyard and served as the public entrance to a large tenement building home to many people. In the dark entryway, Defarge knelt before his old master's daughter and kissed her hand. It was a polite action but done with a tense, serious energy; he had completely changed in just a few seconds. The friendly look was gone from his face, replaced by a secret, angry, and dangerous expression.

"It is a long way up, and a bit difficult. We should start slowly," Defarge said sternly to Mr. Lorry as they began walking up the stairs.

"Is he alone?" Lorry whispered.

"Alone! God help him, who else would be with him?" Defarge replied in the same quiet tone.

"Is he always alone, then?"

"Yes."

"By his own choice?"

"Out of necessity. He is exactly as he was when I first saw him, after they found me and asked if I would take him in and keep his secret at my own risk. As he was then, he is now."

"Is he greatly changed?"

"Changed!"

The wine shop owner stopped, punched the wall with his hand, and swore quietly. No verbal answer could have been half as powerful. Mr. Lorry's heart grew heavier as the three of them climbed higher and higher.

Such a staircase in the old, crowded parts of Paris would be bad enough today, but back then, it was absolutely disgusting to anyone not used to it. Every small apartment in the building left its garbage right outside its door on the landing, besides throwing other trash out of the windows. The resulting pile of rotting waste polluted the air, and combined with the smell of extreme poverty, it made the building almost unlivable. They had to climb up through this dark, dirty chimney of filth. Sensing his own growing anxiety and seeing how upset Miss Manette was becoming, Mr. Lorry stopped twice to rest. Each time, they stopped near a dirty barred window where any fresh air seemed to escape and bad smells crawled in. Looking through the rusty bars, they got a sense of the crowded, dirty neighborhood; nothing they could see, except the distant towers of Notre-Dame Cathedral, held any promise of a healthy or happy life.

Finally, they reached the top landing and stopped for a third time. There was still a final, steeper, and narrower flight of stairs to climb to reach the attic. Defarge, who had walked slightly ahead on Lorry's side—as if avoiding any questions from the young lady—turned around, reached into the pockets of the coat he carried, and pulled out a key.

"Is the door locked, then?" Mr. Lorry asked, surprised.

"Yes, it is," Defarge replied grimly.

"Do you think it necessary to keep the poor man locked away like this?"

"I think it necessary to keep him locked in." Defarge whispered this close to Lorry's ear with a heavy frown.

"Why?"

"Why! Because he has lived locked up for so long that he would panic, lose his mind, hurt himself, or even die of fright if the door was left open."

"Is that really possible!" Mr. Lorry exclaimed.

"Is it possible?" Defarge repeated bitterly. "Yes. And it's a beautiful world we live in, where such things are not only possible, but actually done to people under the sun every single day. Long live the Devil. Let's keep moving."

This conversation was held in such low whispers that the young lady didn't hear a word. But by now, she was trembling with emotion, her face showing such deep worry and fear that Mr. Lorry felt he had to offer some comfort.

"Courage, dear miss! Courage! Focus on the task! The worst will be over in a moment; once we pass the door, the hardest part is done. Then, all the help, comfort, and happiness you are bringing to him will begin. Let our friend here support you on that side. Thank you, Defarge. Come along now. Let's do this!"

They went up slowly and quietly. The stairs were short, and they quickly reached the top. Turning a sharp corner, they suddenly saw three men huddled together near the door, peering intently into the room through cracks and holes in the wall. Hearing footsteps, they turned around, showing themselves to be the three "Jacques" who had been drinking in the shop downstairs.

"I forgot about them in the excitement of your visit," Defarge explained. "Leave us, guys; we have business here."

The three men slipped past them and went quietly downstairs.

Since there was no other door on the floor, and the wine shop owner went straight to this one, Mr. Lorry asked him in a sharp whisper:

"Are you turning Monsieur Manette into an exhibit?"

"I show him to a chosen few, in the way you just saw."

"Is that right?"

"I think it is."

"Who are these few? How do you choose them?"

"I choose men who share my goal—under the name Jacques—for whom this sight will do some good. Anyway, you are English, which is different. Please wait here for a moment."

Signaling them to stay back, he leaned down and looked through the crack in the wall. Looking up, he knocked loudly on the door a few times, clearly just to make noise. He then scraped the key across the door three or four times before inserting it loudly into the lock and turning it with as much noise as possible.

The door slowly opened inward, and he looked in and spoke. A weak voice answered back. They only exchanged a syllable or two.

He looked back over his shoulder and gestured for them to come in. Mr. Lorry put his arm securely around the daughter’s waist to support her, feeling that she was about to faint.

"Remember, focus on the task!" he urged, with a tear running down his cheek that had nothing to do with business. "Come in, come in!"

"I am afraid of it," she whispered, shivering.

"Of what?"

"I mean of him. Of my father."

Feeling desperate because of her state and Defarge's gestures, Mr. Lorry draped her shaking arm over his shoulder, lifted her slightly, and hurried her into the room. He sat her down just inside the door and held her close as she clung to him.

Defarge broke out the key, closed the door, locked it from the inside, removed the key again, and kept it in his hand. He did all of this methodically, making as much noise as possible. Finally, he walked slowly across the room to the window, stopped, and turned around to face them.

The attic, built as a storage room for firewood, was dim and dark. The window was actually a door in the roof, with a small crane over it to hoist goods from the street. It had no glass and opened in the middle like a double door. To keep out the cold, one half of this door was shut tight, and the other was opened only a tiny crack. Very little light came through, making it hard to see anything at first; only long habit could give someone the ability to do fine work in such darkness. Yet, that kind of work was being done: with his back to the door and his face toward the window where Defarge stood watching, a white-haired man sat on a low bench, leaning forward, busy making shoes.

CHAPTER VI: The Shoemaker

"Good day!" said Monsieur Defarge, looking down at the white head that was bent low over his shoemaking.

The head was raised for a moment, and a very faint voice responded to the greeting, sounding as if it came from far away:

"Good day!"

"You are still working hard, I see?"

After a long silence, the head was lifted for another moment, and the voice replied, "Yes—I am working." This time, a pair of exhausted, hollow eyes looked at the questioner before the face dropped down again.

The weakness of the voice was pitiful and sad. It was not the weakness of physical illness, though being locked away and eating bad food had certainly played a part. Its most tragic feature was that it was the weakness of long loneliness and lack of use. It sounded like the last faint echo of a sound made a very long time ago. It had so completely lost the life and fullness of a normal human voice that listening to it felt like looking at a once beautiful color that had faded into a dull, weak stain. It was so low and muffled that it sounded like a voice coming from deep underground. It expressed such a sense of a lost and hopeless soul that a starving traveler, exhausted from wandering alone in a desert, would have remembered home and friends upon hearing such a tone before lying down to die.

A few minutes of silent work passed, and the exhausted eyes looked up again. They showed no interest or curiosity, but only a dull, automatic awareness that the spot where his visitor stood was not yet empty.

"I want," said Defarge, who had not stopped looking at the shoemaker, "to let a little more light in here. Can you handle a little more?"

The shoemaker stopped his work. He looked blankly at the floor on one side, as if listening; then he looked the same way at the floor on the other side; and finally, he looked up at the speaker.

"What did you say?"

"Can you handle a little more light?"

"I must handle it, if you let it in." (He put the faintest hint of emphasis on the second word.)

The wooden half-door was opened a little wider and held in place. A wide beam of light shone into the attic room, showing the worker pausing with an unfinished shoe on his lap. His few simple tools and scraps of leather lay at his feet and on his workbench. He had a messy white beard that was not very long, a hollow face, and extremely bright eyes. The thinness of his face made his eyes look huge under his dark eyebrows and messy white hair, even though they were already naturally large, making them look unnaturally big now. His yellow, ragged shirt was open at the neck, showing his thin, worn-out body. He, his old canvas jacket, his loose stockings, and all his ragged clothes had faded from being locked away from light and fresh air for so long. Everything had turned the same dull, paper-yellow color, so that it was hard to tell his clothes and his skin apart.

He held up a hand to shield his eyes from the light, and the bones inside it looked almost transparent. He sat there, staring blankly, pausing in his work. He never looked at the person standing in front of him without looking down to one side first, and then to the other, as if he had forgotten how to connect sounds with the direction they came from. He never spoke without first looking around in this confused way, sometimes forgetting to speak at all.

"Are you going to finish that pair of shoes today?" asked Defarge, signaling for Mr. Lorry to step forward.

"What did you say?"

"Do you plan to finish that pair of shoes today?"

"I can't say that I plan to. I suppose so. I don't know."

But the question reminded him of his work, and he bent over it again.

Mr. Lorry walked forward quietly, leaving Lucie by the door. After he stood next to Defarge for a minute or two, the shoemaker looked up. He did not seem surprised to see another person, but his shaking fingers touched his lips as he looked at him. (His lips and fingernails were the same pale, lead-gray color.) Then his hand dropped back to his work, and he bent over the shoe again. The look and the movement took only a second.

"You have a visitor, you see," said Monsieur Defarge.

"What did you say?"

"Here is a visitor."

The shoemaker looked up as he had before, but without taking his hands off his work.

"Come!" said Defarge. "Here is a gentleman who knows a good shoe when he sees one. Show him the shoe you are working on. Take it, sir."

Mr. Lorry took it in his hand.

"Tell the gentleman what kind of shoe it is, and the maker's name."

There was a longer pause than usual before the shoemaker answered:

"I forget what you asked me. What did you say?"

"I asked if you could describe the kind of shoe for this gentleman."

"It is a lady's shoe. It is a young lady's walking shoe. It is in the latest fashion. I have never seen the fashion, but I have held the pattern in my hand." He glanced at the shoe with a small, brief hint of pride.

"And the maker's name?" said Defarge.

Now that he had no work to hold, he put the knuckles of his right hand in the palm of his left, and then the knuckles of his left hand in the palm of his right, and then rubbed his hand over his bearded chin. He kept repeating this movement without stopping for a second. Trying to bring his mind back from the blank state it slid into after speaking was like trying to wake up a very weak person from a deep faint, or trying to keep a dying man alive in the hope that he might say one last important word.

"Did you ask me for my name?"

"Yes, I did."

"One Hundred and Five, North Tower."

"Is that all?"

"One Hundred and Five, North Tower."

With a tired sound that was not quite a sigh or a groan, he bent back to his work, until the silence was broken again.

"You are not a shoemaker by trade, are you?" asked Mr. Lorry, looking at him closely.

His tired eyes turned to Defarge, as if he wanted Defarge to answer for him. But when no help came from that side, his eyes looked down at the floor, and then back up at Mr. Lorry.

"I am not a shoemaker by trade? No, I was not a shoemaker by trade. I—I learned it here. I taught myself. I asked permission to—"

He drifted off for several minutes, repeating the same pattern with his hands the entire time. His eyes slowly came back to Mr. Lorry's face. When they focused on him, he started, and continued speaking like a sleeper who had just woken up and gone back to a topic from the night before.

"I asked permission to teach myself, and I got it with great difficulty after a long time. I have made shoes ever since."

As he reached out his hand for the shoe, Mr. Lorry said, still looking directly at him:

"Monsieur Manette, do you remember anything about me?"

The shoe fell to the ground, and he sat staring intensely at the questioner.

"Monsieur Manette," Mr. Lorry said, putting his hand on Defarge's arm, "do you remember nothing of this man? Look at him. Look at me. Is there no memory of your old banker, your old business, your old servant, or your past life coming back to your mind, Monsieur Manette?"

As the long-time prisoner sat looking intensely, first at Mr. Lorry and then at Defarge, some long-lost signs of intelligence and deep thought began to show on his forehead, breaking through the dark cloud that hung over his mind. The signs faded again, grew weaker, and then disappeared—but they had definitely been there. And that exact same expression was matched on the young, beautiful face of his daughter, who had crept along the wall to where she could see him. She stood there looking at him. At first, her hands had been raised in frightened pity, as if to shield herself from the sight of him, but now she was reaching out to him, trembling with eagerness to hold his ghost-like face to her warm chest and love him back to life and hope. The expression was repeated so perfectly on her face (though much stronger and clearer) that it looked as if a moving light had passed directly from him to her.

But the darkness returned and took over his mind again. He looked at the two men with less and less interest, and his eyes wandered down to the floor in a sad, blank stare, looking around in his usual confused way. Finally, with a long, deep sigh, he picked up the shoe and went back to work.

"Did you recognize him, sir?" Defarge asked in a whisper.

"Yes, for a moment. At first I thought it was completely hopeless, but I definitely saw the face I used to know so well for a single second. Shh! Let's step back a little. Shh!"

She had moved away from the attic wall and was now standing very close to the bench where he sat. It was deeply sad and unsettling that he was completely unaware of her standing right there, close enough to reach out and touch him as he bent over his work.

Not a word was spoken; not a sound was made. She stood beside him like a quiet spirit, while he bent over his work.

Eventually, he needed to switch the tool in his hand for his shoemaker's knife. The knife lay on the side away from where she was standing. He picked it up and was leaning down to work again when his eyes caught the edge of her dress. He looked up and saw her face. The two men rushed forward, but she stopped them with a wave of her hand. She was not afraid that he would strike her with the knife, even though the men were.

He stared at her with a frightened look, and after a moment, his lips began to form words, though no sound came out. Gradually, during the pauses in his fast, heavy breathing, they heard him say:

"What is this?"

With tears streaming down her face, she blew him a kiss with both hands, then pressed her hands to her chest, as if holding his broken head against her heart.

"You are not the jailer's daughter?"

She sighed, "No."

"Who are you?"

Not yet trusting herself to speak clearly, she sat down on the bench next to him. He pulled back, but she gently laid her hand on his arm. A strange shiver ran through his entire body when she touched him. He set the knife down gently, continuing to stare at her.

Her golden hair, which she wore in long curls, had been pushed back in her excitement and fell over her neck. Moving his hand very slowly, he reached out, picked up a lock of her hair, and looked at it. But while doing this, his mind drifted away again, and with another deep sigh, he went back to making shoes.

But not for long. Freeing her arm, she laid her hand on his shoulder. He looked at her hand doubtfully two or three times, as if to make sure it was really there. Then he laid down his work, reached for his neck, and pulled off a blackened string with a small piece of folded rag tied to it. He carefully opened the rag on his knee. Inside was a tiny bit of hair—only one or two long golden strands that he had wound around his finger a long time ago.

He took her hair in his hand again and looked closely at it. "It is the same. How can it be! When was this? How did this happen?"

As the deep, focused look returned to his forehead, he seemed to notice that she had the exact same expression. He turned her face directly toward the light and looked at her.

"She laid her head on my shoulder the night I was called away. She was afraid of my leaving, though I was not. And when I was brought to the North Tower, they found these strands of hair on my sleeve. 'Will you let me keep them? They can never help my body escape, but they might help my soul.' Those were the words I said. I remember them very well."

He shaped these words with his lips many times before he could say them out loud. But when he finally spoke, his words made sense, even though they came slowly.

"How could this be? — _Was it you_?"

Once again, the two men jumped in surprise as he turned to her with terrifying suddenness. But she sat completely still in his grip and only said in a quiet voice, "I beg you, gentlemen, do not come near us, do not speak, and do not move!"

"Listen!" he cried. "Whose voice was that?"
