His hands let go of her as he shouted, reaching up to his white hair and pulling at it wildly. But the outburst soon faded, just as everything except his shoemaking always faded from his mind. He folded his little rag packet back up and tried to hide it in his shirt, but he kept looking at her, shaking his head sadly.

"No, no, no. You are too young, too full of life. It cannot be. Look at what a prisoner I am. These are not the hands she knew, this is not the face she knew, and this is not a voice she ever heard. No, no. She and I existed before the long, slow years in the North Tower—ages ago. What is your name, my gentle angel?"

Grateful for his softer tone and gentle manner, his daughter knelt in front of him, placing her pleading hands on his chest.

"Oh, sir, at another time you will know my name, and who my mother and father were, and why I never knew their sad history. But I cannot tell you now, and I cannot tell you here. All I can tell you right here and now is that I beg you to touch me and bless me. Kiss me, kiss me! Oh, my dear, my dear father!"

His cold white head rested against her bright, golden hair, which seemed to warm and light it up as if the light of freedom itself were shining on him.

"If you hear in my voice—I do not know if it is true, but I hope it is—any sound that reminds you of a voice that was once sweet music to your ears, cry for it, cry for it! If, in touching my hair, you feel anything that reminds you of a beloved head that lay on your chest when you were young and free, cry for it, cry for it! If, when I talk about the home waiting for us, where I will care for you with all my duty and faithful love, I bring back the memory of a home that was empty for so long while your poor heart suffered, cry for it, cry for it!"

She held him tighter around the neck, rocking him against her chest like a child.

"If, when I tell you, my dearest, that your suffering is over, and that I have come to take you away from this place to England where we can live in peace and rest, I make you think of your wasted life and how cruel our native France has been to you, cry for it, cry for it! And if, when I tell you my name and about my father who is alive and my mother who is dead, you learn that I must kneel to my honored father and beg his forgiveness for never working all day, staying awake, and crying all night for his sake—because my mother's love kept his suffering a secret from me—cry for it, cry for it! Cry for her, then, and for me! Gentlemen, thank God! I feel his tears on my face, and his sobs touch my heart. Oh, look! Thank God for us, thank God!"

He sank into her arms, and his face fell against her chest. It was a sight so touching, yet so terrible because of the great pain and injustice that had caused it, that the two men watching covered their faces.

When the attic room had been quiet for a long time, and his heavy breathing and trembling body had finally calmed down—showing how all human storms must eventually end in quiet peace—the two men came forward to help the father and daughter up. He had slowly slipped to the floor and lay there in a deep, exhausted sleep. She had huddled down with him so that his head rested on her arm, and her long hair fell over him like a curtain to block out the light.

"If we can do it without waking him," she whispered, raising her hand to Mr. Lorry as he stood over them, blowing his nose repeatedly, "could we arrange to leave Paris immediately? That way, we can take him directly from this door to the carriage."

"But think about it. Is he strong enough to travel?" asked Mr. Lorry.

"He is much readier for that, I think, than to stay in this city that has been so terrible to him."

"That is true," said Defarge, who had knelt down to watch and listen. "In fact, Monsieur Manette is much better off leaving France entirely. Should I go hire a carriage and fresh horses?"

"That is a matter of business," said Mr. Lorry, quickly returning to his organized, professional way of speaking. "And if business needs to be done, I had better take care of it."

"Then please," Miss Manette urged, "leave us here for now. You see how calm he is, and you don't need to worry about leaving him with me. Why would you be? If you lock the door to make sure no one interrupts us, I am sure you will find him just as quiet when you return. I will watch over him until you get back, and then we can move him immediately."

Both Mr. Lorry and Defarge were reluctant to do this and preferred to have one of them stay behind. However, since they had to arrange for the carriage, horses, and travel papers, and because time was running out as the day ended, they quickly split up the tasks and hurried off to complete them.

Then, as darkness filled the room, the daughter laid her head on the hard floor right next to her father and watched him. The darkness grew deeper and deeper, and they both lay quietly until a light shone through the cracks in the wall.

Mr. Lorry and Monsieur Defarge had prepared everything for the trip. They brought traveling coats, blankets, bread, meat, wine, and hot coffee. Monsieur Defarge placed the food and the lamp he was carrying on the shoemaker's workbench (the only other thing in the room was a simple straw bed). Then he and Mr. Lorry woke the prisoner and helped him stand up.

No one could have understood what was going on in his mind by looking at the scared, blank look on his face. Whether he understood what had happened, remembered what they had said, or even knew he was free were questions that no one could answer. They tried talking to him, but he was so confused and slow to respond that they became worried by his state and decided to leave him alone for the time being. He had a wild, lost way of holding his head in his hands sometimes, which they had not seen before. Yet, he seemed to find some comfort in the sound of his daughter's voice, and always turned toward her whenever she spoke.

With the obedient attitude of someone long forced to follow orders, he ate and drank whatever they offered, and put on the coat and blankets they gave him. He willingly let his daughter put her arm through his, and took—and held—her hand in both of his.

They began to walk down the stairs, with Monsieur Defarge leading the way with the lamp and Mr. Lorry walking at the end. They had only gone down a few steps of the long main staircase when the doctor stopped, staring up at the ceiling and around at the walls.

"Do you remember this place, father? Do you remember coming up here?"

"What did you say?"

But before she could repeat the question, he muttered an answer as if she had already done so.

"Remember? No, I don't remember. It was so very long ago."

It was clear that he had no memory of being brought from the prison to this house. They heard him mutter, "One Hundred and Five, North Tower," and when he looked around, he was clearly looking for the thick fortress walls that had surrounded him for so long. When they reached the courtyard, he automatically changed his step, expecting to cross a drawbridge. When there was no drawbridge and he saw the carriage waiting in the open street, he let go of his daughter's hand and held his head in his hands again.

There was no crowd outside the door, no one looking out from the many windows, and not even a single person walking down the street. An unnatural silence and emptiness filled the air. Only one person was visible, and that was Madame Defarge, who leaned against the doorframe, knitting, and appearing to see nothing.

The prisoner climbed into the carriage and his daughter followed him. But just as Mr. Lorry was stepping in, he stopped on the step because the doctor began pleading sadly for his shoemaking tools and the unfinished shoes. Madame Defarge immediately called out to her husband that she would get them, and walked out of the lamplight, knitting, across the courtyard. She returned quickly, handed them into the carriage, and immediately went back to leaning against the doorframe, knitting, and appearing to see nothing.

Defarge climbed onto the driver's seat and shouted, "To the city gate!" The driver cracked his whip, and the carriage clattered away under the dim, hanging streetlamps.

They rode under the hanging streetlamps—which swung brighter in the wealthier streets and dimmer in the poorer ones—passing by lit-up shops, happy crowds, bright cafes, and theater entrances on their way to one of the city gates. Soldiers with lanterns stood at the guardhouse there. "Your papers, travelers!" one called out. "Look here, officer," said Defarge, getting down and pulling him aside. "These are the papers of the gentleman inside with the white hair. He was placed in my care at the—" He lowered his voice, and there was a stir among the soldiers' lanterns. A uniformed arm reached into the carriage with a light, and the eyes behind the arm stared with a look of deep surprise and curiosity at the white-haired man. "Everything is in order. Move along!" said the soldier. "Goodbye!" replied Defarge. And so, they drove under a few last, dim hanging lamps, and out under the vast sky of stars.

Beneath that canopy of quiet, eternal stars—some so far away from our small Earth that scientists say it is doubtful their light has even reached us to show a place where people suffer and live—the shadows of the night were wide and dark. All through the cold, restless hours of the night until dawn, the same question whispered in the mind of Mr. Jarvis Lorry. He sat opposite the man who had been dug out of his living grave, wondering what parts of his mind were lost forever and what could still be healed:

"I hope you care to be recalled to life?"

And the old answer:

"I can't say."

The end of the first book.

Book the Second: the Golden Thread

CHAPTER I: Five Years Later

Tellson’s Bank near Temple Bar was an old-fashioned place, even in the year 1780. It was very small, very dark, very ugly, and very uncomfortable. Moreover, it was old-fashioned in the sense that the bank owners were proud of its smallness, darkness, ugliness, and lack of comfort. They even bragged about how extreme these qualities were, believing that if the bank were any cleaner or more convenient, it would somehow be less respectable. This was not just a quiet belief, but an active argument they used against more modern and convenient businesses. They would say, "Tellson's doesn’t need extra space, Tellson's doesn’t need bright light, Tellson's doesn’t need fancy decorations. Other banks might need those things, but Tellson’s, thank Heaven, does not!"

Any of these owners would have disowned his own son over the question of rebuilding or modernizing Tellson’s Bank. In this regard, the bank was very much like the country itself, which often punished its citizens for suggesting improvements to laws and customs that had long been highly problematic but were valued simply because they were old.

Thus, Tellson’s had become the ultimate example of inconvenience. After pushing open a stubborn door that shook with a weak rattle, you fell down two steps into the bank. There you found yourself in a miserable little shop with two small counters. The oldest clerks working there would take your check with hands that shook so much the paper rustled like leaves, while they examined your signature by the dirtiest windows. These windows were constantly sprayed with mud from Fleet Street and made even darker by heavy iron bars and the shadow of the nearby Temple Bar gate. If your business required meeting with "the owners," you were escorted to a dark, prison-like room at the back where you could meditate on your sins until they finally appeared with their hands in their pockets. Your money came out of or went into rotting old wooden drawers, sending clouds of dust up your nose and down your throat whenever they were opened. The banknotes had a musty smell, as if they were turning back into moldy rags. Silverware and valuables were stored near the sewer pipes, and the damp air quickly ruined their polish. Important legal documents were kept in improvised vaults made from old kitchens and pantries, absorbing the greasy air. Lighter boxes of family papers went upstairs into a useless dining room that had a massive table but never served any meals. Even in 1780, the old love letters or children's notes kept there had only recently been spared from being stared at through the windows by the heads of executed criminals displayed on Temple Bar with a cruelty worthy of the most uncivilized lands.

Indeed, at that time, execution was the standard solution for almost every crime, and Tellson’s Bank fully supported it. Death was nature’s remedy for all problems, so why not the law's? Consequently, anyone who forged a signature was put to death; anyone who passed a bad note was put to death; anyone who opened a letter illegally was put to death; anyone who stole forty shillings and sixpence was put to death; anyone who took a horse from outside Tellson's and rode off with it was put to death; anyone who made a counterfeit coin was put to death. It did not prevent crime at all—in fact, the opposite was true—but it certainly cleared the case off the books and left nothing else to worry about. Thus, Tellson’s Bank, like other major businesses of the era, had caused so many executions that if all the heads of those it had prosecuted were lined up on Temple Bar, they would have blocked what little daylight reached the ground floor.

Cramped in dim cupboards and tiny offices, the oldest clerks carried on the bank's business. When they hired a young man, they hid him away in a dark place like a cheese until he grew old and developed the full, moldy Tellson flavor. Only then was he allowed to be seen by customers, looking solemn as he bent over large ledger books in his old-fashioned breeches and leggings.

Outside Tellson’s Bank—and never allowed inside unless specifically summoned—was an odd-job man who worked as a messenger and porter, serving as the bank's living sign. He was always there during business hours unless he was out running an errand. When he was away, his twelve-year-old son, who looked exactly like him, took his place. The bank tolerated this messenger in a dignified way. The business had always had someone in that role, and time had carried this particular man to the job. His last name was Cruncher, and when he was baptized as a child in Hounsditch, he had been given the first name Jerry.

The scene opens at Mr. Cruncher’s private lodging in Hanging-sword Alley, Whitefriars, at half-past seven on a windy March morning in the year 1780. Mr. Cruncher always referred to the year of our Lord as "Anna Dominoes," apparently believing the era was named after a popular game invented by a lady of that name.

Mr. Cruncher’s rooms were not in a pleasant neighborhood. There were only two rooms, even if you counted a tiny closet with a single pane of glass. However, they were kept very clean. Early as it was on this windy morning, the bedroom had already been scrubbed. A clean white cloth was spread on the heavy wooden table, set with cups and saucers for breakfast.

Mr. Cruncher lay asleep under a patchwork blanket, looking like a colorful clown at rest. At first he slept heavily, but gradually he began to roll and toss in bed until his head emerged, his spiky hair looking sharp enough to tear the sheets to pieces. At that moment, he cried out in great frustration:

"Damn me, if she isn't at it again!"

A neat and hardworking woman rose quickly and nervously from her knees in a corner of the room, showing she was the one he was talking to.

"What!" said Mr. Cruncher, looking out from under the covers for a boot. "You're at it again, are you?"

After greeting the morning with this second shout, he threw a boot at the woman as a third greeting. It was a very muddy boot. This introduces a strange detail about Mr. Cruncher’s household: although he often came home after bank hours with clean boots, he frequently woke up the next morning to find them covered in clay.

"What," said Mr. Cruncher, changing his tone after missing his target, "what are you up to, Aggerawayter?"

"I was only saying my prayers," his wife replied gently.

"Saying your prayers! You're a fine woman! What do you mean by dropping to your knees and praying against me?"

"I was not praying against you, Jerry. I was praying for you."

"You weren't! And even if you were, I won't have you taking such liberties. Look here, young Jerry! Your mother is a fine woman, praying against your father's business success. You have a very dutiful mother, my son. You have a very religious mother, my boy—going and dropping to her knees to pray that the bread and butter be snatched right out of her own child's mouth!"

Young Jerry (who was standing in his shirt) took this very seriously. Turning to his mother, he strongly protested against any prayers that might take away his food.

"And what do you think, you conceited woman," Mr. Cruncher said, with unconscious contradiction, "that your prayers are actually worth? Name the price you put on your prayers!"

"They only come from the heart, Jerry. They are worth no more than that."

"Worth no more than that," Mr. Cruncher repeated. "Then they aren't worth much. Either way, I won't be prayed against, I tell you. I can't afford it. I'm not going to have your sneaking prayers bring me bad luck. If you must drop to your knees, pray for your husband and child, not against them. If I had a normal wife, and this poor boy had a normal mother, I might have made some money last week instead of being prayed against, undermined, and religiously tricked into the worst luck imaginable. Damn me!" said Mr. Cruncher, who was putting on his clothes. "Between your piety and one cursed thing after another, I've been cheated out of my luck this week as badly as any honest tradesman ever was! Young Jerry, get dressed, my boy. While I clean my boots, keep an eye on your mother. If you see her starting to pray again, call me. I tell you," he said, addressing his wife again, "I won't have this. I feel as shaky as an old carriage, as sleepy as if I'd taken opium, and my muscles are so sore that I wouldn't know who I was if it weren't for the pain. Yet I haven't made a single penny from it, and I suspect you've been praying day and night to keep me poor. I won't put up with it, Aggerawayter! What do you have to say to that?"

Growling other remarks like, "Oh yes! You're very religious, aren't you? You wouldn't do anything to hurt your husband and child's interests, would you? Not you!" and muttering in his anger, Mr. Cruncher went to clean his boots and get ready for the day. Meanwhile, his son, whose hair had smaller spikes and whose eyes were set close together like his father's, watched his mother as ordered. He repeatedly startled the poor woman by jumping out of the closet where he was getting dressed, whispering, "You're about to pray, mother! Hey, father!" before disappearing back inside with a mischievous grin.

Mr. Cruncher’s temper did not improve by breakfast time. He was particularly angry when Mrs. Cruncher tried to say grace before the meal.

"Now, Aggerawayter! What are you doing? At it again?"

His wife explained that she was only asking for a blessing on the food.

"Don't do it!" said Mr. Cruncher, looking around as if he expected the loaf of bread to vanish because of her prayers. "I'm not going to be blessed out of my house and home. I won't have my food blessed off my table. Keep quiet!"

With red eyes and a grim face, looking as if he had been up all night at a very unpleasant gathering, Jerry Cruncher grumbled through his breakfast like a wild animal in a zoo. Around nine o'clock, he smoothed down his hair, put on the most respectable and business-like look he could manage, and headed out for the day's work.

It could hardly be called a business, despite his favorite description of himself as an "honest tradesman." His equipment consisted of a wooden stool made from an old chair. Young Jerry carried this stool at his father’s side every morning to a spot beneath the bank window closest to Temple Bar. There, with a handful of straw gathered from passing carriages to keep the cold and wet off his feet, Jerry set up his spot for the day. At this post, Mr. Cruncher was as well known to the people of Fleet Street and the Temple as the Temple Bar gate itself, and he looked almost as dark and gloomy.

Set up by a quarter to nine, in time to tip his hat to the senior clerks as they entered Tellson’s, Jerry took his post on this windy March morning. Young Jerry stood by him when he wasn't running off to tease and annoy smaller boys on the street. Father and son, looking remarkably alike, stood silently watching the morning traffic on Fleet Street. With their heads close together, they looked a bit like a pair of monkeys. The comparison was made even stronger by the fact that the older Jerry chewed and spat out straw, while the younger Jerry watched him and the street with restless, twinkling eyes.

Just then, the head of one of the bank's indoor messengers appeared at the door, and he called out:

"Messenger needed!"

"Hooray, father! Here's an early job to start the day!"

Wishing his father luck, young Jerry sat down on the stool, took over the straw his father had been chewing, and began to think.

"Always rusty! His fingers are always rusty!" muttered young Jerry. "Where does my father get all that iron rust from? He certainly doesn't get it around here!"

CHAPTER II: A Sight

"You know the Old Bailey courthouse well, no doubt?" said one of the oldest clerks to Jerry the messenger.

"Yes, sir," returned Jerry in a stubborn voice. "I _do_ know the Bailey."

"Just so. And you know Mr. Lorry."

"I know Mr. Lorry, sir, much better than I know the Bailey. Much better," said Jerry, sounding like a reluctant witness in court, "than I, as an honest tradesman, wish to know the Bailey."

"Very well. Find the door where the witnesses go in, and show the doorkeeper this note for Mr. Lorry. He will then let you in."

"Into the courtroom, sir?"

"Into the courtroom."

Mr. Cruncher’s eyes seemed to get a little closer together, as if asking each other, "What do you think of this?"

"Am I to wait in the court, sir?" he asked, after considering the idea.

"I am going to tell you. The doorkeeper will pass the note to Mr. Lorry. You must make some gesture to attract Mr. Lorry’s attention and show him where you are standing. Then, your job is simply to stay there until he needs you."

"Is that all, sir?"

"That’s all. He wants to have a messenger nearby. This note is just to let him know you have arrived."

As the old clerk slowly folded and addressed the note, Mr. Cruncher watched him in silence until he began using the blotting paper to dry the ink. Then Jerry remarked:

"I suppose they’ll be trying forgery cases this morning?"

"Treason!"

"That means drawing and quartering," said Jerry. "Barbarous!"

"It is the law," remarked the old clerk, turning his spectacles toward him in surprise. "It is the law."

"I think it’s harsh for the law to ruin a man's body like that. It’s hard enough to kill him, but it’s very hard to slice him up, sir."

"Not at all," replied the old clerk. "Speak well of the law. Take care of your own health, my friend, and leave the law to take care of itself. That is my advice to you."

"It’s the damp weather, sir, that settles in my chest and voice," said Jerry. "You can imagine what a damp way of earning a living mine is."

"Well, well," said the old clerk. "We all have our different ways of making a living. Some of us have damp ways, and some have dry ways. Here is the letter. Off you go."

Jerry took the letter. Muttering to himself with far less respect than he showed on the outside, "You are a withered old man yourself," he bowed, told his son where he was going as he walked past him, and went on his way.

In those days, executions were carried out at Tyburn, so the street outside Newgate Prison had not yet gained the infamous reputation it would have later. However, the prison itself was a filthy, corrupt place where all kinds of vice and crime were practiced and where terrible diseases bred. These illnesses often came into the courtroom with the prisoners, sometimes spreading directly to the Lord Chief Justice himself and killing him. More than once, a judge wearing the black execution cap had pronounced his own death sentence along with the prisoner's, dying even before the condemned man did. Otherwise, the Old Bailey was famous as a deadly departure point from which pale travelers set out in carts and carriages for a violent journey into the next world, traveling two and a half miles through public streets, to the shame of any good citizens who watched. Such is the power of custom, showing how important it is to establish good customs in the first place. The court was also famous for the pillory, a punishment where no one could predict how much harm the crowd might do; for the whipping post, another traditional institution; and for the practice of paying reward money for catching criminals, which systematically led to terrible crimes committed purely for greed. Overall, the Old Bailey at that time was a prime example of the lazy saying, "Whatever is, is right"—an idea that would be simple and final, if it didn't also mean that nothing in the past had ever been wrong.

Making his way through the dirty crowd gathered around this unpleasant scene, with the skill of a man used to moving quietly, the messenger found the door he was looking for and passed his note through a small slot. In those days, people paid to watch trials at the Old Bailey just as they paid to watch the patients at Bedlam asylum—except the trials cost much more. Therefore, all the doors to the Old Bailey were heavily guarded, except for the doors the criminals entered through, which were always left wide open.

After some delay and discussion, the door opened slightly, allowing Mr. Jerry Cruncher to squeeze himself into the courtroom.

"What's happening?" he whispered to the man standing next to him.

"Nothing yet."

"What case is coming up?"

"The treason case."

"The drawing and quartering one, right?"

"Yes!" the man replied with excitement. "He’ll be dragged on a wooden frame to be half-hanged, then cut down and sliced up while he's still alive. His insides will be pulled out and burned before his eyes, then his head will be chopped off and his body cut into four pieces. That’s the sentence."

"If he’s found guilty, you mean?" Jerry added, to clarify.

"Oh, they’ll find him guilty," said the other. "Don't you worry about that."

Mr. Cruncher’s attention was then drawn to the doorkeeper, whom he saw walking toward Mr. Lorry with the note. Mr. Lorry sat at a table among the lawyers in wigs, not far from the prisoner’s defense lawyer, who had a large stack of papers in front of him. Nearly opposite sat another lawyer in a wig, with his hands in his pockets, who seemed entirely focused on staring at the courtroom ceiling. After some coughing, rubbing his chin, and waving, Jerry finally got the attention of Mr. Lorry, who had stood up to look for him. Mr. Lorry nodded quietly and sat back down.

"What does _he_ have to do with the case?" asked the man Jerry had been talking to.

"I have no idea," said Jerry.

"What do _you_ have to do with it, then, if I may ask?"

"I don't know that either," said Jerry.

The entrance of the Judge and the general movement of the crowd settling into their places stopped their conversation. Soon, the prisoner's dock became the center of attention. Two guards who had been standing there stepped out, and the prisoner was brought in and placed at the bar.

Everyone in the courtroom, except the lawyer looking at the ceiling, stared at him. The breath of the crowd seemed to rush toward him like a wave, a wind, or a fire. Eager faces leaned around pillars and corners to see him; spectators in the back rows stood up so they wouldn't miss a single detail; people on the floor put their hands on the shoulders of those in front of them to help themselves get a better view, standing on tiptoe and climbing onto ledges. Standing out among them, looking like a piece of the spiked wall of Newgate Prison, Jerry stood, breathing out the smell of the beer he had drunk on the way, adding to the thick, damp air of the crowded room that misted up the large windows behind the prisoner.

The target of all this attention was a young man of about twenty-five, tall, well-built, with sunburnt skin and dark eyes. He looked like a young gentleman. He was dressed plainly in black or dark gray, and his long, dark hair was tied back with a ribbon, more to keep it out of the way than for style. Just as strong emotions show through any clothing, the paleness caused by his dangerous situation showed through his sunburned cheeks, proving that his inner feelings were stronger than the sun. Otherwise, he was quite calm, bowed to the Judge, and stood quietly.

The interest with which the crowd stared and breathed on this man was not a noble human feeling. If he had been facing a less terrible sentence, or if there had been any chance he would be spared its brutal details, he would have been far less fascinating to them. The body that was doomed to be so horribly mutilated was the main attraction; the human being about to be butchered was the source of the excitement. Whatever excuses the spectators made for their interest, at its root, it was a savage, monstrous curiosity.

"Silence in court!" was called. Charles Darnay had pleaded not guilty the day before to a long, repetitive indictment charging him with being a traitor to our king. He was accused of having assisted Louis, the King of France, in his wars against England on several occasions. The charges claimed he did this by traveling back and forth between England and France, revealing to the French king what military forces England was preparing to send to Canada and North America. Jerry, whose hair seemed to stand up even more as the complicated legal terms were read, listened with satisfaction, figuring out that this Charles Darnay was indeed the man on trial, that the jury was being sworn in, and that the Attorney General was preparing to speak.

The accused man, who knew that everyone in the room was already imagining him hanged, beheaded, and quartered, did not panic or act dramatically. He was quiet and attentive, watching the start of the trial with serious interest. He stood with his hands resting on the wooden bar in front of him so calmly that he didn't even stir the herbs scattered on it. The courtroom was covered in herbs and sprinkled with vinegar to protect against the bad air and prison fever.

Over the prisoner’s head hung a mirror to reflect light down on him. Over the years, crowds of desperate and criminal people had been reflected in it before leaving both the courtroom and the earth. That terrible place would have been haunted indeed if the glass could show all the faces it had once reflected, just as the ocean will one day give up its dead. Perhaps some thought of the disgrace associated with that mirror crossed the prisoner’s mind. In any case, as he shifted his position and felt a beam of light hit his face, he looked up. When he saw the mirror, his face flushed, and his right hand pushed the herbs aside.

This movement caused him to turn his face to the left side of the court. On a level with his eyes, sitting in the corner of the Judge’s bench, were two people who caught his attention immediately. His expression changed so suddenly when he saw them that all eyes in the room followed his gaze.

The spectators saw a young lady of just over twenty and a gentleman who was clearly her father. He was a man of very striking appearance, with pure white hair and an intense, thoughtful face. When he was lost in thought, he looked quite old; but when he was engaged—as he was now, speaking to his daughter—he became a handsome man who seemed to still be in his prime.

His daughter sat beside him with one hand resting on his arm and the other pressing against it. She had drawn close to him in her fear of the courtroom and her pity for the prisoner. Her face showed deep terror and compassion, focused entirely on the danger facing the accused man. This was so clear and natural that even the spectators who felt no pity for him were touched by her expression, and a whisper went around: "Who are they?"

Jerry the messenger, who had been watching in his own way and sucking the rust off his fingers, stretched his neck to hear the answer. The question was passed through the crowd to the nearest court official and then whispered back until it reached Jerry:

"Witnesses."

"For which side?"

"Against."

"Against who?"

"Against the prisoner."

The Judge, whose eyes had followed the crowd's gaze, looked back, leaned back in his seat, and stared steadily at the man whose life was in his hands, as the Attorney General rose to begin building the case for his execution.

CHAPTER III: A Disappointment

The Attorney General informed the jury that the prisoner before them, though young in years, was old in the treasonous acts that now threatened his life. He claimed that this communication with the country's enemy was not new, but had been going on for years. He asserted that the prisoner had repeatedly traveled back and forth between France and England on secret business for which he could give no honest explanation. If treason were capable of succeeding—which, fortunately, it never was—the prisoner’s crimes might have remained hidden. However, Providence had inspired a noble and blameless citizen to uncover the prisoner’s schemes and report them to the government. This patriot, the prosecution promised, would testify. The Attorney General described this witness's actions as heroic: he had been the prisoner’s friend, but upon discovering his treason, had decided to sacrifice his personal feelings on the altar of patriotism. If statues were built in Britain for public benefactors as they were in ancient Greece and Rome, this shining citizen would surely get one. Since Britain did not build such statues, he would not have one, but his virtue—patriotism—was contagious. This noble example had even inspired the prisoner’s servant to search his master’s drawers and pockets to find these incriminating documents. The Attorney General anticipated that the defense would try to discredit this excellent servant, but remarked that he personally respected him more than his own family and urged the jury to do the same. The evidence of these two witnesses, combined with the discovered documents, would prove that the prisoner had lists of British military and naval forces to send to the enemy. Even if the lists were not in the prisoner’s handwriting, the prosecution argued that this only proved how clever the prisoner was in hiding his tracks. The proof would show the treason went back five years, starting just before the first battles of the American Revolutionary War. Therefore, the Attorney General argued, the loyal and responsible jury must find the prisoner guilty. He concluded by demanding the prisoner's execution, saying the jury would never sleep peacefully again if they let him live.

When the Attorney General finished, a loud buzz arose in the courtroom, sounding like a swarm of large bluebottles circling the prisoner in anticipation of his death. When the noise died down, the patriotic witness entered the box.

The Solicitor General then questioned the witness, John Barsad. His testimony perfectly matched the Attorney General's description—perhaps a little too perfectly. After sharing his story, he prepared to leave, but the defense lawyer, sitting near Mr. Lorry, stopped him for cross-examination while the other lawyer opposite continued to stare at the ceiling.

Had he ever been a spy himself? No, he rejected the suggestion with disgust. What did he live on? His own property. Where was it? He couldn't remember exactly. What was it? None of anyone's business. Had he inherited it? Yes. From whom? A distant relation. How distant? Quite distant. Had he ever been in prison? No. Never in a debtors' prison? He didn't see what that had to do with anything. Never? Well, yes, once. How many times? Two or three times. Not five or six? Perhaps. What was his profession? A gentleman. Had he ever been kicked? He might have been. Frequently? No. Kicked downstairs? No, he had once been kicked at the top of a staircase and fell down by himself. Was he kicked for cheating at dice? The drunk liar who kicked him claimed that, but it wasn't true. Was he sure? Absolutely. Had he ever lived by gambling? No more than other gentlemen. Had he borrowed money from the prisoner? Yes. Had he paid it back? No. Was his friendship with the prisoner actually a minor acquaintance forced upon the prisoner during travels? No. Was he sure he saw the prisoner with the lists? Yes. Did he know anything else about them? No. Had he put them there himself? No. Did he expect to get paid for his testimony? No. Was he a government spy? Absolutely not. Did he have any motive other than pure patriotism? None at all.

Next came the servant, Roger Cly. He testified that he had started working for the prisoner four years ago, asking him for work on a ship from Calais. He began to suspect the prisoner soon after. While packing his clothes during travels, he had seen lists like the ones in court in the prisoner’s pockets. He had taken them from the prisoner’s desk drawer, denying that he had placed them there himself. He claimed to have seen the prisoner showing these lists to French gentlemen in Calais and Boulogne. He said he loved his country and had to report it. Under cross-examination, he denied ever being suspected of stealing a silver teapot, claiming he was falsely accused over a plated mustard pot. He admitted knowing the previous witness, John Barsad, for seven or eight years, but claimed it was just a coincidence. He also claimed his only motive was true patriotism, hoping there were many other British citizens like him.

The crowd buzzed again, and the Attorney General called Mr. Jarvis Lorry.

"Mr. Jarvis Lorry, are you a clerk at Tellson’s Bank?"

"I am."

"On a Friday night in November 1775, did you travel from London to Dover by the mail coach?"

"I did."

"Were there other passengers?"

"Two."

"Did they get out along the way during the night?"

"Yes."

"Mr. Lorry, look at the prisoner. Was he one of those passengers?"

"I cannot say for sure."

"Does he resemble either of them?"

"They were so wrapped up, it was so dark, and we were all so quiet that I cannot tell."

"Mr. Lorry, looking at his size and height, is it possible he was one of them?"

"Yes, it is possible."

"You won't swear that he was not?"

"No."

"So he might have been one of them?"

"Yes, though both passengers were terrified of highwaymen, and the prisoner does not look like a fearful man."

"Have you ever seen someone pretend to be afraid, Mr. Lorry?"

"Yes, I have."

"Have you seen the prisoner before, to your certain knowledge?"

"Yes."

"When?"

"A few days later, when I was returning from France. The prisoner came on board the ship at Calais and traveled with me."

"What time did he come on board?"

"A little after midnight."

"In the middle of the night. Was he the only passenger to board at that late hour?"

"Yes, he was."

"Were you traveling alone, Mr. Lorry?"

"With two companions. A gentleman and a lady who are here today."

"Did you speak to the prisoner?"

"Hardly at all. The weather was bad, the crossing was rough, and I lay on a sofa the whole time."

"Miss Manette!" was called.

The young lady stood up. Her father rose with her, holding her hand.

"Miss Manette, look at the prisoner."

Facing her pity and beauty was much harder for the accused man than facing the rest of the courtroom. Standing near her, he seemed to lose his composure for a moment. His hand nervously moved the herbs on the desk as if arranging flowers, and his lips trembled as he tried to control his breathing. The crowd buzzed loudly again.

"Miss Manette, have you seen the prisoner before?"

"Yes, sir."

"Where?"

"On the ship we just discussed, on the same journey."

"Are you the young lady Mr. Lorry mentioned?"

"Oh, unfortunately, yes."

Her sad, compassionate tone was interrupted by the Judge, who said sharply, "Just answer the questions and do not make comments."

"Miss Manette, did you speak with the prisoner during the crossing?"

"Yes, sir."

"Describe the conversation."

In a quiet room, she began: "When the prisoner came on board, he noticed that my father," she looked at him lovingly, "was very tired and weak. I had made a bed for him on the deck, and I sat beside him. There were no other passengers except the four of us. The prisoner kindly advised me on how to shelter my father from the wind. He was very gentle and kind, and that is how we began to speak."

"Did he come on board alone?"

"No."

"How many were with him?"

"Two French gentlemen."

"Did they talk?"

"Yes, until the last moment when the French gentlemen had to return to shore in their boat."

"Did they pass papers like these lists?"

"They passed some papers, but I don't know what they were."

"Were they similar in size and shape?"

"Possibly, but I don't know. They stood near the cabin lamp because it was dim, whispering quietly, and I only saw that they were looking at papers."

"What did the prisoner say to you, Miss Manette?"

"He was very open with me, which was comforting in my difficult situation. I hope," she began to cry, "that I do not harm him with my testimony today."

The crowd buzzed again.

"Miss Manette, the prisoner surely understands that you are testifying because it is your legal duty, not because you want to. Please continue."

"He told me he was traveling on difficult, secret business that could get people into trouble, and that he was using a false name. He said this business would require him to travel between England and France frequently."

"Did he mention America?"

"He tried to explain the conflict to me, saying he thought England was wrong and foolish. He joked that George Washington might become as famous in history as King George the Third. But he said it laughingly, just to pass the time."

The crowd unconsciously imitated her anxious expression as she spoke. When the Judge wrote down her words, everyone watched him. When the Judge looked up to glare at her comment about George Washington, he saw a courtroom full of mirrored, worried faces.

The Attorney General then called her father, Doctor Manette.

"Doctor Manette, have you ever seen the prisoner before?"

"Once. He visited my lodgings in London about three years ago."

"Can you identify him from the ship, or speak to his conversation with your daughter?"

"No, I cannot do either."

"Is there a specific reason why you cannot?"

In a low voice, he answered, "Yes. I suffered a long imprisonment in my home country, without trial or charges."

"Were you recently released at the time of the voyage?"

"They tell me so."

"Do you have any memory of it?"

"None. My mind is a blank from my time in prison making shoes until I found myself living in London with my daughter. I do not remember how I got here."

The Attorney General sat down, and the Manettes did as well.

Then, a strange turn occurred. The prosecution called a witness to identify the prisoner as a man who was waiting at a hotel in a dockyard town to collect military secrets. The defense lawyer was cross-examining this witness with no success, when the lawyer who had been staring at the ceiling wrote something on a piece of paper, folded it, and tossed it to him.

Opening the paper, the defense lawyer looked closely at the prisoner, then asked the witness:

"You say again you are quite sure that it was the prisoner?"

The witness was positive.

"Did you ever see anyone who looked very much like the prisoner?"

The witness said not enough to mistake them.

"Look at my colleague here," the lawyer said, pointing to the man who had thrown the paper. "Now look at the prisoner. Are they not very alike?"

Despite the colleague's messy and careless appearance, they looked so similar that the witness and the entire courtroom were shocked. When the Judge allowed the colleague to take off his wig, the resemblance was even more striking. The Judge joked to the defense lawyer, Mr. Stryver, if they should try Mr. Carton (the colleague) for treason next. Mr. Stryver replied no, but asked the witness if he could still be so confident after seeing such a resemblance. This completely destroyed the witness's credibility and ruined that part of the prosecution's case.

By this time, Jerry Cruncher had chewed a lot of rust off his fingers watching the trial. He then listened as Mr. Stryver presented the defense, showing that the witness Barsad was a hired spy, a traitor, and a scoundrel. He argued that the servant Cly was his partner in crime, and that they had targeted the prisoner because his family business required him to travel to France. He explained that the prisoner could not reveal these family matters because they involved others. He showed that Miss Manette's testimony only described polite conversation, and that the joke about George Washington was harmless. He argued that the prosecution was trying to exploit public fear and prejudice, and that the case rested on untrustworthy witnesses. The Judge stopped Mr. Stryver when he criticized the state trials, but the point had been made.

After the defense called its witnesses, the Attorney General argued that the defense was wrong and the prisoner was guilty. Finally, the Judge summarized the case, shaping his words in a way that pointed toward a death sentence.

The jury then withdrew to make their decision, and the crowd buzzed once more.

Mr. Carton, who had been staring at the ceiling, did not move. His colleague, Mr. Stryver, gathered his papers and whispered with others, watching the jury. The Judge paced the bench nervously. Carton sat back with his torn gown half off, his messy wig crooked, his hands in his pockets, staring at the ceiling. His reckless attitude made him look very different from the prisoner, so the resemblance was less noticeable now. Jerry remarked to his neighbor that he bet Carton didn't get much legal work.

Yet Carton was paying closer attention than he seemed. When Miss Manette’s head fell onto her father’s chest, he was the first to notice, calling out: "Officer! Look to that young lady. Help her father take her out. She is going to faint!"

There was much sympathy for her and her father as they left. Questioning had clearly upset the doctor, reminding him of his prison years.

As the jury left, they announced they could not agree and needed to deliberate. The Judge was surprised but allowed them to retire under guard. As night fell, lamps were lit in the court, and it was rumored the jury would take a long time. The spectators left to get food, and the prisoner sat down in the back of the dock.

Mr. Lorry returned and called Jerry over.

"Jerry, get something to eat if you want, but stay close. Let me know as soon as the jury returns. I want you to take the verdict back to the bank immediately, as you are the fastest messenger."

Jerry thanked him, accepted a shilling, and bowed. Mr. Carton then approached Mr. Lorry.

"How is the young lady?"

"She is very distressed, but her father is comforting her, and she is glad to be out of the court."

"I will let the prisoner know. It wouldn't look good for a respectable banker like you to be seen speaking to him."

Mr. Lorry blushed, and Carton walked toward the bar to speak to Darnay. Jerry followed closely to listen.

"Mr. Darnay!"

The prisoner came forward.

"Miss Manette will be fine. You have seen the worst of her distress."

"I am deeply sorry to have caused it. Could you tell her so, with my thanks?"

"Yes, I will." Carton’s manner was careless, almost rude, as he leaned against the bar.

"Thank you," said Darnay.

"What do you expect, Mr. Darnay?" Carton asked.

"The worst."

"That is the safest thing to expect. But the jury taking this long is a good sign."

Jerry had to move along, so he heard no more, leaving the two men—so alike in face, yet so different in manner—standing side by side.

An hour and a half passed in the crowded hallways below. Jerry was dozing when a loud murmur and a rush of people heading back to the courtroom woke him up.

"Jerry! Jerry!" Mr. Lorry called at the door.

"Here, sir! It’s hard to get through, but I'm here!"

Mr. Lorry handed him a paper through the crowd. "Quick! Do you have it?"

"Yes, sir."

Written on the paper was the single word: "ACQUITTED."

"If you had sent the message 'Recalled to Life' again," Jerry muttered as he ran, "I would have understood it this time."

He ran out of the Old Bailey as the crowd poured into the street, the disappointed onlookers dispersing like flies in search of other prey.

CHAPTER IV: Congratulatory

From the dimly lit hallways of the courthouse, the last of the crowd that had been gathered there all day was finally filtering out. Doctor Manette, Lucie Manette, Mr. Lorry, the defense solicitor, and their lead lawyer, Mr. Stryver, stood gathered around Charles Darnay—who had just been released—congratulating him on his narrow escape from death.

Even in much brighter light, it would have been hard to recognize the old shoemaker from the Paris attic in the intellectual-looking and upright Doctor Manette. Yet no one could look at him once without looking again. He still spoke in a sad, grave voice and would occasionally fall into a quiet, distracted state for no apparent reason. While referencing his long years of suffering in prison would always trigger this state—as it had during the trial—it could also happen on its own, casting a shadow over him. To anyone who did not know his story, it was as puzzling as if they were seeing the shadow of the Bastille prison fall upon him in the bright summer sun, even though the prison itself was hundreds of miles away.

Only his daughter had the power to chase away these dark moods. She was the golden thread that connected him to a past and a present beyond his years of misery. The sound of her voice, the look on her face, and the touch of her hand almost always had a powerful, healing effect on him. It was not absolutely always, as there were a few times when her influence had failed, but those moments were rare, and she believed they were behind them.

Mr. Darnay kissed her hand with deep gratitude, then turned to thank Mr. Stryver. Stryver was a man of just over thirty, but he looked twenty years older. He was heavily built, loud, red-faced, blunt, and completely lacking in politeness or tact. He had a pushy habit of forcing his way into groups and conversations, which suggested he would have no trouble pushing his way up in the world.

Still wearing his wig and gown, he stood broad-shouldered before his former client, crowding the group so much that he squeezed the gentle Mr. Lorry right out of the circle. "I am glad to have cleared your name, Mr. Darnay," he said. "It was an infamous prosecution, absolutely disgraceful, but that only made it more likely to succeed."

"You have put me in your debt for life, in more ways than one," said Darnay, shaking his hand.

"I did my best for you, Mr. Darnay, and my best is as good as anyone else's, I believe."

Since someone clearly needed to say "Much better," Mr. Lorry said it, partly in the hope of squeezing himself back into the group.

"Do you think so?" said Mr. Stryver. "Well, you were here all day, so you ought to know. You are a businessman, too."

"And as a businessman," said Mr. Lorry, whom the lawyer had now let back into the group, "I appeal to Doctor Manette to end this meeting and let us all go home. Miss Lucie looks ill, Mr. Darnay has had a terrible day, and we are all exhausted."

"Speak for yourself, Mr. Lorry," said Stryver. "I still have a whole night's work ahead of me."

"I am speaking for myself," answered Mr. Lorry, "and for Mr. Darnay, and for Miss Lucie. Miss Lucie, don't you agree I should speak for us all?" He asked her directly, with a glance at her father.

Doctor Manette's face had frozen into a strange look as he stared at Darnay. It was an intense look that deepened into a frown of dislike and distrust, mixed with a hint of fear. With this strange expression on his face, his mind seemed to wander far away.

"Father," Lucie said softly, laying her hand on his arm.

He slowly shook off the dark mood and turned to her.

"Shall we go home, father?"

Taking a deep breath, he answered, "Yes."

The friends of the acquitted prisoner had already left, believing Darnay would not be released until the next morning. Most of the lights in the hallways were turned off, the iron gates were closing with a loud rattle, and the gloomy courthouse was deserted until the next day's crowd arrived for more hangings, whippings, and brandings. Walking between her father and Mr. Darnay, Lucie Manette walked out into the fresh air. A carriage was called, and the father and daughter drove away.

Mr. Stryver had left them to head back to the dressing room. Another person, who had not joined the group or spoken to anyone, had been leaning against the wall in the darkest shadow. He quietly walked out after them and watched until the carriage drove off. Now he stepped up to where Mr. Lorry and Mr. Darnay stood on the pavement.

"So, Mr. Lorry! Can businessmen speak to Mr. Darnay now?"

Nobody had thanked Mr. Carton for his help during the trial; indeed, nobody seemed to know he had done anything. He had taken off his lawyer's robes and looked no better for it.

"If you knew the conflict that goes on in a businessman's head, torn between a kind impulse and maintaining a professional appearance, you would be amused, Mr. Darnay," Carton said.

Mr. Lorry reddened and said warmly, "You have mentioned that before, sir. We businessmen who serve a firm are not our own masters. We must think of the bank's reputation before ourselves."

"_I_ know, _I_ know," Carton said carelessly. "Don't be upset, Mr. Lorry. I'm sure you are as good as anyone else, probably better."

"And indeed, sir," Mr. Lorry continued, ignoring the comment, "I really don't see what you have to do with this matter. If you will excuse me for saying so, since I am much older than you, I don't think it is any of your business."

"Business! Bless you, _I_ have no business," said Mr. Carton.

"It is a pity you don't, sir."

"I think so, too."

"If you did," Mr. Lorry added, "perhaps you would attend to it."

"Lord help you, no, I wouldn't!" said Mr. Carton.

"Well, sir!" cried Mr. Lorry, thoroughly annoyed by Carton's indifference. "Business is a very good and respectable thing. And if business requires us to be cautious and silent, Mr. Darnay is a generous young gentleman who understands that. Mr. Darnay, good night, and God bless you! I hope you have been saved today for a long and happy life. Carriage, over here!"

Perhaps a little angry with himself as well as with Carton, Mr. Lorry hurried into his carriage and was driven back to Tellson's Bank. Carton, who smelled of port wine and did not seem entirely sober, laughed and turned to Darnay.

"This is a strange twist of fate that brings us together. It must be a strange night for you, standing alone on the street with your double."

"I hardly feel like I belong to this world yet," Charles Darnay replied.

"I don't wonder. It wasn't long ago that you were on your way to the next world. You speak very quietly."

"I think I _am_ about to faint."

"Then why the devil don't you eat? I dined while those fools were deciding whether you should live or die. Let me show you the nearest tavern where you can get a good meal."

Taking Darnay's arm, Carton led him down Ludgate Hill to Fleet Street, and up a covered path into a tavern. They were shown into a small room, where Darnay began to regain his strength with a plain dinner and good wine. Carton sat opposite him at the table with his own bottle of port, maintaining his rude, half-insolent manner.

"Do you feel like you belong to this world again, Mr. Darnay?"

"I am terribly confused about the time and place, but I feel much better now."

"That must be a wonderful feeling!" Carton said bitterly, refilling his glass. "As for me, my greatest wish is to forget that I belong to this world. There is nothing good in it for me except wine like this, and I do no good for it. So we are not very alike in that way. In fact, I'm beginning to think we aren't alike in any way, you and I."

Confused by the emotions of the day and feeling that sitting with this coarse double was like a dream, Charles Darnay did not know how to respond and remained silent.

"Now that you've finished your dinner," Carton said, "why don't you toast a health, Mr. Darnay? Why don't you give a toast?"

"What toast?"

"It's on the tip of your tongue. I know it's there."

"Miss Manette, then!" said Darnay.

"Miss Manette, then!" Carton repeated.

Looking Darnay straight in the face as he drank, Carton threw his empty glass over his shoulder against the wall, shattering it to pieces. Then he rang the bell and ordered another.

"She is a beautiful young lady to help into a carriage in the dark, Mr. Darnay!" he said, filling his new glass.

Darnay frowned slightly and answered with a quiet, "Yes."

"She is a beautiful young lady to be pitied and wept for! How does it feel? Is it worth being tried for your life to receive such sympathy, Mr. Darnay?"

Again, Darnay did not answer.

"She was very pleased to receive your message when I gave it to her. Not that she showed it, but I assume she was."

This reminded Darnay that, despite his unpleasant attitude, Carton had voluntarily helped save him that day. Darnay changed the subject to thank him.

"I don't want any thanks, and I don't deserve any," Carton replied carelessly. "It was nothing to do in the first place, and I don't know why I did it in the second. Mr. Darnay, let me ask you a question."

"Go ahead."

"Do you think I like you?"

"Really, Mr. Carton," replied Darnay, feeling uncomfortable, "I haven't asked myself that question."

"Well, ask yourself now."

"You have acted as if you do, but I don't think you actually do."

"_I_ don't think I do either," said Carton. "I'm beginning to have a high opinion of your intelligence."

"Even so," said Darnay, rising to ring for the bill, "I hope that won't prevent me from paying for the dinner and parting as friends."

Carton agreed, saying, "Not at all." Darnay paid the bill. "Are you paying for the whole thing?" Carton asked. When Darnay said yes, Carton called to the waiter, "Bring me another pint of this wine, and wake me up at ten o'clock."

With the bill paid, Charles Darnay rose and wished him good night. Without returning the greeting, Carton stood up with a challenging look and said, "One last word, Mr. Darnay: do you think I am drunk?"

"I think you have been drinking, Mr. Carton."

"Think? You know I have."

"Since you insist, yes, I know it."

"Then you should also know why. I am a disappointed, hard-working drudge, sir. I care for no one on earth, and no one on earth cares for me."

"That is very regrettable. You could have used your talents better."

"Maybe so, Mr. Darnay, maybe not. But don't let your sober face make you feel too proud; you don't know what you might become. Good night!"

Left alone, Carton took a candle, went to a mirror on the wall, and studied his face closely.

"Do you like this man?" he muttered to his own reflection. "Why should you like a man who looks like you? There is nothing in you to like, and you know it. Ah, damn you! What a waste you've made of your life! It's a fine reason to like a man because he shows you what you used to be and what you could have been! If you changed places with him, would those blue eyes look at you with such pity and love? Let's be honest about it: you hate him."

He drank the rest of his wine in a few minutes to comfort himself and fell asleep with his head on his arms, his hair messy on the table, as the candle melted down beside him.

CHAPTER V: The Jackal

Those were drinking days, and most men drank heavily. So much has time improved these habits that a moderate description of the amount of wine and punch a man would drink in a night, without hurting his reputation as a gentleman, would seem ridiculous today. The legal profession was certainly not behind any other in its drinking habits; nor was Mr. Stryver, who was rapidly pushing his way toward a large and profitable practice.

As a favorite at the Old Bailey and other courts, Mr. Stryver had begun to carefully secure his path to success. The courts now frequently called on their favorite lawyer. Daily, Mr. Stryver's red face could be seen in the King's Bench court, standing out among the other lawyers' wigs like a large sunflower pushing its way up in a crowded garden.

It had once been noted by other lawyers that while Mr. Stryver was quick-tongued, bold, and ready, he lacked the ability to find the core facts in a pile of documents—a key skill for a lawyer. However, he had improved remarkably in this. The more work he got, the better he became at finding the main points of a case. No matter how late he sat drinking with Sydney Carton, he always had the facts ready by morning.

Sydney Carton, the idlest and most unpromising of men, was Stryver’s great ally. The amount the two drank together between law terms could have floated a battleship. Stryver never had a case without Carton being there, hands in his pockets, staring at the ceiling. They traveled the same court circuits together, staying up late drinking. Carton was rumored to be seen at dawn, walking unsteadily back to his lodgings like a stray cat. Eventually, those in the legal world realized that while Carton would never be the famous leader—the "lion"—he was an excellent helper, a "jackal," doing the hard background work for Stryver.

"Ten o'clock, sir," said the tavern worker who had been ordered to wake Carton. "Ten o'clock, sir."

"_What's_ the matter?"

"Ten o'clock, sir."

"What do you mean? Ten o'clock at night?"

"Yes, sir. You told me to wake you."

"Oh! I remember. Very well, very well."

After trying to fall back asleep, which the worker prevented by stirring the fire for five minutes, Carton got up, put on his hat, and walked out. He walked to the Temple district, clearing his head by walking around King's Bench Walk and Paper Buildings twice, before entering Stryver's offices.

Stryver's clerk had already gone home, so Stryver himself opened the door. He was wearing slippers and a loose robe, with his collar open for comfort. He had the tired, bloodshot look around his eyes typical of heavy drinkers.

"You are a little late, Memory," said Stryver.

"About the usual time; maybe fifteen minutes late."

They went into a dusty room lined with books and messy papers, where a fire was burning. A kettle steamed on the stove, and on a cleared table sat plenty of wine, brandy, rum, sugar, and lemons.

"I see you've had your bottle already, Sydney."

"Two tonight, I think. I dined with today's client—or watched him dine, it's the same thing!"

"That was a great point you made, Sydney, about the resemblance and the identification. How did you think of it? When did it occur to you?"

"I thought he was a handsome fellow, and I realized I would have looked just like him if I had had any luck in life."

Mr. Stryver laughed so hard his stomach shook.

"You and your luck, Sydney! Let's get to work, get to work."

Sullenly, the jackal loosened his collar, went to the next room, and returned with a large jug of cold water, a basin, and a few towels. Soaking the towels in the water and wringing them out slightly, he wrapped them around his head in a strange, ugly way, sat at the table, and said, "Now I'm ready!"

"Not too much work to summarize tonight, Memory," said Mr. Stryver cheerfully, looking through his papers.

"How much?"

"Only two cases."

"Give me the harder one first."

"Here they are, Sydney. Go ahead!"

The "lion" then lay back on a sofa on one side of the table, while the "jackal" sat at his own messy desk on the other side, with bottles and glasses nearby. Both drank constantly, but in different ways. Stryver lay back with his hands on his belt, staring at the fire or occasionally looking at a document. Carton, with a worried frown and a focused face, was so deep in his work that he didn't even look up when reaching for his glass, often feeling around for it for a minute before finding it. Two or three times, the work became so difficult that Carton had to get up and soak his towels again, returning with damp headgear that looked ridiculous despite his serious expression.

Eventually, the jackal summarized the cases for the lion. Stryver went over the notes carefully, making remarks and adjustments with Carton's help. When they finished, Stryver lay back down to think, while Carton drank another glass, adjusted his head towels, and began working on the second case. They did not finish until the clocks struck three in the morning.

"And now that we are done, Sydney, pour us a glass of punch," said Mr. Stryver.

Carton took the steaming towels off his head, shook himself, yawned, shivered, and poured the drinks.

"You were very sharp about those prosecution witnesses today, Sydney. Every question hit its mark."

"I am always sharp, aren't I?"

"I don't deny it. But what has put you in such a bad mood? Have some punch and cheer up."

With a grunt of protest, Carton drank.

"You are the same Sydney Carton from our old school days," said Stryver, looking at him. "Always going up and down—happy one minute and depressed the next."

"Ah!" Carton sighed. "Yes, the same Sydney with the same bad luck. Even back then, I did the homework for other boys and neglected my own."

"And why did you do that?"

"God knows. It was just my way, I suppose."

He sat with his hands in his pockets and his legs stretched out, staring at the fire.

"Carton," said Stryver in his usual bullying tone, "your way is, and always was, a weak way. You have no energy or purpose. Look at me."

"Oh, stop it!" Sydney laughed, sounding more cheerful. "Don't start preaching to _me_!"

"How do you think I got to where I am?" Stryver asked. "How do I do it?"

"Partly by paying me to help you, I suppose. But there's no need to lecture me; what you want to do, you do. You were always in the lead, and I was always behind."

"I had to work to get to the front; I wasn't born there, was I?"

"I wasn't there to see it, but I bet you were," Carton said, and they both laughed.

"Before school, at school, and ever since," Carton continued, "you have taken your place, and I have taken mine. Even when we were students in Paris, learning French and French law, you were always succeeding and I was always getting nowhere."

"And whose fault was that?"

"Honestly, I think it was yours. You were always so busy pushing and shouldering your way forward that I had no choice but to sit back and rest. But it's depressing to talk about the past at dawn. Change the subject before I go."

"Well then! Let's toast the pretty witness," said Stryver, raising his glass. "Is that a better subject?"

Apparently not, as Carton became gloomy again.

"The pretty witness," he muttered, looking at his glass. "I've seen enough witnesses today. Who do you mean?"

"The doctor’s beautiful daughter, Miss Manette."

"_She_ is pretty?"

"Is she not?"

"No."

"Why, everyone in the courtroom was admiring her!"

"Who cares what the courtroom thinks? Since when is the Old Bailey a judge of beauty? She's just a golden-haired doll!"

"Do you know, Sydney," Mr. Stryver said, looking at him closely, "I actually thought you felt sorry for her, and were very quick to notice when she fainted?"

"Quick to notice! If any girl faints right in front of a man's nose, he doesn't need glasses to see it. I will drink to her, but I deny she is beautiful. And now, no more drinking. I'm going to bed."

As Stryver walked him to the stairs with a candle, the cold morning light was beginning to show through the dirty windows. Outside, the air was cold and gloomy, the sky gray, and the river dark—the whole city looked like a lifeless desert. Wind blew dust around the quiet streets.

With wasted potential inside him and a gloomy world around him, Carton stopped on a bridge. For a moment, he imagined a better life of honest ambition, self-control, and hard work. In that vision, he saw happiness, success, and hope. A moment later, the vision vanished. Climbing the stairs to his dusty room, he threw himself onto his bed in his clothes and wept.

Sadly, the sun rose, looking down on no sadder sight than this talented man, unable to use his abilities, unable to help himself or find happiness, fully aware of his wasted life and resigned to letting it ruin him.

CHAPTER VI: Hundreds of People

The quiet lodgings of Doctor Manette were in a quiet street corner not far from Soho Square. On a fine Sunday afternoon, four months after the treason trial had passed and been mostly forgotten by the public, Mr. Jarvis Lorry walked from his home in Clerkenwell to have dinner with the Doctor. After initially focusing only on business, Mr. Lorry had become a true friend of the family, and this quiet street corner had become the happiest part of his life.

On this pleasant Sunday, Mr. Lorry walked toward Soho early in the afternoon for three habitual reasons. First, because on nice Sundays he often walked before dinner with the Doctor and Lucie. Second, because on bad Sundays he spent the day with them as a family friend, talking, reading, looking out the window, and passing the time. Third, because he had some small doubts he wanted to resolve, and he knew this was a good time to observe the Doctor's household.

A more charming corner than the one where the Doctor lived could not be found in London. It was a dead-end street, and the front windows of the lodgings looked out onto a pleasant, quiet street. There were few buildings north of Oxford Road back then, and forest trees and wild flowers still grew in fields that have now vanished. As a result, fresh country air circulated freely in Soho, and nearby walls were lined with peaches ripening in the sun.

The summer sun shone brightly into the corner early in the day; but when the streets grew hot, the corner was in the shade. It was a cool, peaceful but cheerful spot, famous for its echoes, and a quiet refuge from the busy streets.

The Doctor rented two floors of a large, quiet house. A few businesses operated there during the day, but they made very little noise and were empty at night. In a building at the back, reached through a courtyard with a green plane tree, organs were made, silver was engraved, and gold was beaten by some mysterious craftsman who had a sign of a golden arm extending from the front hall wall—looking as if he had turned his own arm to gold and was threatening to do the same to visitors. Very little was ever seen or heard of these trades, or of a quiet lodger upstairs, or of a coachmaker downstairs. Occasionally a workman walked through the hall, a stranger looked around, or a distant clinking sound was heard from the courtyard. Aside from these rare noises, the sparrows in the tree and the echoes in the street had the place to themselves all week.

Doctor Manette saw patients here, drawn by his old reputation and the rumors of his dramatic story. His scientific knowledge and skill in conducting experiments also kept him in demand, earning him as much money as he needed.

Mr. Lorry was thinking of these things when he rang the doorbell on that fine Sunday afternoon.

"Is Doctor Manette at home?"

He was expected back soon.

"Is Miss Lucie at home?"

She was also expected back soon.

"Is Miss Pross at home?"

She might be, but it was impossible for the servant girl to guess whether Miss Pross wanted to see visitors or not.

"Since I feel at home here myself," said Mr. Lorry, "I’ll go upstairs."

Although Lucie had no memory of France, the country of her birth, she seemed to have inherited the French ability to make a place look beautiful with very little money. Simple as the furniture was, it was decorated with so many tasteful little ornaments that the effect was delightful. The arrangement of everything in the rooms, the colors, and the simple decorations showed such care and good sense that as Mr. Lorry looked around, the chairs and tables seemed to ask if he approved.

There were three rooms on the floor, and the doors between them were kept open to let the breeze pass through. Mr. Lorry walked from one room to another, smiling at the pleasant atmosphere. The first was the best room, containing Lucie’s birds, flowers, books, desk, and watercolors. The second was the Doctor’s consulting room, which was also used for dining. The third, shaded by the leaves of the plane tree outside, was the Doctor’s bedroom. In the corner stood the unused shoemaker’s bench and tools, looking just as they had in the dark attic room in Paris.

"I wonder," said Mr. Lorry, looking around, "why he keeps that painful reminder of his suffering!"

"And why shouldn't he?" came a sharp voice that made him jump.

It was Miss Pross, the eccentric, red-haired woman whom Mr. Lorry had first met at the Dover hotel and had since come to know well.

"I would have thought—" Mr. Lorry began.

"Bah! What you would have thought!" said Miss Pross, cutting him off.

"How do you do?" she asked sharply, though not unkindly.

"I am well, thank you," answered Mr. Lorry meekly. "How are you?"

"Nothing to brag about," said Miss Pross.

"Indeed?"

"Yes, indeed!" said Miss Pross. "I am very annoyed about my Ladybird."

"Indeed?"

"For heaven's sake, say something other than 'indeed,' or you will drive me crazy," said Miss Pross, who was known for her bluntness.

"Really, then?" Mr. Lorry corrected himself.

"Really is bad enough," returned Miss Pross, "but better. Yes, I am very upset."

"May I ask why?"

"I don't want dozens of people who are not good enough for Ladybird coming here to see her," said Miss Pross.

"Do dozens of people actually come for that?"

"Hundreds," said Miss Pross.

It was typical of Miss Pross to exaggerate whenever anyone questioned her statements.

"Dear me!" said Mr. Lorry, choosing the safest response.

"I have lived with the darling since she was ten years old," said Miss Pross. "And it’s very hard now."

Not understanding what was so hard, Mr. Lorry simply nodded.

"All sorts of unworthy people are always showing up," said Miss Pross. "Ever since you started it."

"_I_ started it, Miss Pross?"

"Didn't you? Who brought her father back to life?"

"Oh! If _that_ was starting it—" said Mr. Lorry.

"It wasn't ending it, was it? When you started it, it was hard enough. I have no complaint about Doctor Manette, except that he is not worthy of such a daughter—though no one could be. But it is doubly hard to have crowds of people showing up after her to take her affection away from me."

Mr. Lorry knew Miss Pross was very jealous, but he also knew that beneath her eccentric behavior, she was completely devoted to Lucie. She was one of those unselfish women who gladly dedicate their lives to serving the youth, beauty, and success they never had themselves. Mr. Lorry respected her loyal devotion deeply, placing her in his estimation far above many wealthy ladies who kept accounts at Tellson's Bank.

"There never was, and never will be, but one man worthy of Ladybird," said Miss Pross. "And that was my brother Solomon, if he hadn't made a mistake in life."

Mr. Lorry’s past inquiries had revealed that Solomon was actually a heartless thief who had stolen everything Miss Pross owned and abandoned her in poverty. Yet Miss Pross's blind faith in her brother (excusing his behavior as a "slight mistake") only increased Mr. Lorry's respect for her loyalty.

"Since we are alone and are both practical people," he said as they sat down in the living room, "let me ask you: does the Doctor ever mention his shoemaking days to Lucie?"

"Never."

"Yet he keeps the bench and tools next to his bed?"

"Yes," said Miss Pross, shaking her head. "But that doesn't mean he doesn't think about it."

"Do you believe he thinks of it often?"

"I do," said Miss Pross.

"Do you imagine—" Mr. Lorry began.

"Never imagine anything," Miss Pross interrupted. "Don't use your imagination."

"I stand corrected. Do you suppose, then?"

"Now and then," she admitted.

"Do you suppose," Mr. Lorry asked with a twinkle in his eye, "that Doctor Manette has his own theory about why he was imprisoned, or knows the name of the person who put him there?"

"I only know what Ladybird tells me."

"And what is that?"

"She thinks he does."

"Please don't be angry with my questions. I am just a dull businessman, and you are a practical woman."

"Dull?" Miss Pross asked calmly.

Wishing he hadn't used that word, Mr. Lorry quickly said, "No, no. But to return to the subject: isn't it strange that the Doctor, who is clearly innocent, never speaks of his imprisonment? Not with me, and not even with his beloved daughter? I ask this out of genuine care, not curiosity."

"Well," said Miss Pross, softened by his apology, "I think he is simply terrified of the whole subject."

"Terrified?"

"It makes sense. It is a horrible memory. Besides, he lost his mind because of it. Since he doesn't know exactly how he lost his mind or how he recovered, he might fear losing it again. That would make anyone avoid the subject."

This was a deeper observation than Mr. Lorry had expected. "True," he said, "and very frightening to think about. Yet I worry if keeping it bottled up inside is good for him."

"It can't be helped," said Miss Pross. "If we bring it up, he immediately gets worse. It is best to leave it alone. Sometimes he gets up in the middle of the night and walks up and down his room for hours. Ladybird knows this means his mind is pacing in his old prison cell. She goes to him, and they walk up and down together in silence until he calms down. He never explains why he is restless, and she never asks. They just walk together until her presence brings him back to himself."

Despite her claim of having no imagination, Miss Pross's description of this quiet pacing showed she understood his pain well.

The street corner, known for its echoes, now began to echo with the sound of approaching footsteps.

"Here they are!" said Miss Pross, standing up. "And now we will have hundreds of people!"

Due to the strange acoustics of the corner, Mr. Lorry felt as he looked out the window that the footsteps would never arrive. Echoes would fade and new ones would start, sounding close but vanishing. Finally, the father and daughter appeared, and Miss Pross went to open the door.

Miss Pross was a pleasant sight in her devotion, taking off Lucie's bonnet, dusting it, and smoothing her hair with great pride. Lucie was also a pleasant sight, hugging Miss Pross and thanking her for her trouble—which she had to do playfully, or Miss Pross would get upset and cry in her room. The Doctor watched them happily, joking that Miss Pross was spoiling Lucie. Mr. Lorry watched them all, feeling grateful in his later years to be part of such a warm home. But no "hundreds of people" arrived.

By dinner time, there was still no crowd. Miss Pross had prepared a wonderful dinner. Though simple, it was perfectly cooked and served, combining English and French styles.
