In her practical way, she had searched Soho for poor French immigrants who, for a few shillings, taught her their cooking secrets. The servant girls viewed her as a magician who could turn a simple chicken or vegetable into a feast.

On Sundays, Miss Pross ate with the family, though on other days she ate alone in the kitchen or in her private blue room upstairs. Today, she was in a very good mood, and the dinner was very pleasant.

It was a hot, humid day, and after dinner, Lucie suggested they drink their wine outside under the plane tree. They went out, and Lucie poured Mr. Lorry's wine. They sat talking while the houses behind them looked down and the tree rustled above.

Still, the expected crowd did not show up. Charles Darnay arrived, but he was only one person.

Doctor Manette and Lucie received him warmly. However, Miss Pross suddenly developed a nervous twitch and went inside, calling it a "fit of the jerks."

The Doctor was in high spirits and looked remarkably young. Sitting next to Lucie, their strong resemblance was very clear.

They talked about the old buildings of London, and Darnay asked the Doctor, "Have you visited the Tower of London much?"

"Lucie and I have been there, but only briefly. We know it is very interesting, but not much more."

"I was kept there, as you remember," Darnay said with a slight smile, though he colored slightly. "They told me a strange story when I was there."

"What was that?" Lucie asked.

"While making repairs, workmen found an old, forgotten dungeon. The walls were covered with inscriptions carved by prisoners. In a corner stone, one prisoner who was executed had hurriedly carved three letters. At first they looked like D. I. C., but on closer inspection, the last letter was a G. There was no record of a prisoner with those initials, and people guessed what it meant. Finally, someone suggested the letters spelled the word 'DIG'. They dug beneath the stone floor and found the ashes of a paper inside a small leather bag. What the prisoner wrote will never be known, but he had hidden it from his guards."

"Father!" Lucie cried. "You look ill!"

The Doctor had suddenly stood up, putting his hand to his head with a terrified look.

"No, my dear, not ill. A few large rain drops fell on me and startled me. We should go inside."

He recovered his composure almost immediately. Rain was indeed starting to fall, and he showed them the drops on his hand. But he said nothing about the story Darnay had told. As they went inside, Mr. Lorry noticed the same strange look on the Doctor's face as he looked at Darnay—the same expression he had seen at the courthouse.

He recovered so quickly, however, that Mr. Lorry doubted what he had seen. The Doctor was calm and steady as they walked inside, repeating that the sudden rain had startled him.

At tea time, Miss Pross served tea, still twitching, but there was still no crowd. Sydney Carton arrived, making the guests only two.

The night was very hot, and they sat by the open windows to catch a breeze. The curtains blew in the wind like white wings as thunder rolled.

"The rain drops are still few and heavy," said Doctor Manette. "The storm is coming slowly."

"It is coming surely," said Carton.

They spoke quietly, the way people do when waiting for lightning in a dark room.

The streets below were busy with people running for shelter. The quiet corner echoed with the sound of footsteps, though no one was there.

"A crowd of footsteps, yet the street is empty!" Darnay remarked.

"Isn't it strange, Mr. Darnay?" Lucie asked. "Sometimes I sit here in the evening and imagine—though the fancy frightens me tonight in the dark—"

"Tell us. We want to know."

"It sounds silly. I have sat here alone, listening to the echoes, until I imagined they were the footsteps of all the people who will enter our lives in the future."

"There is a huge crowd coming into our lives, then," Sydney Carton remarked gloomily.

The echoes of footsteps grew faster and louder as the storm approached. It sounded as if people were walking right under the windows and in the room, though the street remained empty.

"Are all these people coming to all of us, Miss Manette, or will we divide them?"

"I don't know, Mr. Darnay. It was just a foolish fancy. I imagined them as the people who will affect my life and my father's."

"I will take them into mine!" said Carton. "I make no conditions. A huge crowd is coming toward us, Miss Manette, and I see them—by the lightning." He spoke as a bright flash illuminated his figure by the window.

"And I hear them!" he added after a clap of thunder. "Here they come, fast and fierce!"

He was referring to the sudden downpour of rain, which drowned out his voice. A powerful storm of thunder, lightning, and rain raged until midnight when the moon finally came out.

The great bell of St. Paul's Cathedral was striking one in the clear night air when Mr. Lorry, escorted by Jerry carrying a lantern, set out for home. Because some stretches of the road were dark and dangerous, Mr. Lorry always had Jerry walk with him.

"What a storm! It was enough to wake the dead, Jerry," Mr. Lorry remarked.

"I've never seen a night that could do that, master," Jerry replied.

"Good night, Mr. Carton. Good night, Mr. Darnay," Mr. Lorry called out. "Will we ever see such a night together again?"

Perhaps. And perhaps they would see that great crowd of people, with its rush and roar, coming down upon them as well.

CHAPTER VII: Monseigneur in Town

Monseigneur, one of the most powerful lords at Court, held a reception every two weeks at his grand mansion in Paris. Monseigneur was in his private inner room—his holy sanctuary—while a crowd of worshippers waited in the outer rooms. Monseigneur was preparing to drink his morning hot chocolate. Although Monseigneur was capable of swallowing many things with ease, and was accused by a few bitter minds of rapidly swallowing up the wealth of France, his morning chocolate could not even reach his throat without the help of four strong men in addition to the cook.

Indeed, it took four men, all decorated in gold and fine clothing—with their leader requiring at least two gold watches in his pocket to copy the noble fashion set by Monseigneur—just to get the chocolate to his lips. One servant carried the chocolate pot; a second frothed the drink with a small whisk; a third offered a napkin; and a fourth (the one with the two gold watches) poured it out. It was impossible for Monseigneur to do without any of these servants and still maintain his high social standing. It would have been a terrible disgrace to his family name if his chocolate had been served by only three men; he would have died of shame if there were only two.

Monseigneur had attended a private dinner party the night before, where actresses and opera singers were present. He went out to such parties almost every night with charming company. Monseigneur was so polite and easily influenced that comedies and opera singers had far more influence on him regarding state affairs and secrets than the actual needs of all France. This was a happy circumstance for France, as such things always are for countries favored with similar rulers—just as it had been for England when the merry King Charles II sold his country to France.

Monseigneur had one noble idea about public business in general: to let everything take care of itself. As for his own private business, he believed everything must go his way to increase his power and wealth. Regarding his pleasures, he was convinced that the world had been created solely for his enjoyment. His motto was: "The earth and everything in it belong to me, says Monseigneur."

Yet Monseigneur had slowly realized that financial troubles were creeping into both his public and private affairs. To handle this, he had been forced to partner with a Farmer-General—a wealthy tax collector. Since Monseigneur knew nothing about public finances, he had to lease them to someone who did. For his private finances, because he was growing poor after generations of luxury, he relied on the Farmer-General's wealth. Therefore, Monseigneur had taken his sister out of a convent, preventing her from becoming a nun (which would have been the cheapest way to support her), and married her off to a very rich tax collector from a low-class family. This tax collector, carrying a cane with a golden apple on top, was now in the outer rooms. The crowd bowed low before him—except for the noble relatives of Monseigneur, who, along with his own wife, looked down on him with utter contempt.

The tax collector was a very wealthy man. He kept thirty horses in his stables, twenty-four male servants in his halls, and six ladies-in-waiting for his wife. As someone whose only job was to plunder and gather wealth wherever he could, he was at least the most honest reality among the guests at Monseigneur’s mansion that day.

Although the rooms were beautiful and decorated with the finest art of the time, they did not represent a healthy society. Compared to the starving people in rags and nightcaps living nearby (which the towers of Notre Dame Cathedral could easily see, standing between the two extremes), this mansion would have seemed like an uncomfortable business—if anyone at Monseigneur's house ever bothered to think about it. The guests included military officers with no knowledge of war, naval officers who knew nothing of ships, civil servants with no idea how to govern, and hypocritical priests with greedy eyes and loose morals. All of them were completely unfit for their jobs, but because they were related to Monseigneur's class, they were given public positions purely for profit. There were dozens of them. There were also doctors making fortunes by prescribing expensive remedies for imaginary illnesses, smiling at their wealthy patients in the waiting rooms. Inventors proposed solutions for minor state problems, ignoring the real need to root out corruption. Non-believing philosophers talked of remodeling the world with words, while chemists talked of turning lead into gold. Exquisite gentlemen showed their high breeding by acting completely bored by everything. The spies among the guests—who made up at least half the crowd—would have struggled to find a single wife who acted like a real mother to her children. In high society, children were sent away to be raised by peasant women, while grandmothers of sixty dressed and dined like young women of twenty.

A sense of unreality clung to everyone around Monseigneur. In the outermost room stood a few people who had begun to worry that things in France were going wrong. To fix this, some of them had joined a strange religious sect that fell into fits and convulsions, hoping to find guidance for the future. Others had joined a group that talked about "the Center of Truth," claiming that men could be saved by fasting and talking to spirits.

Fortunately, all the guests were dressed perfectly. If the Day of Judgment were a fashion show, everyone there would have been saved. With their powdered hair, makeup, fine swords, and expensive perfumes, they looked capable of keeping the system going forever. The gentlemen wore gold trinkets that jingled as they walked like little bells. Between the jingling and the rustling of silk and fine linen, the air was filled with a flurry that blew the memory of the starving people of Saint Antoine far away.

Fashionable dress was the magic charm used to keep everything in its place. Everyone acted as if they were at a never-ending costume ball. This fashion extended from the royal palace down to the executioner himself, who was required to perform his duties with powdered hair, a gold-trimmed coat, and white silk stockings. Monsieur Paris, the head executioner, dressed in this elegant fashion at the gallows. No one at the reception in 1780 doubted that a system run by a powdered, silk-stockinged executioner would last forever.

Having finished his chocolate, Monseigneur opened his doors and walked out. The crowd bowed, flattered, and humbled themselves before him. They showed so much devotion that they left nothing for God—which was probably why they never bothered to pray.

Giving a promise here and a smile there, whispering to one happy guest and waving to another, Monseigneur walked through the rooms. He then turned and returned to his private room, shut in once more by his servants, and was seen no more.

With the reception over, the guests hurried downstairs, their gold trinkets jingling. Soon only one man was left, slowly walking past the mirrors on his way out, holding his hat and snuffbox.

"I condemn you," he muttered, stopping at the door and looking back toward Monseigneur's room, "to the Devil!"

He shook the snuff from his fingers as if shaking dust from his shoes, and walked downstairs.

He was a man of about sixty, handsomely dressed, proud, with a face like a mask. His face was very pale, with sharp features and a cold expression. His nose was well-shaped, but pinched at the nostrils. This pinching was the only part of his face that ever changed, turning white or twitching slightly when he was angry, giving him a look of cruelty and treachery. Despite this, he was a handsome and striking man.

He went down to the courtyard, got into his carriage, and drove away. Few people had spoken to him at the reception, and Monseigneur had been cold to him. Under the circumstances, he seemed to enjoy seeing the common people scatter before his horses, barely escaping with their lives. His driver drove recklessly, as if charging into battle, and the master did not stop him. Sometimes people complained that the fast driving in the narrow streets without sidewalks injured and killed poor citizens. But few wealthy people cared, and the poor were left to get out of the way as best they could.

With a loud clatter and a complete lack of concern, the carriage sped through the streets and around corners. Women screamed, and men grabbed their children to pull them out of the way. Suddenly, near a fountain at a street corner, a wheel hit something with a sickening jolt. The horses reared, and the crowd screamed.

If the horses hadn't stopped, the carriage would have driven on, leaving the injured behind. But the driver quickly got down, and several people grabbed the horses' reins.

"What is the matter?" the master asked, looking out calmly.

A tall man in a nightcap had picked up a bundle from under the horses' hooves and laid it by the fountain. He was kneeling in the mud, crying out like a wild animal.

"Pardon, Monsieur the Marquis!" said a poor man, bowing. "It is a child."

"Why is he making that terrible noise? Is it his child?"

"Excuse me, Monsieur the Marquis—yes, unfortunately."

The street opened into a small square around the fountain. As the tall man suddenly stood up and ran toward the carriage, the Marquis put his hand on his sword.

"Killed!" the man shrieked, raising his arms. "Dead!"

The crowd gathered around and looked at the Marquis. Their eyes showed only watchfulness and curiosity, not anger or threats. They remained silent after their first cry. The man who had spoken sounded humble and frightened. The Marquis looked at them as if they were rats coming out of their holes.

He took out his purse.

"It is strange to me," he said, "that you people cannot take care of yourselves and your children. You are always in the way. For all I know, you have injured my horses. Here! Give him this."

He threw a gold coin out for the driver to pick up, and the crowd stared at it as it fell. The tall man cried out again, "Dead!"

He was stopped by the arrival of another man, for whom the crowd made way. Seeing him, the father fell on his shoulder, sobbing and pointing to the fountain where some women were looking at the still bundle. The women were as quiet as the men.

"I know, I know," said the newcomer. "Be brave, Gaspard. It is better for the poor child to die like this than to live in misery. It died in a moment, without pain. Could it have lived a happy life?"

"You are a philosopher," the Marquis said, smiling. "What is your name?"

"They call me Defarge."

"What is your trade?"

"I sell wine, Monsieur the Marquis."

"Pick that up, philosopher and wine seller," said the Marquis, throwing him another gold coin. "Spend it as you like. Are the horses ready?"

Without looking at the crowd again, the Marquis leaned back, preparing to drive away like a gentleman who had broken a cheap object and paid for it. Suddenly, a coin was thrown back into the carriage, ringing on the floor.

"Stop!" the Marquis called out. "Hold the horses! Who threw that?"

He looked toward where Defarge had been standing, but the father was crying on the pavement, and next to him stood a stout, dark woman, knitting.

"You dogs!" the Marquis said calmly, his nostrils twitching. "I would gladly run over all of you and wipe you off the earth. If I knew who threw that coin, I would crush him under the wheels."

The crowd was so cowed and used to his power that no one raised a hand or said a word. But the woman who stood knitting looked the Marquis straight in the eye. He ignored her, looking over them like rats, and told his driver, "Go on!"

He drove away, and other carriages followed in quick succession—the ministers, tax collectors, doctors, and lawyers, all part of the grand costume ball. The poor people stayed in the street for hours, watching through the barrier of soldiers and police. The father had carried his dead child away. The women sat by the fountain, watching the water flow and the carriages pass, while the woman who had stood knitting continued to knit like the figure of Fate. The water ran, the river flowed, day turned to evening, and life in the city turned to death. The poor went back to their dark rooms, the rich lit up their dinners, and everything continued as before.

CHAPTER VIII: Monseigneur in the Country

It was a beautiful landscape, with wheat fields that looked bright but did not produce much. There were patches of poor rye where wheat should have been, patches of poor peas and beans, and patches of the coarsest vegetables used as substitutes for wheat. All of nature, just like the men and women who farmed it, seemed to be growing unwillingly, ready to give up and wither away.

Monsieur the Marquis, in his heavy traveling carriage pulled by four horses and two riders, slowly climbed a steep hill. A red glow on the Marquis's face was not a sign of embarrassment, which would be unlike a nobleman; it was caused by something outside his control—the setting sun.

The sunset shone so brightly into the carriage when it reached the top of the hill that the Marquis was bathed in a deep red light. "It will die out soon," said the Marquis, looking at his hands, "directly."

Indeed, the sun was so low that it dipped below the horizon at that very moment. When the heavy brake was adjusted to the wheel and the carriage slid down the hill in a cloud of dust with a burnt smell, the red glow vanished quickly. The sun and the Marquis went down together, and no light was left when the brake was removed.

What remained was a rugged, open landscape: a small village at the bottom of the hill, a broad rising slope beyond it, a church tower, a windmill, a forest for hunting, and a cliff with a castle on it used as a prison. The Marquis looked at all these darkening objects as night fell, with the air of a man approaching his home.

The village had a single poor street, containing a poor brewery, a poor tannery, a poor tavern, a poor stable yard for changing horses, and a poor fountain. Its people were just as poor. All the villagers were destitute; many sat at their doors chopping up onions for their simple suppers, while others gathered at the fountain to wash leaves and grass that could be eaten. The reasons for their poverty were clear: the state tax, the church tax, the lord's tax, local taxes, and general taxes had to be paid everywhere, until it was a miracle that any village was left at all.

Few children were to be seen, and no dogs. The men and women faced a simple choice: live on the bare minimum down in the village under the mill, or face imprisonment and death in the castle on the cliff.

Announced by a messenger riding ahead and the cracking of the drivers' whips, which coiled like snakes in the evening air, the Marquis arrived at the post house gate. It was next to the fountain, and the peasants stopped what they were doing to watch him. He looked at them and saw the slow, inevitable wearing down of their faces and bodies, which would make the thinness of Frenchmen a stereotype in England for a hundred years.

The Marquis looked over the submissive faces bowing before him, just as his own class bowed before Monseigneur at court. The only difference was that these peasants bowed out of suffering, not to gain favor. Just then, a gray-haired road mender joined the group.

"Bring that man here!" the Marquis ordered his messenger.

The man was brought forward, holding his cap, and the other villagers crowded around to watch and listen, just like the crowd in Paris.

"Did I pass you on the road?" the Marquis asked.

"Monseigneur, it is true. I had the honor of being passed by you."

"Coming up the hill, and at the top?"

"Yes, Monseigneur."

"What were you staring at so closely?"

"Monseigneur, I was looking at the man."

He bent down and pointed his torn blue cap under the carriage. All the villagers leaned down to look as well.

"What man, you pig? And why look there?"

"Pardon, Monseigneur; he was hanging onto the chain of the carriage brake."

"Who?" the Marquis demanded.

"Monseigneur, the man."

"May the Devil take these idiots! What is his name? You know everyone in this area. Who was he?"

"Please, Monseigneur! He was not from around here. I have never seen him in my life."

"Hanging by the chain? Wasn't he suffocated?"

"With your permission, that was the amazing thing, Monseigneur. His head was hanging down—like this!"

He turned sideways to the carriage and leaned back, throwing his face up to the sky and letting his head hang down. Then he straightened up, held his cap, and bowed.

"What did he look like?"

"Monseigneur, he was whiter than a miller. Covered in dust, white as a ghost, and tall as a ghost!"

This description caused a stir in the small crowd, but everyone kept their eyes on the Marquis, perhaps wondering if he had any ghosts on his conscience.

"Well," said the Marquis, feeling that such low people were not worth getting angry at, "you did a fine job, seeing a thief riding on my carriage and keeping your mouth shut. Bah! Put him aside, Monsieur Gabelle!"

Monsieur Gabelle was the postmaster and tax collector. He had come out politely to assist in the questioning, holding the road mender by his sleeve in an official manner.

"Bah! Step aside!" said Monsieur Gabelle.

"Arrest this stranger if he tries to stay in your village tonight, Gabelle, and make sure his business is honest."

"Monseigneur, I am honored to obey your commands."

"Did the man run away, road mender? Where is the rascal?"

The road mender was already under the carriage with a few friends, pointing at the chain. They pulled him out and presented him, breathless, to the Marquis.

"Did the man run away, fool, when we stopped for the brake?"

"Monseigneur, he threw himself over the side of the hill, headfirst, like a man diving into a river."

"See to it, Gabelle. Go on!"

The villagers looking at the chain were still close to the wheels. The carriage started so suddenly that they had to jump back to save their bones. They had very little else to save.

The carriage started quickly but was soon slowed by the steep hill. It climbed at a walking pace, rocking through the sweet-smelling summer night. The drivers quietly fixed their whip lashes, the servant walked beside the horses, and the messenger could be heard riding ahead in the distance.

At the steepest part of the hill stood a small cemetery, with a wooden cross and a thin, worn statue of Christ carved by a local craftsman. It looked terribly thin and spare, perhaps copied from the sculptor's own life.

A woman was kneeling before this sad monument. She turned her head as the carriage approached, stood up quickly, and walked to the carriage door.

"It is you, Monseigneur! Monseigneur, a petition."

With an impatient sigh, but keeping his cold, unchanging face, the Marquis looked out.

"What is it now? Always petitions!"

"Monseigneur, for the love of God! My husband, the forester."

"What about your husband? It is always the same with you people. He cannot pay his taxes?"

"He has paid everything, Monseigneur. He is dead."

"Well, he is at peace. Can I bring him back to life?"

"Alas, no, Monseigneur! But he lies over there under a small mound of poor grass."

"Well?"

"Monseigneur, there are so many of those small mounds of poor grass."

"And?"

She looked like an old woman, but she was actually young. She was crying passionately, clutching her worn hands together and touching the carriage door gently, as if it could feel her appeal.

"Monseigneur, hear me! Hear my petition! My husband died of starvation. So many die of hunger, and many more will die."

"And what can I do? Can I feed them all?"

"God knows, Monseigneur, but I do not ask for that. My petition is that a small marker of stone or wood, with my husband's name, be placed over his grave so we know where he lies. Otherwise, the spot will be forgotten. When I die of the same hunger, I will be buried under another unmarked mound. Monseigneur, there are so many of us, and the hunger is so great! Monseigneur! Monseigneur!"

The servant pushed her away from the door, the carriage sped up, and she was left far behind. The Marquis was driven quickly toward his castle.

The sweet scents of the summer night rose around him, falling impartially like rain on the poor peasants gathered by the fountain, where the road mender was still talking about the ghost-like man. Gradually, the villagers went home, and lights appeared in their windows. As the lights went out and the stars appeared, it looked as if the lights had flown up into the sky.

The Marquis soon entered the shadow of a large house and overhanging trees. The carriage stopped under the light of a torch, and the great door of his castle was opened.

"Has my nephew, Monsieur Charles, whom I expect, arrived from England?"

"Monseigneur, not yet."

CHAPTER IX: The Gorgon’s Head

The Marquis’s castle was a heavy stone building, with a large stone courtyard in front, and two curved stone staircases meeting at a stone terrace before the main entrance. It was a stony place altogether, with heavy stone balustrades, stone urns, stone flowers, and stone faces of men and lions in every direction. It looked as if the mythical Gorgon’s head had looked at it when it was finished two hundred years ago, turning the whole place to stone.

Up the broad, shallow steps walked the Marquis, preceded by a servant carrying a torch. His arrival disturbed the darkness enough to make an owl in the stable roof hoot loudly. Everything else was so quiet that the torch carried up the steps and another held at the entrance burned steadily, as if they were in a closed room rather than the open night air. The only sounds were the owl's hooting and the splashing of the fountain into its stone basin. It was one of those dark nights that seem to hold their breath for hours, sighing softly before holding their breath again.

The great door slammed shut behind him. The Marquis walked through a hall decorated with old boar spears, swords, and hunting knives. It was made even grimmer by the heavy riding whips on the walls, many of which had been felt by the local peasants when their lord was angry.

Avoiding the larger rooms, which were dark and locked for the night, the Marquis followed his torchbearer upstairs to a room in the corridor. This door opened into his private suite of three rooms: his bedroom and two others. They were high-ceilinged, vaulted rooms with cool stone floors and large wood-burning fireplaces for winter. They were filled with luxuries suitable for a nobleman of that wealthy era. The style of King Louis XIV was visible in the rich furniture, alongside decorations showing older pages of French history.

A dinner table was set for two in the third room, which was a round room located in one of the castle's towers. It was a small, high room with its window wide open and the wooden blinds closed, so the dark night showed only as thin black lines between the blinds.

"My nephew," said the Marquis, looking at the table. "They said he had not arrived."

He had not, but he had been expected to arrive with the Marquis.

"Ah! He probably won't arrive tonight, but leave the table as it is. I will be ready in fifteen minutes."

Fifteen minutes later, the Marquis sat down alone to his dinner. His chair faced the window. He had finished his soup and was raising his glass of Bordeaux wine to his lips when he suddenly put it down.

"What is that?" he asked calmly, looking at the blinds.

"Monseigneur? What?"

"Outside the blinds. Open them."

The servant did so.

"Well?"

"Monseigneur, there is nothing. Just the trees and the night."

The servant had opened the blinds and looked out into the darkness, then stood waiting for instructions.

"Good," said the calm master. "Close them again."

This was done, and the Marquis went on eating. He was halfway through his meal when he stopped again, glass in hand, hearing the sound of wheels. A carriage drove up quickly to the front of the castle.

"Find out who has arrived."

It was the Marquis's nephew. He had been traveling behind his uncle earlier in the afternoon, closing the distance but not catching up on the road. At the post houses, they had told him his uncle was ahead of him.

He was told that dinner was waiting for him, and he was asked to join. Soon he entered. In England, he was known as Charles Darnay.

The Marquis received him politely, but they did not shake hands.

"You left Paris yesterday, sir?" Darnay asked as he sat down.

"Yesterday. And you?"

"I came straight here."

"From London?"

"Yes."

"You took a long time to make the journey," the Marquis said with a smile.

"On the contrary, I came directly."

"Excuse me! I don't mean the journey took long; I mean it took you a long time to decide to come."

"I was delayed by various business," the nephew replied.

"No doubt," said the polite uncle.

As long as the servant was in the room, they said nothing else. Once coffee was served and they were alone, the nephew looked at his uncle's mask-like face and began to speak.

"I have returned, sir, to finish the business that took me away. It led me into great and unexpected danger, but it is a sacred duty. Even if it had led to my death, I hope it would have supported me."

"Not to death," the uncle said. "There is no need to say that."

"I doubt, sir," the nephew replied, "that if I had been on the brink of death, you would have done anything to save me."

The pinched nostrils and the sharp lines on the uncle's face tightened ominously. He made a polite gesture of protest, but it was purely a social formality and not reassuring.

"Indeed, sir," the nephew continued, "for all I know, you may have helped make the suspicious circumstances around me look even worse."

"No, no, no," the uncle said pleasantly.

"However that may be," the nephew said, looking at him with deep distrust, "I know you would stop me by any means, and would have no moral objections to those means."

"My friend, I told you so," said the uncle, his nostrils twitching. "Please remember that I told you so long ago."

"I remember."

"Thank you," the Marquis said sweetly.

His polite tone lingered in the air like a musical note.

"In fact, sir," the nephew continued, "I believe it is both your misfortune and my good luck that I am not in a French prison right now."

"I don't quite understand," the uncle said, sipping his coffee. "Would you explain?"

"I believe that if you were not in disgrace with the royal court, you would have used a secret arrest warrant to throw me into a fortress indefinitely."

"It is possible," the uncle said calmly. "For the honor of the family, I would gladly lock you up. Please excuse me!"

"I see that, fortunately for me, your reception at court the day before yesterday was cold as usual," the nephew remarked.

"I wouldn't say fortunately, my friend," the uncle replied politely. "A period of quiet reflection in prison might help you more than you help yourself. But there is no point in discussing it. As you say, I am at a disadvantage now. Those little warrants and favors are hard to get now because so many ask for them. It didn't use to be this way, but France has changed for the worse. Our ancestors held the power of life and death over the peasants. From this very room, many peasants have been taken out to be hanged. In the next room, my bedroom, a man was stabbed to death for protesting when we took his daughter. We have lost many privileges. A new philosophy is fashionable, and asserting our high position today might cause us real inconvenience. It is all very bad!"

The Marquis took a pinch of snuff and shook his head, looking elegantly sad about a country that still had him to guide it.

"We have asserted our high position so much, both in the past and now," the nephew said gloomily, "that I believe our family name is detested more than any other in France."

"Let us hope so," said the uncle. "The hatred of the lower classes is the involuntary respect they pay to the high."

"There is not a single face in this area," the nephew continued, "that looks at me with respect. They only show the fear and submission of slaves."

"A compliment to the family's power," said the Marquis, "which we have maintained well. Ah!" He took another pinch of snuff and crossed his legs.

But when his nephew leaned on the table and covered his eyes in depression, the Marquis looked at him sideways with sharp dislike, dropping his mask of indifference.

"Repression is the only philosophy that lasts. The fear and submission of slaves, my friend," the Marquis remarked, "will keep these dogs obedient to the whip as long as this roof," looking up, "stands."

That might not be as long as the Marquis thought. If he could have seen his castle and fifty others like it a few years in the future, he would not have recognized the burnt, ruined remains. As for the roof he bragged about, it would shut out the sky forever for the men who would be shot by the lead melted from it.

"Meanwhile," said the Marquis, "I will preserve the family honor and peace, even if you won't. But you must be tired. Shall we end our talk for the night?"

"A moment more."

"An hour, if you like."

"Sir," said the nephew, "we have done wrong, and we are paying the price."

"_We_ have done wrong?" the Marquis repeated with a smile, pointing to his nephew and himself.

"Our family; our noble family. Even in my father's time, we did terrible wrongs, injuring anyone who stood in the way of our pleasure. I cannot separate my father from his twin brother, his partner and successor."

"Death has done that!" said the Marquis.

"And left me," the nephew answered, "tied to a system I hate, responsible for it but powerless to change it. I am trying to carry out my mother's last request to show mercy and correct our wrongs, but I am powerless."

"You will never find help or power from me, my nephew," said the Marquis, touching him on the chest with his finger as they stood by the fire.

Every line on his pale face was tightened in a cruel, sharp expression as he looked at his nephew, holding his snuffbox. He touched him on the chest again, as if his finger were a sword running him through, and said:

"My friend, I will die supporting the system under which I have lived."

Having said this, he took a pinch of snuff and put the box in his pocket.

"It is better to be sensible," he added, ringing a bell, "and accept your destiny. But you are lost, Charles, I see."

"This property and France are lost to me," said the nephew sadly. "I give them up."

"Are they yours to give up? France might be, but is the property? It is hardly worth mentioning, but is it yours yet?"

"I did not mean to claim it yet. If it passed to me tomorrow—"

"Which I hope is not likely."

"—or twenty years from now, I would abandon it and live elsewhere. It is little to give up. It is just a wilderness of misery and ruin!"

"Ah!" said the Marquis, looking around the luxurious room.

"It looks beautiful here," the nephew said, "but in reality, it is a crumbling tower of waste, debt, oppression, hunger, and suffering."

"Ah!" the Marquis said again, looking very satisfied.

"If it ever becomes mine, I will put it into hands that can slowly free it from this debt, so the poor people who cannot leave can suffer less in the next generation. But it is not for me. There is a curse on this land."

"And you?" the uncle asked. "Forgive my curiosity, but how do you plan to live?"

"I must do what other noblemen may have to do one day—work."

"In England, for example?"

"Yes. The family name will be safe from me there, because I do not use it."

The ringing of the bell had summoned the servant to light the bedroom next door, which now shone through the open entrance. The Marquis looked that way, listening for his servant's footsteps.

"England is very attractive to you, considering how poorly you have done there," he remarked with a smile.

"I have already said that my difficulties there may be due to you, sir. For the rest, it is my refuge."

"The English boast that their country is a refuge for many. Do you know a fellow countryman who has found refuge there? A Doctor?"

"Yes."

"With a daughter?"

"Yes."

"Yes," said the Marquis. "You are tired. Good night!"

He bowed politely, but there was a secret, mocking look on his face as he spoke those words, which startled his nephew. His thin lips and eyes curved into a diabolical smile.

"Yes," the Marquis repeated. "A Doctor with a daughter. Yes. So begins your new philosophy! You are tired. Good night!"

It would have been useless to ask him any questions. The nephew looked at him in vain as he walked to the door.

"Good night!" said the uncle. "I look forward to seeing you in the morning. Rest well! Show my nephew to his room—and burn him in his bed if you like," he added to himself, before summoning his servant to his own room.

Once the servant left, the Marquis walked around his bedroom in his robe, getting ready for bed on this hot night. Moving quietly in his slippers, he looked like a tiger—or an enchanted marquis from a fairy tale who was about to turn into a beast.

He walked around the luxurious room, thinking of the day's journey: the slow climb up the hill, the sunset, the mill, the prison on the cliff, the poor village, the peasants by the fountain, and the road mender pointing out the man under the carriage. That fountain reminded him of the one in Paris, the dead child, the crying father, and the women looking at the body.

"I am cool now," the Marquis said, "and can go to bed."

Leaving only one light burning, he let his gauze curtains fall around his bed and went to sleep as the night fell silent.

The stone faces on the walls stared blindly into the dark night for three hours. In the stables, the horses rattled their chains, the dogs barked, and the owl hooted.

For three hours, the stone faces of the castle, both human and lion, stared into the night. Darkness lay over the land, and the cemetery's graves were hidden in the dark. In the village, both the tax collectors and the poor peasants were fast asleep, dreaming of food and rest as yoked oxen do.

The village fountain flowed unseen in the dark, and the castle fountain splashed quietly—both counting the minutes of the three dark hours. Then, the water began to look gray in the dawn light, and the stone faces on the castle seemed to open their eyes.

The light grew stronger until the sun touched the trees and shone over the hill. In the red light, the castle fountain water looked like blood, and the stone faces turned red. The birds sang loudly, and a little bird sang on the Marquis's bedroom window sill. The nearest stone face seemed to stare in shock, with its mouth open.

With the sun up, the village awoke. Windows were opened, doors unlocked, and people walked out shivering in the morning air. The day's hard work began. Some went to the fountain, some to the fields to dig, and some led their thin cows to find grass by the roadside. At the cemetery cross, a few people knelt to pray.

The castle awoke later, but gradually. The sun shone on the boar spears and hunting knives, doors and windows were opened, the horses looked around in their stables, and the dogs barked to be let loose.

These were normal morning events. But the sudden ringing of the great castle bell, the sound of people running up and down the stairs, and the panic on the terrace were not normal. Servants saddled horses and rode away in a hurry.

The news quickly reached the road mender working on the hill. He ran as fast as he could down the hill through the dust, stopping only when he reached the village fountain.

All the villagers were gathered there, whispering in suspense and curiosity. The cows stood by, and the castle servants and tax officials stood armed across the street in confusion. The road mender joined them, beating his chest with his blue cap. Gabelle, the tax collector, was hastily mounted on a horse behind a servant and ridden away at a gallop.

It meant there was one stone face too many at the castle.

The Gorgon had looked at the building during the night, adding the final stone face it had waited two hundred years for.

It lay on the Marquis's pillow. It looked like his mask-like face, but frozen in anger and petrified. Driven deep into the stone figure's heart was a knife. Around the handle was a piece of paper, on which was written:

"Drive him fast to his tomb. This, from Jacques."

CHAPTER X: Two Promises

Twelve more months had passed, and Charles Darnay was established in England as a teacher of the French language and literature. Today he would be called a professor, but back then he was a tutor. He taught young men who wanted to learn a living language spoken all over the world. He was also a skilled translator who could write elegant English. Since good teachers were rare in those days, and ruined noblemen had not yet begun working as cooks and carpenters, young Mr. Darnay soon became successful. He was also well-informed about the political situation in France, which was of growing interest to the English. Through hard work and perseverance, he prospered.

He had not expected to find an easy life in London. If he had, he would not have succeeded. He expected hard work, found it, and did it. His success came from his efforts.

He spent part of his time at Cambridge University, teaching students European languages, while others taught Greek and Latin. The rest of his time he spent in London.

Ever since the world began, a man's life has always revolved around one thing—the love of a woman. And so it was for Charles Darnay.

He had loved Lucie Manette from the moment of his trial. He had never heard anything as sweet as her compassionate voice, or seen anyone as beautiful as she was when she stood before him in court. But he had not yet spoken to her about his feelings. A year had passed since the assassination at the castle in France—which now felt like a distant dream—and he had not yet told her that he loved her.

He had good reasons for waiting. It was another summer day when, returning to London from his college work, he went to the quiet corner in Soho to speak to Doctor Manette. It was late afternoon, and he knew Lucie was out with Miss Pross.

He found the Doctor reading in his armchair by the window. The energy that had once supported him in prison had slowly returned. He was now a very active man with strong determination. He was still a bit sudden and fitful at times, but this was becoming rare.

He studied a lot, slept little, handled fatigue easily, and was generally cheerful. When Darnay entered, the Doctor laid aside his book and held out his hand.

"Charles Darnay! I am glad to see you. We have been expecting you for a few days. Mr. Stryver and Sydney Carton were here yesterday, and both said you were overdue."

"I am grateful for their interest," Darnay replied, speaking coldly of them but warmly to the Doctor. "Miss Manette—"

"Is well," the Doctor said, interrupting. "Your return will please us all. She is out buying household goods but will return soon."

"Doctor Manette, I knew she was out. I took this opportunity to speak with you alone."

A long silence followed.

"Yes?" the Doctor said with clear discomfort. "Bring your chair here and speak."

Darnay sat down but found it hard to start.

"I have been close with your family for a year and a half, Doctor Manette, and I hope the subject I want to discuss won't—"

The Doctor put up his hand to stop him. After a moment, he drew his hand back and said:

"Is Lucie the subject?"

"She is."

"It is hard for me to speak of her, Charles Darnay. It is very hard for me to hear her spoken of in that way."

"It is a tone of deep admiration and love, Doctor Manette!" Darnay said respectfully.

Another silence followed before the father replied:

"I believe you. I know you mean it."

The Doctor's discomfort and his reluctance to discuss the subject were so clear that Darnay hesitated.

"Shall I continue, sir?"

Another pause.

"Yes, go on."

"You know what I want to say, though you cannot know how deeply I feel it. Dear Doctor Manette, I love your daughter dearly and devotedly. If there is true love in this world, I feel it for her. You have loved in your life; let that memory speak for me!"

The Doctor sat with his face turned away, looking at the floor. At these words, he stretched out his hand quickly and cried:

"Not that, sir! Leave that alone! I beg you, do not bring that up!"

His cry sounded like real pain, ringing in Darnay's ears. He gestured for Darnay to stop, and Darnay remained silent.

"Forgive me," the Doctor said quietly after a few moments. "I do not doubt your love for Lucie. You may be sure of that."

He turned toward Darnay but did not look him in the eye. He rested his chin on his hand, his white hair covering his face.

"Have you spoken to Lucie?"

"No."

"Or written to her?"

"Never."

"I know your silence is out of respect for me as her father. I thank you for that."

He offered his hand, but did not look up.

"I know, Doctor Manette," Darnay said respectfully, "because I have seen you together every day, that the love between you and Miss Manette is extraordinary. It has few equals, even between father and child. I know that Lucie loves and relies on you completely. Because she had no parent in her childhood, she is now devoted to you with all her heart. I know that in her eyes, you are a sacred figure restored to her from the dead. When she hugs you, she holds you with the love of a baby, a girl, and a woman all in one. I know that in loving you, she sees her mother, and loves you through all your suffering. I have seen this every day."

Her father sat silent, his face down. His breathing was faster, but he controlled his emotions.

"Dear Doctor Manette, knowing this, I have kept silent as long as possible. I felt that bringing my love between you would interfere with your special bond. But I love her. God is my witness that I love her!"

"I believe you," her father said sadly. "I have thought so before. I believe you."

"But please do not think," Darnay said, hearing the sadness in the Doctor's voice, "that if I were lucky enough to marry her, I would ever separate her from you. I would never suggest such a thing. That would be selfish and wrong. If I had any intention of separating you, I could not face you today."

He laid his hand on the Doctor's hand.

"No, dear Doctor Manette. Like you, I am a voluntary exile from France, driven away by its misery and oppression. Like you, I am trying to build a new life here through my own work. I only want to share your lives, and be faithful to you both to the end. I do not want to take Lucie away, but to help support you both."

His hand remained on the Doctor's. Responding to the touch, the Doctor rested his hands on his chair and looked up for the first time. His face showed a struggle with his old look of fear and doubt.

"You speak very sincerely, Charles Darnay. I thank you, and I will open my heart to you. Do you have any reason to believe Lucie loves you?"

"None. Not yet."

"Do you want to find out right away, with my permission?"

"No. I might not have the courage to ask her for weeks; or I might ask her tomorrow."

"Do you want my advice?"

"No, sir. But I thought you might give me some guidance if you thought it right."

"Do you want a promise from me?"

"Yes, I do."

"What is it?"

"I know that without your approval, I have no hope. Even if Lucie loved me, she would never choose me if it hurt her father."

"If that is true, do you see what else it means?"

"I know that a word from you in favor of any suitor would decide the matter for her. For that reason, Doctor Manette," Darnay said firmly, "I would never ask you to speak for me."

"I am sure of that. Charles Darnay, love creates its own mysteries. My daughter Lucie is a mystery to me in this regard; I do not know if she loves you."

"May I ask, sir, if anyone else is seeking her love?"

"You have seen Mr. Carton here. Mr. Stryver is here too. If anyone is, it is one of them."

"Or both," said Darnay.

"I had not thought of both, and I think neither is likely. What is the promise you want from me?"

"It is that if Lucie ever tells you she loves me, you will tell her what I have said today, and that you believe me. I hope you think well enough of me not to speak against me. That is all I ask, and I will keep my part of the agreement."

"I give you the promise without any conditions," said the Doctor. "I believe your love is genuine, and that you want to bring us closer together, not separate us. If she ever tells me that you are necessary for her happiness, I will give her to you. If there were—Charles Darnay, if there were—"

The young man took his hand gratefully as the Doctor continued:

"—any reasons or fears against the man she loved—as long as he was not responsible for them—they would be forgotten for her sake. She is everything to me. She is worth more than all my past suffering. But this is idle talk."

The Doctor suddenly fell silent, his face turning blank. Darnay felt the Doctor's hand turn cold as it released his.

"You said something to me," Doctor Manette said, smiling suddenly. "What was it?"

Darnay was confused, but remembered his offer to share his own secret.

"I want to be completely honest with you, as you are with me. My name is not my real one, though it is similar to my mother's. I want to tell you my real name, and why I am in England."

"Stop!" said the Doctor.

"I want to tell you so there are no secrets between us."

"Stop!"

The Doctor put his hands over his ears, then placed them on Darnay's lips.

"Tell me only if Lucie accepts you. Tell me on your marriage morning. Do you promise?"

"I promise."

"Give me your hand. She will be home soon, and it is better she does not see us together tonight. Go, and God bless you!"

It was dark when Charles Darnay left, and an hour later when Lucie came home. She entered the room alone—Miss Pross having gone upstairs—and was surprised to find her father's chair empty.

"Father!" she called. "Father dear!"

There was no answer, but she heard a low hammering sound from his bedroom. She walked quietly to his door, looked in, and ran back terrified, crying, "What shall I do! What shall I do!"

She hesitated only a moment. She went back, knocked on his door, and called him softly. The hammering stopped, and he came out. They walked up and down the room together for a long time.

She went to check on him in his sleep that night. He was sleeping heavily, and his shoemaking tools and unfinished work were on the table as usual.

CHAPTER XI: A Companion Picture

"Sydney," Mr. Stryver said to his assistant on that same night, or rather morning, "mix another bowl of punch. I have something to tell you."

Sydney had been working double shifts that night, the night before, and for many nights in a row, clearing out Mr. Stryver's legal papers before the start of the summer court vacation. The work was finally done. Stryver’s backlogged cases were completely caught up, leaving everything clear until November when the winter weather and new legal cases would bring work back again.

Sydney was neither more energetic nor more sober for all his hard work. It had taken extra wet towels wrapped around his head to get him through the night, preceded by a large amount of wine. He looked in very poor shape as he took his makeshift turban off and threw it into the basin of water where he had been soaking it for the last six hours.

"Are you mixing that other bowl of punch?" the portly Stryver asked, lying on his back on the sofa with his hands on his belt.

"I am."

"Now, look here! I am going to tell you something that will surprise you, and might make you think I'm not as clever as you usually believe. I plan to marry."

"Do you?"

"Yes. And not for money. What do you say to that?"

"I don't have much to say. Who is she?"

"Guess."

"Do I know her?"

"Guess."

"I am not guessing at five o'clock in the morning, with my brain fried and exhausted. If you want me to guess, you'll have to invite me to dinner."

"Well, then, I'll tell you," said Stryver, slowly sitting up. "Sydney, I despair of explaining this to you, because you are such an unfeeling dog."

"And you," Sydney replied, busy mixing the punch, "are such a sensitive and romantic soul."

"Come on!" Stryver laughed boastfully. "While I don't claim to be a romantic hero, I am still a much warmer person than _you_."

"You are luckier, if that's what you mean."

"I don't mean that. I mean I am a man of more—more—"

"Say gallantry, while you're at it," Carton suggested.

"Well, I'll say gallantry. I mean that I am a man," Stryver said, looking proudly at his friend, "who cares more about being pleasant, who takes more trouble to be pleasant, and who knows better how to behave in a woman's company than you do."

"Go on," said Sydney Carton.

"No, but before I continue," Stryver said, shaking his head in his usual bullying way, "I want to settle this with you. You've been to Doctor Manette's house as much as I have, or more. I have actually been embarrassed by your behavior there! You sit there silent and gloomy. I've been ashamed of your manners, Sydney!"

"It must be very useful for a lawyer like you to feel ashamed of anything," Sydney replied. "You should thank me."

"You won't get out of it that way," Stryver argued. "No, Sydney, it's my duty to tell you—and I tell you to your face for your own good—that you are a very unpleasant fellow in social gatherings. You are disagreeable."

Sydney drank a large glass of the punch he had made and laughed.

"Look at me!" Stryver said, squaring his shoulders. "I have less need to be polite than you do, since I am wealthier and more independent. Why do I do it?"

"I've never seen you do it yet," Carton muttered.

"I do it because it is smart business; I do it on principle. And look at me! I succeed."

"You aren't succeeding in telling me about your marriage plans," Carton answered carelessly. "I wish you would stick to that. As for me—will you never understand that I cannot be reformed?"

He asked the question with a hint of scorn.

"You have no right to be unreformed," his friend answered sharply.

"I have no right to exist at all, as far as I know," said Sydney Carton. "Who is the lady?"

"Now, don't let the name make you uncomfortable, Sydney," Mr. Stryver said, preparing him with a show of friendliness. "I know you don't mean half of what you say, and even if you did, it wouldn't matter. I say this because you once spoke of the young lady in a dismissive way."

"I did?"

"Certainly, right here in these offices."

Sydney Carton looked at his drink, then at his smug friend. He drank and looked at him again.

"You called the young lady a golden-haired doll. The lady is Miss Manette. If you were a sensitive fellow, Sydney, I might be angry with you for using such a term, but you aren't. You lack that sensitivity entirely. Therefore, I am no more annoyed by your opinion than I would be by a blind man's opinion of my painting, or a deaf man's opinion of my music."

Sydney Carton drank the punch quickly, glass after glass, staring at his friend.

"Now you know all about it, Syd," Mr. Stryver said. "I don't care about a fortune. She is a charming girl, and I have decided to please myself. On the whole, I think I can afford to please myself. In me, she will have a wealthy husband, a rapidly rising lawyer, and a man of distinction. It is a great opportunity for her, and she deserves it. Are you surprised?"

Carton, still drinking, replied, "Why should I be surprised?"

"Do you approve?"

Carton, still drinking, replied, "Why should I not approve?"

"Well!" said Stryver. "You take it more easily than I expected, and you aren't as greedy on my behalf as I thought you would be. Though you know by now that your old friend has a strong will. Yes, Sydney, I have had enough of this bachelor life. It is nice for a man to have a home to go to when he wants (and when he doesn't, he can stay away). I think Miss Manette will look good in high society and will always do me credit. So I have made up my mind. And now, Sydney, I want to give _you_ some advice about _your_ future. You are in a bad way, you know. You don't know the value of money, you live too hard, and you will ruin your health one day and end up sick and poor. You really ought to find a wife to look after you."

The smug, patronizing tone in which he spoke made him look twice as big and far more offensive.

"Let me recommend you," Stryver continued, "to face the facts. I have faced them in my own way; you should do the same. Marry. Find someone to take care of you. Don't worry that you don't understand women or have no social skills. Just find someone. Find a respectable woman with a little money—perhaps a landlady or someone who lets lodgings—and marry her to secure your future. Think about it, Sydney."

"I'll think of it," said Sydney.

CHAPTER XII: The Fellow of Delicacy

Mr. Stryver, having decided to generously offer his hand in marriage to the Doctor’s daughter, resolved to let her know of her good fortune before he left town for the summer vacation. After thinking about it, he decided it would be best to get the preliminary details out of the way. They could then decide at leisure whether to marry a week or two before the autumn court term, or during the short Christmas break.

He had no doubt about the strength of his case, viewing it as a guaranteed success. Argued on practical, worldly grounds—the only grounds he considered worth taking into account—it was a simple case with no weak spots. He viewed himself as the perfect choice, with no possible objections. The decision seemed so obvious that no jury would even need to deliberate. Stryver was entirely satisfied that it was a clear-cut case.

Consequently, Mr. Stryver decided to start his vacation by formally proposing to take Miss Manette to Vauxhall Gardens. If that failed, he would try Ranelagh Gardens. If that also failed, he would go directly to Soho and declare his feelings.

He headed toward Soho, pushing his way through the streets from the Temple. Anyone who saw him walking along the pavement, bumping past weaker people, would have seen how confident and strong he felt.

Since his route took him past Tellson’s Bank, where he kept his money, and knowing Mr. Lorry was a close friend of the Manettes, Stryver decided to stop in and share his happy news. He pushed open the stubborn door, walked down the steps past the two old cashiers, and entered the dusty back office where Mr. Lorry sat working on his ledgers.

"Hello!" said Mr. Stryver. "How are you? I hope you are well!"

Stryver had a way of seeming too big for any room. He was so large for the small office at Tellson's that the old clerks in the corners looked up in protest, as if they were being squeezed against the wall. The bank owners, reading the newspaper in the distance, looked displeased at his loud entrance.

Mr. Lorry said in a quiet, professional voice, "How do you do, Mr. Stryver? How do you do, sir?" and shook hands. He did so in the self-effacing way typical of Tellson's clerks when dealing with customers.

"Can I do anything for you, Mr. Stryver?" Mr. Lorry asked.

"No, thank you. This is a private visit, Mr. Lorry. I have come for a confidential word."

"Oh, indeed!" said Mr. Lorry, leaning forward to listen while keeping an eye on his superiors in the distance.

"I am going," Mr. Stryver said, leaning his arms on the desk so heavily that it seemed too small for him, "to offer myself in marriage to your agreeable friend, Miss Manette, Mr. Lorry."

"Oh dear me!" Mr. Lorry cried, rubbing his chin and looking at his visitor with doubt.

"Oh dear you, sir?" Stryver repeated, drawing back. "What do you mean by that, Mr. Lorry?"

"My meaning is, of course, friendly and appreciative," the banker answered. "It does you great credit, and is everything you could wish. But really, Mr. Stryver—" Mr. Lorry paused and shook his head, as if trying not to say that Stryver was simply too overbearing.

"Well!" said Stryver, slapping the desk. "If I understand you, I'll be hanged!"

Mr. Lorry adjusted his wig and bit his pen.

"Damn it all, sir!" Stryver said, staring at him. "Am I not a good match?"

"Oh, yes! You are a good match," Mr. Lorry said. "If you say so, you are."

"Am I not successful?"

"Yes, you are successful," Mr. Lorry agreed.

"And making progress in my career?"

"No one can doubt that," Mr. Lorry admitted.

"Then what on earth do you mean, Mr. Lorry?" Stryver asked, looking disappointed.

"Well, were you going there now?" Mr. Lorry asked.

"Immediately!" said Stryver, hitting the desk with his fist.

"Then I wouldn't do it if I were you."

"Why not?" Stryver asked, pointing his finger at him like a lawyer in court. "You are a businessman, so you must have a reason. Tell me why I shouldn't go."

"Because," said Mr. Lorry, "I wouldn't propose unless I had reason to believe I would be accepted."

"Damn it!" cried Stryver. "This beats everything."

Mr. Lorry looked at his superiors in the distance, then back at the angry lawyer.

"Here is a man of business, age, and experience in a bank," Stryver scoffed, "and after I list three major reasons for success, he says there is no reason at all!"

"When I speak of success, I mean success with the young lady," Mr. Lorry said, gently tapping Stryver's arm. "And when I speak of reasons for success, I mean reasons that matter to her. The young lady, my good sir, comes first."

"So you are telling me, Mr. Lorry," Stryver said, squaring his elbows, "that in your opinion, the young lady in question is a foolish girl?"

"Not at all. I am telling you, Mr. Stryver," Mr. Lorry said, reddening with anger, "that I will not hear a disrespectful word about that young lady from anyone. If any man spoke disrespectfully of her at this desk, not even my job at Tellson’s would stop me from telling him what I think of him."

Both men were now red-faced and angry, trying to keep their voices quiet in the bank.

"That is what I mean to tell you, sir," said Mr. Lorry. "Please make no mistake about it."

Mr. Stryver chewed on a ruler for a moment, tap-tapping it against his teeth in the awkward silence. Finally, he said:

"This is new to me, Mr. Lorry. You are advising me not to go to Soho and offer myself—Stryver of the King's Bench?"

"Did you ask for my advice, Mr. Stryver?"

"Yes, I did."

"Very well. Then I give it, and you have repeated it correctly."

"And all I can say," Stryver laughed bitterly, "is that this beats everything."

"Now understand me," Mr. Lorry continued. "As a businessman, I know nothing of this matter. But as an old friend who has known Miss Lucie since she was a child, and who cares deeply for her and her father, I have spoken. I did not seek this confidence, remember. Do you still think I am wrong?"

"Not at all!" Stryver said, whistling. "I can't force others to have common sense; I can only have it myself. I assumed the family had sense; you say they don't. I'm sure you are right."

"What I believe, Mr. Stryver, I will define for myself," said Mr. Lorry, flushing again. "I won't have anyone else define it for me."

"There! I beg your pardon!" said Stryver.

"Apology accepted. Thank you. Now, Mr. Stryver, I was going to say: it would be painful for you to be rejected, painful for Doctor Manette to have to tell you, and painful for Lucie to reject you. You know my close relationship with the family. If you like, without committing you to anything, I will go to Soho tonight, observe the situation, and let you know what I find. It might spare everyone an uncomfortable scene. What do you say?"

"How long would I have to wait?"

"Only a few hours. I can go to Soho this evening and come to your offices afterward."

"Then I agree," said Stryver. "I won't go there now. I will expect you tonight. Good morning."

Stryver turned and stormed out of the bank, leaving a draft of air that required the old clerks to hold onto their desks.

The lawyer was smart enough to realize that the banker would not have spoken so strongly without being certain. Though he hated to swallow this pill, he accepted it. "And now," Mr. Stryver said to himself, looking toward the Temple, "my way out of this is to put them all in the wrong."

It was a tactic he often used in court. "You won't reject me, young lady," Mr. Stryver thought. "I will reject you first."

Consequently, when Mr. Lorry arrived at his offices at ten o'clock that night, Mr. Stryver was surrounded by books and papers, acting as if he had entirely forgotten the morning's conversation. He even acted surprised to see Mr. Lorry, behaving in a distracted and busy manner.

"Well," the good-natured Mr. Lorry said after thirty minutes of trying to bring up the subject. "I have been to Soho."

"To Soho?" Mr. Stryver repeated coldly. "Oh, of course! What was I thinking!"

"And I have no doubt," Mr. Lorry said, "that I was right. My opinion is confirmed, and I repeat my advice."

"I assure you," Mr. Stryver replied in a friendly tone, "that I am sorry to hear it on your account, and on her father’s account. I know this must be a sensitive subject for the family; let us say no more about it."

"I don't understand you," said Mr. Lorry.

"I'm sure you don't," Stryver said, nodding dismissively. "No matter, no matter."

"But it does matter," Mr. Lorry urged.

"No, it doesn't. Having assumed there was good sense and healthy ambition where there is none, I am glad to be corrected. No harm is done. Young women have made similar foolish decisions before and regretted them in poverty. On one hand, I am sorry it is over, because it would have been a good thing for them; on the other hand, I am glad, because it would have been a burden to me. I would have gained nothing from it. No harm is done. I have not proposed, and on reflection, I'm not sure I ever would have. Mr. Lorry, you cannot control the vanity and silliness of empty-headed girls; you must expect to be disappointed. Let's say no more. I regret it for their sake, but I am satisfied for my own. Thank you for giving me your advice; you know the young lady better than I do. You were right, it never would have worked."

Mr. Lorry was so surprised that he stared at Stryver in disbelief as the lawyer politely pushed him toward the door, acting as if he were showing great generosity and goodwill toward him.

"Make the best of it, my dear sir," said Stryver. "Say no more about it. Thank you again, and good night!"

Mr. Lorry found himself out in the street before he realized what had happened. Mr. Stryver lay back on his sofa, winking at the ceiling.

CHAPTER XIII: The Fellow of No Delicacy

If Sydney Carton ever showed any spark of his former self, he certainly never did so at the home of Doctor Manette. He had visited frequently over the past year, but had always been the same gloomy and depressed lounger. When he chose to talk, he spoke well; but his habit of caring about nothing, which cast a dark shadow over him, was rarely broken by his inner intelligence.

Yet he did care for the streets around that house, and for the very stones of the pavement. Many a night he wandered there unhappily, when wine failed to bring even temporary cheer. Many a gloomy dawn found his solitary figure lingering there, even when the first rays of the sun lit up the church spires and tall buildings. Perhaps these quiet hours brought back memories of better things, now forgotten and out of reach. Lately, he rarely slept in his bed in the Temple, often getting up after only a few minutes to wander around Soho.

On a day in August, after Mr. Stryver had informed his assistant that "he had changed his mind about marrying," taking his blunt manners to Devonshire, Sydney's feet still walked those streets. The sight and smell of flowers in the city streets brought a touch of joy to the poorest, health to the sick, and a feeling of youth to the old. Carton's aimless steps eventually led him to the Doctor’s door.

He was shown upstairs and found Lucie working alone. She had never felt quite comfortable around him, and received him with some embarrassment as he sat near her table. But looking at his face as they exchanged greetings, she noticed a change in him.

"I fear you are not well, Mr. Carton!"

"No. But the life I lead, Miss Manette, is not healthy. What else can be expected of a man like me?"

"Is it not—forgive me for asking—a pity to live such a life?"

"God knows it is a shame!"

"Then why not change it?"

Looking at him gently, she was surprised and saddened to see tears in his eyes. His voice trembled as he answered:

"It is too late for that. I will never be better than I am. I will only sink lower and get worse."

He rested his elbow on the table and covered his eyes with his hand. The table shook in the silence that followed.

She had never seen him show such feeling and was deeply distressed. Sensing her concern without looking up, he said:

"Please forgive me, Miss Manette. I break down when I try to say what I want to tell you. Will you listen to me?"

"If it will help you, Mr. Carton, or make you happier, I would gladly listen!"

"God bless you for your sweet compassion!"

He uncovered his face after a moment and spoke steadily.

"Don't be afraid to listen. Don't shrink from what I say. I am like a man who died young. My life might have been so much more."

"No, Mr. Carton. I am sure the best part of your life is still ahead of you. You could be much more worthy of yourself."

"You mean worthy of you, Miss Manette. Although I know that is impossible, I will never forget that you said so!"

She was pale and trembling. He spoke with a quiet despair that made the conversation unlike any other.

"If it had been possible, Miss Manette, for you to return the love of the man before you—wasted, drunk, and ruined as you know him to be—he would have known, even in his happiness, that he would bring you only misery, sorrow, and disgrace. I know you cannot love me. I ask for no love, and I am even glad that it is impossible."

"Without love, can I not help you, Mr. Carton? Can I not guide you to a better path? Can I repay your trust in no way? I know you would tell this to no one else," she said tearfully. "Can I do nothing for you?"

He shook his head.

"Nothing. No, Miss Manette, nothing. If you will listen to me for just a moment more, you will have done all you can do. I want you to know that you have been the last dream of my soul. In my ruined life, the sight of you with your father in this beautiful home has stirred old hopes I thought were dead. Since I met you, I have felt a remorse I thought I would never feel again, and have heard old voices telling me to improve. I have had ideas of trying again, shaking off my laziness, and fighting the battle I had given up. It was a dream that ends in nothing, leaving me where I fell, but I want you to know that you inspired it."

"Will nothing of it remain? Oh, Mr. Carton, think again! Try again!"

"No, Miss Manette. I have always known I am unworthy. Yet I was weak enough to want you to know that you turned this heap of ashes into a fire—a fire that does no good and simply burns itself out."

"Since it is my misfortune, Mr. Carton, to have made you more unhappy than before—"

"Don't say that, Miss Manette. You would have saved me if anything could. You will not make me worse."

"Since your feelings are due to my influence, can I not use that influence to help you? Do I have no power for good with you at all?"

"The only good I am capable of now, Miss Manette, I have come to do today. Let me carry through the rest of my life the memory of opening my heart to you, and knowing there was something in me you could pity."

"Which I begged you to believe was capable of better things, Mr. Carton!"

"Do not ask me to believe that, Miss Manette. I know myself too well. I am upsetting you, and I must finish. Will you allow me to believe, when I remember this day, that the last confidence of my life was placed in your pure keeping, and will be shared with no one else?"

"If that brings you comfort, yes."

"Not even by the person you will one day love most?"

"Mr. Carton," she answered after a pause, "the secret is yours, not mine, and I promise to keep it."

"Thank you. And again, God bless you."

He kissed her hand and moved toward the door.

"Do not worry, Miss Manette, that I will ever bring this up again. I will never refer to it. It is as secure as if I were dead. In my last hour, I will cherish the memory that my last honest confession was made to you, and that my name and faults were carried gently in your heart. I hope your life is bright and happy!"

He was so different from his usual self, and it was so sad to think of his wasted talents, that Lucie wept for him as he looked back.

"Be comforted!" he said. "I am not worth your tears, Miss Manette. In an hour, the low companions and bad habits I hate but yield to will make me less worthy of your tears than any beggar on the street. Be comforted! But in my heart, I will always be toward you what I am now. My last request but one is that you believe this."

"I will, Mr. Carton."

"My last request of all is this: I will now leave you, a visitor with whom you have nothing in common. It is useless to say, but it comes from my soul: for you, and for anyone dear to you, I would do anything. If my life had any value or opportunity for sacrifice, I would gladly sacrifice it for you and those you love. Remember me as sincere in this. The time will come when new ties will bind you tenderly to your home—the dearest ties you will ever know. Oh, Miss Manette, when you look at a happy child's face, or see your own beauty reflected in your children, remember that there is a man who would gladly give his life to keep the life you love beside you!"

He said, "Farewell!" and left her.

CHAPTER XIV: The Honest Tradesman

To Jeremiah Cruncher, sitting on his stool in Fleet Street with his young son beside him, a vast number of moving things were presented every day. Anyone sitting on Fleet Street during the busy hours would be dazed by the two massive crowds of people—one moving west with the sun, the other moving east from it.

With his piece of straw in his mouth, Mr. Cruncher sat watching these two streams. He had no expectation that they would ever run dry. A small part of his income came from guiding nervous, elderly women across the busy street from Tellson’s Bank. Brief as these encounters were, Mr. Cruncher always took a strong interest in the ladies, wishing he could drink to their health. It was from the tips they gave him for this help that he supported himself, as just noted.

In the old days, poets sat in public places to think and write. Mr. Cruncher, sitting on his stool in public, was no poet; he thought as little as possible and simply watched his surroundings.

He was sitting there during a quiet season when crowds were small, tips were few, and his business was doing poorly. This led him to suspect that Mrs. Cruncher must have been praying against him. Suddenly, a large crowd moving west down Fleet Street caught his attention. Looking closer, Mr. Cruncher saw a funeral procession approaching, and the crowd was shouting and protesting angrily.

"Young Jerry," said Mr. Cruncher to his son, "it’s a burial."

"Hooray, father!" cried Young Jerry.

The young boy shouted this happily. His father took the shout so poorly that he immediately slapped his son on the ear.

"What do you mean? What are you shouting for? What are you trying to tell your own father, you little rascal? This boy is getting to be too much for _me_!" said Mr. Cruncher, looking at him. "Him and his hoorays! Don't let me hear that again, or you'll get another slap. Do you hear?"

"I wasn't doing any harm," Young Jerry protested, rubbing his cheek.

"Quiet then," said Mr. Cruncher. "I won't have any of your 'no harms.' Stand up on that seat and watch the crowd."

His son obeyed, and the crowd came closer. They were shouting and hissing around a dusty hearse and a mourning carriage. Inside the carriage sat a single mourner dressed in black. He looked very uncomfortable as the mob surrounded him, mocking him, making faces, and shouting: "Yah! Spies! Yah! Spies!" along with many other insults.

Funerals always attracted Mr. Cruncher. He became excited whenever one passed Tellson's Bank. Naturally, a funeral surrounded by a mob excited him greatly. He asked the first man who bumped into him:

"What is it, brother? What’s happening?"

"I don’t know," said the man. "Spies! Yah! Spies!"

He asked another man. "Who is it?"

"I don’t know," the man replied, shouting anyway. "Spies! Yah! Spies!"

Finally, a man who knew more bumped into him, and Jerry learned that it was the funeral of Roger Cly.

"Was he a spy?" asked Mr. Cruncher.

"An Old Bailey spy," the man replied. "Yah! Spies!"

"Why, of course!" Jerry exclaimed, remembering the trial he had watched. "I’ve seen him. Is he dead?"

"Dead as mutton," the other replied, "and can’t be dead enough. Pull them out! Spies!"

The crowd caught onto this idea quickly and began mobbing the carriage, forcing it to stop. When they opened the doors, the single mourner scrambled out and was caught by the crowd for a moment. But he was quick and managed to slip away down a side street, shedding his black cloak, hat, and handkerchief to escape.

The people tore his clothes to pieces with great enjoyment. Meanwhile, the local shopkeepers quickly closed their shops, fearing the mob. The crowd had already opened the hearse to pull out the coffin when someone suggested they escort it to the cemetery in celebration. This suggestion was welcomed, and the carriage was immediately filled with people inside and out, while others climbed onto the roof of the hearse. Among the first volunteers was Jerry Cruncher himself, who hid his spiky head in the corner of the carriage to avoid being seen by anyone from Tellson's Bank.

The undertakers protested, but when several voices suggested throwing them into the nearby river to cool them down, their protests stopped.
