The procession started again, with a chimney sweep driving the hearse and a pie seller driving the carriage. A man leading a mangy black bear joined in, adding to the spectacle.

Drinking beer, smoking pipes, and singing songs, the rowdy crowd went on its way, with shops closing ahead of them. They finally reached the old St. Pancras churchyard in the fields. The mob poured into the cemetery and buried Roger Cly in their own rowdy way.

With the funeral over, the crowd looked for other amusement. Someone suggested accusing random passersby of being spies. They chased and beat several innocent people who had never been near the courthouse. Soon they began breaking windows and looting taverns. Finally, after several hours of rioting, a rumor spread that the Guards were coming. At this, the crowd quickly dispersed, as mobs usually did.

Mr. Cruncher did not join the rioting but stayed behind in the churchyard to talk with the undertakers. The quiet cemetery seemed to soothe him. He got a pipe from a nearby tavern and smoked it, studying the grave site.

"Jerry," Mr. Cruncher said to himself, "you saw that Cly today, and you saw with your own eyes that he was a young, strongly built man."

After finishing his pipe, he headed back to his post at Tellson’s Bank before closing time. On his way, he stopped to speak with his medical adviser, a well-known surgeon.

Young Jerry relieved his father, reporting that no jobs had come in. The bank closed, the old clerks left, and Mr. Cruncher and his son went home for tea.

"I tell you what!" Mr. Cruncher said to his wife as they entered. "If my work goes wrong tonight, I will know you’ve been praying against me, and I will punish you for it."

His wife shook her head sadly.

"Why, you’re doing it right in front of me!" Mr. Cruncher cried.

"I am saying nothing, Jerry."

"Well, don't think anything either. Thinking is the same as praying. You are trying to work against me. Stop it altogether."

"Yes, Jerry."

"Yes, Jerry," Mr. Cruncher repeated as he sat down. "You better say yes, Jerry."

Mr. Cruncher muttered as he ate his bread and butter, looking dissatisfied.

"Are you going out tonight?" his wife asked.

"Yes, I am."

"May I go with you, father?" his son asked.

"No, you may not. I am going—as your mother knows—fishing. That's where I'm going. Fishing."

"Your fishing rod gets rather rusty, doesn't it, father?"

"Never you mind."

"Will you bring any fish home?"

"If I don't, you won't have much to eat tomorrow," his father replied. "That's enough questions. I won't go out until you are asleep."

He spent the rest of the evening watching Mrs. Cruncher closely and talking to her so she couldn't pray. He had his son talk to her too, keeping her busy with complaints rather than leaving her to her thoughts. He showed great respect for the power of prayer by how much he feared her doing it.

"And remember!" Mr. Cruncher said. "No games tomorrow! If I succeed in bringing home some meat, you will eat it. If I bring home beer, you won't drink water. When you're in Rome, do as the Romans do. I am your Rome."

Then he complained:

"You are praying against our food and drink! I don't know how much food you've cost us with your praying. Look at your boy: he is as thin as a rail. Do you call yourself a mother, and not feed your own son?"

This touched Young Jerry, who urged his mother to feed him as his father suggested.

The evening passed, and Young Jerry and his mother were sent to bed. Mr. Cruncher smoked his pipe in silence until nearly one o'clock. At that late hour, he stood up, took a key from his pocket, opened a locked cupboard, and took out a sack, a crowbar, a rope, and a chain. Placing these tools in his pockets, he gave his wife a final warning, put out the light, and went out.

Young Jerry, who had only pretended to undress, followed his father. Under cover of the dark, he followed him down the stairs and out into the street. He knew he could get back in easily because the building was full of renters and the front door was left unlocked.

Wanting to learn his father's trade, Young Jerry kept close to the walls and followed him. His father walked north and was soon joined by another man carrying similar tools.

Within thirty minutes, they had walked past the street lamps and watchmen into the countryside. They met a third man silently along the road.

The three men walked on, with Young Jerry following, until they stopped near a brick wall with an iron railing. They turned up a dark lane beside the wall. Peeping around the corner under the moonlight, Young Jerry saw his father climb over the iron gate. The other two followed him. They dropped quietly inside and began crawling on their hands and knees.

Young Jerry approached the gate, holding his breath. Looking through the bars, he saw the three men creeping through the tall grass of a large cemetery. The gravestones looked like white ghosts, and the church tower looked like a giant monster. The men stopped and stood up, and then began to dig.

They used a spade at first. Then his father began using a tool like a large corkscrew. They worked hard in the dark. Suddenly, the church clock struck the hour, terrifying Young Jerry so much that he ran away, his hair standing on end.

But his curiosity stopped him and drew him back. He peeped through the gate again. The men seemed to have hooked something heavy. They strained and pulled, and slowly a coffin emerged from the earth. Young Jerry knew what it was, but seeing his father prepare to pry it open terrified him, and he ran all the way home.

He ran as fast as he could, fearing the coffin was chasing him down the street, hopping on its end. He imagined it hiding in doorways and shadows, ready to grab him. When he reached his door, he was half dead with fear. The nightmare followed him to bed, weighing heavily on his chest as he slept.

He was awakened after dawn by his father returning. Something had gone wrong, as his father was holding Mrs. Cruncher by her ears and hitting her head against the wooden headboard of the bed.

"I told you I would, and I did," Mr. Cruncher growled.

"Jerry, Jerry, Jerry!" his wife begged.

"You are praying against my business," Jerry said, "and making me and my partners suffer. You promised to honor and obey me; why don't you?"

"I try to be a good wife, Jerry," she cried.

"Is it being a good wife to oppose your husband's work? Is it honoring me to ruin my business?"

"You didn't do this terrible work when we married, Jerry."

"That is none of your business," Jerry retorted. "You are the wife of an honest tradesman. A good wife would leave my work alone. You call yourself religious? I'd rather have an irreligious wife! You have no more sense of duty than the riverbed, and I have to beat it into you."

They argued in low voices. Finally, the messenger kicked off his muddy boots and lay down on the floor to sleep. His son watched him for a moment before falling back asleep himself.

There was no food for breakfast. Mr. Cruncher was in a terrible mood, keeping a heavy pot lid nearby to throw at his wife if she tried to say grace. He washed and dressed, and set off with his son for work.

Young Jerry, carrying the stool under his arm beside his father in the sunny street, felt very different from the terrified boy of the night before. His fears were gone with the daylight.

"Father," Young Jerry said as they walked, keeping his distance behind the stool, "what is a Resurrection-Man?"

Mr. Cruncher stopped on the pavement. "How should I know?"

"I thought you knew everything, father," said the boy. "Well," Mr. Cruncher replied, walking again and lifting his hat, "he is a tradesman."

"What does he sell, father?"

"His goods," said Mr. Cruncher, thinking it over, "are a branch of scientific goods."

"People's bodies, right, father?" the boy asked.

"I believe it is something like that," Mr. Cruncher said.

"Oh, father, I want to be a Resurrection-Man when I grow up!"

Mr. Cruncher was pleased but shook his head in a serious way. "It depends on how you develop your talents. Be careful, don't talk too much, and you might do well." As Young Jerry walked ahead to set up the stool near Temple Bar, Mr. Cruncher muttered to himself: "Jerry, you honest tradesman, there is hope that this boy will be a blessing to you, and make up for his mother!"

CHAPTER XV: Knitting

Drinking began earlier than usual in Monsieur Defarge's wine shop. As early as six o’clock in the morning, pale faces looking through the barred windows could see other customers inside, bending over their wine glasses. Defarge sold a very cheap, thin wine at the best of times, but the wine he served now seemed unusually sour and thin. It had a gloomy effect on the mood of those who drank it. No cheerful laughter came from the grapes of Monsieur Defarge; instead, a quiet, smoldering anger lay hidden in the dregs.

This was the third morning in a row that early drinking had occurred at the shop, starting on Monday and continuing to Wednesday. However, there was more quiet plotting than drinking. Many men who had no money to buy wine came in to whisper and listen. They were just as interested in the place as if they could afford whole barrels, moving from seat to seat, eagerly absorbing every word.

Despite the crowd, the master of the wine shop was nowhere to be seen. He was not missed, as no one looked or asked for him. Everyone was used to seeing Madame Defarge sitting at the counter alone, presiding over the wine sales. In front of her sat a bowl of worn, battered coins, which were as damaged and defaced as the poor pockets they had come from.

A sense of suspense and distraction filled the room, which might have been noticed by any spies looking in. Card games were neglected, domino players built towers with their tiles, and drinkers drew shapes on the tables with spilled wine. Madame Defarge herself picked at her sleeve with a toothpick, looking as if she were seeing and hearing things far away.

This was the state of the Saint Antoine neighborhood until midday. At noon, two dusty men walked under the street lamps: Monsieur Defarge and a road mender wearing a blue cap. Covered in dust and thirsty, they entered the wine shop. Their arrival caused a stir in Saint Antoine, with faces appearing at doors and windows as they walked by. Yet no one followed them inside, and no one spoke when they entered, though all eyes turned toward them.

"Good day, gentlemen!" said Monsieur Defarge.

This signal loosened the tongues in the room, eliciting a chorus of "Good day!"

"It is bad weather, gentlemen," said Defarge, shaking his head.

At this, every man looked at his neighbor, then looked down and sat in silence. One man got up and walked out.

"My wife," Defarge said aloud, addressing Madame Defarge. "I have traveled some distance with this good road mender, named Jacques. I met him by accident a day and a half's journey outside Paris. He is a good fellow, this road mender named Jacques. Give him a drink, wife!"

A second man stood up and left. Madame Defarge poured wine for the road mender, who tipped his blue cap to the room and drank. He took some dark bread from his shirt and ate it, sitting near Madame Defarge's counter. A third man stood up and walked out.

Defarge drank a glass of wine himself—taking less than the guest, since wine was no luxury to him—and waited for the countryman to finish his breakfast. He looked at no one, and no one looked at him, not even Madame Defarge, who was busy with her knitting.

"Have you finished your meal, friend?" Defarge asked.

"Yes, thank you."

"Come then! I will show you the room I told you about. It will suit you perfectly."

They walked out of the wine shop into the street, through a courtyard, up a dark staircase, and into an attic—the very attic where a white-haired shoemaker had once sat working on his bench.

No shoemaker was there now. Instead, the three men who had left the wine shop one by one were waiting. They and the white-haired man were connected by one thing: they had all looked at him through the cracks in the attic wall.

Defarge closed the door carefully and spoke quietly:

"Jacques One, Jacques Two, Jacques Three! This is the witness I met by agreement, I, Jacques Four. He will tell you everything. Speak, Jacques Five!"

The road mender, blue cap in hand, wiped his forehead and said, "Where should I start, monsieur?"

"Start at the beginning," Defarge replied.

"I saw him, messieurs," began the road mender, "a year ago during the summer, hanging by the chain under the Marquis's carriage. It happened like this: I had stopped working on the road at sunset, and as the Marquis's carriage slowly climbed the hill, he was hanging by the chain—like this."

The road mender acted out the scene again. He was very good at it, since it had been the main story in his village for a whole year.

Jacques One asked if he had ever seen the man before.

"Never," answered the road mender.

Jacques Three asked how he recognized him later.

"By his height," the road mender whispered, putting his finger to his nose. "When the Marquis asked me that evening, 'What does he look like?' I answered, 'Tall as a ghost.'"

"You should have said short as a dwarf," Jacques Two remarked.

"But how was I to know? The deed had not been done yet, and he had not told me anything. Even then, I did not volunteer to speak. The Marquis pointed his finger at me by the fountain and ordered his men to bring me to him. I had no choice, messieurs."

"He is right," Defarge muttered to the man who had interrupted. "Go on!"

"Good!" said the road mender. "The tall man disappeared, and they searched for him for months. Was it nine, ten, eleven months?"

"It doesn't matter how long," said Defarge. "He hid well, but at last they found him. Go on!"

"I was working on the hillside again at sunset. I was gathering my tools to go home, where it was already dark, when I looked up and saw six soldiers coming over the hill. In the middle of them was the tall man, his arms tied to his sides—like this!"

Using his blue cap, he showed how the man's elbows were tied tightly behind him with knotted ropes.

"I stood by my heap of stones to watch them pass, as it is a quiet road where any spectacle is worth watching. At first, as they approached, I only saw six soldiers and the bound man. They looked black against the setting sun, which gave them a red edge, messieurs. Their long shadows stretched like giants across the hill. They were covered in dust, marching tramp, tramp! But when they came close, I recognized the tall man, and he recognized me. Ah! He would have gladly thrown himself over the hillside again, as he did the first night we met near that very spot!"

He described it vividly, as if he were still watching it.

"I did not show the soldiers that I knew him, and he did not show them that he knew me. We spoke only with our eyes. 'Come on!' the sergeant said, pointing toward the village. 'Bring him to his grave!' and they marched faster. I followed them. His arms were swollen from the tight ropes, his wooden shoes were clumsy, and he was limping. Because he was slow, the soldiers hit him with their gunstocks—like this!"

He acted out a man being pushed forward by musket butts.

"As they ran down the hill like madmen, he fell. They laughed and pulled him up. His face was bleeding and dusty, but he couldn't wipe it because his hands were tied. The soldiers laughed again. They brought him into the village, and everyone ran to see. They took him past the mill and up to the prison. Everyone saw the prison gates open in the dark and swallow him up—like this!"

He opened his mouth wide and snapped his teeth shut. Seeing he wanted to keep his mouth closed for effect, Defarge said, "Go on, Jacques."

"All the villagers went home," the road mender whispered on tiptoe. "Everyone whispered by the fountain, then went to sleep and dreamed of that poor man locked in the prison on the cliff, never to come out alive. In the morning, with my tools on my shoulder, eating my bread, I walked past the prison. I saw him high up, behind the iron bars of a cage, still bloody and dusty, looking out. He could not wave because his hands were tied. I did not dare call out to him. He looked at me like a dead man."

Defarge and the other three men looked at one another with dark, vengeful expressions. They looked like a secret court. Jacques One and Two sat on the bed, chin in hand, watching the road mender. Jacques Three nervously twitched his fingers near his mouth. Defarge stood between them and the witness in the light of the window.

"Go on, Jacques," Defarge said.

"He stayed in that iron cage for several days. The villagers watched him secretly, fearing the soldiers. But everyone looked up at the prison from a distance. In the evening, when they gathered by the fountain, all faces turned toward the prison instead of the post house. They whispered that he might not be executed. They said petitions had been sent to Paris, explaining that he had gone mad from the death of his child, and that a petition had been given to the King himself. I don't know if it's true. Perhaps yes, perhaps no."

"Listen, Jacques," Jacques One said sternly. "A petition was indeed given to the King and Queen. Everyone here, except you, saw the King take it from his carriage in the street. It was Defarge who ran out before the horses at the risk of his life to hand it to him."

"And listen, Jacques!" the kneeling Jacques Three added, his fingers twitching greedily. "The guards surrounded him and beat him. Do you hear?"

"I hear, messieurs."

"Go on then," said Defarge.

"But on the other hand," the road mender continued, "they whisper by the fountain that he will be executed in our village. They say that because he killed the Marquis, who was the lord of the land, he will be executed as a parent-killer. An old man said that his right hand, which held the knife, will be burned off before his eyes. They will pour boiling oil, melted lead, and sulfur into his wounds, and then tear him apart with four horses. The old man said this was done to Damiens, the man who tried to kill King Louis XV. But I don't know if it's true; I am not educated."

"Listen, Jacques!" the nervous Jacques Three said. "The man was named Damiens, and it was done in broad daylight in the streets of Paris. The wealthy ladies of the court watched the execution until nightfall, when the man was still breathing after losing his limbs! It happened when you were ten years old; you could have seen it."

"Enough!" Defarge said impatiently. "Go on."

"Well, some say this and some say that. On Sunday night, when the village was asleep, the soldiers came down from the prison. Their guns clinked in the street. Workmen dug and hammered while the soldiers sang. In the morning, a gallows forty feet high was standing by the fountain, shadowing the water."

The road mender pointed toward the ceiling as if he could see the gallows in the sky.

"All work stopped, and everyone gathered there, even the cows. At noon, the drums rolled. The soldiers brought him out, bound and gagged with a tight string, making him look like he was laughing." The road mender pulled his mouth back with his thumbs to show them. "At the top of the gallows was the knife, blade up. He was hanged forty feet in the air, left hanging to poison the water."

They looked at one another in silence as the road mender wiped his sweaty face with his blue cap.

"It is terrible, messieurs. How can the women draw water now? Who can sit by the fountain under that shadow? When I left the village on Monday evening and looked back from the hill, the shadow of the gallows stretched across the church, the mill, the prison—across the whole earth!"

Jacques Three chewed on his fingers, his hand shaking.

"That is all, messieurs. I left at sunset as I was told, walked all night and half the next day, until I met this comrade. We rode and walked here, and here I am."

After a quiet pause, Jacques One said, "Good! You have told your story well. Wait for us outside the door."

"Gladly," said the road mender. Defarge led him to the stairs and returned to the attic.

The three men stood with their heads together.

"What do you say, Jacques?" Number One asked. "Should they be registered?"

"To be registered for destruction," Defarge replied.

"Excellent!" Jacques Three croaked.

"The castle and the whole family?" the first asked.

"The castle and the whole family," Defarge answered. "Extermination."

Jacques Three croaked happily, "Excellent!" and chewed on another finger.

"Are you sure," Jacques Two asked Defarge, "that there is no danger in how we keep the record? It is safe because no one else can read it, but will we always be able to read it—or rather, will she?"

"Jacques," Defarge said proudly, "if my wife kept the record in her head alone, she would never lose a word of it. Knitted into her own stitches and symbols, it will always be clear to her. Trust Madame Defarge. It would be easier for a man to erase himself from existence than to erase a single letter from her knitted register."

They murmured in agreement, and Jacques Three asked, "Should we send the countryman back soon? He is very simple; is he dangerous?"

"He knows nothing," said Defarge. "Only enough to get himself hanged if he talks. I will take care of him and set him on his way. He wants to see the King, Queen, and court. I will take him to Versailles on Sunday."

"What?" Jacques Three asked in surprise. "Is it a good sign that he wants to see royalty?"

"Jacques," Defarge explained, "if you want a cat to catch mice, show her milk first. Show a dog his prey if you want him to hunt it down one day."

Nothing more was said. They found the road mender asleep on the stairs and told him to rest on the bed. He fell asleep immediately.

For a poor peasant, Defarge’s shop was a comfortable place to stay. Aside from his fear of Madame Defarge, his time in Paris was very pleasant. Madame sat at her counter all day, acting as if he weren't there, which made him shake in his wooden shoes whenever she looked at him. He felt that if she decided to accuse him of a crime, she would carry it through to the end.

Therefore, when Sunday came, the road mender pretended to be happy to go to Versailles with the Defarges. It was uncomfortable to have madame knitting in the public carriage all the way, and even more uncomfortable to have her knitting in the crowd as they waited to see the King and Queen.

"You work hard, madame," a man nearby remarked.

"Yes," Madame Defarge replied. "I have a lot to do."

"What are you making?"

"Many things."

"For example?"

"For example," she replied calmly, "shrouds."

The man moved away quickly, and the road mender fanned himself with his cap, feeling the heat. Soon the King and Queen arrived in their golden coach, surrounded by their wealthy court of lords and ladies. The road mender was so excited by the jewels, silks, and grandeur that he shouted: "Long live the King! Long live the Queen! Long live everyone!" as if he had never heard of the Jacques movement. He wept with excitement at the gardens, fountains, and royalty. During the three hours of the display, he shouted and cried with the crowd. Defarge held him by the collar the whole time, keeping him from getting too close to the carriage.

"Well done!" Defarge said, clapping him on the back afterward. "You are a good boy!"

The road mender began to worry if he had made a mistake by cheering the King.

"You are exactly the kind of man we need," Defarge whispered. "You make these fools believe their system will last forever. That makes them more insolent, and brings the end closer."

"Ah!" said the road mender. "That is true."

"These fools know nothing. They despise you and would kill you before they killed one of their horses, but they only believe what they see. Let them be deceived a little longer."

Madame Defarge looked at him and nodded.

"As for you," she said, "you would cheer for anything that makes a noise. Wouldn't you?"

"Yes, madame. For the moment."

"If you were shown a pile of dolls and told to tear them to pieces for your own profit, you would choose the finest ones, wouldn't you?"

"Yes, madame."

"And if you were shown a flock of fine birds unable to fly and told to pluck their feathers, you would choose the best ones, wouldn't you?"

"That is true, madame."

"You have seen both dolls and birds today," Madame Defarge said, pointing toward the palace. "Now, go home!"

CHAPTER XVI: Still Knitting

Madame Defarge and her husband returned to the Saint Antoine neighborhood, while the road mender in his blue cap walked back through the dark and dusty miles toward the castle of the late Marquis. The stone faces on the castle walls had plenty of time now to listen to the wind and the fountain. The few starving peasants who wandered near the courtyard to search for herbs or wood thought the expressions on the stone faces had changed. A faint rumor existed in the village that when the Marquis was killed, the stone faces changed from expressions of pride to anger and pain; and when Gaspard was hanged forty feet above the fountain, they changed again to a cruel look of being avenged. On the stone face over the bedroom window where the murder occurred, two nostrils seemed pinched in a way no one had noticed before. Whenever a few peasants sneaked a look at the stone face, they quickly ran back into the woods like frightened rabbits.

The castle and the huts, the stone faces and the hanging body, the bloodstains and the village well—all of France lay under the night sky, looking like a tiny speck from a distance. Just as human knowledge can analyze a ray of light, higher minds could read in the earth's light every thought and action of the people on it.

The Defarges returned in a public carriage to the gates of Paris under the stars. The carriage was stopped at the guardhouse for the usual inspection. Defarge got out, greeting a few soldiers he knew, and warmly hugging a policeman he was friendly with.

As they walked through the dark mud of Saint Antoine toward their shop, Madame Defarge spoke to her husband:

"Tell me, my friend, what did the policeman tell you?"

"Very little tonight, but all he knows. There is a new spy assigned to our neighborhood. There might be more, but he only knows of one."

"Well," Madame Defarge said with a cool, business-like look, "we must register him. What is his name?"

"He is English."

"So much the better. His name?"

"Barsad," said Defarge, pronouncing it in a French way, though he spelled it correctly for her.

"Barsad," madame repeated. "Good. First name?"

"John."

"John Barsad," madame repeated. "Good. What does he look like?"

"About forty years old; about five feet nine inches tall; black hair; dark skin; generally handsome; thin, long, pale face with dark eyes; his nose is hooked but bends toward his left cheek, giving him a sinister expression."

"Well, that is a detailed portrait!" madame laughed. "He will be registered tomorrow."

They entered their closed shop at midnight. Madame Defarge immediately went to her desk, counted the money taken in their absence, checked the inventory, and went over the ledger entries. She checked the servant's work in every way before sending him to bed. Then she poured the coins out again and began tying them into her handkerchief in a chain of separate knots for safekeeping through the night. Defarge smoked his pipe and walked up and down, admiring her work without interfering, which was his habit in both business and family life.

The night was hot, and the closed shop smelled of the dirty neighborhood, mixed with the strong smell of wine, rum, and brandy. Defarge put down his pipe and fanned the air.

"You are tired," madame said, looking up from her knots. "It is just the usual smells."

"I am a little tired," her husband admitted.

"And a little depressed too," said madame, whose quick eyes always watched him. "Oh, you men!"

"But my dear!" Defarge began.

"But my dear!" madame repeated firmly. "You are weak-hearted tonight, my dear!"

"Well," Defarge said, sighing, "it _is_ taking a long time."

"It takes a long time," his wife repeated. "Vengeance always takes a long time; that is the rule."

"It doesn't take long for lightning to strike," said Defarge.

"How long," madame asked calmly, "does it take to make and store the lightning? Tell me."

Defarge looked up thoughtfully.

"It doesn't take long," madame said, "for an earthquake to destroy a town. But tell me how long it takes to prepare the earthquake?"

"A long time, I suppose," said Defarge.

"But when it is ready, it happens and crushes everything. Meanwhile, it is always preparing, even if it is quiet. Remember that."

She tied a tight knot as if strangling an enemy.

"I tell you," madame said, waving her hand, "that although it is taking a long time, the revolution is coming. It never stops or retreats. Look at the anger and discontent growing every day. Can the nobles last? Bah! I laugh at your doubts."

"My brave wife," Defarge said, standing before her with his hands behind his back like an attentive student, "I do not doubt it. But it has lasted a long time, and it is possible that the victory won't come during our lives."

"Well, what of it?" madame asked, tying another tight knot.

"Well," Defarge said, shrugging, "we won't see the victory."

"We will have helped bring it about," madame replied, waving her hand. "Nothing we do is in vain. I believe we will see the victory. But even if we don't, if I were shown a tyrant's neck, I would still—"

Madame set her teeth and tied a very tight, terrible knot.

"Stop!" Defarge said, reddening. "I too will stop at nothing."

"Yes! But you are weak because you need to see the victim and the opportunity to keep your courage up. You must stay strong without that. When the time comes, act like a tiger; but wait for the time with the tiger chained and hidden, yet always ready."

Madame hit the counter with her chain of money to emphasize her point, then calmly gathered her handkerchief and said it was time for bed.

The next noon found Madame Defarge at her usual place, knitting. A rose lay beside her, and she occasionally glanced at it. A few customers were in the shop. The day was very hot, and swarms of flies died at the bottom of the sticky glasses near madame. The other flies ignored them, continuing until they met the same fate.

A figure entering the door cast a new shadow over Madame Defarge. Before looking up, she laid down her knitting and pinned the rose into her headdress.

It was strange: the moment Madame Defarge pinned the rose, the customers stopped talking and gradually left the shop.

"Good day, madame," said the newcomer.

"Good day, monsieur."

She said it aloud, but added to herself: "John Barsad, age about forty, height about five feet nine, black hair, handsome but pale face, dark eyes, hooked nose bending left, sinister look! Good day, spy!"

"Please give me a glass of cognac and some fresh water, madame."

Madame served him politely.

"Excellent cognac, madame!"

It was the first time anyone had complimented the cheap cognac, and Madame Defarge knew its poor quality well. She replied that the cognac was flattered and went back to her knitting. The visitor watched her fingers for a moment and looked around the shop.

"You knit very well, madame."

"I am used to it."

"A pretty pattern too!"

"Do _you_ think so?" madame asked with a smile.

"Definitely. May I ask what it is for?"

"A pastime," madame replied, her fingers moving quickly.

"Not for use?"

"That depends. I might find a use for it one day. If I do, I’ll use it!"

It was remarkable, but the customers clearly did not like the rose in Madame Defarge's hair. Two men who entered to buy drinks saw the rose, hesitated, pretended to look for someone, and walked out. Soon the shop was empty of all previous customers. The spy watched closely but saw no signs of plotting. The customers had left in a natural, casual way.

"John," madame thought, her fingers knitting. "Stay long enough, and I will knit 'BARSAD' before you go."

"Do you have a husband, madame?"

"I do."

"Children?"

"No children."

"Business seems poor?"

"Business is very poor; the people are starving."

"Ah, the poor, oppressed people! As you say."

"As _you_ say," madame corrected him, knitting his name into her register.

"Forgive me; yes, I said it, but you must think so too."

"I think?" madame said loudly. "My husband and I have enough work keeping this shop open without thinking. We only think about how to survive. That keeps us busy enough without worrying about others."

The spy, who was there to gather information, kept his calm, polite expression. He leaned on the counter, sipping his cognac.

"A terrible thing, Gaspard's execution," he sighed. "Poor Gaspard!"

"Well," madame replied coldly, "if people use knives to kill, they must pay the price. He knew the cost; he has paid it."

"I believe," the spy whispered, leaning closer, "that there is a lot of anger and sympathy in this neighborhood for him? Just between us."

"Is there?" madame asked blankly.

"Is there not?"

"Here is my husband," Madame Defarge said.

As Defarge entered, the spy touched his hat and said with a smile, "Good day, Jacques!" Defarge stopped and stared at him.

"Good day, Jacques!" the spy repeated, his smile faltering under the stare.

"You are mistaken, monsieur," Defarge replied. "That is not my name. I am Ernest Defarge."

"It's all the same," the spy said quickly. "Good day!"

"Good day," Defarge replied dryly.

"I was just saying to madame that I hear there is much sympathy and anger in Saint Antoine over Gaspard's death."

"No one has told me so," said Defarge. "I know nothing about it."

He walked behind the counter and stood by his wife's chair, looking at the spy whom they both hated.

The spy calmly finished his glass of cognac and ordered another. Madame Defarge poured it, went back to her knitting, and began humming a tune.

"You seem to know this neighborhood well, better than I do?" Defarge remarked.

"Not at all, but I hope to know it better. I am very interested in these poor people."

"Ah," Defarge muttered.

"Speaking with you, Monsieur Defarge, reminds me," the spy continued, "that I have some interesting associations with your name."

"Indeed?" Defarge said indifferently.

"Yes. When Doctor Manette was released, you, as his old servant, took care of him. He was delivered to you. You see I know the details?"

"That is true," said Defarge. His wife had touched his elbow as she knitted, signaling him to answer briefly.

"It was to you," the spy said, "that his daughter came, and you delivered him to her and that banker in the wig—Lorry—who took them to England."

"That is true," Defarge repeated. "I knew Doctor Manette and his daughter in England," the spy said.

"Yes?"

"You don't hear from them now?"

"No," said Defarge.

"In fact," madame interrupted, looking up, "we never hear from them. We received news of their arrival, but since then we have had no correspondence."

"I see," the spy said. "She is going to be married."

"Married?" madame echoed. "She was pretty enough to marry long ago. You English are very cold."

"Oh! You know I am English."

"I can tell by your accent," madame replied. "A man is what his tongue is."

He did not take it as a compliment but laughed it off. He finished his drink and added:

"Yes, Miss Manette is marrying. But not an Englishman; she is marrying a Frenchman. And speaking of Gaspard, it is a strange coincidence: she is marrying the nephew of the Marquis whom Gaspard killed. In other words, the new Marquis. But he lives quietly in England under the name Charles Darnay. D'Aulnais is his mother's family name."

Madame Defarge knitted steadily, but the news clearly upset her husband. His hand shook as he tried to light his pipe. The spy noticed his reaction and recorded it in his mind.

Having delivered this piece of news, and seeing no other customers, Mr. Barsad paid for his drinks and left, saying politely that he hoped to see them again. The Defarges stood still for a few minutes in case he returned.

"Can it be true," Defarge whispered, "what he said about Miss Manette?"

"Since he said it," madame replied, "it is probably a lie. But it might be true."

"If it is—" Defarge began.

"If it is?"

"—and if the revolution comes in our lifetime, I hope for her sake that her husband stays out of France."

"Her husband’s destiny," Madame Defarge said calmly, "will take him where he must go, and lead him to his end. That is all."

"But it is very strange," Defarge said, wanting his wife to agree, "that after all our sympathy for the Doctor and his daughter, her husband's name should be registered for death by your hand, next to the name of that spy who just left?"

"Stranger things will happen when the revolution comes," madame answered. "They are both on the list for their actions, and that is enough."

She rolled up her knitting and took the rose out of her hair. The neighborhood people, seeing the rose was gone, began coming back into the shop, and it returned to its usual state.

In the evening, when the people of Saint Antoine gathered in the streets for fresh air, Madame Defarge walked among them, carrying her knitting. She was like a missionary of anger, and all the women knitted with her. The work kept their hands busy to distract them from their empty stomachs.

As they knitted, their eyes and thoughts moved quickly. Wherever Madame Defarge walked, she left groups of angry women behind her. Her husband watched her with admiration. "A great, strong, and terrifying woman!" he muttered.

Darkness fell, and the church bells rang while military drums beat in the palace. The women sat knitting in the dark. A greater darkness was coming over France, when these church bells would be melted into cannons, and the drums would beat to drown out the screams of the dying. The women sat knitting, preparing for the day when they would sit by the guillotine, counting the falling heads.

CHAPTER XVII: One Night

The sun never set with a brighter glory on the quiet corner in Soho than one memorable evening when the Doctor and his daughter sat together under the plane tree. The moon never rose with a softer light over London than on that night, shining on their faces through the leaves.

Lucie was to be married the next day. She had reserved this last evening for her father, and they sat alone under the tree.

"Are you happy, my dear father?"

"Quite, my child."

They had said very little, though they had been sitting there a long time. When it was still light enough to read and work, she had done neither. Usually, she sat by his side under the tree working or reading to him, but this evening was different from any other.

"I am very happy tonight, dear father. I am deeply happy in my love for Charles, and Charles’s love for me. But if my life were not still dedicated to you, or if my marriage separated us, even by a few streets, I would be very unhappy. Even as it is—"

Her voice faltered, and she could not finish.

In the soft moonlight, she hugged him by the neck and rested her face on his chest. They sat in the moonlight, which always feels a little sad, like human life itself.

"Dearest father! Can you tell me, this last time, that you are sure my new love and duties will never stand between us? I know it, but do you know it in your heart?"

Her father answered with a firm, cheerful conviction: "I am quite sure, my darling! More than that," he added, kissing her tenderly, "my future looks much brighter, Lucie, because of your marriage than it ever did without it."

"If I could only hope that, father!"

"Believe it, my love! Indeed it is true. Think how natural it is. You are young, and you cannot fully understand how much I worried that your life would be wasted—"

She put her hand to his lips to stop him, but he took her hand and repeated the word.

"—wasted, my child, for my sake. Your unselfishness prevents you from seeing how much I thought about this. But ask yourself: how could I be perfectly happy if your happiness was incomplete?"

"If I had never met Charles, father, I would have been perfectly happy with you."

He smiled at her admission that she would be unhappy without Charles now that she had met him, and replied:

"My child, you did meet him. If it hadn't been Charles, it would have been someone else. Or if you had never married, I would have been the cause, and the shadow of my past imprisonment would have ruined your life too."

It was the first time, other than at the trial, that she had heard him speak of his time in prison. Hearing his words gave her a strange feeling, which she remembered long afterward.

"Look!" said the Doctor, pointing toward the moon. "I watched the moon from my prison window when I couldn't bear its light. I watched it when it was torture to think of it shining on everything I had lost, and I beat my head against the prison walls. I watched it when I was so numb and depressed that I could only count the lines I could draw across it. I remember there were twenty lines either way, and the twentieth was hard to squeeze in."

The strange feeling she had hearing him talk of his prison years deepened as he continued, though his tone was calm. He was simply contrasting his present happiness with his past suffering.

"I watched the moon, wondering thousands of times about the child I had been torn from. I wondered if it was alive, or if the shock had killed its mother. I wondered if it was a son who would one day avenge me. (There was a time when my desire for vengeance was unbearable.) I wondered if it was a son who would never know my story, or might believe I had disappeared on purpose. Or if it was a daughter who would grow up to be a woman."

She drew closer, kissing his cheek and hand.

"I imagined my daughter grew up completely ignorant of me. I counted her age, year after year. I imagined her married to a man who knew nothing of my fate. I was entirely forgotten by the living, and my place in the family was a blank."

"My father! Even hearing that you had such thoughts makes my heart ache as if I had been that child."

"You, Lucie? These memories only arise because of the healing and happiness you have brought me. We share them on this last night under the moon. What was I saying just now?"

"You said she knew nothing of you and did not care."

"Yes. But on other nights, when the silence brought a sad peace, I imagined her coming to my cell and leading me out to freedom. I saw her figure in the moonlight, just as I see you now, though I could never hold her. The image stood between the window and the door. But you understand that was not the child I am speaking of?"

"The image was not the real child, but a fancy?"

"No, that was a phantom. The child my mind pursued was more real. I only knew she looked like her mother. The phantom looked like her mother too—as you do—but it was different. Can you follow me, Lucie? Probably not. I think only a prisoner could understand these strange distinctions."

His calm manner did not stop her from feeling a chill as he analyzed his old state of mind.

"In that peaceful state, I imagined her leading me out to show me her married home, which was full of loving memories of her lost father. My picture was on her wall, and I was in her prayers. Her life was active and happy, but my story was part of it."

"I was that child, father. I was not nearly as good, but in my love, that was me."

"And she showed me her children," the Doctor said, "who had been taught to pity me. When they passed a state prison, they looked at the bars and spoke in whispers. She could never rescue me; I imagined she always brought me back to my cell afterward. But then, relieved by tears, I knelt and blessed her."

"I am that child, father. I hope you will bless me just as warmly tomorrow."

"Lucie, I remember these old troubles tonight only because they show how much I love you and how grateful I am to God. My wildest thoughts in prison never imagined the happiness I have found with you, and what lies ahead for us."

He hugged her, blessed her, and thanked God for giving her to him. After a while, they went inside.

No one was invited to the wedding except Mr. Lorry, and Miss Pross was the only bridesmaid. The marriage would not change where they lived. They had simply rented the upper rooms formerly belonging to the invisible lodger, and needed nothing more.

Doctor Manette was very cheerful at dinner. Only the three of them sat at the table: the Doctor, Lucie, and Miss Pross. He regretted that Charles was not there, joking about the custom that kept him away, and drank a toast to him.

Finally, it was time to say good night, and they went to bed. But in the quiet hours of the morning, Lucie came downstairs and crept into his room, feeling a sudden fear.

However, everything was in its place and quiet. He lay asleep, his white hair neat on the pillow, and his hands still. She put her candle in the shadow, went to his bedside, and kissed him.

His face still showed the worn lines of his long imprisonment, but he controlled his expressions even in his sleep. His face showed a quiet, determined struggle with his old memories.

She gently laid her hand on his chest, praying she would always be a good daughter to him. Then she kissed him again and left. Soon the sun rose, and the shadows of the tree leaves moved softly across his face.

CHAPTER XVIII: Nine Days

The wedding day shone brightly. They were waiting outside the closed door of the Doctor's room, where he was speaking with Charles Darnay. The beautiful bride, Mr. Lorry, and Miss Pross were ready to go to church. To Miss Pross, the event would have been perfect happiness, if not for her lingering belief that her brother Solomon should have been the groom.

"And so," said Mr. Lorry, who couldn't stop admiring the bride and walked around her to take in her pretty, simple dress. "And so it was for this, my sweet Lucie, that I brought you across the Channel when you were such a baby! Lord help me! How little I thought what I was doing! How little I valued the service I was doing for my friend Charles!"

"You didn't mean to do it," the practical Miss Pross remarked, "so how could you know? What nonsense!"

"Really? Well, don't cry," said the gentle Mr. Lorry.

"I am not crying," said Miss Pross. "You are."

"I, my Pross?" (By this time, Mr. Lorry dared to joke with her occasionally.)

"You were just now; I saw you do it, and I don't wonder. The silver present you gave them is enough to bring tears to anyone's eyes. There isn't a fork or spoon in the set," said Miss Pross, "that I didn't cry over last night when the box arrived, until I couldn't see them."

"I am very pleased," said Mr. Lorry, "though I certainly didn't mean to make them invisible. Dear me! This is an occasion that makes a man think of everything he has lost. To think that I might have had a Mrs. Lorry at any time during the last fifty years!"

"Not at all!" said Miss Pross.

"You think I never could have had a Mrs. Lorry?" asked the banker.

"Bah!" Miss Pross replied. "You were a bachelor in your cradle."

"Well!" Mr. Lorry smiled, adjusting his wig. "That seems likely too."

"And you were cut out to be a bachelor before you were even put in your cradle," Miss Pross added.

"Then I think I was treated very poorly," said Mr. Lorry, "and I should have had a say in the matter. Enough! Now, my dear Lucie," drawing his arm around her waist, "I hear them moving in the next room. Miss Pross and I, as two practical business people, want to say one last thing to you. You are leaving your good father in hands as loving as your own. He will be taken care of. During the next two weeks, while you are in Warwickshire, even Tellson’s Bank will come second to him. And when he joins you and your husband in Wales in two weeks, you will see that we have sent him to you in the best health and spirits. Now, I hear his footsteps coming to the door. Let me kiss my dear girl and bless her before he comes to claim his bride."

He looked at her face for a moment, remembering her expression, and then held her golden hair against his wig with genuine tenderness.

The door opened, and the Doctor came out with Charles Darnay. He was deadly pale—unlike when they went in—with no color left in his face. But he remained calm, except that Mr. Lorry noticed a faint look of fear and avoidance on his face, like a cold wind.

He gave his arm to his daughter and led her downstairs to the carriage Mr. Lorry had hired. The others followed in another carriage, and soon, in a nearby church with no strangers watching, Charles Darnay and Lucie Manette were happily married.

In addition to the tears of joy among the small group, some bright diamonds shone on the bride’s hand, which Mr. Lorry had just taken from his pocket. They returned home for breakfast, and all went well. In due course, the golden hair that had once mingled with the shoemaker’s white hair in the Paris attic was held against it again in the morning sun as they said goodbye.

It was a difficult parting, though not for long. But her father cheered her, saying as he let her go: "Take her, Charles! She is yours!"

She waved to them from the carriage window, and was gone.

The street corner was quiet and out of the way of the curious, and the preparations had been simple. The Doctor, Mr. Lorry, and Miss Pross were left alone. As they walked back into the cool hall, Mr. Lorry noticed a major change in the Doctor, as if he had received a terrible blow.

He had controlled his feelings for a long time, and some reaction was expected now that the wedding was over. But it was the old scared, lost look that worried Mr. Lorry. Seeing him hold his head and wander off to his room reminded Mr. Lorry of Defarge and the night they rescued him.

"I think," he whispered to Miss Pross after thinking it over, "we should not speak to him right now or disturb him. I must check in at Tellson’s Bank, so I will go there immediately and return soon. Then we will take him for a carriage ride in the country and dine there, and everything will be fine."

It was easier for Mr. Lorry to go to Tellson’s than to leave it. He was delayed there for two hours. When he returned, he went upstairs alone without asking the servant. Entering the Doctor's rooms, he was stopped by a low hammering sound.

"Good God!" he said, starting. "What is that?"

Miss Pross was beside him with a terrified face. "Oh no! Everything is lost!" she cried, wringing her hands. "What will we tell Ladybird? He doesn't recognize me and is making shoes!"

Mr. Lorry tried to calm her and went into the Doctor’s room. The bench was turned toward the window, just as it had been in Paris, and the Doctor was bent over his work, very busy.

"Doctor Manette. My dear friend, Doctor Manette!"

The Doctor looked up for a moment—half questioning, half angry at being interrupted—and went back to his work.

He had taken off his coat and vest. His shirt was open at the throat, just as it used to be in prison, and his face had the same worn, hollow look. He worked hard and quickly, as if impatient at being interrupted.

Mr. Lorry looked at the shoe in his hand, seeing it was the same size and shape as the old ones. He picked up another shoe nearby and asked what it was.

"A young lady’s walking shoe," the Doctor muttered without looking up. "It should have been finished long ago. Let it be."

"But, Doctor Manette. Look at me!"

He looked up in his old submissive way, without stopping his work.

"You know me, my dear friend? Think. This is not your real work. Think, dear friend!"

Nothing could make him speak more. He looked up for only a second when asked, but no persuasion could get a word from him. He worked on in silence, and words fell on him like wind. The only hope Mr. Lorry saw was that he sometimes looked up on his own. In those glances, there seemed a faint look of curiosity or confusion, as if he were trying to understand something.

Two things seemed most important to Mr. Lorry: first, that this must be kept a secret from Lucie; second, that it must be kept a secret from everyone else. He and Miss Pross took steps immediately, telling everyone that the Doctor was unwell and needed a few days of complete rest. To keep the secret from Lucie, Miss Pross was to write to her, saying the Doctor had been called away on professional business, referring to a fake letter they claimed he had written.

These measures were taken in the hope that he would recover soon. If that happened, Mr. Lorry planned to consult a trusted medical specialist about the Doctor's case.

Hoping for his recovery, Mr. Lorry decided to watch him closely without appearing to do so. He arranged to miss work at Tellson's Bank for the first time in his life, and sat by the window in the same room.

He soon realized that speaking to the Doctor only made him anxious. He stopped trying to talk to him on the first day, deciding simply to stay in the room as a silent protest against his delusion. He sat by the window reading and writing, trying to make the room feel normal.

Doctor Manette ate and drank what was given to him and worked on. On that first day, he worked until it was too dark to see—working for thirty minutes after Mr. Lorry could no longer read. When he finally put his tools away, Mr. Lorry stood up and said:

"Will you go out?"

The Doctor looked at the floor, then looked up in his old way, repeating in his low voice:

"Out?"

"Yes, for a walk with me. Why not?"

He made no effort to answer and said nothing more. But as he sat in the dark with his head in his hands, Mr. Lorry thought he saw him asking himself, "Why not?" The banker saw this as a good sign and decided to persist.

Miss Pross and he divided the night into watches, observing him from the next room. The Doctor walked up and down his room for a long time before finally going to sleep. In the morning, he got up early and went straight to his bench to work.

On this second day, Mr. Lorry greeted him cheerfully and spoke of things they had recently discussed. The Doctor did not answer, but he clearly heard and thought about what was said. This encouraged Mr. Lorry to have Miss Pross bring her work into the room several times. They spoke normally of Lucie and her father, acting as if nothing were wrong. They did this briefly so as not to upset him. Mr. Lorry was encouraged to see that the Doctor looked up more often, appearing to notice that something was strange.

When night fell, Mr. Lorry asked again:

"Dear Doctor, will you go out?"

As before, he repeated, "Out?"

"Yes, for a walk with me. Why not?"

This time, when the Doctor didn't answer, Mr. Lorry pretended to go out alone, returning after an hour. While he was gone, the Doctor had sat by the window looking at the plane tree, but when Mr. Lorry returned, he went back to his bench.

Time passed slowly, and Mr. Lorry's hopes began to fade as his heart grew heavier each day. The third day passed, then the fourth, the fifth. Five days, six days, seven days, eight days, nine days.

With fading hope and a heavy heart, Mr. Lorry watched him. The secret was kept from Lucie, who was happy. But Mr. Lorry saw that the Doctor, who had been a bit clumsy at first, was becoming very skilled at shoemaking again, working quickly and expertly by the evening of the ninth day.

CHAPTER XIX: An Opinion

Exhausted by his anxious watch, Mr. Lorry fell asleep at his post. On the tenth morning of his suspense, he was startled by the sun shining into the room, where a heavy sleep had overtaken him when it was still dark.

He rubbed his eyes and woke up, but at first he doubted if he was actually awake. Looking into the Doctor’s room, he saw that the shoemaker’s bench and tools were put away, and the Doctor himself sat reading by the window. He was dressed in his usual morning clothes, and his face, though still very pale, looked calm and focused.

Even when he was sure he was awake, Mr. Lorry felt uncertain for a few moments whether the shoemaking had been a dream. His friend sat before him in his usual clothes and appearance, working as normal, with no sign that any change had occurred.

But this was only his first confusion. The answer was obvious: if the relapse hadn't happened, why was he, Jarvis Lorry, sleeping in his clothes on the sofa in the Doctor’s office?

Within a few minutes, Miss Pross stood whispering at his side. Her confirmation settled any remaining doubts. Mr. Lorry advised that they should wait until the usual breakfast hour and meet the Doctor as if nothing had happened. If he appeared normal, Mr. Lorry would then carefully ask the Doctor for his professional opinion on the case.

Miss Pross agreed, and they carried out the plan carefully. After dressing methodically, Mr. Lorry arrived for breakfast in his usual neat clothes. The Doctor was summoned normally and joined them.

As far as Mr. Lorry could tell through careful observation, the Doctor at first believed his daughter’s wedding had occurred only the day before. A casual comment about the date set him thinking, which clearly made him uneasy. Otherwise, he was so completely himself that Mr. Lorry decided to seek his advice.

Therefore, when breakfast was cleared away and they were left alone, Mr. Lorry said:

"My dear Manette, I want to ask your confidential opinion on a very strange case in which I am interested. It seems very strange to me, though it might be simple to you."

Looking at his hands, which were stained from his late work, the Doctor looked worried and listened closely. He had already looked at his hands several times.

"Doctor Manette," Mr. Lorry said, touching his arm, "the case concerns a very dear friend of mine. Please advise me for his sake, and especially for his daughter's sake."

"I understand," the Doctor said quietly. "A mental shock?"

"Yes!"

"Tell me everything," said the Doctor. "Spare no detail."

Mr. Lorry saw they understood each other, and continued:

"My dear Manette, it is the case of a severe mental shock that affected the sufferer's mind. He was overwhelmed by it for a long time, though he cannot count the years himself. He eventually recovered through a process he cannot explain. He became a highly intelligent man, capable of hard study and physical exertion, with a large store of knowledge. But unfortunately, he has had a slight relapse."

In a low voice, the Doctor asked, "How long did it last?"

"Nine days and nights."

"How did it show itself? I assume," looking at his hands again, "by returning to some old work associated with his past suffering?"

"Yes, exactly."

"Did you ever see him do that work originally?"

"Once."

"And during the relapse, was he in the same state as before?"

"Yes, in every way."

"You mentioned his daughter. Does she know about the relapse?"

"No. We have kept it from her, and hope to keep it a secret forever. Only I and one other trusted person know."

The Doctor squeezed his hand and murmured, "That was very kind and thoughtful." Mr. Lorry squeezed his hand in return, and they sat in silence.

"Now, my dear Manette," Mr. Lorry said gently, "I am only a businessman, and I don't understand these difficult matters. I need guidance, and there is no one I trust more than you. Tell me, how does such a relapse happen? Is there a danger of it happening again? Can we prevent it? How should we treat it? What can I do for my friend? I want to help him if I only knew how. If your knowledge and experience can guide me, I can do a lot; without guidance, I can do very little. Please explain it to me."

Doctor Manette sat thinking in the silence.

"I think it is likely," the Doctor said, breaking the silence with an effort, "that the relapse was not entirely unexpected by the patient."

"Was he afraid of it?" Mr. Lorry asked.

"Very much," the Doctor said with a shudder. "You cannot imagine how that fear weighs on a person, and how hard it is for him to speak of it."

"Would it help him," Mr. Lorry asked, "if he could bring himself to share that secret fear with someone when it is happening?"

"I think so. But as I said, it is almost impossible. In some cases, completely impossible."

"Now," Mr. Lorry said, laying his hand on the Doctor’s arm, "what caused this attack?"

"I believe," Doctor Manette said, "that there was a sudden and powerful return of the thoughts and memories that caused his original illness. Some distressing associations were vividly recalled. He probably feared those memories would return under certain circumstances—on a particular occasion. He tried to prepare himself in vain, and perhaps the effort to prepare made him more vulnerable."

"Would he remember what happened during the relapse?" Mr. Lorry asked hesitantly.

The Doctor looked around the room, shook his head, and answered quietly, "Not at all."

"And what about the future?" Mr. Lorry asked.

"As for the future," the Doctor said with more confidence, "I would have great hope. Since he recovered so quickly, I would have great hope. Having yielded to a long-feared pressure and recovered after the crisis passed, I would hope the worst is over."

"Well, that is comforting. I am grateful!" said Mr. Lorry.

"I am grateful!" the Doctor repeated reverently.

"There are two other points," Mr. Lorry said. "May I ask?"

"You cannot do your friend a better service," the Doctor said, offering his hand.

"First: he is a studious and energetic man, working hard on his experiments and studies. Does he do too much?"

"I think not. His mind may simply need constant occupation. That may be partly natural, and partly the result of his past suffering. The less his mind is busy with healthy things, the more it is in danger of turning to unhealthy thoughts. He may have realized this himself."

"You are sure he is not under too much strain?"

"Yes, I am sure of it. There has been a violent stress in one direction, and he needs a counterweight."

"Forgive me, as a practical businessman. If he _were_ overworked, would it trigger another relapse?"

"I don't think so. I believe only that one specific set of memories would trigger it. Only a sudden shock to that chord could cause it. After his recovery, I doubt that will happen again. I believe the circumstances likely to cause it are gone."

He spoke with the caution of a doctor who knew how delicate the mind is, but also with the confidence of a man who had survived it himself. Mr. Lorry pretended to be fully relieved, and approached his last point. He knew it was the hardest, but remembered his talk with Miss Pross and what he had seen.

"The work he returned to during this relapse," Mr. Lorry said, clearing his throat, "let us call it blacksmith’s work. Let us say, for example, that he had worked at a small forge in his prison years, and was found at his forge again. Isn't it a pity that he keeps that forge with him?"

The Doctor covered his forehead with his hand and tapped his foot nervously on the floor.

"He has always kept it near him," Mr. Lorry said. "Wouldn't it be better if he let it go?"

The Doctor, still covering his face, kept tapping his foot.

"Is it hard to advise me?" Mr. Lorry asked. "I know it is a difficult question. And yet..." He shook his head and stopped.

"You see," Doctor Manette said after an uneasy pause, "it is very hard to explain the inner workings of this man’s mind. He once longed for that work so much, and it helped relieve his mental pain by keeping his hands busy. Because it helped him survive his mental torture, he could never bear to put it out of reach. Even now, when he feels more confident in himself, the thought of needing that work and not finding it terrifies him, like a lost child."

He looked like a worried child himself as he looked at Mr. Lorry.

"But," Mr. Lorry said, "as a practical man who deals only with money and concrete things, wouldn't keeping the tools keep the memory alive? If the tools were gone, might not the fear go with it? Isn't keeping the forge a sign of doubt in his own recovery?"

Another silence followed.

"You must understand," the Doctor said tremulously, "it is such an old companion."

"I would not keep it," Mr. Lorry said, shaking his head. He grew firmer as he saw the Doctor's hesitation. "I would advise him to sacrifice it. I only want your approval. It does no good. Give me your approval for his daughter’s sake!"

It was clear a great struggle was going on inside the Doctor.

"In her name, then, let it be done; I approve. But do not take it while he is there. Remove it when he is away, so he misses his companion after he leaves."

Mr. Lorry agreed, and their talk ended. They spent the day in the country, and the Doctor seemed completely recovered. Over the next three days he remained well, and on the fourteenth day he left to join Lucie and her husband. Mr. Lorry had explained the excuse they used for his silence, and the Doctor wrote to Lucie accordingly, so she suspected nothing.

On the night after the Doctor left, Mr. Lorry went into his bedroom with a saw, hammer, chisel, and chopper, accompanied by Miss Pross holding a candle. Working in secret behind closed doors, Mr. Lorry chopped the shoemaker’s bench to pieces, while Miss Pross held the light like an accomplice to a murder. They burned the wood in the kitchen fireplace and buried the tools, shoes, and leather in the garden. The work of destruction and secrecy felt so wrong that Mr. Lorry and Miss Pross felt like criminals hiding a body.

CHAPTER XX: A Plea

When the newly married couple returned home, the first person to offer his congratulations was Sydney Carton. They had been home for only a few hours when he arrived. He had not improved in his habits, appearance, or manners, but Charles Darnay noticed a new, rugged look of loyalty in him.

Carton waited for an opportunity to take Darnay aside by the window, where no one else could hear them speak.

"Mr. Darnay," Carton said, "I wish we might be friends."

"We are already friends, I hope."

"You are polite enough to say so as a formality, but I don't mean it as a mere formality. Indeed, when I say I wish we might be friends, I don't even mean exactly that, either."

Charles Darnay naturally asked him, in a friendly and good-natured way, what he did mean.

"Honestly," Carton said with a smile, "I find that easier to understand in my own mind than to explain to you. But let me try. You remember a certain occasion when I was more drunk than usual?"

"I remember an occasion when you made me admit you had been drinking."

"I remember it too. The regret of those moments weighs heavily on me, because I always remember them. I hope that will be taken into account one day when my life is over! Don't worry; I'm not going to give you a sermon."

"I am not worried at all. Sincerity in you is not alarming to me."

"Ah!" Carton said, waving his hand as if dismissing the thought. "On that occasion—one of many, as you know—I was obnoxious about whether I liked you or not. I wish you would forget it."

"I forgot it long ago."

"Another polite formality! But, Mr. Darnay, forgetting is not as easy for me as you say it is for you. I haven't forgotten it, and a casual reply doesn't help me forget."

"If it was a casual reply," Darnay said, "I ask your forgiveness. I only wanted to dismiss a minor thing that seems to worry you too much. I assure you, on my honor as a gentleman, that I have forgotten it. And really, what was there to dismiss compared to the great help you gave me that day?"

"As for that help," Carton said, "I must admit that it was just professional legal showmanship. I don't know that I cared what happened to you when I did it. Remember, I am speaking of the past."

"You make light of the favor," Darnay replied, "but I won't argue with your reply."

"It's the absolute truth, Mr. Darnay, believe me! But I have wandered from my point; I was speaking of our friendship. You know me; you know I am incapable of the better qualities of men. If you doubt it, ask Stryver, and he’ll tell you so."

"I prefer to form my own opinion, without his help."

"Well! At any rate, you know me as a ruined man who has never done any good, and never will."

"I don't know that you 'never will.'"

"But I do, and you must take my word for it. Well! If you can tolerate having such a worthless fellow with a bad reputation visiting at odd times, I ask that I might be allowed to visit as a privileged guest. I would be like a useless piece of furniture, tolerated for past services and ignored. I doubt I would abuse the privilege; I probably wouldn't visit more than four times a year. It would simply comfort me to know I had the permission."

"Will you try?"

"That means I am accepted on those terms. Thank you, Darnay. May I use your first name?"

"I think so, Carton, by this time."

They shook hands, and Sydney turned away. Within a minute, he returned to his usual careless manner.

When he was gone, during an evening spent with Miss Pross, the Doctor, and Mr. Lorry, Charles Darnay mentioned this talk. He described Sydney Carton as a puzzle of carelessness and irresponsibility. He did not speak bitterly, but simply as anyone would who saw Carton as he presented himself.

He had no idea that his young wife was thinking deeply about this. But when he joined her in their room later, he found her waiting for him with a serious look on her face.

"You look thoughtful tonight!" Darnay said, putting his arm around her.

"Yes, dearest Charles," she said, placing her hands on his chest and looking at him closely. "I am rather thoughtful tonight, because I have something on my mind."

"What is it, my Lucie?"

"Will you promise not to ask me why, if I beg you not to ask?"

"Will I promise? What wouldn't I promise to my love?"

He pushed her golden hair back from her cheek, holding her close.

"I think, Charles, that poor Mr. Carton deserves more respect and consideration than you showed for him tonight."

"Really, my love? Why so?"

"That is what you must not ask me. But I think—I know—he does."

"If you know it, that is enough. What do you want me to do, my life?"

"I ask you, dearest, to always be very generous with him, and very lenient about his faults when he is not here. I want you to believe that he has a heart he rarely reveals, and that there are deep wounds in it. Dearest, I have seen his heart bleeding."

"It makes me sad," Charles Darnay said in surprise, "to think I have wronged him. I never thought this of him."

"My husband, it is true. I fear he cannot be reformed; there is little hope for his character or future. But I am sure he is capable of good, gentle, and noble things."

She looked so beautiful in her faith in this lost man that her husband could have stared at her for hours.

"And, oh my dearest love!" she urged, clinging closer to him and looking into his eyes. "Remember how happy and strong we are, and how weak he is in his misery!"

Her plea touched his heart. "I will always remember it, my love! I will remember it as long as I live."

He bent over her, kissing her lips and holding her in his arms. If the lonely wanderer walking the dark streets outside could have heard her words and seen her husband kiss away her tears of pity, he might have cried out to the night:

"God bless her for her sweet compassion!"

CHAPTER XXI: Echoing Footsteps

The corner where the Doctor lived was famous for its echoes. Always busy keeping her husband, father, and family happy, Lucie sat in the quiet house listening to the echoes of the passing years.

At first, though she was a perfectly happy young wife, she would occasionally drop her work as her eyes filled with tears. There was a faint sound coming in the echoes—a light, distant step—that stirred her heart. She felt a mix of hopes and fears: hopes for a child she did not yet know, and fears that she might not survive to enjoy that happiness. In those moments, she imagined the sound of footsteps at her own early grave, and wept for the husband who would be left behind.

But that time passed, and her daughter, little Lucie, lay on her bosom. Soon, the echoes brought the sound of tiny feet and baby talk. No matter how loud other echoes grew, the young mother could always hear these. The quiet house was soon filled with a child’s laughter, bringing her sacred joy.

Lucie heard only friendly and peaceful sounds in the echoes. Her husband’s step was strong and successful; her father’s step was firm. Even Miss Pross joined in the noise, running around the garden under the plane tree.

Even when there were sounds of sadness, they were not harsh. When a little golden-haired boy—who looked just like her—lay dying on his pillow, he said with a smile: "Dear papa and mama, I am sorry to leave you both and my sister, but I must go!" The tears on his mother's cheek were not purely of agony as his spirit left. Children are at peace in heaven.

Thus, the memory of an angel was blended with the other echoes. The wind blowing over the little garden grave was audible to Lucie like the gentle sound of a summer sea, while little Lucie played at her feet, speaking both French and English.

The echoes rarely brought the sound of Sydney Carton’s steps. Only about six times a year, he used his privilege to visit uninvited, sitting with them through the evening as he used to do. He never came after drinking. And the echoes whispered something about him that true echoes have whispered for ages.

No man ever loved a woman, lost her, and watched her become a wife and mother with a pure mind, without her children feeling a strange sympathy and pity for him. Little Lucie was always quick to hug Carton, and the dying boy had said at the end: "Poor Carton! Kiss him for me!"

Mr. Stryver pushed his way through the legal world like a powerful boat, dragging Carton behind him like a towed barge. Carton’s life was swamped and difficult. But habit made it the life he led, and he never thought of trying to become a leader himself. Stryver was now rich, having married a wealthy widow with three dull sons.

Stryver had brought these three boys to Soho like sheep, offering them as pupils to Darnay, saying proudly: "Here are three pupils to help pay for your marriage, Darnay!" Darnay's polite refusal had made Stryver furious. He warned the boys to beware of the pride of beggars like that tutor. He also bragged to his wife that Lucie had once tried to "trap" him into marriage, but that he had been too clever to be caught. His colleagues excused his lies, saying he had told them so often he believed them himself.

These were the echoes Lucie listened to, sometimes sad and sometimes amused, until her daughter was six years old. The echoes of her child's steps, her active father's steps, and her husband's steps were music to her. Her father often told her he found her even more devoted now that she was married, and her husband asked: "What is your secret, my darling, for taking care of all of us without ever seeming hurried?"

But there were other echoes, coming from a distance, that rumbled threateningly. Around little Lucie’s sixth birthday, they began to sound like a great storm rising in France.

On a night in mid-July 1789, Mr. Lorry came in late from the bank and sat with Lucie and Darnay by the window. It was a hot, wild night, reminding them of the storm years before.

"I thought I would have to sleep at the bank," Mr. Lorry said, pushing back his wig. "We have been so busy all day. There is such worry in Paris that customers are sending their property to us in England as fast as they can."

"That looks bad," Darnay said.

"Yes, but people are unreasonable. Some of us at Tellson’s are getting old and don't want to be disturbed without good reason."

"Still," Darnay said, "you know how dangerous the situation in France is."

"I know," Mr. Lorry agreed, grumbling. "Where is Manette?"

"Here I am," the Doctor said, entering.

"I am glad you are home; the worries at the bank have made me nervous. You aren't going out, I hope?"

"No, I will play backgammon with you if you like," the Doctor said.

"I don't think I can play tonight; I am too tired. Is there still tea, Lucie?"

"Yes, we kept it for you."

"Thank you. The child is in bed?"

"And sleeping soundly."

"That’s right. Everything is safe and well here, thank God. But I am not as young as I was! Let us sit quietly and listen to the echoes."

"It was just a fancy, not a theory," Lucie said.

"A fancy, then," Mr. Lorry said, patting her hand. "But they are very loud tonight. Listen!"

They were dangerous, angry footsteps that were about to force their way into their lives—footsteps of a mob raging in Saint Antoine far away.

That morning, Saint Antoine was a massive crowd of poor people, armed with steel blades and bayonets shining in the sun. A loud roar rose from the crowd, and a forest of naked arms reached for weapons.

Muskets, cartridges, powder, iron bars, knives, and axes were distributed. People with no weapons used their bare hands to tear stones and bricks from the walls. Every heart in Saint Antoine was at a fever pitch. Everyone held their life as worthless, ready to sacrifice it.

All this anger centered around Defarge’s wine shop. Defarge, covered in gunpowder and sweat, was directing the crowd, handing out weapons and leading the attack.

"Stay near me, Jacques Three!" Defarge shouted. "Jacques One and Two, lead the patriots. Where is my wife?"

"Here I am!" madame said, calm as ever, but not knitting today. She held an axe in her hand, with a pistol and a knife in her belt.

"Where are you going, wife?"

"With you for now. I will lead the women soon."

"Come then!" Defarge shouted. "We are ready! To the Bastille!"

With a roar from the crowd, the living sea rose and rushed toward the great prison. Bells rang and drums beat as the attack began.

Deep ditches, double drawbridges, massive stone walls, eight great towers, cannons, muskets, fire, and smoke. Defarge worked like a soldier at a cannon for two hours.

One drawbridge fell! "Work, comrades, work! In the name of God or the Devil, work!" Defarge shouted, his cannon hot from firing.

"To me, women!" his wife cried. "We can kill as well as the men!" A crowd of armed, starving women followed her.

Cannons, muskets, fire, and smoke, but the walls and towers still stood. The wounded fell, weapons flashed, and torches burned. The mob roared, but the fortress held, and Defarge kept firing for four hours.

A white flag appeared from the fortress, signaling a surrender. The crowd rushed over the lowered drawbridge, past the stone walls, into the eight surrendered towers.

The force of the crowd carried Defarge into the outer courtyard of the Bastille. He stood against a wall, looking around. Jacques Three was beside him, and Madame Defarge was leading her women, knife in hand. The noise and confusion were deafening.

"The prisoners!"

"The records!"

"The secret cells!"

"The torture instruments!"

The crowd shouted for the prisoners. Defarge grabbed a gray-haired guard carrying a torch and pulled him against the wall.

"Show me the North Tower! Quick!" Defarge ordered.

"I will," the guard said, "but no one is there."

"What does 'One Hundred and Five, North Tower' mean? Quick!"

"It is a cell, monsieur."

"Show it to me!"

"This way."

Jacques Three followed them, disappointed that there was no bloodshed yet. They walked through dark vaults, past prison cages, down stone steps, and up into a tower. The thick walls muffled the noise of the crowd outside.

The guard stopped at a low door, unlocked it, and they entered.

"One hundred and five, North Tower!"
