There was a small, grated window high in the wall, a barred chimney, wood ashes on the hearth, a stool, a table, and a straw bed. An iron ring was fixed in the blackened wall.

"Pass the torch along the walls so I can see," Defarge said.

The guard obeyed, and Defarge followed the light.

"Stop! Look here, Jacques!"

"A. M.!" Jacques Three croaked.

"Alexandre Manette," Defarge whispered, tracing the letters with his dusty finger. "And here he wrote 'a poor physician.' He must have carved this calendar on the stone. Give me that crowbar!"

Defarge took the crowbar and smashed the old stool and table to pieces.

"Hold the light higher!" he ordered the guard. "Search the fragments, Jacques. Here is my knife; rip open the straw mattress. Hold the light higher!"

Defarge crawled onto the hearth, looking up the chimney and prying at the iron grate with the crowbar. Mortar and dust fell down. He reached into a crevice in the chimney, searching carefully.

"Nothing in the wood or the straw, Jacques?"

"Nothing."

"Gather them in the middle of the cell and burn them!"

The guard lit the pile, which burned hot. They left it burning and returned to the courtyard, where the noise of the crowd hit them again.

The crowd was searching for Defarge. Saint Antoine wanted the wine shop keeper to guard the prison governor who had shot at the people. They wanted to take him to the City Hall for judgment, ensuring he did not escape.

In the middle of the shouting crowd around the gray-coated governor, Madame Defarge stood immovable. "There is my husband!" she shouted, pointing to Defarge. She stayed close to the governor as they dragged him through the streets. When the crowd began hitting him, she was right beside him. When he fell dead, she put her foot on his neck and cut off his head with her knife.

Saint Antoine began hanging its enemies from the street lamps. The governor's body lay on the steps of the City Hall, and his blood stained Madame Defarge's shoe. The crowd rushed on.

It was a dangerous, angry sea of people, hardened by suffering and seeking vengeance.

In that ocean of faces, two groups of seven faces stood out. Seven released prisoners were carried on the shoulders of the crowd, looking confused and amazed. Higher up, seven dead heads on pikes were carried, their eyelids closed. They looked like witnesses waiting to say to their captors, "You did this!"

Seven prisoners released, seven heads on pikes, the keys of the Bastille, and the records of dead prisoners—these were the sights in the streets of Paris in mid-July 1789. May these dangerous, bloody feet stay far from Lucie Darnay's life! For once stained in blood, they are not easily made clean.

CHAPTER XXII: The Sea Still Rises

The starving people of Saint Antoine had only one week of celebration, softening their hard bread with hugs and congratulations, before Madame Defarge sat at her counter as usual, presiding over her customers. Madame Defarge wore no rose in her headdress now, for the spies had become very cautious about coming into the neighborhood. The street lamps had an ominous swing to them.

Madame Defarge sat in the morning light with her arms folded, watching the shop and the street. In both, there were groups of poor, dirty people, but they now felt a new sense of power. The raggedest nightcap worn by the poorest man seemed to say: "It has been hard for me to survive, but it has become very easy for me to kill you." Every lean arm that had been jobless before now had work always ready—to strike. The fingers of the knitting women were sharp and dangerous. There was a change in Saint Antoine; the neighborhood had been prepared for this for hundreds of years, and the final blows had changed its face.

Madame Defarge watched with quiet approval, as a leader of the women should. Another woman knitted beside her: the short, plump wife of a grocer, who was also a mother of two. This assistant had earned the name The Vengeance.

"Listen!" said The Vengeance. "Who is coming?"

A fast-spreading murmur came down the street, like a lit fuse rushing toward the shop.

"It is Defarge," madame said. "Silence, patriots!"

Defarge entered out of breath, pulled off his red cap, and looked around. "Listen!" madame ordered. "Listen to him!" Defarge stood panting, surrounded by eager faces at the door, while everyone in the shop stood up.

"Tell us, husband. What is it?"

"News from the other world!"

"What?" madame asked contemptuously. "The other world?"

"Does everyone remember old Foulon, who told the starving people they could eat grass, and who died and went to hell?"

"Everyone!" the crowd shouted.

"The news is about him. He is here among us!"

"Among us!" the crowd shouted. "And not dead?"

"Not dead! He feared us so much that he pretended to be dead and had a fake funeral. But they found him alive, hiding in the country, and have brought him in. I just saw him on his way to the City Hall, a prisoner. I said he had reason to fear us. Am I right?"

If the wicked old man of seventy-four had never known it, he would have known it in his heart if he could have heard the crowd's response.

A deep silence followed. Defarge and his wife looked at each other. The Vengeance reached down and pulled a drum to her feet behind the counter.

"Patriots!" Defarge said firmly. "Are we ready?"

Instantly, Madame Defarge put her knife in her belt, the drum began beating in the street, and The Vengeance ran screaming from house to house to rouse the women.

The men looked out of their windows with bloody anger, grabbed whatever weapons they had, and poured into the streets. But the women were a terrifying sight. Leaving their poor chores, their children, and their sick, they ran out with messy hair, screaming and driving one another to madness. "Foulon is caught, sister! Old Foulon is caught, mother! Foulon is caught, daughter!" Others ran into the crowd, screaming: "Foulon is alive! Foulon who told the starving people to eat grass! Foulon who told my old father to eat grass when I had no bread! Foulon who told my baby to suck grass when my breasts were dry! I swear on my knees to avenge you on Foulon! Give us his blood, his head, his heart! Tear him to pieces and bury him, so grass grows from him!" Many women, driven to a frenzy, began hitting and tearing at their own friends until they fainted, saved from being trampled only by their husbands.

No time was lost. Foulon was at the City Hall and might be released. Never, if Saint Antoine could help it! Armed men and women poured out of the neighborhood so fast that within fifteen minutes, only a few old women and crying children were left.

The crowd packed the examination room where the wicked old man was held, overflowing into the streets. The Defarges, The Vengeance, and Jacques Three were in the front of the crowd, close to him.

"Look!" madame cried, pointing her knife. "See the old villain tied with ropes. They tied a bunch of grass to his back! That was well done. Let him eat it now!" Madame put her knife under her arm and clapped her hands as if watching a play.

The people behind her explained her reaction to those behind them, and soon the streets clapped in response. During the hours of legal delays, Madame Defarge's gestures of impatience were copied by the crowd outside. Men who had climbed the walls to look through the windows signaled her reactions to the mob below.

Finally, the sun rose high, shining on the old prisoner’s head. The crowd could wait no longer. In an instant, the guards were pushed aside, and Saint Antoine grabbed him!

The news spread instantly. Defarge leaped over a railing and grabbed the old man in a tight embrace. Madame Defarge followed, wrapping her hand in the ropes he was tied with. The Vengeance and Jacques Three followed, while the crowd screamed: "Bring him out! To the lamp post!"

Dragged up and down the steps, now on his knees, now on his back, beaten and choked with grass and straw stuffed into his face, the old man begged for mercy. The crowd drew back to watch him struggle. They dragged him to the nearest street corner where a lamp post hung. Madame Defarge let him go like a cat releasing a mouse, watching quietly while they prepared the rope, as he begged her for help. The women screamed at him, and the men called for him to be hanged with grass in his mouth. Twice they hoisted him up, and twice the rope broke, dropping him screaming to the ground. The third time, the rope held him, and his head was soon placed on a pike with grass stuffed in his mouth, while Saint Antoine danced around it.

This was not the end of the day's violence. The crowd danced until they heard that Foulon's son-in-law, another enemy of the people, was being brought to Paris under a guard of five hundred cavalry. The mob wrote his crimes on paper, seized him from the soldiers, cut off his head and heart, and carried the three bloody trophies on pikes through the streets.

Only at dark did the men and women return to their hungry children. They stood in long lines at the bakeries to buy bad bread. As they waited with empty stomachs, they comforted themselves by talking about the day's triumphs. Gradually, the lines disappeared, and small fires were lit in the streets where neighbors cooked and ate together at their doors.

It was a poor supper with no meat, but sharing it brought some comfort and cheer. Fathers and mothers who had taken part in the violence played gently with their children, and lovers hoped for a better future.

It was almost morning when the last customers left Defarge’s shop. Monsieur Defarge closed the door and said quietly to his wife:

"At last it is here, my dear!"

"Well," madame replied, "almost."

Saint Antoine went to sleep, and the drum was quiet. The drum was the only thing in the neighborhood that the violence had not changed; its sound was the same as before the Bastille fell. But the voices of the men and women of Saint Antoine were now hoarse and altered.

CHAPTER XXIII: Fire Rises

A change came over the village with the fountain, where the road-mender worked every day, breaking stones on the highway just to earn enough bread to keep his poor body and soul together. The prison on the cliff was no longer as powerful as it used to be. There were soldiers guarding it, but not many, and there were officers to watch the soldiers. However, none of the officers knew what their men would do—except they knew the soldiers probably wouldn't obey orders.

All around, the countryside was ruined and desolate. Every green leaf, blade of grass, and stalk of grain was as shriveled and poor as the miserable people themselves. Everything was beaten down, depressed, oppressed, and broken. The houses, fences, farm animals, men, women, children, and even the soil they lived on were completely worn out.

The aristocrats (who were often perfectly nice gentlemen individually) were supposed to be a blessing to the nation, setting a polite example of luxury and fine living. Yet, as a class, they had somehow brought the country to this state. It was strange that the world, which seemed to be made just for them, was so quickly squeezed dry. Surely, there must have been some mistake in the universe's design! But that was the reality. Now that the last drop of blood had been squeezed from the poorest people, and the screws of taxes and oppression could not be tightened any further without breaking, the aristocrats began to run away from this messy, confusing situation.

But this running away was not the real change in this village, or in the many others like it. For decades, the aristocrats had squeezed the people dry, rarely visiting except to go hunting—sometimes hunting wild animals, and sometimes hunting the people themselves, keeping large areas of land wild and useless just for their sports. No, the real change was the appearance of strange, poor-looking faces in the area, rather than the disappearance of the handsome, elegant faces of the lords.

Lately, as the road-mender worked alone in the dust—not thinking about life and death, but mostly about how little he had for dinner and how much more he would eat if he could—he would look up and see a rough traveler approaching on foot. Such travelers used to be rare, but now they were a common sight. As the figure came closer, the road-mender was not surprised to see a tall, shaggy-haired man who looked almost wild. The man wore wooden shoes that looked clumsy even to the road-mender. He was dirty, dark-skinned, covered in the mud and dust of many roads, damp from marshes, and covered in thorns, leaves, and moss from walking through the woods.

Such a man walked up to him like a ghost at noon in the hot July weather, while the road-mender sat on his pile of stones under a bank, sheltering as best he could from a sudden hailstorm.

The traveler looked at him, then at the village in the valley, at the mill, and at the prison on the cliff. Once he had recognized these landmarks in his simple mind, he spoke in a rough dialect that was barely understandable:

“How is it going, Jacques?”

“All well, Jacques.”

“Join hands then!”

They shook hands, and the man sat down on the pile of stones.

“No lunch?”

“Nothing but dinner tonight,” said the road-mender with a hungry look.

“That's the style these days,” growled the man. “I don't find lunch anywhere.”

He pulled out a blackened pipe, filled it, lit it with flint and steel, and puffed until it glowed brightly. Then, he held it away and dropped a tiny pinch of something from his fingers into the bowl, which flared up and went out in a puff of smoke.

“Join hands then,” said the road-mender, after watching this. They shook hands again.

“Tonight?” asked the road-mender.

“Tonight,” said the man, putting the pipe back in his mouth.

“Where?”

“Here.”

He and the road-mender sat on the pile of stones, staring silently at each other while the hail fell between them like a tiny army of bayonets, until the sky began to clear over the village.

“Show me!” said the traveler, walking to the edge of the hill.

“Look!” the road-mender replied, pointing his finger. “You go down here, straight through the street, and past the fountain—”

“To the devil with all that!” the other interrupted, looking out over the landscape. “_I_ am not going through streets or past fountains. What else?”

“Well, it is about six miles past the top of that hill above the village.”

“Good. When do you finish work?”

“At sunset.”

“Will you wake me before you leave? I have walked for two nights without resting. Let me finish my pipe, and I will sleep like a baby. Will you wake me?”

“Of course.”

The traveler finished smoking, put the pipe in his coat, slipped off his heavy wooden shoes, and lay down on his back on the pile of stones. He fell fast asleep immediately.

As the road-mender continued his dusty work, and the hail-clouds moved away to show bright streaks of sky that lit up the landscape with silver gleams, the little man (who now wore a red cap instead of his blue one) was fascinated by the sleeping figure. He looked at the traveler so often that he used his tools automatically and did very little actual work. The tanned face, the shaggy black hair and beard, the coarse red wool cap, the rough mixture of homespun clothes and animal skins, the strong body thinned by starvation, and the tight, desperate look of the lips in sleep filled the road-mender with awe. The traveler had walked a long way; his feet were sore, and his ankles were raw and bleeding. His large wooden shoes, packed with leaves and grass, must have been heavy to drag for so many miles, and his clothes were as full of holes as his body was of sores. Bending down next to him, the road-mender tried to peek at any weapons hidden in his coat, but could see nothing because the man slept with his arms crossed tightly over his chest, as firm as his closed mouth. To the road-mender, fortified towns with their fences, guardhouses, gates, ditches, and drawbridges seemed useless against a man like this. And when he looked up from the sleeper to the horizon, he imagined thousands of similar men, stopped by no obstacles, marching toward targets all over France.

The man slept on, ignoring the hail and the sunshine, the shadows, and the bits of ice on his body that the sun melted into glittering drops, until the sun was low in the west and the sky was glowing. Then, the road-mender gathered his tools, got ready to go down to the village, and woke him up.

“Good!” said the sleeper, sitting up on his elbow. “About six miles past the top of the hill?”

“About that.”

“About that. Good!”

The road-mender walked home, the wind blowing the dust ahead of him. Soon he was at the fountain, squeezing in among the thin cattle brought there to drink, looking as if he were whispering to them just as he was whispering to the rest of the village. Once the villagers had eaten their poor dinner, they did not go to bed as they usually did, but came back outside and stayed there. A strange epidemic of whispering spread among them, and as they gathered in the dark around the fountain, another strange urge made them all look at the sky in the same direction. Monsieur Gabelle, the local official, became nervous. He went out on his rooftop alone and looked in that direction too. He peered down from behind his chimneys at the gloomy faces around the fountain below, and sent a message to the church official who had the keys, telling him they might need to ring the alarm bell soon.

The night grew darker. The trees surrounding the old chateau, which stood isolated and grand, swayed in a rising wind as if threatening the massive, dark building. Rain poured down the two stone terraces and beat against the great door like a messenger trying to wake the people inside. Drafts of wind blew through the hall, rattling the old spears and knives, and swept up the stairs, shaking the curtains of the bed where the late Marquis used to sleep. From the East, West, North, and South, through the woods, four rough, messy figures stepped heavily through the high grass and snapped branches, walking cautiously to meet in the courtyard. Four lights suddenly appeared there, moved off in different directions, and then everything went dark again.

But not for long. Soon, the chateau began to glow with a strange light of its own. A flickering flame danced behind the front of the building, lighting up the arches, balconies, and windows from within. Then the fire rose higher, growing wider and brighter. Soon, flames burst out of twenty large windows, and the stone faces carved on the walls seemed to wake up and stare out from the fire.

A quiet panic arose inside the house among the few servants left behind. A horse was quickly saddled, and someone rode away. Spurring the horse through the dark, splashing in the mud, the rider stopped at the village fountain, the sweating horse panting at Monsieur Gabelle's door. “Help, Gabelle! Help, everyone!” The church alarm bell began to ring urgently, but no help came. The road-mender and two hundred and fifty of his fellow villagers stood by the fountain with their arms crossed, watching the pillar of fire in the sky. “It must be forty feet high,” they said grimly, and none of them moved a muscle to help.

The rider from the chateau, on his sweating horse, clattered out of the village and galloped up the rocky path to the prison on the cliff. At the gate, a group of officers stood watching the fire, and a little distance away, a group of soldiers watched too. “Help, officers! The chateau is on fire! We can save valuable things if we act now! Help!” The officers looked at the soldiers, who just stared at the fire. The officers gave no orders and, shrugging their shoulders and biting their lips, replied, “It must burn.”

As the rider rode back down the hill and through the street, the village was lighting up in celebration. The road-mender and his two hundred and fifty friends, all sharing the same idea, had run into their houses to put candles in every small window pane. Because candles were scarce like everything else, they demanded to borrow them from Monsieur Gabelle. When the official hesitated to give them up, the road-mender—who had once been so obedient—remarked that carriages made great bonfires and carriage-horses would roast nicely.

The chateau was left alone to burn. In the roaring heat of the fire, a scorching wind, looking as if it blew straight from hell, seemed to tear at the building. As the flames flickered, the stone faces carved on the walls looked as if they were crying out in pain. When huge chunks of stone and wood fell, the face with the two dents in the nose was hidden for a moment, then peeked out of the smoke again, looking like the face of the cruel Marquis himself, burning at the stake and fighting the fire.

The chateau burned. The nearest trees caught fire and shriveled up, while trees further away, set on fire by the four mysterious men, surrounded the burning building with a thick forest of smoke. Melted lead and iron bubbled in the marble fountain basin. The water dried up completely. The pointed roofs of the towers melted like ice in the heat, dripping down into four fiery pits. Giant cracks split the solid walls, birds flew in confused circles before falling into the heat, and the four fierce men walked away to the East, West, North, and South along the dark roads, guided by the massive light they had created. In the lit-up village, the people took over the church bell, kicked out the official bell-ringer, and rang the bell in celebration.

Not only that, but the villagers, dizzy from hunger, excitement, and bell-ringing, remembered that Monsieur Gabelle was the one who collected rents and taxes—even though he had collected very little lately. They wanted to speak with him, so they surrounded his house and shouted for him to come out. Monsieur Gabelle quickly locked and barred his doors and sat down to think about what to do. His decision was to climb back onto his roof behind the chimneys. He resolved that if the crowd broke down his door, he—being a small man from the south of France with a stubborn streak—would throw himself off the roof to crush one or two of them on his way down.

Monsieur Gabelle spent a very long night up there, using the burning chateau in the distance as his light, and the pounding on his door and the joyous bell-ringing as his music. Outside his gate, a street lantern hung across the road, and the villagers were dropping hints that they would love to hang him from it. It was a terrifying way to spend a summer night, standing on the edge of death, ready to jump if he had to. But when morning finally came and the villagers' candles burned out, the crowd dispersed, and Monsieur Gabelle was able to climb down, safe for the moment.

Within a hundred miles, by the light of other fires, other officials were not as lucky. That night and over the next few nights, the morning sun found many of them hanging dead over the streets where they had grown up. On the other side, some villagers were less fortunate than the road-mender and his friends, as soldiers and officials caught them and hanged them instead. But the mysterious men continued their march to the East, West, North, and South. No matter who was hanged, the fires of rebellion kept burning, and no official could figure out how to put them out.

CHAPTER XXIV: Drawn to the Loadstone Rock

Three years of storm passed with these rising fires and rising seas—the steady earth shaken by the rushes of an angry ocean that never ebbed but kept rising higher and higher, terrifying and amazing everyone on the shore. During this time, three more birthdays of little Lucie were woven by the golden thread into the peaceful life of her home.

Many a night and day, the family listened to the echoes in the corner of their house, their hearts sinking when they heard the sound of marching feet. In their minds, those footsteps had become the steps of a rioting mob under a red flag, their country declared in danger, transformed into wild beasts by a terrible spell of long-standing anger.

The French aristocrats, as a class, had run away from the fact that they were no longer wanted in France, where they faced the danger of being kicked out of the country and of life itself. Like the legendary peasant who worked hard to summon the Devil but was so terrified when he appeared that he fled without asking a single question, the aristocrats, after spending years doing everything to bring about disaster, ran away in fear the moment they saw the monster they had created.

The glittering court of the King was gone, or else it would have been targeted by a storm of bullets. It had never been a wise or observant court anyway—it had long been blinded by pride, luxury, and ignorance—but now it was completely gone. The royal court, from its inner circle to its outer rings of lies, corruption, and plotting, had vanished. The monarchy was gone, having been besieged in the palace and suspended, according to the latest news.

By August of 1792, the French aristocrats were scattered far and wide.

Naturally, the main gathering place for these exiled French lords in London was Tellson's Bank. Just as ghosts are said to haunt the places they frequented in life, these penniless aristocrats hung around the place where their money used to be kept. It was also the spot where the most reliable news from France arrived quickest. Tellson's was very generous to its old customers who had lost their wealth. Furthermore, the nobles who had foreseen the storm and sent their money to Tellson's in advance could be found there, helping their needy friends. Additionally, almost every new traveler arriving from France reported to Tellson's to share their news. For all these reasons, Tellson's was the central hub for French news, and so many people came to ask questions that the bank sometimes wrote the latest updates on a paper and posted them in the windows for anyone walking by Temple Bar to read.

On a hot, misty afternoon, Mr. Lorry sat at his desk while Charles Darnay stood leaning on it, talking to him quietly. The gloomy room once used for private meetings was now packed with people exchanging news. It was about half an hour before closing time.

“Even though you are the most youthful-spirited man alive,” Charles Darnay said, hesitating a bit, “I still have to suggest to you—”

“I understand. You think I am too old?” said Mr. Lorry.

“Bad weather, a long journey, unreliable transport, a chaotic country, and a city that might not even be safe for you.”

“My dear Charles,” Mr. Lorry said with cheerful confidence, “those are the exact reasons I must go, not stay. I will be safe enough. No one will bother an old man of nearly eighty when there are so many other people worth targeting. As for the chaos, if the city weren't chaotic, we wouldn't need to send someone from our London office to our Paris branch who knows the place and the business, and whom Tellson's trusts completely. As for the difficult travel, the long journey, and the winter weather, if I am not willing to face a few hardships for Tellson's after all these years, who should be?”

“I wish I were going myself,” Charles Darnay said restlessly, as if thinking aloud.

“Really! You are a fine one to complain and give advice!” Mr. Lorry exclaimed. “You wish you were going? A born Frenchman? You are a very wise adviser indeed!”

“My dear Mr. Lorry, it is precisely because I am a Frenchman that the thought has crossed my mind so often, though I didn't mean to say it aloud. Having felt sympathy for the poor people and having given up my property to them,” he spoke thoughtfully, “I cannot help but think that they might listen to me. I might be able to persuade them to show some mercy. Only last night, after you left, I was talking to Lucie—”

“Talking to Lucie,” Mr. Lorry repeated. “Yes! I'm surprised you're not ashamed to mention her name while wishing you were going to France at a time like this!”

“Well, I am not going,” Charles Darnay said with a smile. “It is more important that you are.”

“And I really am. The truth is, my dear Charles,” Mr. Lorry said, looking toward the other bank managers and lowering his voice, “you have no idea how hard it is to do business there, or how much danger our records and papers are in. God only knows what terrible trouble it would cause for many people if our documents were seized or destroyed. And that could happen at any moment—who knows if Paris will be set on fire today or looted tomorrow! Choosing the key papers quickly, burying them, or hiding them safely is something only I can really do without wasting time. Should I refuse, when Tellson's needs me—Tellson's, who has paid my salary for sixty years—just because my joints are a little stiff? Why, I'm a boy compared to half the old men working here!”

“I really admire your youthful courage, Mr. Lorry.”

“Nonsense, sir! And, my dear Charles,” Mr. Lorry said, looking back at the bank managers, “you must remember that getting anything out of Paris right now is almost impossible. Just today, papers and valuables were brought to us here—I tell you this in strict confidence, as it's not professional to gossip, even to you—by the strangest messengers, who were in constant danger of losing their heads as they crossed the borders. Normally, our mail would come and go as easily as in England, but now, everything is blocked.”

“And are you really leaving tonight?”

“I really am, because the situation has become too urgent to wait.”

“Are you going alone?”

“Many people were suggested to me, but I refused them all. I plan to take Jerry. Jerry has been my Sunday night bodyguard for a long time, and I am used to him. No one will suspect Jerry of being anything other than an English bulldog, ready to attack anyone who touches his master.”

“I must say again that I truly admire your youthfulness and courage.”

“And I must say again, nonsense! Once I finish this little job, maybe I will accept Tellson's offer to retire and live comfortably. That will be the time to think about getting old.”

This conversation took place at Mr. Lorry's desk, with French aristocrats gathering just a few feet away, boasting about how they would soon get revenge on the common people. It was typical of the refugees, and of the conservative English public, to talk about this terrible Revolution as if it were a disaster that had come out of nowhere—as if nothing had ever been done to cause it. They ignored the fact that anyone who had observed the starving millions in France and the greed of the rulers had seen it coming years ago and warned about it in plain words. Hearing these empty boasts and their unrealistic plans to restore the old, broken system was hard to endure without speaking up. All this chatter, combined with a deep uneasiness in his own mind, made Charles Darnay feel very restless.

Among the talkers was Stryver, the ambitious lawyer, who was speaking loudly to the aristocrats. He was explaining his ideas for blowing up the French peasants and wiping them off the face of the earth, which was about as realistic as trying to catch eagles by putting salt on their tails. Darnay listened to him with disgust. He was torn between walking away to avoid hearing any more, and staying to speak up, when the next event began to unfold.

One of the bank managers came over to Mr. Lorry and laid a dirty, unopened letter on the desk, asking if he had found any trace of the person to whom it was addressed. The manager placed the letter so close to Darnay that he could read the address—and he recognized it immediately because it was his real name. The address read:

“Very urgent. To Monsieur, formerly the Marquis St. Evremonde, of France. Care of Messrs. Tellson and Co., Bankers, London, England.”

On the morning of his wedding, Doctor Manette had earnestly requested Charles Darnay to keep the secret of his real family name between the two of them. No one else knew it. Even his wife had no idea, and Mr. Lorry certainly did not know.

“No,” Mr. Lorry replied to the manager. “I have asked almost everyone here, and no one knows where this gentleman can be found.”

As the bank's closing hour approached, the crowd of talkers began to move past Mr. Lorry's desk. He held up the letter to show them. One angry refugee after another looked at it, and each had something insulting to say, in French or in English, about the missing Marquis.

“He is the nephew—or at least the worthless successor—of the refined Marquis who was murdered,” said one. “I am glad to say I never knew him.”

“A coward who abandoned his position years ago,” said another—a man who had himself escaped Paris hidden under a load of hay, upside down and half suffocated.

“Infected with those new revolutionary ideas,” said a third, looking at the address through his glass as he passed. “He opposed the late Marquis, abandoned his estate when he inherited it, and left it to the mob. I hope they reward him now as he deserves.”

“Hey?” cried the loud Stryver. “Did he really? Is that the kind of guy he is? Let me see his shameful name. Damn him!”

Darnay, unable to control himself any longer, touched Mr. Stryver on the shoulder and said:

“I know him.”

“Do you, by Jove?” said Stryver. “I am sorry to hear that.”

“Why?”

“Why, Mr. Darnay? Did you hear what he did? Don't ask why in times like these.”

“But I do ask why.”

“Then I tell you again, Mr. Darnay, I am sorry. I am sorry to hear you asking such strange questions. Here is a man who, influenced by the most wicked and ungodly ideas ever known, gave up his property to the worst scum of the earth who murder people wholesale. And you ask me why I'm sorry that a teacher of youth knows him? Well, I will tell you. I'm sorry because I think such a scoundrel is contagious. That's why.”

Remembering his secret, Darnay controlled himself with great effort and said, “You might not understand the gentleman.”

“I know how to put _you_ in a corner, Mr. Darnay,” Stryver bullied, “and I'll do it. If that fellow is a gentleman, then I _don't_ understand him. You can tell him so, with my compliments. You can also tell him from me that since he gave up his wealth and status to this murderous mob, I wonder he isn't leading them. But no, gentlemen,” Stryver said, looking around and snapping his fingers, “I know a bit about human nature, and I tell you that you'll never find a guy like him trusting himself to the mercy of those people. No, gentlemen; he'll run away at the first sign of trouble and hide.”

With those words and a final snap of his fingers, Mr. Stryver pushed his way out into Fleet Street, to the general approval of the listeners. Mr. Lorry and Charles Darnay were left alone at the desk as everyone else left the bank.

“Will you take care of this letter?” Mr. Lorry asked. “Do you know where to deliver it?”

“I do.”

“Will you explain to him that we think it was sent here in the hope that we would know where to find him, and that it has been sitting here for a while?”

“I will. Are you leaving for Paris from here?”

“From here, at eight o'clock.”

“I will come back to see you off.”

Feeling very uncomfortable with himself, Stryver, and everyone else, Darnay walked back to the quiet of the Temple, opened the letter, and read it. This is what it said:

“Abbaye Prison, Paris.”

“June 21, 1792. “MONSIEUR, FORMERLY THE MARQUIS.

“After being in danger for a long time at the hands of the villagers, I have been arrested with great violence and insult, and forced to walk all the way to Paris. I suffered terribly on the road. Not only that, but my house has been completely destroyed and flattened to the ground.

“The crime for which I am locked up, Monsieur, and for which I will be tried and executed (unless you generously help me), is, they say, treason against the people because I managed the estate of an exile. It does no good for me to tell them that I acted in their favor, not against them, following your orders. It does no good for me to explain that, before the exile properties were seized, I had canceled the taxes they stopped paying, collected no rents, and took no legal action against anyone. The only answer I get is that I worked for an exile, and they ask: where is that exile?

“Ah, most gracious Monsieur, where is that exile? I cry out in my sleep, asking where he is. I ask Heaven: will he not come to save me? There is no answer. Ah, Monsieur, I send my desperate cry across the sea, hoping it might reach your ears through the famous bank of Tellson which is known in Paris!

“For the love of Heaven, of justice, of generosity, and for the honor of your noble family name, I beg you, Monsieur, to help and free me. My only crime is that I have been faithful to you. Oh, Monsieur, I pray that you will be faithful to me!

“From this horrible prison, where every hour brings me closer to death, I send you, Monsieur, my sad and miserable respects.

“Your suffering servant,

“Gabelle.”

The quiet anxiety in Darnay's mind flared into intense life upon reading this letter. The danger facing an old and loyal servant, whose only crime was loyalty to him and his family, felt like a direct accusation. As he walked back and forth in the Temple thinking about what to do, he was so ashamed that he almost hid his face from passersby.

He knew very well that in his horror at the family's past cruelty and bad reputation, in his hatred for his uncle, and in his desire to have nothing to do with the estate he was supposed to inherit, he had acted imperfectly. He knew that because of his love for Lucie, his decision to give up his title and property—though not a new idea to him—had been rushed and incomplete. He knew he should have systematically managed the transition and supervised it, and though he had intended to, he had never actually done it.

The happiness of his new English home, the need to stay busy, and the rapid pace of events in France—where the news of one week destroyed the plans of the week before, and the following week changed everything again—had overwhelmed him. He had let things drift, feeling uneasy but not doing anything to stop it. He had waited for the right moment to take action, but the situation had changed so fast that the opportunity had passed. Now, the nobles were fleeing France along every road, their properties were being seized and destroyed, and their very names were being erased—facts he knew just as well as the new French authorities who might accuse him of abandonment.

Yet, he had oppressed no one and imprisoned no one. Far from demanding high taxes or rents, he had given them up voluntarily, started a new life in England with no special help, built his own career, and earned his own living. Monsieur Gabelle had managed the poor estate based on Darnay's written instructions to spare the people and give them whatever they could—such as winter firewood and summer crops. Gabelle would surely use these instructions to prove his innocence, so the truth would have to come out.

This supported the desperate decision Charles Darnay was starting to make: he would go to Paris.

Yes. Like the sailor in the old story, the winds and currents had driven him into the magnetic pull of the Loadstone Rock, and it was drawing him in. He had to go. Every thought pushed him faster and more steadily toward this dangerous attraction. His hidden guilt had been that bad deeds were being done in France by bad people, while he—who knew he had better intentions—was not there to try to stop the bloodshed and call for mercy. With this guilt troubling him, he had compared himself to the brave old Mr. Lorry, whose sense of duty was so strong. Right after that embarrassing comparison, he had been stung by the insults of the refugees and Stryver's coarse sneers. And finally, Gabelle's letter had arrived: an appeal from an innocent prisoner in danger of death, calling on his justice, honor, and family name.

His decision was made. He had to go to Paris.

Yes, the Loadstone Rock was pulling him, and he had to sail on until he reached it. He didn't see the danger. He believed that his good intentions, even if incomplete, would be recognized and appreciated in France once he arrived to explain them. Then, the idealistic dream of doing good—which often misleads well-meaning people—filled his mind, and he even imagined himself having the influence to guide this wild, raging Revolution.

As he walked back and forth, he decided that neither Lucie nor her father must know about his plans until he had left. Lucie should be spared the pain of a long goodbye, and her father, who always avoided thinking about his painful past in France, should only hear about the journey after it had already begun, to avoid any suspense or worry. Darnay did not admit to himself how much of his delay in sorting out his French estate had been due to his fear of upsetting Doctor Manette. But that had certainly played a part in his inaction.

He walked back and forth, his mind racing, until it was time to return to Tellson's and say goodbye to Mr. Lorry. As soon as he got to Paris, he would meet his old friend, but he could say nothing about his plans now.

A carriage with fresh horses was ready at the bank door, and Jerry stood booted and ready.

“I delivered that letter,” Charles Darnay said to Mr. Lorry. “I didn't want you to carry a written reply, but perhaps you can deliver a verbal message?”

“I will gladly do that,” Mr. Lorry said, “if it isn't dangerous.”

“Not at all. Though it is for a prisoner in the Abbaye.”

“What is his name?” Mr. Lorry asked, opening his notebook.

“Gabelle.”

“Gabelle. And what is the message for poor Gabelle in prison?”

“Simply: ‘He received the letter and will come.’”

“Did he mention when?”

“He will start his journey tomorrow night.”

“Did he mention any name?”

“No.”

He helped Mr. Lorry wrap himself in several coats and cloaks, and walked out with him from the warm bank into the misty air of Fleet Street. “Give my love to Lucie and little Lucie,” Mr. Lorry said as they parted, “and take good care of them until I return.” Charles Darnay shook his head and smiled doubtfully as the carriage drove off.

That night—it was August fourteenth—he stayed up late and wrote two emotional letters. One was to Lucie, explaining the heavy duty that forced him to go to Paris and explaining in detail why he was confident he would not be in any personal danger. The other was to the Doctor, entrusting Lucie and their child to his care and repeating the same strong reassurances. He promised both of them that he would send letters proving his safety as soon as he arrived.

It had been a difficult day, spending it with his family while keeping this major secret from them for the first time in their marriage. It was hard to keep up the innocent deception, which they did not suspect at all. But looking affectionately at his happy, busy wife, he remained resolved not to tell her what was happening (though he had been tempted to, since it felt so strange to do anything without her quiet support). The day went by quickly. Early in the evening, he hugged her and their daughter, pretending he would be back soon—making up excuse to go out, having already hidden a small suitcase of clothes. And so he stepped out into the thick mist of the heavy streets with an even heavier heart.

The unseen force was drawing him in quickly now, and all the winds and tides were pushing him directly toward it. He left his two letters with a trusted messenger, to be delivered exactly half an hour before midnight and no earlier. Then he took a horse for Dover and began his journey. “For the love of Heaven, of justice, of generosity, and for the honor of your noble name!” was the poor prisoner's cry that he repeated to strengthen his failing courage, as he left everything he loved behind and floated toward the Loadstone Rock.

The end of the second book.

Book the Third: the Track of a Storm

CHAPTER I: In Secret

The traveler made slow progress as he journeyed from England toward Paris in the autumn of 1792. Even if the fallen King of France were still on his throne, the bad roads, poor carriages, and weak horses would have delayed him. But the revolutionary times brought far worse obstacles. Every town gate and village tollhouse was guarded by a band of citizen-patriots holding loaded muskets, ready to shoot at any moment. They stopped everyone, cross-examined them, checked their papers, searched for their names on custom lists, and turned them back, sent them forward, or arrested them, depending on whatever their random judgment decided was best for the new Republic of Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death.

After only a few miles in France, Charles Darnay realized that there was no turning back for him along these country roads until he was officially declared a good citizen in Paris. Whatever happened, he had to keep moving forward. Every small village he passed through and every gate that closed behind him felt like another iron door locking him away from England. The constant surveillance surrounded him so completely that he felt as if he were being carried to his destination in a cage.

This constant watchfulness stopped him dozens of times during each leg of the trip. Soldiers would ride after him to bring him back, ride ahead to wait for him, or ride alongside him to keep him under guard. He had been traveling through France for days when he finally went to bed exhausted in a small town on the highway, still a long way from Paris.

Only showing Gabelle's letter from the Abbaye Prison had allowed him to get this far. The trouble he faced at the guardhouse in this small town made him realize that his journey had reached a turning point. Therefore, he was not surprised when he was awakened in the middle of the night at the small inn where he was being kept until morning.

He was woken up by a nervous local official and three armed patriots wearing rough red caps, who sat right on his bed with pipes in their mouths.

“Emigrant,” the official said, “I am sending you to Paris under guard.”

“Citizen, I want nothing more than to get to Paris, though I could do without the guard.”

“Silence!” growled one of the men in a red cap, hitting the bed cover with the butt of his gun. “Quiet, aristocrat!”

“It is just as the patriot says,” the nervous official remarked. “You are an aristocrat, so you must have a guard—and you have to pay for it.”

“I have no choice,” said Charles Darnay.

“Choice! Listen to him!” cried the scowling patriot. “As if it isn't a favor to be protected from being hanged from a street lamp!”

“It is always as the patriot says,” the official observed. “Get up and get dressed, emigrant.”

Darnay did as he was told and was taken back to the guardhouse, where other patriots in red caps were smoking, drinking, and sleeping by a campfire. He paid a high price for his guard, and they set out together on the muddy, wet roads at three o'clock in the morning.

His guard consisted of two mounted patriots wearing red caps and ribbons showing the revolutionary colors, armed with muskets and swords, riding on either side of him.

Darnay controlled his own horse, but a loose rope was tied to his bridle, with the other end wrapped around the wrist of one of the guards. In this manner, they set out in the pouring rain, trotting over the bumpy town pavement and out onto the muddy roads. They traveled this way all the miles to Paris, changing only their horses and their speed.

They traveled by night, stopping an hour or two after dawn and hiding out until twilight fell. The guards were so poorly dressed that they wrapped straw around their bare legs and placed bundles of straw on their shoulders to keep the rain off. Despite the discomfort of traveling this way, and the danger of one guard being constantly drunk and carrying his gun carelessly, Darnay did not let himself panic. He reasoned that his specific case had not been heard yet, and the facts, which Gabelle could confirm, had not yet been presented.

But when they reached the town of Beauvais in the evening, when the streets were crowded with people, he had to admit that things looked very bad. An angry crowd gathered to watch him get off his horse at the station, and many voices shouted, “Down with the emigrant!”

He paused as he was about to get off his horse and, staying in the saddle as the safest place, said:

“An emigrant, my friends? Don't you see that I came back to France of my own free will?”

“You are a cursed emigrant,” cried a blacksmith, pushing through the crowd toward him with a hammer in his hand, “and you are a cursed aristocrat!”

The postmaster stepped between the blacksmith and Darnay's horse, saying calmly, “Leave him alone, leave him alone! He will be judged in Paris.”

“Judged!” the blacksmith repeated, swinging his hammer. “Yes! And condemned as a traitor!” The crowd roared in agreement.

Stopping the postmaster, who was trying to lead his horse into the yard (while the drunken guard watched calmly from his saddle, holding the rope wrapped around his wrist), Darnay spoke as loudly as he could:

“Friends, you are mistaken. I am not a traitor.”

“He lies!” cried the blacksmith. “He is a traitor under the new law. His life belongs to the people. His cursed life is not his own!”

Just as Darnay saw the crowd prepare to rush him, the postmaster pulled his horse into the yard, the guards followed close behind, and the postmaster shut and locked the creaky double gates. The blacksmith hit the gates with his hammer, and the crowd groaned in disappointment, but they did not try to break in.

“What is this new law the blacksmith mentioned?” Darnay asked the postmaster, after thanking him.

“It is a law to sell the property of all exiles.”

“When was it passed?”

“On the fourteenth.”

“The very day I left England!”

“Everyone says it is just the first of many laws. They say there will be others—if they aren't passed already—banishing all emigrants, and sentencing anyone who returns to death. That is why he said your life is not your own.”

“But those laws haven't actually been passed yet, have they?”

“How should I know!” the postmaster said, shrugging. “Maybe they have, or maybe they will. It is all the same. What can you do?”

They rested on some straw in a loft until the middle of the night, then rode on while the town was asleep. Among the strange changes that made this ride feel like a dream, the rarest thing seemed to be sleep. After riding for miles over lonely, dark roads, they would come across small villages that were completely lit up. In the middle of the night, they would see groups of villagers holding hands and dancing around a dry "Liberty Tree" or gathering to sing revolutionary songs. Fortunately, the people of Beauvais were sleeping, allowing them to pass through quietly into the cold, wet night. They rode past barren fields that had grown no food that year, ruined houses blackened by fire, and patrols of patriots who suddenly jumped out to stop them on the road.

At dawn, they finally arrived at the gates of Paris. The barrier was closed and heavily guarded when they approached.

“Where are this prisoner's papers?” demanded a stern official who came out of the guardhouse.

Stunned by the word "prisoner," Charles Darnay spoke up, explaining that he was a free traveler and a French citizen traveling with a guard that he had paid for because of the chaotic state of the country.

“Where,” the official repeated, completely ignoring him, “are this prisoner's papers?”

The drunken guard pulled the papers out of his cap and handed them over. Reading Gabelle's letter, the official looked surprised and stared closely at Darnay.

Without another word, the official went inside the guardroom, leaving them waiting on their horses outside the gate. During this tense wait, Darnay noticed that the gate was guarded by a mix of soldiers and patriots, with the patriots far outnumbering the soldiers. He saw that while it was easy for carts and peasants to enter the city, it was very difficult for anyone to leave. A large crowd of people, animals, and carriages waited to exit, but the security check was so strict that they could only trickle through. Some people knew they would have to wait so long that they lay down on the ground to sleep or smoke, while others chatted or walked around. Everyone, both men and women, wore red caps and revolutionary ribbons.

After Darnay had waited in his saddle for about half an hour, the official returned and ordered the guards to open the gate. He gave the two guards a receipt for Darnay and told Darnay to get off his horse. He did so, and the two guards took his tired horse, turned around, and rode back without entering Paris.

He followed his guide into a guardroom that smelled of cheap wine and tobacco, where soldiers and patriots in various states of sleep and drunkenness were lounging around. The light in the room, coming partly from dying oil lamps and partly from the cloudy morning, was dim and gloomy. Several record books lay open on a desk, where a rough, dark-looking officer sat in charge.

“Citizen Defarge,” the officer said to Darnay's guide, picking up a slip of paper. “Is this the emigrant Evremonde?”

“This is the man.”

“Your age, Evremonde?”

“Thirty-seven.”

“Married, Evremonde?”

“Yes.”

“Where were you married?”

“In England.”

“Of course. Where is your wife, Evremonde?”

“In England.”

“Of course. You are sent, Evremonde, to the prison of La Force.”

“Good heavens!” Darnay exclaimed. “Under what law, and for what crime?”

The officer looked up from his paper for a moment.

“We have new laws, Evremonde, and new crimes, since you were last here,” he said with a cold smile, and continued writing.

“Please understand that I came here voluntarily, responding to the letter of a fellow countryman which you have there. All I want is the chance to explain my case. Is that not my right?”

“Emigrants have no rights, Evremonde,” was the flat reply. The officer finished writing, read it over, dried the ink with sand, and handed the paper to Defarge, saying, “In secret.”

Defarge gestured with the paper that the prisoner had to follow him. Darnay obeyed, escorted by two armed patriots.

“Are you the one,” Defarge asked quietly as they walked down the steps into Paris, “who married the daughter of Doctor Manette, who was once a prisoner in the Bastille?”

“Yes,” Darnay replied, looking at him in surprise.

“My name is Defarge. I own a wine shop in the Saint Antoine district. Perhaps you have heard of me.”

“My wife came to your shop to find her father? Yes!”

The word “wife” seemed to upset Defarge, who said with sudden anger, “In the name of that sharp new machine called the Guillotine, why on earth did you come back to France?”

“You heard me explain why just a minute ago. Don't you believe it is the truth?”

“It is a bad truth for you,” Defarge said, frowning and looking straight ahead.

“I am completely lost here. Everything is so different, so sudden, and so unfair. Will you help me a little?”

“No.” Defarge spoke without looking at him.

“Will you answer just one question?”

“Maybe, depending on what it is. Ask it.”

“In this prison where I am being sent so unfairly, will I be allowed to communicate with the outside world?”

“You will see.”

“I won't be buried there forever without any chance to explain my case, will I?”

“You will see. But what of it? Many others have been buried in worse prisons before now.”

“But never by me, Citizen Defarge.”

Defarge gave him a dark look and kept walking in silence. As Defarge remained silent, Darnay lost hope of changing his mind. So, he quickly added:

“It is extremely important—and you must know how important it is—that I let Mr. Lorry of Tellson's Bank, who is currently in Paris, know that I have been put in La Force prison. Will you get that message to him?”

“I will do nothing for you,” Defarge replied stubbornly. “My duty is to my country and the People. I am sworn to serve them against you. I will do nothing for you.”

Darnay knew it was useless to beg, and his pride was hurt as well. As they walked in silence, he noticed that the citizens were completely used to seeing prisoners led through the streets. Even children barely looked at him. A few people turned to look, and some shook their fists at him because he was an aristocrat, but otherwise, seeing a well-dressed man going to prison was as ordinary as seeing a worker going to a job. In a narrow, dirty street, an angry speaker on a stool was shouting to a crowd about the crimes of the king and the royal family. The few words Darnay caught made him realize for the first time that the king had been imprisoned and that all foreign ambassadors had left Paris. He had heard nothing on his journey except at Beauvais, because his guards and the security checks had kept him completely isolated.

He knew now that he was in far greater danger than he had realized when leaving England, and that the threats around him were growing quickly. He admitted to himself that he would not have made this trip if he had known what would happen. Yet, his fears were not as dark as they might seem to us looking back. The future was scary, but it was still unknown, and its mystery left room for hope. He had no idea that a horrible massacre, lasting days and nights, would soon stain the city with blood. The name of the Guillotine was barely known to him or to most people. The terrible acts that were about to happen were probably not even imagined yet by the people who would commit them. How could such horrors exist in the mind of a gentle man?

He expected unfair treatment, hardships, and the painful separation from his wife and child, but he did not fear anything worse than that. With these heavy thoughts, he arrived at the gates of La Force prison.

A man with a red, swollen face opened the small gate, and Defarge announced, “The Emigrant Evremonde.”

“What the devil! How many more of them are coming!” exclaimed the bloated man.

Defarge took his receipt without answering the jailer, and left with the two patriots.

“What the devil, I say again!” the jailer said to his wife. “How many more!”

Having no answer, his wife just said, “We must be patient, my dear!” Three assistant jailers who came in when she rang a bell agreed with her, and one added, “For the love of Liberty!”—which sounded very out of place in that grim prison.

La Force was a dark, filthy, and gloomy prison that smelled terribly of sweaty, locked-up bodies. It is amazing how quickly the bad smell of crowded prisoners develops in places that are not kept clean.

“In secret too,” grumbled the jailer, looking at the paper. “As if my prison wasn't already packed to bursting!”

He angrily filed the paper, and Darnay was left waiting for half an hour. Darnay walked back and forth in the arched stone room or rested on a stone bench, while the chief jailer and his assistants stared at him to memorize his face.

“Come!” the chief jailer said finally, taking his keys. “Come with me, emigrant.”

Darnay followed him through the dark prison corridors and up staircases, with many heavy doors slamming and locking behind them. Finally, they entered a large, low room with arched ceilings that was crowded with male and female prisoners. The women sat at a long table reading, writing, knitting, sewing, and embroidering, while most of the men stood behind their chairs or walked around the room.

Thinking that prisoners must be criminals, Darnay shrank back at first. But the strangest part of his journey was that all of them stood up to welcome him with the most polite, elegant manners of high society.

These refined manners looked so strange in the dark, dirty prison that Darnay felt as if he were standing among the dead. They seemed like ghosts—the ghost of beauty, the ghost of elegance, the ghost of pride, the ghost of humor, the ghost of youth, and the ghost of old age—all waiting to be sent away from this miserable shore, looking at him with eyes changed by their imprisonment.

He stood frozen. The rough jailers looked incredibly crude next to the sad mothers, young girls, and elegant women in the room. This complete reversal of normal life made the scene feel even more unreal. Surely they were all ghosts, and his long, strange journey was just a fever dream that had brought him to the underworld.

“In the name of your fellow prisoners,” said an elegant gentleman, stepping forward, “I have the honor of welcoming you to La Force, and of offering our sympathy for the misfortune that brought you here. We hope you will be released soon. It would be rude to ask elsewhere, but here it is normal: what is your name and background?”

Charles Darnay shook off his shock and gave them his name and details as politely as he could.

“I hope,” the gentleman said, watching the chief jailer walk across the room, “that you are not placed 'in secret'?”

“I don't know what that means, but I heard them use those words.”

“Ah, what a pity! We are so sorry to hear that. But take courage; several of us were kept in solitary confinement at first, and it didn't last long.” Then he announced to the room, raising his voice, “I regret to inform everyone—he is in secret.”

A murmur of sympathy spread through the room as Darnay walked toward a barred door where the jailer was waiting. Many voices, especially the gentle voices of the women, offered him encouragement. He turned at the door to thank them, but the jailer shut the gate, and the ghostly crowd vanished from his sight forever.

The door opened to a stone staircase going up. After climbing forty steps (which Darnay had already counted), the jailer opened a low black door, and they entered a solitary cell. It was cold and damp, but not completely dark.

“This is yours,” said the jailer.

“Why am I locked up alone?”

“How should I know!”

“Can I buy a pen, ink, and paper?”

“Those are not my orders. Someone will visit you later, and you can ask then. For now, you can buy food and nothing else.”

The cell contained a chair, a table, and a straw mattress. As the jailer inspected the room and the walls before leaving, Darnay leaned against the wall and had a strange thought: the jailer's swollen face and body made him look like a drowned corpse filled with water. Once the jailer left, Darnay thought, “Now I am left here as if I were dead.” Looking down at the mattress, he felt sick and thought, “And the bugs crawling here are like the worms that eat a body after death.”

“Five paces by four and a half, five paces by four and a half, five paces by four and a half.” The prisoner paced back and forth in his cell, measuring the room over and over, while the distant roar of the city sounded like muffled drums mixed with shouting voices. “He made shoes, he made shoes, he made shoes.” Darnay measured the cell again, walking faster to try to block out that repetitive thought. “The ghosts who vanished when the gate shut. There was one woman dressed in black, leaning near a window, with light shining on her golden hair, and she looked like... Let's ride on again, for God's sake, through the lit-up villages where everyone is awake! He made shoes, he made shoes, he made shoes. Five paces by four and a half.” With these random thoughts spinning in his mind, the prisoner walked faster and faster, stubbornly counting his steps. The roar of the city outside still sounded like muffled drums, but now he imagined hearing the cries of voices he knew in the noise.

CHAPTER II: The Grindstone

Tellson’s Bank in Paris was located in a wing of a large house, accessible through a courtyard and shut off from the street by a high wall and a strong gate. The house belonged to a great nobleman who had lived there until he fled the revolution, disguised in his cook's clothes, and crossed the border. Running away like a wild animal fleeing hunters, he was still the same proud lord who once required three strong men besides that cook just to prepare his morning chocolate.

With the lord gone, and his three servants clearing their consciences of serving him by declaring themselves ready to cut his throat for the new Republic of Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death, the nobleman's house was first seized and then taken by the government. Everything moved so fast, and new laws were passed so quickly, that by the night of September third, revolutionary officials were occupying the house, marking it with the tri-color flag, and drinking brandy in its elegant rooms.

If the London office of Tellson's had looked like the Paris branch, it would have driven the British managers crazy and forced them into bankruptcy. For what would serious, respectable British businessmen think of orange trees in boxes in the courtyard, or a statue of Cupid over the counter? Yet those things existed in Paris. Tellson's had painted over the Cupid, but it could still be seen on the ceiling, aiming its bow at money from morning to night. In serious London, such a decoration, along with a curtained alcove in the back, a mirror on the wall, and young clerks who danced in public, would have spelled ruin. Yet the French branch of Tellson's got along with these features just fine, and as long as times were stable, no one had been scared into withdrawing their money.

No one that night could say how much money would be withdrawn from Tellson's from now on, or how much would lie there lost and forgotten; what gold and jewels would tarnish in the bank's vaults while their owners rotted in prisons or died violent deaths; or how many accounts would never be settled in this world. Mr. Jarvis Lorry couldn't answer these questions either, though he was thinking deeply about them. He sat by a newly lit wood fire—for the poor harvest year had brought an early winter cold—and his honest face showed a deep shadow of horror that was not caused by the hanging lamp or any reflection in the room.

In his loyalty to the bank, Mr. Lorry lived in rooms inside the building, having become as much a part of the place as ivy on a wall. It happened that his rooms were somewhat protected because the patriots had taken over the main building, but the honest old gentleman never thought about his own safety. He only cared about doing his duty. On the opposite side of the courtyard, under a roofed walkway, was a parking area for carriages, where some of the nobleman's carriages still sat. Two large, burning torches were tied to the pillars, and in their light, out in the open air, stood a large grindstone. It was a rough machine that looked like it had been hurriedly brought there from a nearby blacksmith's shop. Standing up and looking out the window at these objects, Mr. Lorry shivered and went back to his seat by the fire. He had opened both the glass window and the wooden shutter outside, closed them both again, and shuddered from head to toe.

From the streets outside the high wall and the gate came the usual night sounds of the city, but with a strange, eerie tone now and then, as if terrible, unusual noises were rising up to the sky.

“Thank God,” said Mr. Lorry, folding his hands, “that no one I love is in this dreadful town tonight. May God have mercy on everyone in danger!”

Soon after, the gate bell rang, and he thought, “They have come back!” and sat listening. But there was no loud crowd rushing into the courtyard as he had expected; he heard the gate shut again, and everything went quiet.

His nervousness and fear made him anxious about the bank's security, which was a natural worry in such times. The bank was well guarded, and he stood up to check on the guards when his door suddenly opened and two people rushed in. At the sight of them, he stepped back in absolute shock.

It was Lucie and her father! Lucie held her arms out to him with a look of intense, desperate seriousness, looking as if her face had been molded just to show this strength at this single moment of her life.

“What is this?” cried Mr. Lorry, breathless and confused. “What is the matter? Lucie! Manette! What happened? What brought you here? What is going on?”

Staring at him wildly, pale and terrified, she gasped in his arms, pleading, “Oh, my dear friend! My husband!”

“Your husband, Lucie?”

“Charles.”

“What about Charles?”

“Here.”

“Here, in Paris?”

“He has been here a few days—three or four, I don't know, I can't think straight. A generous mission brought him here without our knowledge. He was stopped at the border and sent to prison.”

The old man let out a cry he couldn't stop. Almost at the same instant, the gate bell rang again, and the sound of many feet and voices poured into the courtyard.

“What is that noise?” the Doctor asked, turning toward the window.

“Don't look!” Mr. Lorry cried. “Don't look out! Manette, for your life, don't touch the shutter!”

The Doctor turned, with his hand on the window lock, and said with a calm, confident smile:

“My dear friend, I am safe in this city. I was a prisoner in the Bastille. There is no patriot in Paris—or in all of France—who would hurt me if they knew that. Instead, they would hug me or carry me in celebration. My past suffering has given me a status that brought us through the border, got us news of Charles, and brought us here. I knew it would be this way. I knew I could save Charles from danger, and I told Lucie so. What is that noise?” His hand went back to the window.

“Don't look!” Mr. Lorry cried out in desperation. “No, Lucie, my dear, don't you look either!” He wrapped his arm around her and held her tight. “Don't be so scared, my love. I swear to you that I don't know of any harm coming to Charles, and I had no idea he was even in this dangerous city. Which prison is he in?”

“La Force!”

“La Force! Lucie, my child, if you have ever been brave and helpful—and you have always been both—you must calm down now and do exactly as I tell you. More depends on this than you can imagine. You cannot help him by doing anything tonight; you cannot go outside. I say this because what I must ask you to do for Charles's sake is the hardest thing of all: you must be obedient, still, and quiet. Let me put you in a back room. You must leave your father and me alone for two minutes. Since this is a matter of life and death, you must not delay.”

“I will do as you say. I can see in your face that you know I have no other choice. I know you are a true friend.”

The old man kissed her, hurried her into his bedroom, and locked the door. Then he ran back to the Doctor, opened the window, pushed the shutter slightly open, put his hand on the Doctor's arm, and looked out with him into the courtyard.

They saw a crowd of about forty or fifty men and women. The patriots occupying the building had let them through the gate, and they had rushed in to use the grindstone. It had been set up there for them, as it was a convenient and private spot.

But what terrifying workers they were, doing such a terrifying job!

The grindstone had handles on both sides, spun madly by two men. Their long hair flew back as they turned, revealing faces more horrible and cruel than those of wild warriors in war paint. They had glued on fake eyebrows and mustaches, and their ugly faces were covered in sweat and blood, twisted as they howled, their eyes wide with animal excitement and lack of sleep. As these thugs turned the stone, their messy hair flying about, some women held wine to their lips to let them drink. Between the dripping blood, the spilling wine, and the sparks flying from the stone, the whole scene seemed made of fire and blood. Not a single person in the crowd was free from bloodstains. Pushing each other to get to the stone next were men shirtless to the waist, with blood smeared all over their bodies; men in rags stained with blood; and men wearing stolen women's lace, silk, and ribbons, all dyed with blood. Hatchets, knives, bayonets, and swords brought to be sharpened were all covered in red. Some of the bent swords were tied to the owners' wrists with strips of cloth torn from clothing—all stained the same deep red color. As the frantic people grabbed their weapons from the sparks and ran back into the streets, their eyes glowed with the same red madness. Any normal person watching would have given twenty years of life to stop them with a gun.

All of this was seen in a single second, like the quick flash of images a drowning man sees. They stepped back from the window, and the Doctor looked at his friend's pale face for an explanation.

“They are,” Mr. Lorry whispered, looking fearfully toward the locked bedroom door, “murdering the prisoners. If you are sure of what you said—if you really have the influence you think you have, and I believe you do—go show yourself to these monsters and have them take you to La Force. It might be too late already, but don't waste another second!”

Doctor Manette squeezed his hand, ran out of the room without a hat, and was already in the courtyard by the time Mr. Lorry reached the shutter again.

His flowing white hair, his striking face, and his confident manner as he pushed the weapons aside allowed him to reach the center of the crowd by the grindstone immediately. For a few moments, the crowd paused, murmured, and listened to his voice. Then Mr. Lorry saw the Doctor, surrounded by the crowd and walking in the middle of a line of twenty men holding hands and shoulders, being hurried out. The crowd shouted, “Long live the Bastille prisoner! Help the Bastille prisoner's relative in La Force! Make way for the Bastille prisoner! Save the prisoner Evremonde at La Force!” along with thousands of answering cheers.

With a beating heart, Mr. Lorry closed the shutter, the window, and the curtain. He ran to Lucie and told her that her father had the help of the crowd and had gone to find her husband. He found her child and Miss Pross with her, though it did not occur to him to wonder how they had arrived until much later, when he sat watching them in the quiet night.

By that time, Lucie had collapsed onto the floor at his feet, holding his hand in a daze. Miss Pross had put the child to sleep on his bed and rested her head on the pillow next to her. It was a long, terrible night, filled with the quiet cries of the poor wife, with no sign of her father and no news of Charles!

Twice more during the night, the gate bell rang, the crowd rushed in, and the grindstone spun and sparked again. “What is that?” Lucie asked in terror. “Hush! They are sharpening the soldiers' swords,” Mr. Lorry said. “The building belongs to the nation now, and they are using it as a workshop, my love.”

Those were the only times, and the last session of sharpening was short and quiet. Soon dawn broke, and Mr. Lorry gently let go of Lucie's hand and looked out the window again. A man covered in blood and dirt, looking like a wounded soldier waking up on a battlefield, got up from the ground near the grindstone and looked around blankly. Soon, this exhausted killer saw one of the nobleman's carriages parked in the yard. Staggering over to the fancy carriage, he climbed inside and shut the door to sleep on its soft cushions.

The earth had turned, and the sun cast a red glow over the courtyard when Mr. Lorry looked out again. But the other grindstone stood alone in the quiet morning air, stained with a red color that the sun had not created, and would never wash away.

CHAPTER III: The Shadow

One of the first things Mr. Lorry thought about when the bank opened was that he had no right to put Tellson's Bank in danger by keeping the wife of a French exile prisoner under the bank's roof. He would have risked his own money, safety, and life for Lucie and her child without hesitation. However, the bank was a trust that did not belong to him, and he had to be a strict businessman about it.

At first, he thought of Defarge, wondering if he should go back to the wine shop to ask him where the safest place to stay would be in this chaotic city. But the same reason that made him think of Defarge also made him change his mind: Defarge lived in the most violent district, was very influential there, and was deeply involved in the dangerous revolution.

When noon came and the Doctor had not returned, and with every delay putting the bank at risk, Mr. Lorry talked with Lucie. She said her father had mentioned renting an apartment for a short time in this district, near the bank. Since there was no business objection to this, and since Mr. Lorry realized that even if Charles were released, they could not leave the city yet, he went out to look for lodgings. He found a suitable place high up in a quiet side street where the closed shutters of the other windows in a tall, gloomy square showed that the houses were empty.

He immediately moved Lucie, her child, and Miss Pross to this apartment, making them as comfortable as possible—much more comfortable than he was himself. He left Jerry Cruncher with them as a guard who could handle a fight if necessary, and returned to his work. He felt anxious and sad, and the day dragged on slowly and heavily.

The day finally ended, leaving him exhausted by the time the bank closed. He was alone again in his room from the night before, thinking about what to do next, when he heard footsteps on the stairs. A moment later, a man entered, looked at him closely, and called him by name.

“At your service,” Mr. Lorry said. “Do you know me?”

The visitor was a strong man with dark, curly hair, between forty-five and fifty years old. For an answer, he repeated the question in the same tone:

“Do you know me?”

“I have seen you somewhere.”

“Perhaps at my wine shop?”

Excited and nervous, Mr. Lorry said, “You come from Doctor Manette?”

“Yes. I come from Doctor Manette.”

“And what does he say? What did he send for me?”

Defarge handed him a folded scrap of paper. It had these words written in the Doctor's handwriting:

“Charles is safe, but I cannot safely leave this place yet. I have arranged for the messenger to carry a short note from Charles to his wife. Let the messenger see his wife.”

It was dated from La Force, written less than an hour ago.

“Will you come with me,” Mr. Lorry said, relieved and happy after reading the note, “to where his wife is staying?”

“Yes,” Defarge replied.

Without noticing how cold and robotic Defarge's voice sounded, Mr. Lorry put on his hat, and they went down into the courtyard. There they found two women, one of whom was knitting.

“Madame Defarge, surely!” said Mr. Lorry, who remembered leaving her in that exact same pose seventeen years ago.

“It is she,” her husband remarked.

“Is Madame coming with us?” Mr. Lorry asked, seeing that she walked along with them.

“Yes. So that she can recognize their faces and know who they are. It is for their safety.”

Starting to feel uneasy about Defarge's behavior, Mr. Lorry looked at him doubtfully and led the way. Both women followed them; the second woman was the one known as The Vengeance.

They walked through the streets as quickly as they could, went up the stairs of the new apartment, and were let in by Jerry. They found Lucie crying alone. She was filled with joy at the news of her husband and kissed the hand of Defarge as he handed her the note—never imagining what that hand had been doing near Charles the night before, and what it might have done to him if things had gone differently.

“DEAREST,—Take courage. I am well, and your father has influence here. You cannot reply to this. Kiss our child for me.”

That was the entire message. But it meant so much to Lucie that she turned to Madame Defarge and kissed one of the hands that was knitting. It was a loving, thankful gesture, but the hand did not respond—it dropped cold and heavy, and then immediately went back to its knitting.
