The cold feel of the hand startled Lucie. She paused as she was putting the note inside her dress, her hands frozen at her collar, and looked at Madame Defarge in fear. Madame Defarge returned her worried look with a cold, blank stare.

“My dear,” Mr. Lorry interrupted to explain, “there are frequent riots in the streets. Although they probably won't bother you, Madame Defarge wants to see the people she has the power to protect during these times, so that she can recognize them. I believe,” Mr. Lorry said, hesitating as the cold behavior of the three visitors began to make him nervous, “I am explaining this correctly, Citizen Defarge?”

Defarge looked gloomily at his wife and answered only with a rough grunt of agreement.

“You had better bring the dear child here, Lucie,” Mr. Lorry said, trying to sound as friendly as possible, “and our good Miss Pross. Our friend Pross, Defarge, is an English lady and doesn't speak French.”

Miss Pross, who firmly believed she was more than a match for any foreigner even in times of danger, came in with her arms crossed. Looking at The Vengeance, she said in English, “Well, I must say, Boldface! I hope _you_ are doing well!” She also coughed loudly at Madame Defarge, but neither woman paid any attention to her.

“Is that his child?” Madame Defarge asked, stopping her knitting for the first time and pointing her needle at little Lucie like the finger of fate.

“Yes, madame,” Mr. Lorry answered. “This is our poor prisoner's darling daughter and only child.”

The threatening presence of Madame Defarge and her companions seemed to cast such a dark shadow over the child that Lucie instinctively knelt down and held her daughter to her chest. The dark shadow seemed to hang over both mother and child.

“It is enough, husband,” Madame Defarge said. “I have seen them. We can go.”

But her quiet behavior carried such a hidden threat that Lucie was frightened into saying, grabbing Madame Defarge's dress pleadingly:

“Please be kind to my poor husband. Do not let them hurt him. Will you help me see him if you can?”

“Your husband is not my concern here,” Madame Defarge replied, looking down at her calmly. “I came here to see the daughter of your father.”

“For my sake, then, show mercy to my husband! For my child's sake! She will fold her hands and beg you to be merciful. We are more afraid of you than of anyone else.”

Madame Defarge took this as a compliment and looked at her husband. Defarge, who had been nervously biting his nail and watching her, forced his face into a stern expression.

“What does your husband say in that little letter?” Madame Defarge asked with a dark smile. “Influence? Did he mention something about influence?”

“He says my father,” Lucie said, quickly pulling out the paper but keeping her eyes fixed on Madame Defarge, “has a lot of influence there.”

“Then surely it will set him free!” Madame Defarge said. “Let it do that.”

“As a wife and mother,” Lucie cried out, “I beg you to have pity on me. Do not use your power against my innocent husband, but help him instead. Oh, think of me as another woman—as a wife and mother!”

Madame Defarge looked at the pleading woman as coldly as ever, and turned to her friend The Vengeance:

“Have the wives and mothers we have seen since we were children ever been treated with mercy? Haven't we seen _their_ husbands and fathers thrown in prison and kept from them for years? All our lives, haven't we watched other women and their children suffer poverty, starvation, sickness, pain, and oppression?”

“We have seen nothing else,” The Vengeance replied.

“We have suffered this for a very long time,” Madame Defarge said, turning back to Lucie. “So tell me: why should the trouble of one single wife and mother matter to us now?”

She went back to her knitting and walked out of the room. The Vengeance followed her, and Defarge left last, closing the door behind him.

“Have courage, my dear Lucie,” Mr. Lorry said as he helped her up. “Courage, courage! So far, everything is going well for us—much better than for many other poor souls. Cheer up and try to be hopeful.”

“I am not ungrateful, but that terrifying woman seems to cast a dark shadow over all my hopes.”

“Come, come!” Mr. Lorry said. “Why is my brave girl losing heart? A shadow is just a shadow! There is nothing real to fear in it, Lucie.”

But the dark attitude of the Defarges worried Mr. Lorry deeply as well, though he kept his fears to himself.

CHAPTER IV: Calm in Storm

Doctor Manette did not return until the morning of the fourth day. They hid most of the terrible events from Lucie so well that only long afterward, when she was far away from France, did she learn that eleven hundred defenseless prisoners of all ages and genders had been killed by the mob. She didn't know that four days and nights had been filled with this horror, or that the very air was smelly from the dead. She only knew that the prisons had been attacked, that political prisoners were in danger, and that some had been dragged out and killed.

The Doctor swore Mr. Lorry to secrecy and explained that the mob had led him through a bloodbath to La Force prison. Inside, he found a self-appointed court judging the prisoners one by one, quickly ordering them to be killed by the mob outside, released, or sent back to their cells. When he was presented to this court, he announced his name and profession, stating that he had been held in the Bastille for eighteen years without a trial. One of the judges stood up and recognized him—and that man was Defarge.

The Doctor checked the record books on the table and saw that his son-in-law was still alive. He begged the court—whose members were half-asleep, dirty with blood, sober, or drunk—for Charles's life. Because the court respected the Doctor as a famous victim of the old government, they agreed to bring Charles Darnay in for questioning. It seemed like Charles was about to be released, but then the mood changed for some reason the Doctor didn't understand, and the judges whispered together. The president of the court then told Doctor Manette that the prisoner had to stay in jail, but for the Doctor's sake, he would be kept safe. Charles was sent back inside, but the Doctor begged so hard to stay and make sure Charles wasn't sent to the murderous mob outside, that the judges let him remain in that bloody room until the danger had passed.

The horrors the Doctor saw there, while getting almost no food or sleep, will not be described. The crazy joy the mob showed for the prisoners who were saved surprised him as much as their anger toward those they killed. He said one prisoner was set free, but a confused rioter stabbed him with a spear as he walked out. Asked to help, the Doctor went outside and found the wounded man being held by a group of killers who were sitting on the bodies of their victims. With a hypocrisy as crazy as a nightmare, these killers helped the Doctor and treated the wounded man gently, carrying him away on a stretcher. But then they immediately grabbed their weapons and went back to murdering people so brutally that the Doctor covered his eyes and fainted.

As Mr. Lorry listened to these secrets and looked at his sixty-two-year-old friend, he worried that these terrible experiences would bring back the Doctor's old mental illness.

But Mr. Lorry had never seen the Doctor look like this before. For the first time, the Doctor felt that his past suffering gave him power. He felt that his eighteen years in prison had forged the strength he needed to break his daughter's husband out of jail. “It all had a purpose, my friend; it wasn't just wasted suffering. Since my beloved daughter helped save my mind, I will help save the most important person in her life. With God's help, I will do it!” said Doctor Manette. And when Mr. Lorry saw his bright eyes, resolute face, and strong bearing—after years of seeming like a stopped clock that was now running with new energy—he believed him.

Even greater obstacles would have crumbled before the Doctor's determination. Working as a doctor who treated everyone—rich and poor, good and bad—he used his influence so well that he was appointed the medical inspector of three prisons, including La Force. He could now tell Lucie that Charles was no longer in solitary confinement, but was with the other prisoners. The Doctor saw Charles every week and brought her messages directly from him. Sometimes Charles even sent letters (though never carried by the Doctor), but Lucie was not allowed to write back, because the authorities suspected that prisoners were plotting with friends abroad.

This new life was stressful for the Doctor, but Mr. Lorry saw that it gave him a new sense of pride. It was a natural and worthy pride, but Mr. Lorry found it interesting to observe. Until now, the Doctor's imprisonment had been remembered as a time of weakness and suffering. Now that those years in the Bastille gave him the power to protect Charles, he felt proud to take charge, asking Lucie and Mr. Lorry to rely on him as the strong one. His role with Lucie was reversed, but only out of love and gratitude, since he wanted nothing more than to help the daughter who had saved him. “It is interesting to watch,” Mr. Lorry thought, “but completely natural and right. Take the lead, my friend, and keep it; it couldn't be in better hands.”

But even though the Doctor tried constantly to set Charles free or at least get him a trial, the revolution was moving too fast. The new era began: the king was put on trial, condemned, and beheaded. The Republic declared war against the world. A black flag waved day and night from the towers of Notre Dame Cathedral. Three hundred thousand men were drafted to fight, rising up from every corner of France as if soldiers grew directly out of the soil. How could the worries of one family stand against the massive flood of the Year One of Liberty—a flood rising from the anger of the people below?

There was no pause, no pity, no peace, and no concept of time. Although days and nights passed normally, no one kept track of dates. Time was lost in the nation's madness, just like in a patient's fever. The executioner showed the king's head to the crowd, and soon after, the head of the queen, whose hair had turned gray from eight months of misery in prison.

And yet, while time flew by, it also felt incredibly long. There was a revolutionary court in Paris and thousands of local committees all over the country. A new law concerning "suspected" people destroyed all safety, making it easy for any bad person to accuse an innocent one. Prisons were packed with people who had done nothing wrong and could get no trial. These horrors became the daily routine. Above all, one terrifying machine became as familiar as if it had existed since the beginning of the world: the Guillotine.

It was the subject of popular jokes: people called it the best cure for a headache, a way to keep hair from turning gray, and the "National Razor" that shaved close. They joked that anyone who kissed it looked through the little window and sneezed into the sack. It was seen as the symbol of a new era, replacing the Christian cross. People wore small models of the Guillotine on their chests instead of crosses, bowing down to it instead of God.

It cut off so many heads that the machine and the ground around it were stained dark red. It could be taken apart and put back together easily when needed. It silenced speakers, destroyed the powerful, and killed the beautiful and the good. In one morning, it executed twenty-one famous politicians in twenty-one minutes. The chief executioner was nicknamed Samson after the strong man in the Bible; but this executioner was stronger than the biblical Samson, and blinder, destroying lives every day.

Amid these terrors, the Doctor kept his head clear. He remained confident in his power, working quietly to save Lucie's husband. Yet the time went by so fast that Charles had been in prison for a year and three months while the Doctor remained patient and hopeful. By December, the Revolution had grown even more violent; rivers in the south were filled with drowned bodies, and prisoners were executed by firing squads. Still, the Doctor walked through this danger calmly. No one was better known in Paris, and no one was in a stranger position. He was quiet, kind, and indispensable in hospitals and prisons, treating killers and victims alike. His past as a Bastille prisoner kept him safe from suspicion, as if he really were a ghost walking among the living.

CHAPTER V: The Wood-Sawyer

One year and three months passed. During all that time, Lucie was never sure from one hour to the next whether the Guillotine would cut off her husband's head the following day. Every day, heavy carts rumbled through the cobblestone streets, packed with condemned prisoners. Beautiful girls, young women, dark-haired, brown-haired, and gray-haired women, boys, strong men, and the elderly—aristocrats and peasants alike—were all food for the Guillotine. They were brought daily from the dark cells of the filthy prisons and carried through the streets to satisfy its endless thirst. Liberty, equality, fraternity, or death—and death was by far the easiest for the Guillotine to give!

If the sudden shock of this disaster and the chaotic pace of the times had caused the Doctor's daughter to give up in despair, she would have been just like many others. But ever since the day she had held her father's white-haired head in the attic of Saint Antoine, she had always done her duty. She was most loyal during this time of trial, just as all good and quiet people are.

As soon as they moved into their new apartment and her father began his daily routine, she organized the household exactly as if Charles were there. Everything had its place and time. She taught little Lucie as regularly as if they were still in their peaceful English home. The small tricks she played on herself to pretend they would soon be together again—like setting aside his chair and books for his return, and praying at night for her dear prisoner among the many suffering in the shadow of death—were the only ways she could find relief for her heavy heart.

She did not change much in appearance. The simple dark dresses, almost like mourning clothes, that she and her child wore were as neat and clean as their colorful clothes from happier days. She lost some of her color, and her worried, serious expression was now permanent, but otherwise she remained very pretty. Sometimes at night, when kissing her father goodnight, she would cry and let out the grief she had hidden all day, telling him he was her only hope. He always answered firmly, “Nothing can happen to him without my knowing, and I know I can save him, Lucie.”

They had only lived this way for a few weeks when her father said to her one evening upon coming home:

“My dear, there is a high window in the prison that Charles can sometimes reach at three in the afternoon. When he can get to it—which depends on many things—he thinks he might be able to see you in the street if you stand in a specific spot I can show you. But you won't be able to see him, my poor child, and even if you could, it would be unsafe to wave or show that you recognize him.”

“Oh, show me the place, Father, and I will go there every day.”

From then on, in all kinds of weather, she waited there for two hours. She arrived at two o'clock and left at four. When it was not too wet or cold for her daughter, they went together; other times she went alone, but she never missed a day.

The spot was a dark, dirty corner of a narrow, winding street. The shack of a wood-sawyer was the only building there; everything else was just a high wall. On her third day of waiting there, he noticed her.

“Good day, citizeness.”

“Good day, citizen.”

This way of greeting was now required by law. It had started voluntarily among the most loyal patriots, but now everyone had to use it.

“Walking here again, citizeness?”

“You see me, citizen!”

The wood-sawyer was a small man who made a lot of gestures—he was the same man who had once been the road-mender. He looked at the prison, pointed to it, and held up his ten fingers like bars, joking as he peeked through them.

“But it's none of my business,” he said, and went back to sawing his wood.

The next day, he was watching for her and spoke to her the moment she arrived.

“What? Walking here again, citizeness?”

“Yes, citizen.”

“Ah! A child too! That is your mother, isn't it, my little citizeness?”

“Should I say yes, Mama?” whispered little Lucie, holding close to her.

“Yes, dearest.”

“Yes, citizen.”

“Ah! But it's none of my business. My work is my business. Look at my saw! I call it my Little Guillotine. La, la, la; La, la, la! And off his head comes!”

A log fell as he spoke, and he tossed it into a basket.

“I call myself the executioner Samson of the firewood guillotine. Look here again! Loo, loo, loo; Loo, loo, loo! And off _her_ head comes! Now, a child. Tickle, tickle; Pickle, pickle! And off _its_ head comes. The whole family!”

Lucie shivered as he threw two more logs into his basket, but she couldn't stand there without being in his sight. From then on, to keep him friendly, she always spoke to him first and often gave him tip money, which he was happy to take.

He was very curious, and sometimes when she had forgotten all about him while staring up at the prison roof and praying for her husband, she would look over to find him watching her, with his knee on his bench and his saw stopped. “But it's none of my business!” he would usually say, and then start sawing again.

Through snow, winter frost, bitter spring winds, hot summer sun, and autumn rain, Lucie spent two hours there every day. Every day, as she left, she kissed the prison wall. Her husband saw her—according to her father—maybe once every five or six times, sometimes twice in a row, and sometimes not for weeks. Knowing that he could see her when he had the chance was enough, and for that possibility she would have stood there all day, seven days a week.

These daily visits brought her to the month of December, when her father was working calmly amid the city's terrors. One snowy afternoon, she arrived at her usual corner. It was a day of celebration. As she walked, she had seen houses decorated with small toy spears, red caps, tri-color ribbons, and the slogan written in red, white, and blue: "Republic One and Indivisible. Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death!"

The wood-sawyer's tiny shop was too small to fit the long slogan easily. He had paid someone to paint it for him, and they had squeezed the word "Death" onto the end with great difficulty. On his roof, he displayed a toy spear and cap like a good citizen, and in the window he kept his saw labeled "Little Saint Guillotine"—since the crowd now spoke of the machine as if it were a saint. His shop was closed and he was gone, which was a relief to Lucie, leaving her alone.

But he was not far away. Soon, she heard shouting and a loud crowd approaching, which frightened her. A moment later, a mob of people came rushing around the corner. In the middle of them was the wood-sawyer, holding hands with The Vengeance. There were at least five hundred people, dancing like demons. They had no music other than their own singing, chanting the popular revolutionary song to a fierce rhythm that sounded like the grinding of teeth. Men and women danced together in a storm of red caps and rags. They surrounded Lucie and danced wildly around her. They moved forward and backward, clutched at each other's heads, spun around in pairs, and spun in giant circles until many fell from exhaustion. Then they broke into smaller groups, spinning faster and faster, before suddenly stopping, forming a line across the street, and running off screaming. No physical fight could have looked as terrifying as this dance. It was the Carmagnole, a once-innocent pastime now turned into a ritual of anger and madness. Its wild, dark energy showed how twisted the country had become, with young women and girls caught up in this dirty, bloody display.

This was the Carmagnole dance. As it moved away, leaving Lucie frightened and confused in the doorway of the wood-sawyer's shop, the light snow fell quietly and lay white and soft, as if nothing had happened.

“Oh, Father!” she said when she looked up and saw him standing there. “It was such a cruel, horrible sight.”

“I know, my dear, I know. I have seen it many times. Don't be scared! None of them would hurt you.”

“I am not afraid for myself, Father. But when I think of my husband, and the mercy of these people—”

“We will save him from them very soon. I left him climbing to the window, and I came to tell you. No one is watching. You can blow a kiss toward that high roof.”

“I am doing it, Father, and I send him my soul with it!”

“You can't see him, my poor child?”

“No, Father,” Lucie said, crying as she blew a kiss, “no.”

A footstep sounded in the snow. It was Madame Defarge. “Greetings, citizeness,” said the Doctor. “Greetings, citizen,” she replied as she walked by. Nothing more. Madame Defarge vanished like a shadow over the white road.

“Take my arm, my love. Walk away from here looking cheerful and brave, for his sake. That's it,” he said as they left the corner. “It won't be in vain. Charles is summoned for trial tomorrow.”

“For tomorrow!”

“There is no time to lose. I am well prepared, but there are steps we have to take that we couldn't take until he was actually summoned. He hasn't received the notice yet, but I know he will be called tomorrow and moved to the Conciergerie prison. I got the information early. You are not afraid, are you?”

She could barely reply, “I trust you.”

“Trust me completely. The waiting is almost over, my darling. He will be back with you in a few hours. I have arranged every protection for him. I must see Lorry.”

He stopped. They heard the heavy rumble of wheels nearby. They both knew exactly what it meant. One. Two. Three. Three carts carrying their terrible loads away over the silent snow.

“I must see Lorry,” the Doctor repeated, leading her down another street.

The loyal old gentleman was still working at his post. He and his records were often consulted by the government regarding seized property. Whatever he could save for the bank's customers, he saved. There was no better man to guard Tellson's assets and keep his mouth shut.

A cloudy red and yellow sky and mist rising from the Seine River showed that night was falling. It was almost dark when they reached the bank. The lord's grand house was completely ruined and empty. Above a pile of dust and ashes in the yard, painted letters read: "National Property. Republic One and Indivisible. Liberty, Equality, Fraternity, or Death!"

Who could that be with Mr. Lorry—the owner of the coat sitting on the chair—who had to remain hidden? From which newly arrived traveler did Mr. Lorry come out, excited and surprised, to hug Lucie? To whom was he repeating her worried words when he raised his voice, looked back toward the closed door of the private room, and said: “Moved to the Conciergerie, and summoned for trial tomorrow?”

CHAPTER VI: Triumph

The terrible court of five judges, a public prosecutor, and a determined jury met every day. Their list of the condemned went out every evening, read aloud by the jailers to the prisoners in each jail. The jailers' favorite joke was, “Come out and listen to the Evening Paper, you guys inside!”

“Charles Evremonde, called Darnay!”

So at last began the reading of the list at La Force prison.

When a name was called, the prisoner stepped aside into an area reserved for those marked for death. Charles Darnay knew the routine well; he had seen hundreds of prisoners go this way.

His bloated jailer, wearing glasses to read, looked over them to make sure Darnay had stepped aside, and then continued through the list, pausing briefly at each name. There were twenty-three names, but only twenty people answered. One of the summoned prisoners had already died in jail and been forgotten, and two had already been executed and forgotten. The list was read in the same arched room where Darnay had seen the polite crowd on his first night. Every single person from that crowd had died in the massacre, and everyone he had met since then had been executed.

There were quick goodbyes and kind words, but the parting was over fast. It was an everyday event, and the prisoners of La Force were busy planning games and a small concert for that evening. They crowded to the bars to cry, but twenty spots in the evening's show now had to be filled by others. Time was short before lockup, when the rooms and halls would be guarded by the large guard dogs for the night. The prisoners were not cold or heartless; their behavior was just a reaction to the terrible times. Indeed, a strange urge to face the Guillotine unnecessarily and die had infected many people—not out of boasting, but as a mental illness spreading through the population. In times of plague, some people feel a weird urge to get sick, and all of us have hidden instincts that only appear in extreme circumstances.

The trip to the Conciergerie prison was short and dark, and the night spent in its dirty cells was long and cold. The next day, fifteen prisoners were tried before Darnay's name was called. All fifteen were condemned to death, and their trials took only an hour and a half in total.

“Charles Evremonde, called Darnay,” was finally called to the front.

His judges sat on the bench wearing feathered hats, while almost everyone else in the room wore red caps and tri-color ribbons. Looking at the jury and the noisy crowd, one might have thought that criminals were putting honest men on trial. The poorest and cruelest people in the city ruled the courtroom, shouting comments, cheering, booing, and deciding the verdicts without any control. Most of the men were armed, and some of the women carried knives or daggers, eating and drinking while they watched, and many knitted. Among the knitters was one woman sitting in the front row with a spare piece of knitting under her arm. She sat next to a man Darnay had not seen since the city gates, but whom he immediately recognized as Defarge. He noticed that she whispered to him once or twice and seemed to be his wife. But what he noticed most was that, even though they sat very close to him, they never looked at him. They seemed to be waiting for something with stubborn determination, staring only at the jury. Below the president of the court sat Doctor Manette in his usual quiet clothes. As far as Darnay could see, the Doctor and Mr. Lorry were the only men in the room who wore normal clothes instead of the rough revolutionary dress of the Carmagnole.

Charles Darnay was accused of being an emigrant whose life belonged to the Republic under the law that banished all exiles under penalty of death. It did not matter that the law was passed after he had already returned to France. He was there, the law was there, he had been captured in France, and they demanded his execution.

“Cut off his head!” cried the audience. “He is an enemy of the Republic!”

The president rang his bell to quiet the crowd and asked the prisoner if it was true that he had lived for many years in England.

Yes, it was true.

Was he not an emigrant then? What did he call himself?

Not an emigrant, he hoped, according to the spirit of the law.

Why not? the president asked.

Because he had voluntarily given up a title and a position that he hated, leaving his country long before the word "emigrant" was used in this way, to live by his own hard work in England rather than off the labor of the heavily taxed people of France.

What proof did he have of this?

He gave the names of two witnesses: Theophile Gabelle and Alexandre Manette.

But he had married in England, the president reminded him.

Yes, but not an English woman.

A citizeness of France?

Yes, by birth.

What was her name and family?

“Lucie Manette, the only daughter of Doctor Manette, the good physician sitting right there.”

This answer had a great effect on the crowd. Shouts praising the well-known doctor filled the hall. The crowd was so unpredictable that tears rolled down the faces of several fierce-looking people who, just a moment before, had been glaring at the prisoner as if they wanted to drag him outside and kill him.

During these first steps of his trial, Charles Darnay followed Doctor Manette's exact instructions. The same careful plan guided every step that lay ahead of him, prepared in advance.

The president asked why he had returned to France when he did, and not earlier.

He replied that he had not returned sooner because he had no way of making a living in France other than the wealth he had given up, whereas in England he earned his living by teaching French. He returned when he did because of the urgent letter from a French citizen who said his life was in danger because of Darnay's absence. He had come back to save a citizen's life and tell the truth, risking his own safety. Was that a crime in the eyes of the Republic?

The crowd shouted enthusiastically, “No!” and the president rang his bell to quiet them. It didn't work, as they kept shouting “No!” until they decided to stop on their own.

The president asked for the name of that citizen. Darnay explained that the citizen was Gabelle, his first witness. He also pointed to Gabelle's letter, which had been taken from him at the gates but was surely among the papers on the president's desk.

The Doctor had made sure the letter was there. At this point, it was produced and read aloud. Gabelle was called to confirm it, and did so. Gabelle politely mentioned that because the court was so busy dealing with so many enemies of the Republic, he had been slightly forgotten in the Abbaye Prison until three days ago, when he was finally released after the jury decided that his charges were answered by the arrival of Citizen Darnay.

Doctor Manette was questioned next. His great popularity and clear answers made a strong impression. He explained that Charles was his first friend after being released from the Bastille, and that Charles had stayed in England to care for him and Lucie in their exile. He also revealed that, far from being favored by the English government, Charles had actually been tried for his life in London for being a friend to the United States and an enemy to England. As he explained these facts clearly and honestly, the jury and the crowd were completely won over. Finally, when he called on Mr. Lorry to confirm the details of the English trial, the jury declared they had heard enough and were ready to vote.

As each juryman voted aloud, the crowd cheered. Every vote was in the prisoner's favor, and the president declared him free.

Then began one of those wild scenes where the crowd showed their unpredictable generosity and mercy. It was probably a mix of emotions. As soon as the acquittal was announced, tears flowed as freely as blood had on other days. Men and women rushed to hug Darnay, nearly causing him to faint from exhaustion after his long imprisonment. He was well aware that these same people, under a different mood, would have rushed at him just as eagerly to tear him to pieces in the streets.

He was led away to make room for other prisoners, saving him from the crowd for a moment. Five prisoners were to be tried next, accused of being enemies of the Republic because they had not active helped it. The court worked so quickly to make up for freeing Darnay that these five were condemned to die within twenty-four hours before Darnay even left the building. The first one told Darnay the news with the usual prison sign of death—a raised finger—and they all said, “Long live the Republic!”

The five prisoners had no audience to watch their trial, because when Darnay and Doctor Manette walked out the gates, the entire courtroom crowd was outside waiting. Only two faces were missing, whom Darnay looked for in vain. As he came out, the crowd rushed him again, crying, hugging, and shouting, until the scene by the riverbank seemed completely wild.

They placed him in a large chair they had brought from the courthouse. They threw a red flag over the chair and tied a spear with a red cap to the back of it. In this triumphal seat, despite the Doctor's objections, he was carried home on men's shoulders. Surrounded by a sea of red caps and wild faces, Darnay felt so confused that he feared he was actually in a cart on his way to the Guillotine.

In a dreamlike procession, hugging strangers and pointing Darnay out, they carried him along. The red flags and caps colored the snowy streets as they marched toward his apartment. Her father had run ahead to warn Lucie, and when Darnay finally stood before her, she fainted in his arms.

As he held her to his chest and turned her head away from the noisy crowd so he could kiss her unseen, some people began to dance. Instantly, everyone joined in, and the courtyard filled with the Carmagnole. They placed a young woman from the crowd into the empty chair to carry her as the Goddess of Liberty, and then the dancing crowd spilled out into the streets, along the river, and over the bridge, carrying everyone away.

After shaking the hand of the proud, victorious Doctor, shaking the hand of Mr. Lorry, who came in panting from the crowd, kissing little Lucie as she was held up to hug him, and thanking Miss Pross, Charles took his wife in his arms and carried her upstairs to their rooms.

“Lucie! My own! I am safe.”

“Oh, dearest Charles, let me thank God on my knees, just as I prayed to Him.”

They all bowed their heads in prayer. When she was back in his arms, he said to her:

“And now speak to your father, dearest. No other man in France could have done what he did for me.”

She rested her head on her father's chest, just as she had held his poor head long ago. He was happy to have returned the favor, rewarded for his past suffering, and proud of his strength. “You must not be weak, my darling,” he said. “Don't tremble so. I have saved him.”

CHAPTER VII: A Knock at the Door

“I have saved him.” This was not another of the dreams in which he had so often returned; he was really here. Yet his wife shivered, filled with a quiet but heavy fear.

The atmosphere in the city was so dark, the people so vengeful and unpredictable, and innocent citizens were executed so often on mere suspicion and hatred, that she couldn't forget that many others just as innocent as her husband were executed every day. Her heart was still loaded with worry. As the winter afternoon grew dark, she knew the terrible carts were rumbled through the streets. She imagined Charles in them, and she clung tighter to his arm and shivered even more.

Her father, cheered her up, showing a kind confidence that looked past her fears. There was no shoemaking or memory of his old cell in the North Tower now! He had done what he promised: he had saved Charles. He wanted them all to rely on him.

They lived very simply, not only because it was safer to avoid showing off, but because they were short on money. During his imprisonment, Charles had been forced to pay large amounts for his food, his guards, and to help support the poorer prisoners. For this reason, and to avoid hiring a servant who might spy on them, they did their own housework. The gatekeepers of the building helped them occasionally, and Jerry Cruncher (lent to them by Mr. Lorry) acted as their daily helper and slept in the apartment every night.

The revolutionary government required by law that the names of all residents be clearly painted on the doorpost of every house at a specific height. Jerry Cruncher's name was already written on the doorpost downstairs. As it grew dark, Jerry returned after watching a painter whom Doctor Manette had hired to add the name of Charles Evremonde, called Darnay, to the list.

Because of the fear and distrust during this time, everyday life was completely different. Like many others, Doctor Manette's household bought their supplies every evening in small amounts from different shops. Everyone wanted to avoid attracting attention, gossip, or jealousy.

For several months, Miss Pross and Jerry Cruncher had been the shoppers, Miss Pross carrying the money and Jerry carrying the basket. Every afternoon when the street lamps were lit, they went out to buy what they needed. Although Miss Pross had lived with a French family for years, she refused to learn a single word of the language. She knew no more of that “nonsense” (as she called it) than Jerry did. Her way of shopping was to shout the English name of the item at the shopkeeper. If the shopkeeper didn't understand, she would look around, grab the item, and hold onto it until they agreed on a price. She always bargained by holding up one less finger than the shopkeeper, no matter what price they asked.

“Now, Mr. Cruncher,” said Miss Pross, her eyes red from crying tears of joy. “If you are ready, I am.”

Jerry hoarsely replied that he was ready. He had lost his rusty attitude long ago, but his spiky hair was still as wild as ever.

“We need all sorts of things,” said Miss Pross, “and it will be a task. We need wine, too. I'm sure those Redheads in the streets will be drinking toasts with it.”

“It will be much the same to your knowledge, miss, I should think,” Jerry replied, “whether they drink to your health or to the Old One's.”

“Who is he?” Miss Pross asked.

Jerry sheepishly explained that he meant the Devil.

“Ha!” said Miss Pross. “We don't need anyone to explain these French rebels. They only care about murder and mischief.”

“Hush, dear! Please, please be careful!” Lucie cried.

“Yes, yes, I'll be careful,” Miss Pross said. “But I hope those smelly men in the streets won't try to hug us. Now, Ladybird, don't move from the fire until I get back! Take care of the husband you just got back, and keep your head on his shoulder until you see me again! Doctor Manette, may I ask a question before I leave?”

“I think you may take that liberty,” the Doctor smiled.

“For heaven's sake, don't talk about liberty! We have more than enough of that here,” said Miss Pross.

“Hush, dear! Again?” Lucie gently chided.

“Well, my sweet,” Miss Pross said, nodding her head firmly, “the simple truth is that I am a subject of King George the Third.” She curtsied at the king's name. “And because of that, my motto is: Confound their politics, frustrate their tricks, and God save the King!”

Jerry, feeling suddenly patriotic, repeated the words after Miss Pross like a response in church.

“I'm glad you are so patriotic, though I wish you didn't have that rough voice,” Miss Pross said. “But here is my question, Doctor Manette. Is there—” she asked casually, which was her way of bringing up their biggest worry, “any chance of us leaving this place soon?”

“I'm afraid not yet. It would still be too dangerous for Charles.”

“Oh well!” Miss Pross said, hiding a sigh as she looked at Lucie's golden hair by the fire. “Then we must be patient and wait. We must keep our heads high, as my brother Solomon used to say. Let's go, Mr. Cruncher! Don't move, Ladybird!”

They went out, leaving Lucie, Charles, the Doctor, and the child by the warm fire. Mr. Lorry was expected to return soon from the bank. Miss Pross had lit the lamp but placed it in the corner so they could enjoy the firelight. Little Lucie sat next to her grandfather, holding his arm, while he whispered a story about a powerful fairy who broke open a prison wall to free a captive. Everything was quiet and peaceful, and Lucie felt more relaxed than she had in months.

“What is that?” she suddenly cried out.

“My dear!” her father said, stopping the story and placing his hand on hers. “Calm down. You are so nervous! The smallest noise startles you! You are my daughter!”

“I thought, Father,” Lucie said, looking pale and speaking in a shaking voice, “that I heard footsteps on the stairs.”

“My love, the stairs are completely silent.”

Just as he said those words, a loud knock shook the door.

“Oh Father, Father! What is it? Hide Charles! Save him!”

“My child,” the Doctor said, standing up and placing his hand on her shoulder. “I _have_ saved him. Do not be weak, my dear. Let me answer the door.”

He took the lamp, walked through the two outer rooms, and opened the door. Heavy footsteps clattered across the floor as four rough men wearing red caps and armed with swords and pistols entered the room.

“Citizen Evremonde, called Darnay,” said the leader.

“Who wants him?” Darnay answered.

“I want him. We want him. I know you, Evremonde; I saw you at the court today. You are a prisoner of the Republic once again.”

The four men surrounded Charles, who stood with his wife and child holding onto him.

“Tell me why I am a prisoner again.”

“You only need to know that you are going back to the Conciergerie prison, and you will find out the reasons tomorrow. You are summoned for trial tomorrow.”

Doctor Manette was frozen in shock, standing with the lamp like a statue. Finally, he put the lamp down, confronted the leader, and grabbed the front of the man's red wool shirt, asking gently:

“You said you know him. Do you know me?”

“Yes, I know you, Citizen Doctor.”

“We all know you, Citizen Doctor,” the other three added.

He looked blankly from one man to another and asked in a quiet voice after a moment:

“Will you answer his question for me then? How did this happen?”

“Citizen Doctor,” the leader said reluctantly, “he has been accused in the Saint Antoine district. This citizen,” he pointed to the second man who had entered, “is from Saint Antoine.”

The second man nodded his head and added:

“He is accused by Saint Antoine.”

“Of what?” the Doctor asked.

“Citizen Doctor,” the leader said with reluctance, “ask no more questions. If the Republic demands sacrifices from you, surely as a good patriot you will be happy to make them. The Republic comes before everything. The People are supreme. Evremonde, we must hurry.”

“Just one word,” the Doctor begged. “Can you tell me who accused him?”

“It is against the rules,” the leader replied, “but you can ask the man from Saint Antoine here.”

The Doctor looked at the second man, who shifted his weight, rubbed his beard, and finally spoke:

“Well, it really is against the rules. But he is accused—very seriously—by Citizen and Citizeness Defarge. And by one other person.”

“Who else?”

“Do _you_ have to ask, Citizen Doctor?”

“Yes.”

“Then,” the man from Saint Antoine said with a strange look, “you will get your answer tomorrow. I will say no more!”

CHAPTER VIII: A Hand at Cards

Happily unaware of the new disaster at home, Miss Pross made her way through the narrow streets and crossed the river by the Pont-Neuf bridge, counting in her head the essential things she had to buy. Jerry Cruncher walked at her side carrying the basket. They both looked left and right into the shops they passed, kept a careful eye out for any crowds, and took side paths to avoid excited groups of talkers. It was a cold evening. The misty river was lit up with bright lights and filled with the harsh noise of metalworking barges, where blacksmiths were making guns for the Republic's army. Woe to anyone who crossed _that_ army or got a promotion they didn't deserve! It would have been better for him if his beard had never grown, for the Guillotine would cut his head off.

After buying a few groceries and some lamp oil, Miss Pross remembered they needed wine. After looking into several wine shops, she stopped at one called "The Good Republican Brutus of Antiquity" near the National Palace (which used to be the Tuileries Palace). The place looked cleaner and quieter than the other shops they had passed. Although it had some people in red caps inside, it didn't look as wild as the others. Asking Jerry, and finding that he agreed, Miss Pross went inside, accompanied by her guard.

Taking in the smoky lights, the customers smoking pipes and playing with worn cards and yellow dominoes, a shirtless, soot-covered worker reading a newspaper aloud to others, the weapons lying around, and two or three customers asleep on tables—looking like sleeping bears in their heavy black coats—the two English customers walked up to the counter and showed what they wanted.

As their wine was being poured, a man in the corner said goodbye to his friend and got up to leave. To walk out, he had to face Miss Pross. The moment she saw his face, she screamed and clapped her hands.

Instantly, everyone in the shop stood up. They assumed someone had been murdered over a political argument, as that was a common occurrence. Everyone looked around for a body, but they only saw a man and a woman staring at each other. The man looked like a typical French Republican, while the woman was clearly English.

The customers in the shop started shouting and talking rapidly, which sounded like a foreign language to Miss Pross and Jerry. But the two were too surprised to pay attention. Not only was Miss Pross shocked and upset, but Jerry Cruncher—for his own secret reasons—was staring in absolute disbelief.

“What is the matter?” said the man who had made Miss Pross scream. He spoke in a quiet, annoyed voice in English.

“Oh, Solomon, dear Solomon!” cried Miss Pross, clapping her hands again. “After not seeing you or hearing from you for so long, to find you here!”

“Don't call me Solomon. Do you want to get me killed?” the man asked in a quiet, frightened way.

“Brother, brother!” cried Miss Pross, bursting into tears. “Have I ever been so mean to you that you ask me such a cruel question?”

“Then hold your tongue,” Solomon said, “and come outside if you want to talk to me. Pay for your wine and come out. Who is this man?”

Miss Pross, shaking her head sadly at her cold brother, said through her tears, “Mr. Cruncher.”

“Let him come out too,” Solomon said. “Does he think I am a ghost?”

Judging by Jerry's face, he definitely did. He said nothing, however, while Miss Pross, searching through her purse through her tears, paid for the wine. As she did so, Solomon turned to the other customers and said a few words in French to explain, which made everyone go back to their cards and drinks.

“Now,” Solomon said, stopping at a dark corner, “what do you want?”

“How unkind of a brother whom I have always loved!” cried Miss Pross. “To greet me like this and show no affection at all.”

“Fine, damn it! There,” Solomon said, giving her a quick kiss on the lips. “Are you happy now?”

Miss Pross only shook her head and cried quietly.

“If you expect me to be surprised, I am not,” her brother Solomon said. “I knew you were here; I know about most people who are in Paris. If you really don't want to put my life in danger—which I think you might—leave as soon as possible and let me go my way. I am busy. I am a government official.”

“My English brother Solomon,” Miss Pross wept, looking up with tearful eyes. “He had the potential to be a great man in his own country, and now he is an official among foreigners—and such terrible foreigners! I would almost rather have seen him lying in his—”

“I knew it!” her brother interrupted. “You want to get me killed. You will make me a 'suspected' person, my own sister, just as I am getting ahead!”

“Heaven forbid!” cried Miss Pross. “I would rather never see you again, dear Solomon, even though I have always loved you. Just say one kind word to me, tell me you aren't angry with me, and I will let you go.”

Good Miss Pross! As if their estrangement were her fault, when Mr. Lorry knew for a fact years ago in London that this worthless brother had stolen all her savings and abandoned her!

He was saying a cold, patronizing word to her—looking down on her even though he was the one in the wrong—when Jerry Cruncher touched him on the shoulder and hoarsely asked a very strange question:

“Excuse me, but could I ask a favor? Is your name John Solomon, or Solomon John?”

The official turned to him with sudden suspicion. Jerry had not said a single word until now.

“Come on,” Jerry said. “Speak up, you know.” (Which was hard for Jerry to do with his rough throat.) “John Solomon, or Solomon John? She calls you Solomon, so she must know since she is your sister. But _I_ know you are John. Which name goes first? And what about that name Pross? That wasn't your name back in England.”

“What do you mean?”

“Well, I don't know exactly, because I can't quite remember what your name was back in England.”

“No?”

“No. But I swear it was a two-syllable name.”

“Indeed?”

“Yes. The other spy's name was one syllable. I know you. You were a spy witness at the Old Bailey court. What on earth did you call yourself back then?”

“Barsad,” said another voice, interrupting them.

“That's the name! I'd bet a thousand pounds on it!” Jerry cried.

The speaker was Sydney Carton. He stood with his hands behind him under his coat, leaning next to Jerry as casually as if he were back in the London courtroom.

“Don't be scared, my dear Miss Pross. I arrived at Mr. Lorry's yesterday evening, surprising him. We agreed I would stay hidden until everything was resolved or I could be useful. I am here now to ask for a quiet chat with your brother. I wish your brother had a more honest job, Mr. Barsad. I wish for your sake he wasn't a spy in the prisons.”

"Sheep" was slang at the time for a prison spy. The spy, who was already pale, turned even whiter and asked Carton how he dared—

“I will tell you,” Sydney said. “I saw you, Mr. Barsad, coming out of the Conciergerie prison about an hour ago while I was looking at the walls. You have a memorable face, and I remember faces well. Curious to see you there, and knowing you were involved in the trial of a friend who is now in great trouble, I followed you. I came into this wine shop right after you and sat nearby. Listening to you talk openly and hearing the rumors from the people around you, it was easy to figure out your job. Gradually, my curiosity turned into a plan, Mr. Barsad.”

“What plan?” the spy asked.

“It would be difficult and dangerous to explain in the street. Could you join me for a few minutes in private—at Tellson's Bank, for example?”

“Is that a threat?”

“Oh! Did I say that?”

“Then why should I go?”

“Really, Mr. Barsad, if you don't know, I can't tell you.”

“Do you mean you won't tell me, sir?” the spy asked hesitantly.

“You understand me perfectly, Mr. Barsad. I won't.”

Carton's casual, careless attitude made him seem very formidable. His sharp eyes saw the spy's fear and took advantage of it.

“See, I told you so,” the spy said, looking at his sister angrily. “If I get into trouble, it is your fault.”

“Come, come, Mr. Barsad!” Sydney said. “Don't be ungrateful. Out of respect for your sister, I have been polite about the proposal I want to make. Are you coming to the bank with me?”

“I'll hear what you have to say. Yes, I'll go with you.”

“First, let's walk your sister safely to the corner of her street. May I take your arm, Miss Pross? Paris is not a safe city for you to walk around alone right now. Since your guard knows Mr. Barsad, I will invite him to Mr. Lorry's with us. Ready? Let's go!”

Miss Pross remembered for the rest of her life that when she held Sydney's arm and begged him not to hurt Solomon, his arm felt strong and his eyes looked inspired, completely changing his usual lazy look. She was too worried about her worthless brother and comforted by Sydney's kind words to think much about it then.

They left her at the corner, and Carton led the way to Mr. Lorry's apartment nearby. John Barsad, also known as Solomon Pross, walked next to him.

Mr. Lorry had just finished dinner and sat before a cozy fire, perhaps remembering himself as a younger man at the inn in Dover many years ago. He turned around as they entered, looking surprised to see a stranger.

“This is Miss Pross's brother, sir,” Sydney said. “Mr. Barsad.”

“Barsad?” the old gentleman repeated. “Barsad? I remember that name—and that face.”

“I told you that you had a memorable face, Mr. Barsad,” Carton remarked calmly. “Please sit down.”

As Carton sat down, he reminded Mr. Lorry by saying with a frown, “He was a witness at Charles's trial in London.” Mr. Lorry remembered immediately and looked at the visitor with clear disgust.

“Mr. Barsad has been recognized by Miss Pross as her brother, and he has admitted it,” Sydney said. “Now, here is the bad news. Darnay has been arrested again.”

Shocked, Mr. Lorry cried out, “What are you saying! I left him safe and free just two hours ago, and I was about to go back to see him!”

“He was arrested anyway. When did it happen, Mr. Barsad?”

“Just now, if it's true.”

“Mr. Barsad is the best authority,” Sydney said. “I heard him tell a fellow spy over a bottle of wine that the arrest was happening. He left the guards at the gate and saw them let in. There is no doubt Charles has been captured again.”

Mr. Lorry saw from Carton's face that there was no use arguing. Though confused, he knew he had to keep his head clear, so he calmed himself and listened quietly.

“Now, I hope,” Sydney said to Mr. Lorry, “that Doctor Manette's influence will help him tomorrow. You said he would be tried again tomorrow, Mr. Barsad?”

“Yes, I believe so.”

“Let's hope it helps him tomorrow as much as it did today. But it might not. I admit I am worried, Mr. Lorry, because Doctor Manette was unable to stop this arrest.”

“He might not have known about it in advance,” Mr. Lorry suggested.

“But that fact itself is alarming, considering how close he is to his son-in-law.”

“That's true,” Mr. Lorry admitted, rubbing his chin anxiously and staring at Carton.

“In short,” Sydney said, “this is a desperate time, and we must play a desperate game. Let the Doctor try to win. I will prepare for a loss. No one's life is safe here. Anyone cheered by the mob today might be executed tomorrow. The prize I want to win, if the worst happens, is to have a friend inside the Conciergerie prison. And the friend I intend to win over is Mr. Barsad.”

“You had better have good cards to play, sir,” said the spy.

“Let's look at them. I will see what I have. Mr. Lorry, you know my bad habits; could you give me some brandy?”

The bottle was placed before him, and he drank two glasses quickly before pushing the bottle away.

“Mr. Barsad,” he continued, like a man looking at his playing cards, “you are a prison spy, a government agent, sometimes a guard, sometimes a prisoner, always an informer. You are extra valuable because you are English, meaning you look less suspicious. But you are using a fake name. That is a very good card. You currently work for the French Republic, but you used to work for the English government, France's enemy. That is an excellent card. In this suspicious city, everyone will assume that you are still in the pay of England—a spy for Pitt, a traitor hiding in the heart of France. That card cannot be beaten. Do you see my cards, Mr. Barsad?”

“I don't understand what you are playing at,” the spy said nervously.

“I am playing my Ace: reporting you to the nearest revolutionary committee. Look at your own cards, Mr. Barsad, and see what you have. Take your time.”

He poured another glass of brandy and drank it. He saw the spy was worried Carton would drink enough to get angry and report him immediately, so Carton deliberately drank another glass.

“Look at your hand carefully, Mr. Barsad. Take your time.”

The spy's hand was even worse than Carton realized, containing secrets Carton didn't even know. Having lost his job in England because his lies in court were exposed, he had come to France to work as a spy—first spying on English exiles, then on the French citizens. Under the old king, he had spied on Saint Antoine and Defarge's wine shop, using police records about Doctor Manette to try to trick the Defarges into talking. But he had failed completely. He remembered with terror how Madame Defarge had knitted while talking to him, looking at him coldly. Since then, he had seen her in the neighborhood meetings pointing to her knitting to accuse people who were then executed. He knew he was never safe, flight was impossible, and one word could send him to the Guillotine. If he were reported on these charges, Madame Defarge would surely produce her knitted records against him. The spy turned pale as he realized how helpless he was.

“You don't seem to like your cards,” Sydney said calmly. “Are you going to play?”

“I think, sir,” the spy said in a groveling tone, turning to Mr. Lorry, “I can ask a kind gentleman of your age to ask this younger man if he really thinks it is right to report me. I admit I am a spy, and that it's a dirty job—though someone has to do it. But this gentleman is not a spy, so why should he lower himself to act like one?”

“I am playing my Ace, Mr. Barsad,” Carton answered, looking at his watch, “without any hesitation, in just a few minutes.”

“I hoped, gentlemen,” the spy said, trying to get Mr. Lorry to speak, “that out of respect for my sister—”

“I couldn't show more respect for your sister than by freeing her of her brother once and for all,” said Sydney Carton.

“You think so, sir?”

“I have completely made up my mind.”

The spy's polite manners, which didn't match his rough clothes, were useless against Carton's blank, mysterious expression. Not knowing what to do, the spy remained quiet while Carton spoke again, looking at his imaginary cards:

“And indeed, now I think again, I have a strong impression that I have another good card here, not yet enumerated. That friend and fellow-Sheep, who spoke of himself as pasturing in the country prisons; who was he?”

“He is French. You don't know him,” the spy said quickly.

“French, is he?” Carton repeated thoughtfully, looking away. “Well, maybe he is.”

“He is, I assure you,” said the spy, “though it doesn't matter.”

“Though it doesn't matter,” Carton repeated slowly. “No, it doesn't matter. But I know his face.”

“I think not. I am sure you don't. It's impossible,” said the spy.

“It's impossible,” Sydney Carton muttered, spinning his glass. “Impossible. He spoke good French, but he sounded like a foreigner, didn't he?”

“He just has an accent from the countryside,” said the spy.

“No, he is a foreigner!” Carton cried, hitting the table as he suddenly realized the truth. “It is Roger Cly! He is in disguise, but it's him. We saw him at the trial in London.”

“Now, you are being hasty, sir,” Barsad said, smiling. “You are making a mistake. Cly—who was my partner, I admit now—has been dead for years. I was with him when he died. He was buried in London at Saint Pancras Church. The crowd hated him so much that I couldn't attend the funeral service, but I helped put him in his coffin.”

Here, Mr. Lorry noticed a strange, spiky shadow on the wall. Looking over, he saw it was caused by all the hair on Jerry Cruncher's head standing straight up in excitement.

“Let's be reasonable,” the spy said. “To show you that you are wrong, I have Cly's burial certificate right here in my wallet.” He quickly pulled it out. “Here it is. Look at it. You can hold it; it is real.”

Mr. Lorry saw the shadow move, and Jerry Cruncher stepped forward. His spiky hair was standing on end as if he had been shocked by electricity.

Without the spy seeing him, Jerry stood at his side and tapped his shoulder like a ghost.

“That Roger Cly,” Jerry said with a grim face. “So _you_ put him in his coffin?”

“I did.”

“Then who took him out of it?”

Barsad leaned back and stammered, “What do you mean?”

“I mean,” Jerry said, “that he was never in it! No, not him! I will lose my head if he was ever in that coffin.”

The spy looked at Carton and Lorry, who were both staring at Jerry in absolute surprise.

“I tell you,” Jerry said, “that you buried paving stones and dirt in that coffin. Don't tell me you buried Cly. It was a trick. I and two other men know it.”

“How do you know that?”

“What is that to you? Damn!” Jerry growled. “So it's you I have an old grudge against, with your trickery! I'd choke you right now for half a coin.”

Sydney Carton, who was just as surprised as Mr. Lorry, told Jerry to calm down and explain.

“Some other time, sir,” Jerry replied, avoiding the question. “Right now is not a good time to explain. All I say is that he knows very well Cly was never in that coffin. Let him deny it, and I will choke him, or I will go and report him to the police.”

“Well, I see one thing,” Carton said. “I have another card, Mr. Barsad. It is impossible for you to survive in Paris if I report you for working with another English spy who faked his death. They will call it a foreign plot in the prisons against the Republic. That is a winning card—a Guillotine card! Do you play?”

“No!” the spy replied. “I give up. I admit we were so hated in England that faking Cly's death was the only way he could escape. How this man knows it was a trick is a mystery to me.”

“Don't worry about me,” Jerry replied. “You have enough trouble dealing with this gentleman. And listen, I'd still choke you for half a coin!”

The spy turned back to Carton and said, “Let's get to the point. I have to go on duty soon. What is your proposal? Don't ask too much of me. If you ask me to help someone escape, which puts my head on the line, I'd rather refuse. We are all desperate. Remember, I can report you too, and I can lie in court to protect myself. What do you want?”

“Not much. You are a guard at the Conciergerie?”

“I tell you now, an escape is completely impossible,” the spy said firmly.

“Why are you telling me what I didn't ask? You are a guard at the Conciergerie?”

“Sometimes.”

“You can get inside when you choose?”

“I can go in and out when I choose.”

Sydney Carton filled another glass with brandy, poured it slowly onto the hearth, and watched it drip away. Once the glass was empty, he stood up and said:

“We spoke in front of these two so they could witness the cards I hold. Now, come into this back room, and let's have one final word alone.”

CHAPTER IX: The Game Made

While Sydney Carton and the prison spy were in the adjoining back room, speaking so quietly that not a sound could be heard, Mr. Lorry looked at Jerry Cruncher with great doubt and suspicion. Jerry's reaction did not inspire confidence; he shifted his weight from one leg to the other as if he had fifty legs and were trying them all out. He examined his fingernails closely, and whenever Mr. Lorry looked at him, he let out a fake little cough into his hand—a clear sign that he was hiding something.

“Jerry,” Mr. Lorry said. “Come here.”

Jerry stepped forward sideways, keeping one shoulder in front of him.

“What have you been doing, besides working as a messenger?”

After thinking for a moment and looking closely at his boss, Jerry came up with the idea of replying, “I'm an agricultural character.”

“I suspect,” Mr. Lorry said, shaking his finger at him angrily, “that you have used Tellson's Bank as a cover, and that you have been doing some illegal and shameful work. If you have, don't expect me to help you when we get back to England, and don't expect me to keep your secret. Tellson's will not be associated with a criminal.”

“I hope, sir,” the embarrassed Jerry pleaded, “that a gentleman like you, for whom I have worked until I'm gray, would think twice before hurting me, even if it were true—I'm not saying it is, but even if it were. And you have to consider that there are two sides to it. There are doctors right now earning lots of money where an honest worker like me doesn't earn a penny, putting their money in Tellson's and looking down on me while they drive away in their carriages. Well, they are using Tellson's too. You have to treat everyone the same. And my wife back in England was always praying against my business, which was ruining it! Meanwhile, the doctors' wives don't do that. Plus, what with greedy undertakers, clerks, grave-diggers, and watchmen all wanting a cut, a man doesn't make much money from it anyway. And what little he does get never brings him luck, Mr. Lorry. He never gets any good from it and wants to get out of the business if he can, once he's in—even if it were true.”

“Ugh!” Mr. Lorry said, softening a bit anyway. “I am shocked by you.”

“Now, what I would humbly suggest, sir,” Jerry continued, “even if it were true, which I'm not saying it is—”

“Don't lie,” said Mr. Lorry.

“No, I won't, sir,” Jerry replied as if lying were the last thing he would do, “even if it were true. What I would suggest is this: back in London, my son sits on my stool at the bank, ready to do any errands or light jobs for you as hard as he can. If it were true—which I still don't admit—please let my boy keep his job to support his mother. Don't expose him because of his father. Let his father go into honest grave-digging instead, making up for what he dug up by burying people properly from now on. That, Mr. Lorry,” Jerry said, wiping his forehead with his arm to finish his speech, “is what I suggest. A man can't see all this death around him in Paris, with so many headless bodies, without thinking seriously about life. And these are my thoughts, begging you to remember that I spoke up today to help you when I could have kept quiet.”

“That at least is true,” Mr. Lorry said. “Say no more now. Perhaps I will still help you, if you deserve it and show you are sorry through your actions, not just words. I don't want to hear any more.”

Jerry saluted by touching his forehead as Sydney Carton and the spy returned from the back room. “Goodbye, Mr. Barsad,” Carton said. “Now that we have made our agreement, you have nothing to fear from me.”

He sat down in a chair by the fire, opposite Mr. Lorry. Once they were alone, Mr. Lorry asked him what he had accomplished.

“Not much. If things go badly for the prisoner, I have arranged to visit him once.”

Mr. Lorry's face fell.

“It is all I could do,” Carton said. “Asking for too much would have put the spy's own head under the axe, and as he said, nothing worse could happen to him if he were reported. That was the limit of what we could get. We have to accept it.”

“But visiting him,” Mr. Lorry said, “won't save him if he is condemned by the court.”

“I never said it would.”

Mr. Lorry looked back at the fire. His sympathy for Lucie and the shock of Charles's second arrest overwhelmed him. He was an old man, worn out by worry, and he began to cry.

“You are a good man and a true friend,” Carton said in a different, gentler voice. “Forgive me for noticing your tears. I could not watch my own father cry and do nothing, and I could not respect your grief more if you were my father. Though you are spared that misfortune, at least.”

Although Carton made a joke at the end in his usual style, there was a real feeling of respect in his voice and touch. Mr. Lorry, who had never seen this side of him before, was completely surprised. He held out his hand, and Carton squeezed it gently.

“Regarding Darnay,” Carton said. “Don't tell Lucie about this meeting or our agreement. It wouldn't allow her to see him anyway, and she might worry that we are planning for his execution, or trying to smuggle in a way for him to end his life early.”

Mr. Lorry had not thought of that, and he looked at Carton quickly to see if that was what he meant. It seemed to be; Carton returned his look, showing he understood.

“She might worry about a thousand things,” Carton said, “and that would only add to her pain. Don't mention me to her. As I said when I arrived, it is better if I don't see her. I can help her from a distance. You are going to see her now, I hope? She must be very lonely tonight.”

“I am going right now.”

“I am glad. She loves and relies on you so much. How does she look?”

“Anxious and sad, but very beautiful.”

“Ah!”

It was a long, sad sound, like a sigh or a sob. Mr. Lorry looked at Carton's face, which was turned toward the fire. A look of emotion passed over it quickly. Carton raised his foot to push back a burning log that was rolling out. He wore a white coat and boots, and the firelight made him look very pale, with his long, uncombed brown hair hanging loose. He seemed so distracted that he didn't even notice his boot was resting on the hot coals until the wood broke under his foot, prompting Mr. Lorry to warn him.

“I forgot about it,” he said.

Mr. Lorry looked at his face again. Seeing the tired, worn look on Carton's handsome features, he was reminded of the look he had seen on the faces of the prisoners.

“And is your work here finished, sir?” Carton asked, turning to him.

“Yes. As I was telling you last night before Lucie arrived, I have done everything I can here. I hoped to leave them safe and then exit Paris. I already have my travel papers. I was ready to go.”

They were both silent.

“You have a long life to look back on, sir?” Carton asked thoughtfully.

“I am seventy-seven years old.”

“You have been useful all your life, always busy, trusted, respected, and looked up to?”

“I have been a businessman ever since I grew up. Indeed, I was doing business even as a boy.”

“Look at the important place you fill at your age. So many people will miss you when you are gone!”

“I am just a lonely bachelor,” Mr. Lorry replied, shaking his head. “There is no one to cry for me.”

“How can you say that? Wouldn't Lucie cry for you? Wouldn't her child?”

“Yes, yes, thank God. I didn't quite mean that.”

“That is something to be thankful for, isn't it?”

“Of course, of course.”

“If you had to say to yourself tonight, 'No one loves or respects me, I have no place in anyone's heart, and I have done nothing good to be remembered by,' then your seventy-seven years would be a heavy curse, wouldn't they?”

“You are right, Mr. Carton. I think they would be.”

Sydney looked back at the fire and, after a short silence, said:

“I want to ask you: does your childhood seem very far away? Do the days when you sat with your mother feel like a lifetime ago?”

Responding to Carton's gentle tone, Mr. Lorry answered:

“Twenty years ago, yes. But at my age, no. As I get closer to the end of my life, I feel like I am circling back to the beginning. It feels like a gentle way of preparing for death. I keep remembering things from my childhood that I had forgotten, like my beautiful young mother—even though I am so old now—and the days before I grew hard and set in my ways.”

“I understand that feeling!” Carton said, his face flushing. “And does it make you feel better?”

“I hope so.”

Carton ended the conversation by standing up to help him with his coat. “But you,” Mr. Lorry said, returning to the subject, “you are still young.”

“Yes,” Carton said. “I am not old, but the way I lived my youth was never going to lead to a long old age. Enough about me.”

“And enough about me, too,” Mr. Lorry said. “Are you going out?”

“I'll walk with you to her gate. You know my habit of wandering around. If I stay out walking in the streets all night, don't worry. I will see you in the morning. You are going to the court tomorrow?”

“Yes, unfortunately.”

“I will be there too, just as part of the crowd. My spy will find a place for me. Take my arm, sir.”

Mr. Lorry took his arm, and they walked downstairs into the street. A few minutes later, they arrived at Lucie's building. Carton said goodbye, but stayed nearby. After the gate shut, he walked back and touched it. He had heard about her daily walks to the prison. “She stood here,” he said, looking around. “She walked this way, stepping on these very stones. Let me follow her steps.”

It was ten o'clock at night when he stood outside La Force prison, where Lucie had waited so many times. The little wood-sawyer, having closed his shop, was smoking a pipe at his door.

“Good night, citizen,” Sydney Carton said as he walked past, since the man was staring at him curiously.

“Good night, citizen.”

“How is the Republic doing?”

“You mean the Guillotine. Not bad. Sixty-three people today. We will reach a hundred soon. The executioner Samson and his men complain they are tired. Ha, ha, ha! He is so funny, that Samson. What a barber!”

“Do you go to watch him often?”

“Shave? Always. Every day. What a barber! Have you seen him work?”

“Never.”

“Go watch him when he has a big group. Imagine this, citizen: he executed all sixty-three today in less time than it takes to smoke two pipes! Less than two pipes, I swear!”

As the grinning man held out his pipe to show how he timed the executions, Carton felt such a strong urge to punch him that he had to turn away.

“But you aren't French, are you, even though you wear English clothes?”

“Yes,” Carton said, pausing and looking back over his shoulder.

“You speak like a Frenchman.”

“I used to study here.”

“Aha, a perfect Frenchman! Good night, Englishman.”

“Good night, citizen.”

“But go watch that funny guy,” the little man called after him. “And take a pipe with you!”

Sydney walked a short distance, stopped under a street lamp, and wrote on a piece of paper. Then, walking confidently through several dark, dirty streets—which were neglected during the revolution—he stopped at a pharmacy that the owner was closing. It was a tiny, dim, crooked shop on a steep street, owned by a small, crooked man.

Greeting the pharmacist, Carton laid his paper on the counter. The chemist whistled softly as he read it, murmuring, “Oh! My, my!”

Sydney Carton ignored him, and the chemist asked:

“Is this for you, citizen?”

“For me.”

“You will keep them separate, citizen? You know what happens if you mix them?”

“Perfectly.”

The chemist prepared small packets and handed them over. Carton put them in his inside coat pocket, paid for them, and walked out. “There is nothing left to do,” he said, looking up at the moon, “until tomorrow. I can't sleep.”

His voice was not careless or angry. It was the calm voice of a tired man who had been lost for a long time but had finally found his path and could see the end of it.

Long ago, when he was a promising young student, he had attended his father's funeral. His mother had died years before that. The religious words read at his father's grave came back to him now as he walked through the dark, shadowy streets under the moon: “I am the resurrection and the life, saith the Lord: he that believeth in me, though he were dead, yet shall he live: and whosoever liveth and believeth in me, shall never die.”

In a city ruled by the Guillotine, walking alone at night, he felt sad for the sixty-three people executed that day and for the next day's victims waiting in their cells. The words comforted him like an anchor in a storm. He did not think about why they came to him, but simply repeated them as he walked.

He looked at the lit windows where people were going to sleep, escaping the day's horrors for a few hours. He saw the dark church towers where no one prayed anymore, because the people had rejected religion after years of corrupt priests. He thought of the cemeteries with signs reading "Eternal Sleep," the crowded jails, and the streets where cartloads of people rode to their deaths daily. Walking through this city of death as it quieted down for the night, Carton crossed the river again.

There were few carriages on the streets, as riding in them looked suspicious. Wealthy people wore simple red caps and heavy shoes to look like common workers. However, the theaters were packed, and crowds of people walked home chatting happily. Near a theater door, he saw a mother and a little girl trying to cross the muddy street. He carried the child across, and before she let go of his neck, he asked her for a kiss.

“I am the resurrection and the life, saith the Lord: he that believeth in me, though he were dead, yet shall he live: and whosoever liveth and believeth in me, shall never die.”

Now that the streets were quiet and the night grew late, the words seemed to echo in his footsteps and in the air. Calm and steady, he repeated them to himself as he walked, hearing them constantly.

The night ended. As he stood on the bridge listening to the river against the walls of the city, where houses and the cathedral shone under the moon, dawn broke coldly like a dead face in the sky. The stars faded, and for a moment, the world seemed cold and lifeless.

But when the sun rose, its warm rays seemed to bring the words straight to his heart. Shading his eyes, he saw a bridge of light stretching across the sparkling river toward the sun.

The deep, swift river felt like a friend in the quiet morning. He walked along the bank far from any houses and fell asleep in the warm sun. When he woke up, he stayed a bit longer, watching a small whirlpool spin uselessly until the river swept it away toward the sea. “Just like me,” he thought.

A boat with a sail the color of a dead leaf floated past him and disappeared. As the ripples in the water faded, the prayer in his heart for forgiveness for all his past mistakes ended with the words, “I am the resurrection and the life.”

Mr. Lorry was already gone when Carton returned, and it was easy to guess where the old man had went. Carton drank some coffee, ate a bit of bread, washed his face, changed his clothes, and walked to the courtroom.

The courtroom was crowded and noisy when the spy—whom everyone avoided in fear—guided Carton into a quiet corner in the crowd. Mr. Lorry and Doctor Manette were there, and Lucie sat next to her father.

When Charles was brought in, she looked at him with such love, encouragement, and courage that it brought color back to his face and gave him strength. If anyone had looked at Sydney Carton's face at that moment, they would have seen the exact same look of devotion in his eyes.

In this unfair court, there was no real legal procedure to give the accused a fair trial. The Revolution had only happened because the old laws had been so badly abused, and now the angry Republic had thrown all legal rules out the window.

Everyone stared at the jury. They were the same fierce patriots who sat there every day. Prominent among them was one man with a hungry look who kept touching his lips, which pleased the crowd. He was Jacques Three of Saint Antoine, a bloodthirsty juror. The whole jury looked like a pack of wild dogs gathered to try a deer.

Then everyone looked at the five judges and the prosecutor. There was no sympathy there today; they looked grim and ready to execute. The people in the crowd looked at each other and nodded in approval, leaning forward to pay close attention.

Charles Evremonde, called Darnay. Released yesterday. Re-accused and arrested again yesterday. Charges delivered to him last night. Suspected and accused enemy of the Republic, an aristocrat from a family of tyrants, whose class was outlawed for oppressing the common people. Because of this, Charles Darnay was already considered dead under the law.

The public prosecutor spoke to this effect in very few words.

The president asked if the accused was reported openly or in secret.

“Openly, President.”

“By whom?”

“Three voices. Ernest Defarge, wine shop owner of Saint Antoine.”

“Good.”

“Therese Defarge, his wife.”

“Good.”

“Alexandre Manette, physician.”

A huge uproar broke out in the courtroom. In the middle of the noise, Doctor Manette stood up, pale and shaking.

“President, I protest! This is a lie and a fraud!” he cried. “You know the accused is my daughter's husband. My daughter and her family are dearer to me than my own life. Who is the liar saying that I accused my daughter's husband!”

“Citizen Manette, calm down. Refusing to obey the court will make you an outlaw. As for what is dearer than life, nothing can be more precious to a good citizen than the Republic.”
