The crowd cheered loudly at this warning. The president rang his bell and continued passionately:

“If the Republic demands the sacrifice of your own child, your duty is to give her up. Listen to what is next, and stay silent!”

The crowd cheered wildly again. Doctor Manette sat down, his eyes wide and his lips trembling, while Lucie drew closer to him. The bloodthirsty juror rubbed his hands together and touched his lips again.

When the room quieted down, Defarge was brought forward. He explained how he had been a boy working for the Doctor, how the Doctor was released, and the state he was in when he was delivered to the wine shop. A short questioning followed, as the court worked quickly.

“You did good work at the capture of the Bastille, citizen?”

“I believe so.”

An excited woman in the crowd LSM screamed, “You were one of the best patriots there! Why not say it? You worked the cannons that day, and you were among the first to enter the fortress when it fell. Patriots, I tell the truth!”

It was The Vengeance shouting this, to the cheers of the crowd. The president rang his bell, but she screamed, “I defy that bell!” which pleased the crowd even more.

“Tell the court what you did inside the Bastille that day, citizen.”

“I knew,” Defarge said, looking down at his wife, who stood below him watching him closely, “that this prisoner had been kept in cell One Hundred and Five, North Tower. I knew it from the Doctor himself. He called himself by that name when he made shoes in my attic. While working my cannon that day, I resolved to search that cell once the Bastille fell. When it did, I went up to the cell with a fellow citizen who is now on the jury. In a hole in the chimney where a stone had been loose, I found a paper. This is that paper. I have compared it with Doctor Manette's handwriting, and they match. I hand this paper over to the President of the court.”

“Let it be read.”

In absolute silence, Charles looked lovingly at his wife, who looked back and forth between him and her father. Doctor Manette kept his eyes on the reader, Madame Defarge stared at the prisoner, Defarge watched his wife, and everyone else looked at the Doctor. The paper was then read aloud as follows.

CHAPTER X: The Substance of the Shadow

“I, Alexandre Manette, an unfortunate doctor from Beauvais who later lived in Paris, write this sad paper in my dark cell in the Bastille during the last month of the year 1767. I write it in secret moments, facing every difficulty. I plan to hide it in the chimney wall, where I have slowly and carefully made a hiding place for it. Some kind hand may find it there when I and my sorrows are nothing but dust.

“These words are written with a rusty iron point, which I use with great difficulty, using soot and charcoal scraped from the chimney and mixed with my own blood. It is now the last month of the tenth year of my imprisonment. Hope has completely left my heart. I know from terrible warning signs in myself that my mind will not stay clear for much longer. However, I solemnly declare that right now I am in my right mind—that my memory is exact and detailed—and that I write the truth as I will have to answer for these last recorded words at God's final judgment, whether they are ever read by men or not.

“One cloudy, moonlit night in the third week of December (I think it was the twenty-second) in the year 1757, I was walking on a quiet part of the path along the Seine River to get some fresh, frosty air. I was about an hour's walk from my home on the Street of the School of Medicine when a carriage came up behind me, driving very fast. I stepped aside to let the carriage pass, worried that it might run me over, when a head popped out of the window and a voice called for the driver to stop.

“The carriage stopped as soon as the driver could pull back his horses, and the same voice called out my name. I answered. The carriage was so far ahead of me that two gentlemen had time to open the door and step down before I reached it.

“I noticed that they were both wrapped in cloaks and seemed to be hiding their identities. As they stood side by side near the carriage door, I also noticed that they both looked to be about my age, or perhaps younger, and they were extremely similar in height, behavior, voice, and (from what I could see) face as well.

“‘Are you Doctor Manette?’ one asked.

“I am.”

“‘Doctor Manette, formerly of Beauvais,’ said the other, ‘the young doctor, originally a skilled surgeon, who has made a growing reputation in Paris over the last year or two?’

“‘Gentlemen,’ I replied, ‘I am indeed the Doctor Manette you speak of so kindly.’

“‘We went to your home,’ said the first man, ‘but we were not lucky enough to find you. When we were told you were probably walking in this direction, we followed you, hoping to catch up. Please get into the carriage.’

“The manner of both men was very commanding, and as they spoke, they moved to trap me between them and the carriage door. They were armed. I was not.

“‘Gentlemen,’ I said, ‘excuse me, but I usually ask who is asking for my help, and what kind of medical case I am being called to see.’

“The second man replied to this. ‘Doctor, your patients are people of high social standing. As for the nature of the illness, we are so confident in your skill that we know you will figure it out for yourself better than we could describe it. That is enough. Please get into the carriage.’

“I had no choice but to obey, and I got in silently. They both followed me—the last one jumping in after folding up the carriage steps. The carriage turned around and drove off at the same high speed as before.

“I repeat this conversation exactly as it happened. I have no doubt it is word-for-word correct. I describe everything exactly as it occurred, forcing my mind not to wander from the task. Wherever I make the star marks that follow, it means I stopped writing for a while and put my paper back in its hiding place.

*****

“The carriage left the city streets, passed through the North Gate, and went onto a country road. About two miles from the gate—I did not measure the distance then, but did so later when I traveled it again—the carriage turned off the main road and soon stopped at a lonely house. The three of us got out and walked down a damp, soft garden path, past a neglected, overflowing fountain, to the door. It was not opened right away when we rang the bell, so one of my guides struck the servant who finally opened it across the face with his heavy riding glove.

“There was nothing unusual in this to catch my attention, for I had seen common people beaten more often than dogs. But the other gentleman, who was also angry, struck the servant in the same way with his arm. The looks and actions of the two men were so identical that I realized for the first time they were twin brothers.

“From the moment we got out at the outer gate (which we found locked, and which one of the brothers unlocked to let us in and then locked again), I had heard screams coming from an upstairs room. I was taken straight to this room, the screams growing louder as we climbed the stairs, and I found a patient with a severe brain fever lying on a bed.

“The patient was a very beautiful young woman, certainly not much over twenty. Her hair was torn and messy, and her arms were tied to her sides with sashes and handkerchiefs. I noticed that these ties were all parts of a gentleman's clothing. On one of them, which was a fringed scarf used for formal wear, I saw the coat of arms of a nobleman and the letter E.

“I saw this during my very first minute of examining the patient. In her restless struggling, she had turned over onto her face at the edge of the bed, drawing the end of the scarf into her mouth, and was in danger of choking. My first act was to reach out and help her breathe, and as I moved the scarf away, I saw the embroidered coat of arms in the corner.

“I turned her over gently, placed my hands on her chest to calm her and keep her still, and looked into her face. Her eyes were wide and wild, and she kept screaming and repeating the words, ‘My husband, my father, and my brother!’ She would then count up to twelve and say, ‘Hush!’ For just a second, she would stop to listen, and then the piercing screams would start all over again. She would repeat the cry, ‘My husband, my father, and my brother!’ count to twelve, and say, ‘Hush!’ She never changed the order or the way she said it. She never stopped, except for that regular, brief moment of listening.

“‘How long,’ I asked, ‘has this been going on?’

“To tell the brothers apart, I will call them the older and the younger. By the older, I mean the one who showed the most authority. It was the older brother who replied, ‘Since about this time last night.’

“‘Does she have a husband, a father, and a brother?’

“‘A brother.’

“‘Am I speaking to her brother?’

“He answered with great scorn, ‘No.’

“‘Has she recently associated something with the number twelve?’

“The younger brother added impatiently, ‘With twelve o'clock?’

“‘Look, gentlemen,’ I said, keeping my hands on her chest, ‘I am useless to you since you brought me here like this! If I had known what kind of case I was coming to see, I could have brought the right supplies. Now we must waste time. There are no medicines to be found in this lonely place.’

“The older brother looked at the younger, who said proudly, ‘There is a box of medicines here,’ and brought it out of a closet, placing it on the table.

*****

“I opened some of the bottles, smelled them, and put the stoppers to my lips. If I had wanted to use anything other than strong sedatives—which are poisons in large doses—I would not have used any of them.

“‘Do you doubt the medicines?’ asked the younger brother.

“‘You see, monsieur, I am about to use them,’ I replied, and said nothing more.

“With great difficulty and after many attempts, I got the patient to swallow the dose I wanted to give her. Since I planned to give it to her again later and needed to watch how it affected her, I sat down next to the bed. There was a quiet, timid woman helping (the wife of the servant downstairs) who had shrunk into a corner. The house was damp, decaying, and poorly furnished—clearly it had been occupied recently and only for temporary use. Thick old curtains had been nailed over the windows to muffle the sound of her screams. She kept screaming in the same regular order: the cry of ‘My husband, my father, and my brother!’ the count to twelve, and ‘Hush!’ Her madness was so violent that I did not loosen the bandages holding her arms, though I checked to make sure they were not hurting her. The only encouraging sign was that my hand on the poor girl's chest had a soothing effect, quietening her body for minutes at a time. However, it did not stop her cries; no clock pendulum could have been more regular.

“Probably because my hand had this calming effect, I sat by the bedside for half an hour with the two brothers watching before the older one spoke:

“‘There is another patient.’

“I was startled and asked, ‘Is it an urgent case?’

“‘You had better see for yourself,’ he answered carelessly, and picked up a candle.

*****

“The other patient was in a back room across a second staircase, which was a kind of loft over a stable. Part of the room had a low, plastered ceiling, while the rest was open to the tiled roof with wooden beams running across. Hay and straw were stored in that part of the loft, along with firewood and a pile of apples stored in sand. I had to walk through that storage area to reach the patient. My memory is detailed and firm. I test it with these details, and I can see them all here in my Bastille cell, near the end of the tenth year of my imprisonment, just as I saw them that night.

“On some hay on the ground, with a cushion placed under his head, lay a handsome peasant boy—he could not have been more than seventeen years old. He lay on his back with his teeth clenched, his right hand pressed tightly against his chest, and his staring eyes looking straight up. I could not see where his wound was as I knelt beside him, but I could tell he was dying from a puncture wound.

“‘I am a doctor, my poor boy,’ I said. ‘Let me look at your wound.’

“‘I don't want it examined,’ he answered. ‘Leave it alone.’

“The wound was under his hand, and I calmed him until he let me move his hand away. It was a sword wound received twenty to twenty-four hours earlier, but no medical skill could have saved him even if he had been treated immediately. He was dying fast. When I looked up at the older brother, I saw him looking down at this handsome, dying boy as if he were just a wounded bird, a hare, or a rabbit—not a fellow human being at all.

“‘How did this happen, monsieur?’ I asked.

“‘A crazy young peasant dog! A serf! He forced my brother to draw his sword against him, and fell by my brother's blade—like a gentleman.’

“There was no trace of pity, sadness, or human feeling in his answer. The speaker spoke as if it were simply inconvenient to have this lower kind of creature dying there, and that it would have been better if he had died in the usual quiet way of his vermin class. He was completely unable to feel any sympathy for the boy or his fate.

“The boy's eyes had moved slowly to the man as he spoke, and now they slowly turned to me.

“‘Doctor, they are very proud, these Nobles; but we common dogs are proud too, sometimes. They rob us, abuse us, beat us, and kill us, but we still have a little pride left sometimes. She—have you seen her, Doctor?’

“Her screams and cries could be heard there, though they were quieted by the distance. He spoke of her as if she were lying right there with us.

“I said, ‘I have seen her.’

“‘She is my sister, Doctor. For many years, these Nobles have taken their shameful rights with our sisters' modesty and virtue, but we have had good girls among us. I know it, and I've heard my father say so. She was a good girl. She was engaged to a good young man, too, who was a tenant of his. We were all tenants of his—that man standing there. The other one is his brother, the worst of a bad family.’

“It was extremely hard for the boy to find the strength to speak, but his spirit spoke with terrifying power.

“‘We were robbed so badly by that man standing there, just as all of us common dogs are by those superior Beings—taxed by him without mercy, forced to work for him without pay, forced to grind our grain at his mill, forced to feed dozens of his tame birds on our poor crops, while we were forbidden under pain of death to keep a single tame bird of our own. We were plundered to the point that if we happened to have a piece of meat, we ate it in fear with the door locked and the shutters closed so his servants wouldn't see it and take it from us. I tell you, we were so robbed and hunted, and made so poor, that our father told us it was a terrible thing to bring a child into the world. He said we should pray that our women would be barren and our miserable family line would die out!’

“I had never before seen the pain of oppression burst out like a fire. I had assumed it must be hidden in the common people somewhere, but I had never seen it break out until I saw it in this dying boy.

“‘Even so, Doctor, my sister married. Her lover was sick at the time, poor guy, and she married him so she could care for and comfort him in our cottage—our dog-hut, as that nobleman would call it. She had only been married a few weeks when that man's brother saw her, took a liking to her, and asked that nobleman to lend her to him—because what are husbands to people like us! The nobleman was willing enough, but my sister was good and pure, and she hated the brother as much as I did. So what did the two of them do to force her husband to make her give in?’

“The boy's eyes, which had been fixed on mine, turned slowly toward the older brother, and I could see from both of their faces that everything he said was true. Even now in the Bastille, I can still picture those two opposite kinds of pride facing each other: the nobleman's careless indifference, and the peasant's crushed feelings and passionate desire for revenge.

“‘You know, Doctor, that these Nobles have the legal right to harness us common dogs to carts and drive us like horses. They harnessed him and drove him that way. You know it is their right to keep us in their swamps all night, beating the water to quiet the frogs so their noble sleep won't be disturbed. They kept him out in the unhealthy night mists and ordered him back to work during the day. But he would not give in. No! Taken out of the harness one day at noon to eat—if he could find any food—he sobbed twelve times, once for each strike of the clock, and died in her arms.’

"Nothing human could have kept the boy alive except his determination to tell of his wrongs. He fought off the approaching shadows of death, just as he forced his clenched right hand to stay closed over his wound.

“‘Then, with that nobleman's permission and even his help, his brother took her away. In spite of what I know she must have told the brother—and you will find out what that was soon enough, Doctor, if you don't know already—his brother took her away for his own pleasure and amusement for a little while. I saw her pass me on the road. When I brought the news home, our father's heart broke; he died without saying a word of the pain that filled him. I took my youngest sister (for I have another one) to a place far away out of this man's reach, where she will never be _his_ servant. Then I followed the brother here, and last night I climbed in—a common dog, but with a sword in my hand. Where is the loft window? It was around here somewhere.’

“The room was growing dark to his eyes; the world was closing in on him. I looked around and saw that the hay and straw were scattered all over the floor, as if there had been a fight.

“‘She heard me and ran in. I told her not to come near us until he was dead. He came in and first threw some coins at me, then struck at me with a whip. But even though I am a common dog, I attacked him so hard that I forced him to draw his sword. Let him break that sword into a thousand pieces—the sword he stained with my common blood—but he had to draw it to defend himself and fight with all his skill for his life.’

“Just a few moments earlier, my eyes had fallen on the pieces of a broken sword lying in the hay. That weapon belonged to a gentleman. In another spot lay an old sword that looked like it had belonged to a soldier.

“‘Now lift me up, Doctor; lift me up. Where is he?’

“‘He is not here,’ I said, holding the boy up, thinking he was talking about the brother.

“‘Him! As proud as these nobles are, he is afraid to look at me. Where is the man who was standing here? Turn my face toward him.’

“I did so, resting the boy's head against my knee. But suddenly filled with an unbelievable strength, he pulled himself all the way up, forcing me to stand up too so I could keep supporting him.

“‘Marquis,’ the boy said, turning to him with wide eyes and his right hand raised, ‘in the days when all these things must be answered for, I summon you and your family, to the very last of your evil race, to answer for them. I mark this cross of blood upon you as a sign of my summons. In the days when all these things must be answered for, I summon your brother, the worst of your evil family, to answer for them separately. I mark this cross of blood upon him as a sign of my summons.’

“Twice he touched the wound in his chest and drew a cross in the air with his index finger. He stood for a moment with his finger still raised, and when it fell, he fell with it. I laid him down, dead.

*****

“When I went back to the young woman's bedside, I found her screaming in the exact same order as before. I knew this might go on for many hours, and that it would likely end only in death.

“I gave her another dose of the medicine and sat by the bed until late in the night. She never stopped her piercing screams, and never made a mistake in the clear order of her words. They were always: ‘My husband, my father, and my brother! One, two, three, four, five, six, seven, eight, nine, ten, eleven, twelve. Hush!’

“This went on for twenty-six hours from the time I first saw her. I had left and returned twice, and was sitting by her again when her strength began to fail. I did what little I could to help her rest, and soon she fell into a deep sleep and lay as still as a corpse. It was as if the wind and rain had finally stopped after a long, terrifying storm. I untied her arms and called the servant's wife to help me straighten her body and the clothes she had torn. It was then I realized she was pregnant, and at that moment I lost the little hope I had left for her.

“‘Is she dead?’ asked the Marquis, the older brother, walking into the room still wearing his riding boots.

“‘Not dead,’ I said, ‘but close to it.’

“‘What strength there is in these peasant bodies!’ he said, looking down at her with curiosity.

“‘There is enormous strength,’ I answered him, ‘in grief and despair.’

“He laughed at my words at first, then frowned. He kicked a chair over near mine, ordered the woman to leave, and said in a quiet voice:

“‘Doctor, when I found my brother in this trouble with these peasants, I suggested we call for your help. Your reputation is high, and as a young man trying to make a career, you probably care about your own success. The things you see here are meant to be seen, but never spoken of.’

“I listened to the patient's breathing and avoided answering him.

“‘Are you listening to me, Doctor?’

“‘Monsieur,’ I said, ‘in my profession, whatever a patient tells a doctor is always kept strictly confidential.’ I was careful with my reply because I was deeply troubled by what I had seen and heard.

“Her breathing was so faint that I carefully checked her pulse and heartbeat. She was barely alive. Looking up as I sat back down, I saw both brothers watching me closely.

*****

“I write with so much difficulty, the cold is so bitter, and I am so afraid of being caught and thrown into a dark dungeon that I must shorten this story. There is no confusion or memory loss; I can remember and detail every single word spoken between me and those brothers.

“She hung onto life for a week. Near the end, I could make out a few words she whispered by putting my ear close to her lips. She asked me where she was and who I was, and I told her. I tried in vain to ask for her family name. She just shook her head weakly on the pillow, keeping her secret just as her brother had done.

"I had no chance to ask her any questions until I told the brothers she was dying and could not last another day. Until then, even though she only saw the servant's wife and me, one of the brothers had always sat watchfully behind the curtain at the head of the bed whenever I was there. But when she was near death, they didn't seem to care what I said to her—as if, a sudden thought struck me, they planned for me to die too.

“I noticed that their pride was deeply hurt by the fact that the younger brother had fought a peasant boy. The only thing that seemed to bother either of them was that this fight was degrading to their family name and made them look ridiculous. Whenever I met the younger brother's eyes, I could see that he hated me for knowing what the dying boy had told me. He was more polite and pleasant to me than the older brother, but I could see right through him. I also realized that the older brother saw me as an annoying problem to be dealt with.

“My patient died two hours before midnight—almost to the exact minute I had first seen her a week earlier. I was alone with her when her poor, young head fell gently to the side, and all her earthly pain and sorrows ended.

“The brothers were waiting in a room downstairs, eager to ride away. While I sat alone by the bedside, I had heard them pacing up and down, tapping their boots with their riding whips.

“‘Is she finally dead?’ the older one asked when I went downstairs.

“‘She is dead,’ I said.

“‘Congratulations, brother,’ he said, turning to his twin.

“He had offered me money earlier, which I had put off taking. Now he handed me a roll of gold coins. I took it from his hand but set it down on the table. I had thought it over and decided not to accept anything from them.

“‘Please excuse me,’ I said. ‘Under these circumstances, I cannot take it.’

“They looked at each other, but bowed their heads to me as I bowed to them, and we parted without saying another word.

*****

“I am so tired, tired, tired—worn down by misery. I can barely read what my thin hand has written.

“Early the next morning, the roll of gold was left at my door in a small box with my name written on it. From the beginning, I had worried about what I should do. That day, I decided to write a private letter to the government Minister, explaining the two patients I had been called to see, the location of the house, and all the details. I knew how much influence the Nobles had at Court and how they were above the law, so I expected nothing would be done about it; however, I wanted to clear my own conscience. I had kept the secret completely from my wife, and I decided to mention that in the letter too. I had no fear for my own safety, but I knew there could be danger for others if they were dragged into this by knowing what I knew.

“I was very busy that day and couldn't finish the letter that night. I woke up long before my usual time the next morning to complete it. It was the last day of the year. The letter was lying finished on the desk in front of me when I was told that a lady was waiting to see me.

*****

“I am becoming less and less able to finish this task. It is so cold and dark, my senses are so numb, and the sadness around me is so terrible.

“The lady was young, charming, and beautiful, but she looked pale and weak. She was extremely upset. She introduced herself as the wife of the Marquis St. Evrémonde. I connected the title the dying boy had used for the older brother with the letter embroidered on the scarf, and immediately realized I had met her husband very recently.

“My memory is still clear, but I cannot write down all the words of our conversation. I suspect I am being watched more closely now, and I do not know when they are spying on me. She had guessed some of the facts and discovered the rest of the cruel story—her husband's part in it, and how they had called for my help. She did not know the young woman had died. She told me in great distress that she had hoped to show the poor girl a woman's sympathy in secret. She had hoped to turn away God's anger from a noble family that had long been hated by the suffering people.

“She had reasons to believe there was a younger sister still alive, and her greatest wish was to help her. I could only tell her that the sister did exist; beyond that, I knew nothing. She had come to me, trusting my confidentiality, hoping I could tell her the girl's name and where she lived. But to this miserable hour, I still know neither.

*****

“I am running out of paper. One piece was taken from me yesterday, along with a warning. I must finish my record today.

“She was a good, kind lady, and she was not happy in her marriage. How could she be! The brother-in-law distrusted and disliked her, and his influence was completely against her; she was terrified of him, and of her husband too. When I walked her down to the carriage, I saw a child inside—a pretty little boy about two or three years old.

“‘For his sake, Doctor,’ she said, pointing to the boy through her tears, ‘I want to do everything I can to make up for this. Otherwise, he will never find happiness in his inheritance. I have a feeling that if no other innocent person pays for this crime, it will one day be demanded of him. The little I have of my own—which is hardly more than a few jewels—I will make it his first duty in life to give to this injured family, along with the love and sorrow of his dead mother, if the sister can be found.’

“She kissed the boy and said as she held him close, ‘It is for your own dear sake. You will be faithful to this promise, little Charles?’ The child answered her bravely, ‘Yes!’ I kissed her hand, and she took him in her arms and drove away. I never saw her again.

“Since she had mentioned her husband's name, assuming I already knew it, I did not add it to my letter. I sealed the envelope, and not trusting anyone else, I delivered it myself that day.

“That night, the last night of the year, around nine o'clock, a man dressed in black rang my doorbell, asked to see me, and quietly followed my young servant, Ernest Defarge, upstairs. When my servant entered the room where I sat with my wife—oh, my beloved wife, the joy of my heart, my beautiful young English wife!—we saw the man standing silently behind him.

“He said there was an urgent case on Rue Saint-Honoré. He promised it wouldn't take long and said a carriage was waiting.

“It brought me here; it brought me to my grave. As soon as I stepped out of the house, a black scarf was pulled tightly over my mouth from behind, and my arms were tied. The two brothers walked across the road from a dark corner and pointed me out with a simple gesture. The Marquis took the letter I had written from his pocket, showed it to me, burned it in the light of a lantern, and stamped out the ashes with his foot. Not a single word was spoken. I was brought here—to my living grave.

“If it had pleased _God_ to put it in the hard hearts of either of the brothers during all these terrible years to give me any news of my dearest wife—even just a single word to tell me if she was alive or dead—I might have believed that He had not completely abandoned them. But now I believe that the mark of the red cross has doomed them, and that they have no share in His mercy. So, on this last night of the year 1767, in my unbearable agony, I, Alexandre Manette, an unhappy prisoner, denounce them and their descendants to the very last of their family line, to the time when all these crimes must be answered for. I denounce them to Heaven and to earth.”

A terrifying noise arose when the reading of this document finished. It was a sound of hungry eagerness, with no clear words except a cry for blood. The story stirred up the most vengeful feelings of the time, and there was not a noble head in the country that would not have fallen after hearing it.

There was no need, in front of that court and audience, to explain why the Defarges had not made the paper public with the other captured Bastille records during the parades. They had kept it hidden, waiting for the perfect time. There was no need to show that this hated family name had long been cursed by the people of Saint Antoine, and was already written in the secret registry. No man had ever lived whose good deeds and service could have saved him in that courtroom today against such an accusation.

And it was all the worse for the doomed man that his accuser was a respected citizen, his own close friend, and his wife's father. The crowd's crazy desire was to imitate the harsh public virtues of ancient times, offering up human sacrifices on the altar of the people. Therefore, when the President declared (knowing his own head would roll if he didn't), that the good doctor of the Republic would serve the country even better by wiping out a hated family of aristocrats, and would surely feel a holy joy in making his own daughter a widow and her child an orphan, there was wild excitement and patriotic passion, but not a single drop of human sympathy.

“Does that Doctor have a lot of influence now?” whispered Madame Defarge, smiling at The Vengeance. “Save him now, my Doctor, save him!”

With every juror's vote, a roar went up. Another and another. Roar after roar.

The vote was unanimous. By birth and by nature an aristocrat, an enemy of the Republic, and a well-known oppressor of the people. Back to the Conciergerie prison, and death within twenty-four hours!

CHAPTER XI: Dusk

The poor wife of the innocent man condemned to die collapsed when she heard the sentence, as if she had been struck with a deadly blow. But she made no sound. A strong voice inside told her that she, of all people in the world, had to support him in his misery and not make it worse. This thought quickly helped her recover, even from such a terrible shock.

The judges had to take part in a public parade outside, so the court was adjourned. The loud noise and movement of the courtroom emptying through many exits was still going on when Lucie stood up, stretching out her arms toward her husband with nothing but love and comfort on her face.

“If only I could touch him! If only I could hug him just once! Oh, good citizens, if you would only have some pity on us!”

Only a jailer was left, along with two of the four guards who had arrested Darnay the night before, and Barsad. The rest of the crowd had poured out into the streets to watch the parade. Barsad suggested to the others, “Let her hug him, then; it's only for a moment.” They silently agreed, and they helped her over the seats in the hall to a high platform. There, by leaning over the wooden barrier, Darnay could fold her in his arms.

“Goodbye, dear darling of my soul. My parting blessing to you, my love. We will meet again, where the tired are at peace!”

These were her husband's words as he held her close to his chest.

“I can bear it, dear Charles. I am being supported from heaven; do not worry about me. Give a parting blessing for our child.”

“I send it to her through you. I kiss her through you. I say goodbye to her through you.”

“My husband. No! Just a moment!” He was pulling himself away from her. “We will not be separated for long. I feel that this will break my heart soon, but I will do my duty while I can. And when I leave her, God will bring friends to help her, just as He did for me.”

Her father had followed her, and would have fallen to his knees before them, but Darnay reached out and caught him, crying:

“No, no! What have you done, what have you done, that you should kneel to us! We know now what a struggle you went through in the past. We know now what you suffered when you suspected who my family was, and when you finally knew it. We know now the natural hatred you fought against and conquered for her dear sake. We thank you with all our hearts, and all our love and respect. May Heaven be with you!”

Her father's only answer was to run his hands through his white hair and wring them, letting out a cry of agony.

“It could not be any other way,” said the prisoner. “Everything has worked out exactly as it had to. It was my failed attempt to carry out my poor mother's last wishes that first brought my dangerous presence near you. Good could never come from such evil, and a happy ending was not possible after such a sad beginning. Find comfort, and forgive me. May Heaven bless you!”

As he was led away, his wife let go of him and stood watching him go, her hands pressed together in prayer. There was a bright look on her face, and even a comforting smile. As he walked through the prisoner's door, she turned, laid her head lovingly on her father's chest, tried to speak to him, and fainted at his feet.

Then, stepping out from the dark corner where he had stood without moving, Sydney Carton came over and picked her up. Only her father and Mr. Lorry were with her. Carton's arm shook as he lifted her and supported her head. Yet, there was something in his look that was not just pity—there was a glow of pride in it too.

“Shall I carry her to a carriage? She feels as light as a feather to me.”

He carried her easily to the door and laid her down gently in a carriage. Her father and their old friend got inside, and Carton took his seat next to the driver.

When they arrived at the courtyard gate where he had paused in the dark just hours earlier to imagine which paving stones her feet had touched, he lifted her again. He carried her up the stairs to their rooms. There, he laid her down on a couch, where her child and Miss Pross wept over her.

“Don't wake her up yet,” he said softly to Miss Pross. “She is better off like this. Don't bring her back to consciousness while she is only fainted.”

“Oh, Carton, Carton, dear Carton!” cried little Lucie, jumping up and throwing her arms tightly around him in a burst of tears. “Now that you are here, I know you will do something to help mamma and save papa! Oh, look at her, dear Carton! Can you, of all the people who love her, bear to see her like this?”

He bent over the child and rested her warm cheek against his face. He gently set her aside and looked at her unconscious mother.

“Before I go,” he said, and paused, “may I kiss her?”

It was remembered later that when he bent down and touched her face with his lips, he whispered a few words. The child, who was closest to him, told them later, and repeated them to her own grandchildren when she was an old lady, that she had heard him say, “A life you love.”

When he went into the next room, he turned suddenly to Mr. Lorry and her father, who were following him, and said to Dr. Manette:

“You had great influence only yesterday, Doctor Manette. Let us at least try to use it. These judges and all the men in power are very friendly to you and appreciate your services, don't they?”

“Nothing about Charles was kept from me. I had the strongest promises that I would save him, and I did.” He answered in great distress and very slowly.

“Try them again. The hours between now and tomorrow afternoon are few and short, but try.”

“I plan to try. I will not rest for a second.”

“That is good. I have seen energy like yours do great things before—though never,” he added with a smile and a sigh, “anything as great as this. But try! As worthless as life is when we waste it, it is worth this effort. If it weren't, it would be easy to give up.”

“I will go,” said Doctor Manette, “straight to the Prosecutor and the President, and I will go to others whom it is better not to name. I will write letters too, and—but wait! There is a festival in the streets, and no one will be reachable until dark.”

“That is true. Well, it is a desperate hope at best, and not much more desperate for being delayed until dark. I would like to know how you succeed, though remember, I expect nothing! When will you likely have seen these powerful men, Doctor Manette?”

“Right after dark, I hope. Within an hour or two from now.”

“It will be dark soon after four. Let us give it more time. If I go to Mr. Lorry's at nine, will I hear what you have done, either from our friend or from you?”

“Yes.”

“May you succeed!”

Mr. Lorry followed Sydney to the front door, and touching him on the shoulder as he was leaving, made him turn around.

“I have no hope,” said Mr. Lorry in a quiet, sad whisper.

“Neither do I.”

“Even if one of these men, or all of them, wanted to spare him—which is highly unlikely, for what is his life or any man's life to them!—I doubt they would dare to save him after that demonstration in the courtroom.”

“I agree. I heard the sound of the Guillotine's blade falling in those cheers.”

Mr. Lorry leaned his arm against the doorframe and rested his face on it.

“Don't lose hope,” Carton said very gently. “Don't grieve. I encouraged Doctor Manette to try because I felt it might one day bring her comfort. Otherwise, she might always think his life was pointlessly thrown away or wasted, and that thought would torture her.”

“Yes, yes, yes,” Mr. Lorry replied, wiping his eyes. “You are right. But he will die; there is no real hope.”

“Yes. He will die; there is no real hope,” Carton repeated.

And he walked with a steady, determined step downstairs.

CHAPTER XII: Darkness

Sydney Carton paused in the street, not quite sure where to go next. “At Tellson's Bank at nine,” he said with a thoughtful face. “Would it be a good idea to show myself in the meantime? I think so. It is best that these people know there is someone here who looks like Charles. It is a smart precaution, and it might be a necessary step. But I must be careful, careful, careful! Let me think this through!”

Stopping himself from walking too quickly toward his destination, he paced up and down the darkening street, tracing his plan to its possible consequences. His first instinct was confirmed. “It is best,” he decided at last, “that these people know someone like me is here.” And he turned toward the neighborhood of Saint Antoine.

Defarge had described himself in court that day as a wine-shop owner in Saint Antoine. For someone who knew Paris well, it was not hard to find the shop without asking directions. Once he located it, Carton left the narrow streets, ate dinner at a small restaurant, and fell fast asleep afterward. For the first time in many years, he did not touch any hard alcohol. Since the night before, he had drunk nothing but a little light wine, and last night he had poured his brandy onto Mr. Lorry's hearth, like a man who was finished with drinking for good.

It was seven o'clock when he woke up feeling refreshed and went back out into the streets. On his way to Saint Antoine, he stopped at a shop window with a mirror and adjusted his messy tie, his coat collar, and his wild hair. Once he was presentable, he walked straight to Defarge's wine-shop and went inside.

There were no customers in the shop except Jacques Three, the man with the twitchy fingers and the croaking voice. This man, whom Carton had seen on the jury, stood drinking at the small counter, talking with Monsieur and Madame Defarge. The Vengeance also joined in the conversation as if she were a regular employee.

When Carton walked in, sat down, and asked in very poor French for a small glass of wine, Madame Defarge glanced at him carelessly. Then she looked closer, and even closer, before walking over to him herself to ask what he had ordered.

He repeated his order.

“Are you English?” asked Madame Defarge, raising her dark eyebrows curiously.

He looked at her as if it took him a long time to understand even a single French word, then answered in a thick English accent. “Yes, madame, yes. I am English!”

Madame Defarge went back to her counter to pour the wine. As he picked up a revolutionary newspaper and pretended to struggle through reading it, he heard her whisper, “I swear to you, he looks just like Evrémonde!”

Defarge brought him the wine and said good evening.

“What?”

“Good evening.”

“Oh! Good evening, citizen,” Carton said, filling his glass. “Ah, and good wine. I drink to the Republic.”

Defarge went back to the counter and said, “He does look a little like him.” Madame Defarge snapped back, “I tell you, he looks a lot like him!” Jacques Three calmly remarked, “He is on your mind so much, you see, madame.” The Vengeance laughed and added, “Yes, indeed! And you are looking forward so much to seeing Evrémonde one last time tomorrow!”

Carton followed the lines of the newspaper with his finger, looking completely focused and absorbed. The group at the counter leaned close together, speaking quietly. After a brief silence, during which they all stared at him without breaking his focus on the revolutionary article, they went back to their conversation.

“What madame says is true,” Jacques Three agreed. “Why stop? There is great reason in that. Why stop?”

“Well, yes,” Defarge reasoned, “but we must stop somewhere. After all, the question is: where?”

“At total destruction,” Madame Defarge said.

“Magnificent!” croaked Jacques Three. The Vengeance also strongly agreed.

“Destroying them all is a good policy, my wife,” said Defarge, looking troubled. “Usually, I don't argue against it. But this Doctor has suffered so much. You saw him today; you saw his face when his letter was read.”

“I saw his face!” Madame Defarge repeated angrily and scornfully. “Yes, I saw it. I saw that his face is not the face of a true friend of the Republic. He had better watch his step!”

“And you must have seen, my wife,” Defarge said softly, “the terrible pain of his daughter, which must be agonizing for him!”

“I have watched his daughter,” Madame Defarge repeated. “Yes, I have watched her more than once. I saw her today, and I've seen her on other days. I've watched her in the courtroom, and I've watched her standing in the street near the prison. If I only lift my finger—!” She raised her finger—while Carton pretended to keep reading—and let it drop hard on the counter, making a sound like the heavy blade of the Guillotine falling.

“The citizenness is magnificent!” croaked the juror.

“She is an angel!” said The Vengeance, hugging her.

“As for you,” Madame Defarge continued, looking coldly at her husband, “if it were up to you—which, thank goodness, it is not—you would save this man even now.”

“No!” Defarge protested. “Not even if lifting this glass could do it! But I would stop there. I say, let us stop with Darnay.”

“Listen to me, Jacques,” Madame Defarge said furiously, “and listen to me too, my little Vengeance. Listen to both of us! I have had this whole family name written down in my secret register for a long time, doomed to total destruction for their crimes as cruel tyrants. Ask my husband if that is true.”

“It is true,” Defarge agreed, without even being asked.

“At the start of our great revolution, when the Bastille fell, he found the letter read in court today. He brought it home, and in the middle of the night, when this shop was empty and locked, we read it right here by the light of this lamp. Ask him if that is true.”

“It is true,” Defarge agreed.

“That night, when we finished reading the paper and the lamp went out, as daylight started shining through the shutters and bars, I told him I had a secret to share. Ask him if that is true.”

“It is true,” Defarge agreed again.

“I told him that secret. I beat my chest with my hands, just as I do now, and I told him, ‘Defarge, I was raised among seashore fishermen. That peasant family so horribly treated by the Evrémonde brothers, which the Bastille letter describes, was _my_ family. Defarge, that sister of the dying boy on the ground was my sister. Her husband was my brother-in-law. Their unborn child was my niece or nephew. That boy was my brother, that father was my father. Those dead are my dead, and it is up to me to make sure those crimes are answered for!’ Ask him if that is true.”

“It is true,” Defarge agreed once more.

“Then go tell the Wind and the Fire where to stop,” Madame Defarge replied, “but do not tell me.”

Both of her listeners took a dark pleasure in her deadly anger—Carton could tell how pale and intense she was, even without looking at her—and both praised her. Defarge, standing alone, tried to say a few words in memory of the kind wife of the Marquis, but his wife only repeated her response: “Tell the Wind and the Fire where to stop; not me!”

More customers entered, and the group split up. The English customer paid for his wine, pretended to struggle to count his change, and asked for directions to the National Palace. Madame Defarge walked him to the door, placing her hand on his arm to point down the street. Carton could not help thinking that it would be a good deed to grab that arm, lift it, and plunge a knife deep into her heart.

But he walked away and was soon lost in the shadow of the prison wall. At the agreed time, he left the darkness and went to Mr. Lorry's room, where he found the old gentleman pacing back and forth in great worry. Mr. Lorry said he had been with Lucie until just now, leaving her only to keep his meeting with Carton. Her father had not been seen since he left the bank around four o'clock. Lucie had a tiny bit of hope that her father's influence might save Charles, but it was very small. He had been gone for more than five hours; where could he be?

Mr. Lorry waited until ten o'clock. Since Dr. Manette still had not returned, and Mr. Lorry did not want to leave Lucie alone any longer, they decided that he would go back to her and return to the bank at midnight. In the meantime, Carton would wait by the fire for the Doctor.

Carton waited and waited until the clock struck midnight, but Dr. Manette did not return. Mr. Lorry came back, having heard no news and bringing none. Where could the Doctor be?

They were discussing this and trying to find some small hope in his long absence when they heard him walking up the stairs. The moment he walked into the room, it was clear that all hope was gone.

Whether he had actually met with anyone or had spent all those hours just wandering the streets was never known. As he stood there staring at them, they did not ask any questions because his face told them everything.

“I can't find it,” he said, “ and I must have it. Where is it?”

His head and neck were bare, and as he spoke with a helpless, wandering look, he took off his coat and let it drop onto the floor.

“Where is my shoemaker's bench? I've been looking everywhere for my bench and I can't find it. What have they done with my work? Time is running out; I must finish those shoes.”

Mr. Lorry and Carton looked at each other, their hearts sinking.

“Come on, come on!” Dr. Manette whimpered in a miserable voice. “Let me get to work. Give me my tools.”

When they did not answer, he pulled his hair and stamped his feet on the ground like a panicked child.

“Don't torture a poor, miserable man,” he begged with a terrible cry. “Just give me my work! What will happen to us if these shoes are not finished tonight?”

He was lost, completely lost!

It was so obviously useless to argue with him or try to bring him back to his senses that—as if they had agreed without speaking—they each placed a hand on his shoulder and gently guided him to sit by the fire, promising that he would have his work soon. He sank into the chair, staring sadly at the hot coals and crying. It was as if everything that had happened since his release from the attic room in Paris was just a passing dream; Mr. Lorry saw him shrink back into the exact same broken state he was in when Defarge first kept him.

Although they were both deeply moved and terrified by this sight of his mental collapse, it was not a time to give in to sadness. His poor daughter, now stripped of her last hope and support, needed them too much. Again, as if by silent agreement, they looked at each other with the same thought in their minds. Carton spoke first:

“The last chance is gone; it wasn't much anyway. Yes, he had better be taken back to Lucie. But before you go, will you listen to me carefully for a moment? Don't ask me why I am making these demands, or why I need you to promise what I'm about to ask. I have a reason—a very good one.”

“I don't doubt it,” Mr. Lorry replied. “Tell me.”

The broken doctor sat in the chair between them, rocking back and forth and moaning. They spoke in the quiet tones people use when watching over a sickbed in the middle of the night.

Carton bent down to pick up the Doctor's coat, which was lying near his feet. As he did, a small leather case that the Doctor used to carry his daily schedule fell onto the floor. Carton picked it up and found a folded paper inside. “We should look at this!” he said. Mr. Lorry nodded. Carton opened it and exclaimed, “Thank _God_!”

“What is it?” Mr. Lorry asked eagerly.

“Just a moment! Let me explain that in a second. First,” he reached into his coat and pulled out another paper, “this is the travel pass that allows me to leave the city. Look at it. You see—Sydney Carton, an Englishman?”

Mr. Lorry held it open, looking at Carton's serious face.

“Keep it for me until tomorrow. Remember, I am going to see Charles tomorrow, and it is better if I don't bring this pass into the prison.”

“Why not?”

“I'm not sure; I just prefer not to. Now, take this paper that Doctor Manette had on him. It is a similar travel pass, allowing him, his daughter, and her child to pass through the city gates and cross the border at any time! You see?”

“Yes!”

“Perhaps he got it yesterday as a final precaution. When is it dated? Actually, it doesn't matter; don't waste time looking now. Put it away safely with mine and yours. Listen carefully! I didn't know until a couple of hours ago that he even had such a paper. It is valid until it is canceled. But it might be canceled very soon, and I have reason to believe it will be.”

“Are they in danger?”

“They are in extreme danger. They are about to be accused by Madame Defarge. I know it because I heard it from her own mouth tonight. What I overheard showed me their danger in terrifying detail. I didn't waste any time, and since then I have met with Barsad the spy. He confirmed it. He knows that a wood-cutter living by the prison wall is controlled by the Defarges, and that Madame Defarge has coached him to testify that he saw her”—he avoided saying Lucie's name—“making signals to the prisoners. It is easy to see that they will accuse her of plotting a prison escape. It will cost her life, and probably her child's, and her father's too—since both have been seen with her near the prison. Don't look so horrified. You are going to save them all.”

“May God help me do it, Carton! But how?”

“I am going to tell you how. It will all depend on you, and there is no better man for the job. This new accusation won't happen until after tomorrow—probably not for two or three days, or even a week. You know that it is a crime punishable by death to mourn for or show sympathy for anyone executed by the Guillotine. Lucie and her father will definitely show their grief, and Madame Defarge—who will never stop hunting them—will wait for that to happen to make her case even stronger and doubly sure. Do you understand?”

“I am listening so closely, and with so much trust in you, that for a moment,” Mr. Lorry said, touching the back of the Doctor's chair, “I even forgot about this terrible situation.”

“You have money, and you can buy carriage passage to the coast as fast as possible. You have already finished your preparations to return to England. Early tomorrow, have your horses and carriage ready so they are set to leave at two o'clock in the afternoon.”

“It will be done!”

Carton's manner was so passionate and inspiring that Mr. Lorry caught his excitement, moving as quickly as a young man.

“You have a noble heart. Did I say we could depend on no better man? Tonight, tell her about the danger to her child and her father. Focus on that, because she would gladly die with her husband if it were just her own life at stake.” He paused for a second, then went on. “For the sake of her child and her father, convince her that she must leave Paris with them and you at that exact hour. Tell her it was her husband's last request. Tell her that more depends on this than she can possibly imagine. You believe her father, even in this broken state, will do whatever she asks, don't you?”

“I am sure of it.”

“I thought so. Quietly and steadily make all these arrangements here in the courtyard, even sitting in your seat in the carriage. The moment I come to you, pull me in and drive away.”

“So I must wait for you, no matter what happens?”

“You have my travel pass with the others, and you will save my seat. Wait for nothing except for me to get into that seat, and then drive straight for England!”

“Well, then,” said Mr. Lorry, gripping Carton's eager but steady hand, “it won't all depend on one old man. I will have a young, passionate man at my side.”

“With Heaven's help, you will! Promise me solemnly that nothing will make you change the plan we have just agreed to.”

“Nothing, Carton.”

“Remember these words tomorrow: if you change the plan, or delay it for any reason, no one can be saved, and many lives will be lost.”

“I will remember them. I hope to do my part faithfully.”

“And I hope to do mine. Now, goodbye!”

Although he said this with a serious, earnest smile, and even kissed the old man's hand, he did not leave him just yet. He helped Mr. Lorry wake up the rocking doctor by the dying fire, putting a coat and hat on him, and leading him outside under the pretense of finding the shoemaker's bench and tools he kept crying for. Carton walked on the other side of him, protecting the broken doctor all the way back to the courtyard of the house where Lucie—who had been so happy back when Carton first confessed his lonely heart to her—was staying awake through this terrible night. He entered the courtyard and stood alone for a few moments, looking up at the light in her window. Before he walked away, he whispered a blessing toward the window, and a final goodbye.

CHAPTER XIII: Fifty-two

In the dark prison of the Conciergerie, the people condemned to die that day waited for their fate. There were fifty-two of them, matching the number of weeks in a year. That afternoon, fifty-two lives would be carried away on the city's tide of death to the endless ocean of eternity. Before they even left their cells, new prisoners were assigned to replace them. Before their blood could join the blood spilled yesterday, the blood of those to be executed tomorrow was already being chosen.

Exactly fifty-two people were counted off. They ranged from a seventy-year-old wealthy tax collector, whose money could not buy his freedom, to a twenty-year-old seamstress, whose poverty and simple life could not save her. Just as physical diseases, born of human vice and neglect, strike down people of all classes, this terrifying moral sickness, born of unspeakable suffering, unbearable oppression, and cruel indifference, struck everyone down without distinction.

Charles Darnay, alone in a cell, had not fooled himself with any false hopes since returning from the courtroom. In every line of the story he had heard read, he heard his death sentence. He fully understood that no personal influence could save him now, that he was essentially condemned by millions of people, and that a few individuals could do nothing to help him.

Still, with the face of his beloved wife fresh in his memory, it was not easy to prepare his mind for what was coming. His grip on life was strong, and it was very, very hard to let go. As he slowly pried one finger loose, another gripped even tighter; and when he forced his hand to open, it clamped shut again. His thoughts were racing, and his heart pounded with a wild energy that fought against giving up. If he felt at peace with his fate for even a second, the thought of his wife and child living on without him made his peace feel selfish.

But that was only at first. Before long, the thought that there was no shame in the death he faced, and that many others went to the same death unjustly and stood strong every day, helped encourage him. Next came the thought that much of his family's future peace of mind depended on his quiet courage. So, step by step, he calmed down, lifting his thoughts to higher things and finding comfort.

Before darkness fell on the night of his sentencing, he had traveled this far along his path to death. Being allowed to buy writing materials and a candle, he sat down to write until the prison lights were put out.

He wrote a long letter to Lucie, explaining that he had known nothing about her father's time in prison until she told him about it. He had been just as ignorant as she was about his father's and uncle's responsibility for that suffering until the paper was read in court. He reminded her that hiding his real family name from her was the one condition—which made complete sense now—that her father had placed on their engagement, and the one promise he had kept on their wedding morning. He begged her, for her father's sake, never to try to find out if her father had forgotten about the paper or had been reminded of it by the story of the Tower on that old Sunday under the plane tree in the garden. Darnay wrote that if Doctor Manette had remembered it at all, he surely thought it was destroyed when the Bastille fell, since it wasn't mentioned among the records found there. He begged her—though he knew he didn't need to ask—to comfort her father by gently showing him that he had done nothing to blame himself for, but had always put himself last for their sakes. After asking her to hold onto his grateful love and blessing, and to overcome her grief to focus on their dear child, he begged her, by their hope of meeting in Heaven, to comfort her father.

To her father, he wrote in the same way, but he explicitly left his wife and child in the Doctor's care. He wrote this very strongly, hoping to keep the Doctor from falling into despair or dwelling on the dangerous past, which Darnay feared might happen.

To Mr. Lorry, he entrusted the family and explained his financial affairs. Once that was done, along with many words of grateful friendship and love, his task was complete. He never thought of Carton. His mind was so full of his family that he did not think of him once.

He had time to finish these letters before the lights were turned off. When he lay down on his straw bed, he believed he was done with this world for good.

But the world pulled him back in his sleep, showing itself in beautiful dreams. Free and happy, back in the old house in Soho (though the dream house looked nothing like the real one), mysteriously released and lighthearted, he was with Lucie again. She told him it was all a dream and that he had never left. After a moment of blankness, he dreamed he had suffered and come back to her, dead and at peace, yet looking exactly the same. Another moment of sleep, and then he woke up in the gloomy morning, not knowing where he was or what had happened, until it flashed in his mind: “This is the day of my death!”

So he had lived through the hours to the day when the fifty-two heads were to fall. Now, though he was calm and hoped to face the end with quiet courage, a new train of thought began in his mind that was very hard to control.

He had never seen the Guillotine that was to end his life. How high was it from the ground? How many steps did it have? Where would he stand? How would they touch him, and would the hands that touched him be stained red? Which way would his face be turned? Would he be the first to die, or the last? These and many similar questions, which he did not want to think about, forced themselves into his mind over and over again. It was not out of fear; he felt no fear. Rather, it came from a strange, nagging desire to know what to do when the moment arrived—a desire far larger than the few quick seconds it concerned. It was a curiosity that felt more like it belonged to some other spirit inside him than to himself.

The hours passed as he walked back and forth, and the clocks struck the hours he would never hear again. Nine o'clock gone forever, ten o'clock gone forever, eleven o'clock gone forever, and twelve o'clock coming and going. After a hard struggle with the strange thoughts that had bothered him, he finally overcame them. He walked up and down, softly repeating the names of his loved ones to himself. The worst of the struggle was over. He could walk back and forth, free from distracting thoughts, praying for himself and for them.

Twelve o'clock gone forever.

He had been told that the execution was at three o'clock, and he knew he would be called earlier, since the death carts traveled heavily and slowly through the streets. Therefore, he decided to focus on two o'clock as the final hour, using the time before then to build up his strength so he could help others when the time came.

Walking steadily back and forth with his arms crossed over his chest—looking very different from the panicked prisoner who had paced in La Force prison—he heard the clock strike one without surprise. The hour had felt like any other hour. Deeply thankful to Heaven for regaining his self-control, he thought, “There is only one hour left now,” and turned to walk again.

Footsteps in the stone hallway outside the door. He stopped.

A key was put in the lock and turned. Before the door opened, a man said in a quiet English voice: “He has never seen me here; I have stayed out of his sight. Go in alone; I'll wait nearby. Hurry!”

The door was quickly opened and closed, and there standing face-to-face with him, quiet and focused, with a gentle smile and a warning finger on his lips, was Sydney Carton.

There was something so bright and unusual in Carton's look that, at first, Darnay thought he was just a ghost created by his own imagination. But Carton spoke, and it was truly his voice; he took Darnay's hand, and it was a real, solid grip.

“Of all the people on earth, I'm the last one you expected to see?” he said.

“I couldn't believe it was you. I can barely believe it now. You aren't”—a sudden fear struck him—“a prisoner?”

“No. By chance, I have leverage over one of the guards here, and that is why I am standing in front of you. I come from her—your wife, dear Darnay.”

Darnay squeezed his hand.

“I bring you a request from her.”

“What is it?”

“A very earnest, urgent, and important plea, spoken in the saddest tones of the voice you love so much and remember so well.”

Darnay turned his face away slightly.

“You don't have time to ask why I bring this request or what it means, and I don't have time to tell you. You must obey—take off your boots and put on mine.”

There was a chair against the cell wall behind the prisoner. Carton pushed forward and, with lightning speed, forced Darnay down into the chair and stood over him, barefoot.

“Put on my boots. Reach down and pull them on; focus your mind. Quick!”

“Carton, there is no escaping from this place; it's impossible. You will only die with me. It is madness.”

“It would be madness if I asked you to escape, but did I? When I actually ask you to walk out that door, then you can call me crazy and stay here. Change your tie for mine, and your coat for mine. While you do that, let me take the ribbon from your hair and shake it out so it looks like mine!”

With incredible speed and a strength of will and action that seemed almost superhuman, Carton forced all these changes on him. Darnay was like a little child in his hands.

“Carton! Dear Carton! This is madness. It can't be done; it has been tried before and always fails. I beg you not to add your death to the bitterness of my own.”

“Am I asking you to walk out the door, my dear Darnay? When I ask that, you can say no. There is a pen, ink, and paper on this table. Is your hand steady enough to write?”

“It was before you came in.”

“Steady it again, and write what I tell you. Quick, friend, quick!”

Holding his confused head in his hand, Darnay sat at the table. Carton stood close beside him with his right hand hidden inside his coat.

“Write exactly what I say.”

“Who is this to?”

“To no one.” Carton still kept his hand inside his coat.

“Should I date it?”

“No.”

Darnay looked up at each question. Carton stood over him, looking down, with his hand still inside his coat.

“‘If you remember,’” Carton dictated, “‘the words we spoke to each other long ago, you will understand this when you see it. I know you remember them. It is not in your nature to forget them.’”

Carton was drawing his hand out of his coat. When Darnay happened to look up in surprise as he wrote, Carton's hand stopped, holding onto something.

“Have you written ‘forget them’?” Carton asked.

“I have. Is that a weapon in your hand?”

“No, I am not armed.”

“What is in your hand?”

“You will find out in a moment. Keep writing; there are only a few words left.” He dictated again. “‘I am thankful that the time has come when I can prove those words. That I am doing this is no reason for regret or sadness.’” As Carton spoke, keeping his eyes fixed on Darnay, his hand slowly and gently drifted down close to Darnay's face.

The pen slipped from Darnay's fingers onto the table, and he looked around blankly.

“What is that smell?” he asked.

“Smell?”

“Something that just passed by me?”

“I don't smell anything; there can't be anything here. Pick up the pen and finish writing. Hurry, hurry!”

As if his mind were failing or his thoughts were confused, Darnay tried to focus. He stared at Carton with foggy eyes, his breathing changing, while Carton—keeping his hand in his coat once more—looked steadily back at him.

“Hurry, hurry!”

Darnay bent over the paper again.

“‘If it had been otherwise,’” Carton's hand crept watchfully and gently down again. “‘I never would have used this longer opportunity. If it had been otherwise,’” Carton's hand was now right at Darnay's face. “‘I would only have had more to answer for. If it had been otherwise—’” Carton looked at the pen and saw that the writing was trailing off into squiggles.

Carton did not hide his hand inside his coat again. Darnay jumped up with a look of surprise and blame, but Carton pressed the drugged cloth firmly over his nose and mouth, catching him around the waist with his left arm. For a few seconds, Darnay struggled weakly against the man who had come to die for him; but within a minute, he collapsed unconscious on the floor.

Quickly, and with hands as steady as his heart, Carton dressed himself in the clothes Darnay had taken off. He combed back his hair and tied it with Darnay's hair ribbon. Then he called softly, “Come in! Come in!” and Barsad the spy entered.

“You see?” said Carton, looking up as he knelt beside the unconscious man, putting the letter into his coat pocket. “Is your risk very big?”

“Mr. Carton,” the spy answered, snapping his fingers nervously, “my risk is not even _that_ much in all the busy rush here, as long as you keep your end of the deal.”

“Do not fear. I will keep my word to the death.”

“You must, Mr. Carton, if our count of fifty-two is to be correct. Since you will replace him in those clothes, I have no fear.”

“Have no fear! I will soon be gone where I cannot harm you, and the others will soon be far away, God willing! Now, get some help and take him to the carriage.”

“You?” the spy asked nervously.

“Him, man, the one I swapped places with! You are going out through the same gate you brought me in, aren't you?”

“Of course.”

“I was weak and dizzy when you brought me in, and I am even weaker now that you are taking me out. The final goodbye was too much for me. This sort of thing has happened here many times. Your life is in your own hands. Quick, call for help!”

“You swear you won't betray me?” the trembling spy asked, pausing one last time.

“Man, man!” Carton replied, stamping his foot. “Haven't I already sworn a solemn oath to go through with this? Why are you wasting precious time? Take him to the courtyard yourself, put him in the carriage yourself, show him to Mr. Lorry yourself, tell Mr. Lorry to give him nothing but fresh air to wake him up, tell him to remember my words and promise from last night, and drive away!”

The spy left, and Carton sat at the table, resting his head in his hands. The spy returned immediately with two helpers.

“What's this?” one of them said, looking down at the unconscious man. “Is he so sad to find that his friend has won a ticket to Saint Guillotine?”

“A good patriot,” said the other, “could hardly be sadder if the aristocrat had escaped.”

They lifted the unconscious Darnay, placed him on a stretcher they had brought to the door, and carried him away.

“Time is short, Evrémonde,” the spy said in a warning voice.

“I know it well,” Carton replied. “Take care of my friend, I beg you, and leave me.”

“Come on, my men,” said Barsad. “Lift him and let's go!”

The door closed, and Carton was left alone. Listening as hard as he could, he waited for any sound of suspicion or alarm. There was none. Keys turned, doors slammed, and footsteps echoed down distant hallways; no one shouted or rushed in a way that seemed unusual. Breathing a little easier after a while, he sat down at the table and listened until the clock struck two.

Sounds that did not frighten him, because he knew what they meant, began to echo. Several cell doors were opened one after another, and finally his own. A jailer holding a list looked in and simply said, “Follow me, Evrémonde!” Carton followed him down to a large, dark room. It was a gloomy winter day, and between the shadows inside and out, he could barely see the other prisoners who had been brought there to have their arms tied. Some were standing, some were sitting. A few were crying and pacing restlessly, but they were the minority. The great majority were silent and still, staring fixedly at the ground.

As Carton stood against the wall in a dark corner while others of the fifty-two were brought in, one man stopped as he passed to hug him, thinking he knew him. This filled Carton with a terrible fear of being discovered, but the man walked on. A few moments later, a young woman with a slender, girlish figure, a sweet, pale face, and large, patient eyes, got up from her seat and walked over to speak to him.

“Citizen Evrémonde,” she said, touching him with her cold hand. “I am a poor seamstress who was in La Force prison with you.”

He whispered in reply: “Yes. I forget, what were you accused of?”

“Plotting. Though Heaven knows I am completely innocent. Is it likely? Who would think to plot with a poor, weak little creature like me?”

The sad little smile she gave touched him so deeply that tears came to his eyes.

“I am not afraid to die, Citizen Evrémonde, but I did nothing wrong. I am willing to die if it helps the Republic, which is supposed to do so much good for us poor people. But I don't see how my death can help anyone, Citizen Evrémonde. I am such a poor, weak little thing!”

As the very last thing on earth to touch his heart, he felt a deep warmth and sympathy for this poor girl.

“I heard you were set free, Citizen Evrémonde. I hoped it was true.”

“It was. But I was arrested again and sentenced to death.”

“If I can ride in the cart with you, Citizen Evrémonde, will you let me hold your hand? I am not afraid, but I am small and weak, and it will give me courage.”

As she raised her patient eyes to his face, he saw a sudden doubt in them, followed by shock. He squeezed her thin, work-worn, hungry fingers and pressed them to his lips.

“Are you dying for him?” she whispered.

“And for his wife and child. Hush! Yes.”

“Oh, will you let me hold your brave hand, stranger?”

“Hush! Yes, my poor sister; all the way to the end.”

*****

The same shadows falling on the prison were also falling, at that very hour of the early afternoon, on the city gates. A crowd stood around as a carriage leaving Paris pulled up to be checked.

“Who goes there? Who is inside? Let me see your papers!”

The papers were handed out and read.

“Alexandre Manette. Doctor. French. Which one is he?”

This was he—the helpless, mumbling, confused old man they pointed to.

“It seems the Citizen-Doctor is not in his right mind? The fever of the Revolution must have been too much for him?”

Much too much for him.

“Hah! Many people suffer from it. Lucie. His daughter. French. Which one is she?”

This was she.

“Clearly it is. Lucie, the wife of Evrémonde, correct?”

Yes, it is.

“Hah! Evrémonde has an appointment elsewhere. Lucie, her child. English. Is this she?”

She and no other.

“Kiss me, child of Evrémonde. Now you have kissed a good Republican—something new in your family! Remember it. Sydney Carton. Lawyer. English. Which one is he?”

He was lying in the corner of the carriage. They pointed him out too.

“It seems the English lawyer has fainted?”

They hoped he would wake up in the fresh air. They explained that he was in poor health and had just said a sad goodbye to a friend who was in trouble with the Republic.

“Is that all? That's not much! Many people are in trouble with the Republic, and have to look out the little window of the Guillotine. Jarvis Lorry. Banker. English. Which one is he?”

“I am he. Naturally, since I'm the last one.”

Jarvis Lorry had answered all the previous questions. He had stepped down from the carriage and stood with his hand on the door, talking to a group of officials. They slowly walked around the carriage and slowly climbed up to check the small amount of luggage on the roof. The local peasants standing nearby crowded closer to the carriage doors, staring greedily inside. A mother held out her little child's hand so it could touch the wife of the aristocrat who was going to the Guillotine.

“Here are your papers, Jarvis Lorry, stamped and signed.”

“We may leave, citizen?”

“You may leave. Go on, drivers! Have a safe trip!”

“Thank you, citizens.—And the first danger is passed!”

These were Mr. Lorry's words as he folded his hands and looked up at the sky. There was terror and crying in the carriage, along with the heavy breathing of the unconscious traveler.

“Aren't we going too slowly? Can't we make them go faster?” Lucie asked, clinging to the old man.

“It would look like we are fleeing, my darling. I must not push them too hard; it would make them suspicious.”

“Look back, look back, and see if anyone is chasing us!”

“The road is empty, my dearest. So far, no one is chasing us.”

Houses passed by in twos and threes, along with lonely farms, ruined buildings, tanneries, open fields, and rows of leafless trees. The carriage rode over rough, bumpy stone roads, with deep mud on both sides. Sometimes we drove into the mud to avoid the stones that shook us so violently; sometimes we got stuck in ruts and puddles. The torture of waiting was so great that in our panic and hurry, we felt like jumping out of the carriage to run and hide—anything was better than sitting still.

Out of the open country and back among ruined buildings, lonely farms, tanneries, cottages, and leafless trees. Had the drivers tricked us and taken us back toward Paris? Was this the same place we passed before? Thank goodness, no. It was a village. Look back, look back, and see if we are pursued! Shh, here is the carriage station.

Slowly, our four horses were unhitched. Slowly, the carriage stood horse-less in the street, looking like it would never move again. Slowly, the new horses appeared one by one. Slowly, the new drivers followed, braiding the leather of their whips. Slowly, the old drivers counted their tips, did their math wrong, and grumbled about the results. All this time, our terrified hearts were beating at a speed that would outrun the fastest horses in the world.

At last, the new drivers were in their saddles and the old ones were left behind. We drove through the village, up and down a hill, and onto wet, low ground. Suddenly, the drivers called out to each other with wild gestures, and the horses were pulled to a sudden halt, nearly sliding on their hind legs. Are we being chased?

“Hey! Inside the carriage! Answer me!”

“What's wrong?” Mr. Lorry asked, looking out the window.

“How many did they say today?”

“I don't understand you.”

“At the last station. How many were sent to the Guillotine today?”

“Fifty-two.”

“I knew it! What a great number! My friend here thought it was only forty-two; ten extra heads are always good to have. The Guillotine is doing fine work. I love it. Move on! Giddy up!”

Night fell dark. The traveler in the corner moved more; he was starting to wake up and speak clearly. He thought they were still in the cell together. He asked Carton, calling him by name, what Carton had in his hand. Oh, have mercy on us, kind Heaven, and help us! Look out, look out, and see if we are being chased.

The wind rushed after us, the clouds flew above us, the moon followed us, and the whole wild night seemed to be chasing us; but, so far, nothing else was pursuing us.

CHAPTER XIV: The Knitting Done
